Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin
תלמוד ירושלמי קידושין
The Jerusalem Talmud, translation and commentary by Heinrich W. Guggenheimer. Berlin, De Gruyter, 1999-2015
https://www.nli.org.il/he/books/NNL_ALEPH001901012/NLI

Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin



Chapter 1



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A wife may be acquired<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In matters of criminal law, any relations the woman might have with another man would be adultery. The husband cannot live with his wife (and does not acquire any dowry she might bring with her) unless he accepts the financial responsibilities incumbent upon a husband in a public ceremony, attended by at least 10 witnesses (cf. Peah 6:2, Note 46). The acquisition, or betrothal, mentioned here only needs the presence of two witnesses.</i> in three ways; she regains her autonomy in two ways. She is acquired by money<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The woman agrees to be preliminarily married for a monetary consideration given in the presence of two witnesses.</i>, or by a document<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">A written promise of marriage.</i>, or by intercourse<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Attested to by two witnesses. It is enough for the witnesses to confirm that the parties were together in a locked bedroom for a period of time sufficient for intercourse. Since one speaks of preliminary marriage, the couple is in principle forbidden to live together after such intercourse and before the definitive marriage. There is a midrashic doctrine that no woman can become pregnant from her first intercourse unless she had lesbian or autoerotic experiences [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 45:5" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.45.5"><i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i>45(5</a>), 51(11); <i>Pesiqta rabbati</i> 42, p. 177].</i>. By money, the House of Shammai say, by a denar<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">A Roman silver denar; under Augustus and his first successors about 3.5 g of silver.</i> or a denar’s value; but the House of Hillel say, by a <i>peruṭa</i> or a <i>peruṭa’s</i> value. How much is a <i>peruṭa</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>peruṭa</i> was a small Hasmonean copper coin which disappeared with the end of the Jewish commonwealth, more than 100 years before the compilation of the Mishnah. Therefore, its value has to be defined in terms of Roman coins.</i>? One eighth of an Italic <i>assarius</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Corresponding to <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup>/<sub>192</sub> of a denar. <i>Assarius</i> is the old name of the Roman <i>as</i>. In rabbinic practice, a <i>peruṭa</i> is defined as half a grain (<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup>/<sub>960</sub> of a troy oz. or 0.0324 g) of sterling silver.</i>. She regains her autonomy by a bill of divorce or by the husband’s death. A sister-in-law<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The widow of a brother who died childless; cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Yebamot</i>.</i> is acquired<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In definitive marriage. By biblical standards there is no preliminary levirate marriage.</i> by intercourse; she regains her autonomy by <i>ḥaliṣah</i> or by the brother-in-law’s death<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Before she entered the levirate marriage. Once she is married, she is a wife in all respects and regains her autonomy by divorce or widowhood.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A wife may be acquired in three ways,” etc. So is the Mishnah: Either by money, or by document, or by intercourse. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated as follows: Not only by all three together, but even by any one of them.
By money, from where? “After he acquires,” this tells you that she is acquired by money.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 2a" href="/Kiddushin.2a">Babli 2a</a>. In rabbinic Hebrew, לקח means “to buy”. The Babli shows from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 23:13" href="/Genesis.23.13"><i>Gen</i>. 23:13</a> that לקח in biblical Hebrew may mean “to accept a deal involving money”. By the talmudic doctrine of invariability of lexemes in biblical language, the same meaning must apply in all cases.</i> By marital relations, from where? “And has marital relations with her,” this tells you that she is acquired by intercourse<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 9b" href="/Kiddushin.9b">Babli 9b</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>: “After a man takes a wife and has marital relations with her” is the preamble to the rules of divorce. The verse is read to mean: “After a man buys and/or has marital relations with a woman, if he desires to terminate the relationship he is required to follow the rules of divorce.” Since there is no divorce without a preceding marriage, it follows that acquiring a wife by money and/or intercourse establishes marriage by biblical standards.</i>. I would say, by both together. Money without intercourse and intercourse without money, from where? <sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">This elliptic statement is shortened from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 3:6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.3.6.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 3:6 (Notes 88–92</a>). The man who sleeps with a virgin preliminarily married to another man is stoned; one who sleeps with a married woman who is not a virgin is strangled. Since the verse mentions only intercourse, it is deduced that even if no money was given, the girl is legally bound to her husband and the seducer or rapist is executed for adultery.</i> Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, it is written: “If a man is found lying with a woman having had intercourse with her husband.” Think of it, even if he did only acquire her by intercourse, the one coming after him is [executed] by strangulation. Not only regular intercourse but even perverse<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Sex play which leads to satisfaction without penetration and ejaculation.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, it is necessary to mention perverse intercourse for if it were regular, why mention her husband<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">If the verse was simply intended to state that the punishment of the adulterer with a non-virgin is different from that with a virgin, it would not have mentioned “her husband”. The husband makes his wife a non-virgin even by perverse intercourse, any other man only by penetration.</i>? As we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 7:11:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.7.11.1">Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i> 7:15</a>.</i>: “If she was raped by two men, the first is stoned, the second strangled<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">If the first one penetrated her.</i>.”
So we learned intercourse without money. Money without intercourse, from where? “She shall go free, without money.” “If he did acquire another one.”<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:11" href="/Exodus.21.11"><i>Ex</i>. 21:11</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:10" href="/Exodus.21.10">10</a>, speaking of the “Hebrew slave girl”, the underage girl sold by her father with the understanding that the buyer may use the amount he paid for the girl as bride-money in case he wants to marry her or give her to his son. This certainly is marriage by money. V. 11 notes that if she is not married either within 6 years or by the time she reaches adulthood, she goes free; the money given for her was to buy her working power. V. 10 states that once she is married, she cannot be treated differently from any other wife. It is inferred that as a matter of principle her acquisition cannot be different from that of any other wife.</i> Since one was by money, so the other is by money<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Simeon bar Ioḥai</i> (p. 167) the argument is inverted: Since in general a woman may be acquired by a document, so Hebrew slave girls may be acquired by a document.</i>.
By a document? “He shall write her a bill of divorce, hand it to her, and send her out of his house. If she left his house and went to be another man’s.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>.</i>” It brackets her being with her leaving<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 5a" href="/Kiddushin.5a">Babli 5a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 15b" href="/Eruvin.15b"><i>Erubin</i> 15b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 25a" href="/Sukkah.25a"><i>Sukkah</i>25a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 47a" href="/Ketubot.47a"><i>Ketubot</i> 47a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 21b" href="/Gittin.21b"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 21b</a>; <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 269, end.</i>. Since her leaving was by a document, so her being is by a document.
Rebbi Abin said that Ḥisqiah stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifry Deut</i>. 268; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 4b" href="/Kiddushin.4b">Babli 4b</a>.</i>“After he acquires,” which means that she is acquired by money<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 2a" href="/Kiddushin.2a">Babli 2a</a>. In rabbinic Hebrew, לקח means “to buy”. The Babli shows from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 23:13" href="/Genesis.23.13"><i>Gen</i>. 23:13</a> that לקח in biblical Hebrew may mean “to accept a deal involving money”. By the talmudic doctrine of invariability of lexemes in biblical language, the same meaning must apply in all cases.</i>. Would it not be an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i>?<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Then the verse would be redundant.</i> Since the Hebrew slave girl, who cannot be acquired by intercourse, can be acquired by money, would it not be logical that this one, who may be acquired by intercourse, should be acquired by money? This is proved<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">In these kinds of arguments, “proves” means “disproves the possibility of an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i>” because the rules are not comparable.</i> by the sister-in-law, who cannot be acquired by money but is acquired by intercourse<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In definitive marriage. By biblical standards there is no preliminary levirate marriage.</i>. Therefore you would not be astonished if this one, who may be acquired by intercourse, might not be acquired by money. The verse says, “after he acquires,” this tells you that she is acquired by money<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The verse is necessary to understand the rules.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifry Deut</i>. 268; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 5a" href="/Kiddushin.5a">Babli 5a</a>. The argument is completely parallel to that of the preceding paragraph.</i>“And has marital relations with her,” this tells you that she is acquired by intercourse. Would it not be an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i>? Since the sister-in-law, who cannot be acquired by money, can be acquired by intercourse, would it not be logical that this one, who may be acquired by money, should be acquired by intercourse? The Hebrew slave girl proves<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">In these kinds of arguments, “proves” means “disproves the possibility of an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i>” because the rules are not comparable.</i>, who cannot be acquired by intercourse but is acquired by money. You likewise do not wonder that this one, who may be acquired by money, might not be acquired by intercourse. The verse says, “after he acquires,” this tells you that she is acquired by money; “and has marital relations with her,” this tells you that she is acquired by intercourse.
<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifry Deut</i>. 268; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 4b" href="/Kiddushin.4b">Babli 4b</a>.</i> By a document. Since money which does not send out permits to enter, would it not be logical that a document which sends out should permit to enter<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Money is used in preliminary marriage, to permit the woman to enter her husband’s family. Money cannot be used for divorce; the wife cannot pay the husband as a formal means of divorce.</i>? No. If you speak about money which eliminates dedication through redemption<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">All valuables dedicated to the Temple regain their profane status by redemption when that money is given to the Temple treasury, and in no other way.</i>, what can you say about a document which does not eliminate dedication through redemption<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Dedications cannot be redeemed by an I.O.U.</i>? The argument <i>de minore ad majus</i> is broken and you have to return to Scripture. Therefore, it was necessary to say: “He shall write her a bill of divorce, hand it to her, and send her out of his house. If she left his house and went to be another man’s.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>.</i>” It brackets her being with her leaving<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 5a" href="/Kiddushin.5a">Babli 5a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 15b" href="/Eruvin.15b"><i>Erubin</i> 15b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 25a" href="/Sukkah.25a"><i>Sukkah</i>25a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 47a" href="/Ketubot.47a"><i>Ketubot</i> 47a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 21b" href="/Gittin.21b"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 21b</a>; <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 269, end.</i>. Since her leaving was by a document, so her being is by a document.
Rebbi Yudan said, an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i> that a free woman should be acquired by actual possession<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">His proof that arguments <i>de minore ad majus</i> are inappropriate here is explained at length in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.4.3"><i>Ketubot</i>5:5, Note 100</a>.<br>As a legal term, חֲזָקָה may have two very different meanings. What seems to be intended here is that ownerless property can be acquired by active actual possession (i. e., possession combined with use.) The idea seems to be that a woman, performing a wife’s duty in a man’s house, by this act should become his wife. The comparison is to a slave woman who belonged to a proselyte who failed to start a Jewish family and dies without heirs. Any Jew who gets hold of her and lets her perform a servile job for himself has acquired her in law.<br>A second meaning of חֲזָקָה is “permanence of the <i>status quo ante</i>,” cf. <i>Giṭṭin</i> 3:3, Notes 81,89; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 9:2:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.9.2.8"><i>Nazir</i> 9:2, Note 90</a>. This meaning is referred to, somewhat incongruously, in the verse quoted at the end; property is inherited by the permanence of the state of “belonging to”.<br>A subcategory of “permanence of the <i>status quo ante</i>” is the validation of squatter’s rights after three years of undisturbed possession, if accompanied by a claim of rightful acquisition.</i>. Is it not logical that since a Gentile slave woman, who cannot be acquired by intercourse, can be acquired by actual possession, by an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i> a free woman, who can be acquired by intercourse, could be acquired by actual possession? The verse says, “and has marital relations with her.” She can be acquired by intercourse but cannot be acquired by actual possession. An argument <i>de minore ad majus</i> that a Gentile slave woman should be acquired by intercourse: Is it not logical that since a free woman, who cannot be acquired by actual possession, can be acquired by intercourse, by an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i> a Gentile slave woman, who can be acquired by actual possession, could be acquired by intercourse? The verse<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:46" href="/Leviticus.25.46"><i>Lev</i>. 25:46</a>.</i> says, “you may leave them as inheritance to your sons after you, to inherit them as property.” She is acquired by actual possession; she is not acquired by intercourse. So we inferred that a woman can be acquired in three ways: by money, or by document, or by intercourse.
So far for Israel. For the Gentiles? Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i> 18(8), a parallel source for this and the following paragraphs: R. Abbahu in the name of R. Joḥanan.</i>: It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 20:3" href="/Genesis.20.3"><i>Gen</i>. 20:3</a>, speaking to the Gentile Abimelech.</i> “you shall die because of the woman whom you took, since she is having marital relations with a husband.” They are guilty for those having marital relations; they are not guilty for the betrothed<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 57b" href="/Sanhedrin.57b">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 57b</a>.</i>. The words of Rebbi Eleazar imply, only if he had intention of acquiring her; the words of Samuel imply, even if he had no intention of acquiring her; since Rebbi Jonah said in the name of Samuel<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i>: “Rebbi Samuel.” For chronological reasons, and also because he is quoted after R. Eleazar, the reading of <i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i> is the only acceptable one.</i>: “A prostitute stands in a window<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">To attract customers.</i>. Two men sleep with her. The first one<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Who deflowers her. In R. Samuel’s opinion, a Gentile marriage can be contracted only by intercourse and there is no divorce. Therefore, a free woman is automatically married to the man who deflowers her and any subsequent intercourse with any other man (during the first man’s lifetime) is adultery.</i> is not killed, the second one is killed because of him<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">As an adulterer. In his theory, a Gentile can sleep without guilt only with a wife whom he married as a virgin, or a chaste widow, or a servile prostitute who as a slave cannot marry.</i>.” But did the first one intend to acquire her<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Eleazar, Gentiles can be married only by mutual consent. Then they also can repudiate the marriage.<br>In <i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i> 18(8) only R. Samuel’s opinion is quoted, with the addition that “for Gentiles, intercourse acquires automatically.”</i>?
“A man.” Why does the verse<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18:5" href="/Leviticus.18.5"><i>Lev</i>. 18:5</a>: “Any man to any blood relation shall not come to uncover nakedness.” Parallel interpretations are in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 57b" href="/Sanhedrin.57b">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i>57b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Acharei Mot, Chapter 13 1" href="/Sifra,_Acharei_Mot,_Chapter_13.1"><i>Sifra Aḥare Pereq</i> 13(1</a>).</i> say, “any man”? To include that Gentiles who committed adultery or incest<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">עֶרְוָה “nakedness”, abbreviated for גִּלּוּי עֶרְוָה “uncovering of nakedness”, is the legal term for “incest and/or adultery”.</i> by Gentile rules should be judged by Gentile law, but if they committed adultery with a Jewishwoman, one judges them by Jewish law. Rebbi Eleazar said, of all of them there is only the Jewish betrothed<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The Gentile cannot commit incest with a Jewish partner since he has no family relationship with her. If he commits adultery with a married woman, he is guilty under Gentile law. The only case is intercourse with a preliminarily married woman, which is adultery in Jewish law but a relationship with an unmarried woman by Gentile standards.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 57b" href="/Sanhedrin.57b">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 57b</a>, this also is a statement of R. Joḥanan.</i>, for if he had intercourse with a Jewish preliminarily married woman he is guilty, with a betrothed Gentile woman he cannot be prosecuted. If he had intercourse with a Jewish preliminarily married woman he is guilty. How is he found to be guilty? By their rules or by Jewish rules? If you say by Jewish rules, by two witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 19:15" href="/Deuteronomy.19.15"><i>Deut</i>. 19:15</a>.</i>, 23 judges<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 1:2:1-4:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.1.2.1-4.10">Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i> 1:4</a>.</i>, after warning<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:4:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.4.7"><i>Soṭah</i> 3:4, Note 121</a>.</i>, and by stoning. If you say by their rules, by one witness, one judge, without warning, and by the sword<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of the <i>editio princeps</i>. The corrector wrote בהונקו. No root הנק is known.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">This does not belong here; except for the name, it is the corrector’s text taken from <i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i> 34(19) where from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 9:6" href="/Genesis.9.6"><i>Gen</i>. 9:6</a> it is inferred that Gentiles are executed by the sword. The verse reads: שוֹפֵךְ דַּם הָאָדָם בָּאָדָם דָּמוֹ יִשָּׁפֵךְ “He who spills a human’s blood, by humans his blood shall be spilled.” Then the question arises whether a murderer who kills by strangling can be prosecuted under Noahide law. To this, R. Jehudah <i>bar Simon</i> gives a positive answer by reading the verse as: “He who spills a human’s blood <i>within a</i> human, his blood shall be spilled.” [In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 57b" href="/Sanhedrin.57b">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 57b</a>, R. Ismael is quoted as deducing that for a Gentile, the killing of a foetus, a “human within a human”, is a capital crime. In rabbinic law, abortion is not prosecutable and is even required if the mother’s life is at risk (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 7:6" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.7.6">Mishnah <i>Ahilut</i> 7:6</a>).]</i> Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi added one who strangles, as a separate offense. What is the reason? For “blood in a human.”
If you say, by Jewish rules, he<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The Gentile who had raped a preliminarily married virgin, mentioned in the paragraph before the last.</i> is guilty if he converted. If you say, by their rules, he cannot be prosecuted if he converted, as Rebbi Ḥanina said, a descendant of Noah who blasphemed and then converted cannot be prosecuted since the law changed for him<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Nobody can be convicted under a law which did not apply to him at the moment the criminal act was committed. Since after conversion, Jewish law applies to him, he cannot be prosecuted for prior crimes. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 71b" href="/Sanhedrin.71b">Babli agrees, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 71b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: The descendants of Noah are warned about nakedness<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">עֶרְוָה “nakedness”, abbreviated for גִּלּוּי עֶרְוָה “uncovering of nakedness”, is the legal term for “incest and/or adultery”.</i> as Jews are<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i> 18(8), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 58b" href="/Sanhedrin.58b">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 58b</a>.</i>; <sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 2:24" href="/Genesis.2.24"><i>Gen</i>. 2:24</a>. Cf. <i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i> 18(8), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 57b" href="/Sanhedrin.57b">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 57b</a>.</i> the verse says, “he shall cling to his wife”, not to somebody else’s wife. “He shall cling to his wife”, not to a male, nor to an animal<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">In addition, it is deduced there that the wording “father and mother” prohibits incest of father with daughter or stepdaughter, and son with mother or father’s wife. From <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 20:12" href="/Genesis.20.12"><i>Gen</i>. 20:12</a> it is inferred that the uterine half-sister also is forbidden.</i>. Rebbi Samuel, Rebbi Abbahu, Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: A descendant of Noah who had perverse intercourse<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Any sex play which does not include penetration and ejaculation.</i> with his wife is killed. What is the reason? “He shall cling to his wife so they become one flesh,” at the place where they together create one flesh<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Any intercourse which excludes the possibility of creating a pregnancy is sinful, except regular intercourse with one’s pregnant wife.</i>.
Rebbi Yose asked: What is the legal status of “touching”<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">If the two persons’ genitals are touching. This is the definition of a sex act for criminal and marriage laws (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 4:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.4.2.3"><i>Yebamot</i>, 4:2 Note 59</a>; 6:1, Note 11 ff.)</i> a male? What is the legal status of “touching” an animal? Were not all incest prohibitions inferred from the menstruating woman<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Not the prohibition, but the technicalities which make the act prosecutable. It is spelled out in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 20:18" href="/Leviticus.20.18"><i>Lev</i>. 20:18</a> that in the case of the menstruating woman, “touching” establishes the criminality of the act. Therefore, for Jews “touching” is the equivalent of completed intercourse in criminal law. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 54b" href="/Yevamot.54b">Babli, <i>Yebamot</i> 54b</a>, seems to reject the equation of bestiality with homosexual activity.</i>? The male is included; the animal is included. So far, for Israel. For Gentiles? Rebbi Mana said, not from this: “He shall cling to his wife”? Not in any way to somebody else’s wife, not in any way to a male or an animal.
So we have established that Gentiles have no preliminary marriage. Do they have divorce? Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi and Rebbi Ḥanin in the name of the great Rebbi Ḥuna of Sepphoris: Either they have no divorce, or either of them may divorce the other. Rebbi Joḥanan from Sepphoris, Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Ḥinena, in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman: “For the divorcer is hateful, says the Eternal, Israel’s God.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Malachi 2:16" href="/Malachi.2.16"><i>Mal</i>. 2:16</a>, speaking of the person divorcing his first wife without necessity.</i>” In Israel I gave divorce, I did not give divorce among the nations of the world. Rebbi Ḥananiah in the name of Rebbi Phineas: In the entire paragraph it is written “the Eternal, Sabaot”; only here it is written “Israel’s God.” To teach you that the Holy One, praise to Him, did lend his name only to Jewish divorce.
The word of the elder Rebbi Ḥiyya implies that Gentiles have no divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Not that divorce were impossible for Gentiles but that Gentile divorce does not have the legal implications of Jewish divorce.</i>, as Rebbi Ḥiyya stated in this matter<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i> 18(8).</i>: A Gentile who divorced his wife, who herself then went and married another, when the latter divorced her and then both of them<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The first husband and the wife.</i> converted, I do not read<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Reading with <i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i> איני for אני.</i> for them: “Her first husband who had divorced her cannot return to marry her.” It was stated in this matter: A case came before Rebbi and he approved<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">He lets the first husband remarry the divorced wife who, while Gentile, had another husband.</i>.
By a document. This means, by a document which is not worth a <i>peruṭah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Corresponding to <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup>/<sub>192</sub> of a denar. <i>Assarius</i> is the old name of the Roman <i>as</i>. In rabbinic practice, a <i>peruṭa</i> is defined as half a grain (<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup>/<sub>960</sub> of a troy oz. or 0.0324 g) of sterling silver.</i>, but a document which is worth a <i>peruṭah</i> is money’s worth<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Since betrothal by document is deduced from divorce by document (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:18" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.18">Note 21</a>), if the document was invalid because it was not written for the specific woman for whom it was used, the preliminary marriage would be effected if and only if the material used for the document was worth a <i>peruṭah</i> (interpretation of R. Nissim Gerondi).</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated in this respect<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kiddushin (Lieberman) 1:2" href="/Tosefta_Kiddushin_(Lieberman).1.2">Tosephta 1:2</a>.</i>: “Not only a document which is worth a <i>peruṭah</i> since a woman can have herself preliminarily married by anything which is worth a <i>peruṭah</i>. But even if he wrote it on an <i>ostrakon</i> or on papyrus<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The document must only read: הרי את מקודשת לי “you are preliminarily married to me”. It is possible to write it on a snippet of paper worth less than a penny.</i> and gave it to her, she is preliminarily married.”
If he wrote it<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The declaration of marriage.</i> on material forbidden for usufruct? Rebbi Ḥanin<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Rashba, <i>Responsa</i> vol. 1, #603, reads: <i>Zenon</i>.</i> stated: A case came before Rebbi who said, she is divorced<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted as anonymous statement in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 20a" href="/Gittin.20a">Babli, <i>Giṭṭin</i> 20a</a>, which also states as undisputed Galilean rule that a divorce document may be written on material forbidden for usufruct. By implication, a declaration of marriage also may be written on such material.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Rashba, <i>loc</i>.<i>cit</i>., reads “R. Eliezer.”</i> said, she is not divorced<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">This opinion is not mentioned in the Babli. Rashba holds that the Babli, which simply quotes the rule without discussion, by reference incorporates the Yerushalmi discussion and decision.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, the rabbis disagree: For him who said, she is not preliminary married, she is not divorced. For him who said, she is preliminary married, she is divorced<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 5a" href="/Kiddushin.5a">Babli 5a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 15b" href="/Eruvin.15b"><i>Erubin</i> 15b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 25a" href="/Sukkah.25a"><i>Sukkah</i>25a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 47a" href="/Ketubot.47a"><i>Ketubot</i> 47a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 21b" href="/Gittin.21b"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 21b</a>; <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 269, end.</i>. The colleagues say, for restrictions. Rebbi Yose asked, what means “for restrictions”? She is not preliminarily married but divorced, is this “for restrictions”? Or she is not divorced but preliminarily married, is this “for restrictions”? What about it? The rabbis of Caesarea in the name of Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa: He who says that she is divorced, involving material forbidden for usufruct by their words<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Material permitted by biblical law, only forbidden by rabbinic rules.</i>. But he who says that she is not divorced, involving material forbidden for usufruct by words of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Most material prohibited for ususfruct by biblical law must be burned or otherwise disposed of. It seems that the authorities quoted follow the opinion, not mentioned in the Yerushalmi and attributed in the Babli to R. Simeon as minority opinion (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 12b" href="/Pesachim.12b"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 12b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 79b" href="/Menachot.79b"><i>Menaḥot</i> 79b</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 102b" href="/Menachot.102b">102b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 24b" href="/Keritot.24b"><i>Keritut</i> 24b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 23a" href="/Temurah.23a"><i>Temurah</i> 23a</a>) that anything which must be burned by biblical command is legally considered ashes even if not burned.</i>. Therefore, by material forbidden for usufruct by their words she is preliminarily married. If you say so, does this not disagree with Rav? For Rav said, the words of Rebbi Meïr: A person who uses leavened material for preliminary marriage after noontime did not do anything. Is leavened material of any use after noontime<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">According to everybody, leavened matter in possession of a Jew is forbidden for usufruct after noontime on Passover eve, the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan. For the majority, this is a biblical decree, derived from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:18" href="/Exodus.23.18"><i>Ex</i>. 23:18</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:25" href="/Exodus.34.25">34:25</a>. For R. Meïr it is a rabbinic decree; biblical prohibition of leavened matter only starts shortly before nightfall on the fourteenth (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:4:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.4.2-7"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 1:4</a>, 27c 1. 48 ff.). Why does R. Meïr invalidate a preliminary marriage document for which material was used which is only rabbinically prohibited for usufruct?</i>? There, he used the material for preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Since material rabbinically prohibited for usufruct has no monetary value, it cannot be used as a gift for preliminary marriage. It is possible that R. Meïr would validate a marriage by text written on paper sized with a solution of flour in water executed on Passover eve.</i>, but here he effected the preliminary marriage by the [written] conditions. But then she should be preliminarily married even with material forbidden for usufruct by the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">If used as writing material, rather than for its intrinsic worth.</i>! What is the difference between this and a document not worth a <i>peruṭah</i>? There, it cannot be used as filler. But here, it can be used as filler<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Material not worth a <i>peruṭah</i> (in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 20b" href="/Gittin.20b">Babli, <i>Giṭṭin</i> 20b</a>, the example is a single leaf of an olive tree) can be used together with other materials as stuffing. Material forbidden for ususfruct cannot be used for anything.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The original of this and the following paragraphs is in <i>Šebuot</i> 6:1 (36d, 11. 21–50, <b>ו</b>).</i> There, we have stated: “A judicial oath<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The oath required by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:8</a> for a person denying a claim when neither he nor the claimant have proof. The defendant can be made to swear only if he admits that at least one <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth of the claim is true; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.1.4"><i>Ketubot</i> 2:1, Note 12</a> (<i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Ismael</i>,<i>Neziqin</i>2).</i>, for a claim of two silver pieces and a judicial acknowlegdment of a <i>peruṭah</i>. The claim, the House of Shammai say, an obolus<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">One sixth of a drachma identified with the imperial silver denar.</i>, but the House of Hillel say, two obols.” The argument of the House of Shammai seems inverted. There<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:1. The formulation shows that the origin of the text is in <i>Ševuot</i>.</i>, they say, “silver” is a denar, but here, they say that “silver” is an obolus. The argument of the House of Hillel seems inverted. There<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:1. The formulation shows that the origin of the text is in <i>Ševuot</i>.</i>, they say, “money” is a <i>peruṭah</i>, but here, they say that “money” is two obols. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: The house of Shammai learn from the initial sale of a Hebrew girl<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:11" href="/Exodus.21.11"><i>Ex</i>. 21:11</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:10" href="/Exodus.21.10">10</a>, speaking of the “Hebrew slave girl”, the underage girl sold by her father with the understanding that the buyer may use the amount he paid for the girl as bride-money in case he wants to marry her or give her to his son. This certainly is marriage by money. V. 11 notes that if she is not married either within 6 years or by the time she reaches adulthood, she goes free; the money given for her was to buy her working power. V. 10 states that once she is married, she cannot be treated differently from any other wife. It is inferred that as a matter of principle her acquisition cannot be different from that of any other wife.</i>. Since her initial sale was by [at least] a denar, so her preliminary marriage is by [at least] a denar<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">It is clear from the biblical text, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:7-11" href="/Exodus.21.7-11"><i>Ex</i>. 21:7–11</a>, that the person buying an underage Hebrew girl is supposed to marry her when she grows up, or to marry her to his son. The money given for her services is at the same time the money for preliminary marriage. The House of Shammai hold that the original buying price, which later is shown to be at least a silver denar, is the bridal money used for the legal acquisition of the girl as preliminarily wedded wife. The House of Hillel hold that only the amount not amortized by the girl’s work in the meantime is bridal money. Since the master can marry the girl even on the last day of her servitude, he can marry her for a <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth and, as shown before, he can marry any other woman also for a <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth.</i>. The House of Hillel learn from the end of her diminution<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:8" href="/Exodus.21.8"><i>Ex</i>. 21:8</a> states that “if she is bad in her master’s eyes, who does not intend her for himself, let her be redeemed”, it is concluded that the Hebrew girl-servant can be redeemed during her servitude, in contrast to the Hebrew indentured servant who has to serve his full six years. Since it is prescribed for the Hebrew servant of a Gentile master that he can be redeemed by paying off the proportion of his original price not amortized by the time spent in servitude (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:50" href="/Leviticus.25.50"><i>Lev</i>. 25:50</a>), the same must hold for the servant girl. As long as she still is a servant, the redemption price is at least a <i>peruṭah</i>.</i>. Since the end of her diminution is a <i>peruṭah</i>, so her preliminary marriage is by a <i>peruṭah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:11" href="/Exodus.21.11"><i>Ex</i>. 21:11</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:10" href="/Exodus.21.10">10</a>, speaking of the “Hebrew slave girl”, the underage girl sold by her father with the understanding that the buyer may use the amount he paid for the girl as bride-money in case he wants to marry her or give her to his son. This certainly is marriage by money. V. 11 notes that if she is not married either within 6 years or by the time she reaches adulthood, she goes free; the money given for her was to buy her working power. V. 10 states that once she is married, she cannot be treated differently from any other wife. It is inferred that as a matter of principle her acquisition cannot be different from that of any other wife.</i>. What is the reason of the House of Shammai? As it is said: “She leaves gratis, without silver<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:11" href="/Exodus.21.11"><i>Ex</i>. 21:11</a>. At the end of six years, the master cannot ask for money to let her go.</i>.” Would we not know that it is without money? Why does the verse say, “without silver”<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Since “gratis” means “without payment”.</i>? From there, that she is sold for more than silver. And what is more than silver? A denar. But maybe “silver” is a <i>peruṭah</i>, more than silver two <i>peruṭot?</i> The smallest silver coin is an obolus<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Even though in Achaemenid Persia, the silver half-obolus was currency (also minted in the province of Yehud), in later times, prior to the Roman conquest, the obolus was the smallest silver coin. In Roman currency, the denarius was the smallest silver coin.</i>. So why is it not an obolus? Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Judah bar Pazi: For if she wants to diminish, she diminishes every year by an obolus and leaves<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Since she serves for six years, her price must be a six-fold multiple of a silver coin. This determines the drachma as the minimum price.</i>. Could she not diminish by a <i>peruṭah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">And be bought at the start for 6 <i>peruṭot</i>.</i>? Rebbi Abun said, think of it. If she wanted to compute the diminution at the start of the sixth year, there would be a <i>peruṭah</i> left, and at the end of the sixth year, there would be a <i>peruṭah</i> left. But the start of the diminution must be an obolus, the end of the diminution a <i>peruṭah</i>. If there is only one <i>peruṭah</i> left, can she not pay the diminished amount and leave? Just as the last diminished amount is a <i>peruṭah</i>, so her preliminary marriage should be a <i>peruṭah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">This argument is correctly missing in <i>Šebuot</i> since the House of Shammai consider the original sum as bride-money, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.19">Note 81</a>. The Yerushalmi’s interpretation of the House of Shammai is explicitly rejected in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 12a" href="/Kiddushin.12a">Babli, 12a</a>.</i>! What is the reason of the House of Hillel? Since her last diminished amount is a <i>peruṭah</i>, you know that her preliminary marriage is by a <i>peruṭah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">81,</sup><i class="footnote">It is clear from the biblical text, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:7-11" href="/Exodus.21.7-11"><i>Ex</i>. 21:7–11</a>, that the person buying an underage Hebrew girl is supposed to marry her when she grows up, or to marry her to his son. The money given for her services is at the same time the money for preliminary marriage. The House of Shammai hold that the original buying price, which later is shown to be at least a silver denar, is the bridal money used for the legal acquisition of the girl as preliminarily wedded wife. The House of Hillel hold that only the amount not amortized by the girl’s work in the meantime is bridal money. Since the master can marry the girl even on the last day of her servitude, he can marry her for a <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth and, as shown before, he can marry any other woman also for a <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The Šebuot text has here an additional text similar to the text used in Qiddušin in the discussion of the House of Shammai: הַגַּע עַצְמְךָ שֶׁאִם נִשְׁתַּיֵּר שָׁם שְׁוֵה פְּרוּטָה שֶׁמָּא אֵינָהּ מְגָרָעַתּוֹ וְיוֹצָא. כְּשֵׁם שֶׁסּוֹף גֵּרוּעֶיהָ בִּפְרוּטָה אַף קִדּוּשֶׁיהָ בִּפְרוּטָה. "Think of it, if there is one peruṭah's worth left, can she not pay the diminished amount and leave? Just as the last diminished amount is a peruṭah, so her preliminary marriage is a peruṭah."</i>.
The argument of the House of Hillel seems inverted. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6" href="/Exodus.22.6"><i>Ex</i>. 22:6</a>. The verse is the introduction to the judicial oath prescribed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8">v. 8</a> (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.19">Note 78</a>) and establishes the parameters of judicial intervention. The arguments in this paragraph are to some extent paralleled in <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Simeon ben Ioḥai</i>, ed. Epstein-Melamed, p. 199.</i>: “If a person give to his neighbor money or vessels to watch over.” If to teach that the court will not act on less that a <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth, is it not already written: “To incur liability for it”<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:26" href="/Leviticus.5.26"><i>Lev</i>. 5:26</a>. One of the topics treated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:20-26" href="/Leviticus.5.20-26"><i>Lev</i>. 5:20–26</a> is the guilt of the person swearing falsely the oath required in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:8</a>. For the translation of the verbal root אשם as “to incur liability”, cf. J. Milgram, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1-16" href="/Leviticus.1-16"><i>Leviticus</i>1–16</a>, Anchor Bible 1991, pp. 339–345 (the author does not refer to the rabbinic texts.) Since restitution is possible only by payment, anything worth less than the smallest coin cannot be restituted: There can be no oath for a residual claim not worth a <i>peruṭah (Sifra Wayyiqra Pereq</i> 23, end.)</i>? To exclude anything not worth a <i>peruṭah</i>. (Why does the verse say, “without silver”?)<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">This text is an intrusion from the parallel discussion in the preceding paragraph; it is correctly missing in <i>Šebuot</i>.</i> From there, that it should be more than silver<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The argument seems to be that nobody will give a worthless thing to be watched over. If the verse emphasizes “silver” or “money”, it must mean more than the minimum.</i>. And what is more than silver? Two obols. But maybe “silver” is a <i>peruṭah</i>, more than silver two <i>peruṭot</i>? The smallest silver coin is an obolus<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Even though in Achaemenid Persia, the silver half-obolus was currency (also minted in the province of Yehud), in later times, prior to the Roman conquest, the obolus was the smallest silver coin. In Roman currency, the denarius was the smallest silver coin.</i>. So why is it not an obolus? “Or vessels”; since “vessels” are two<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">An indeterminate plural always means its minimum, 2. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Niddah 2:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Niddah.2.6.2"><i>Niddah</i> 2:5, Note 90</a>.</i>, also “money” is two<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 11b" href="/Kiddushin.11b">Babli 11b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 39b" href="/Shevuot.39b"><i>Šebuot</i> 39b</a>.</i>. How do the House of Shammai interpret “or vessels”? As we have stated: Rebbi Nathan says, “or vessels”, to include all kinds of vessels<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">This argument can only be understood by reference to <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Simeon ben Ioḥai</i> (Note 90). There it is argued that since silver is mined from the earth, also “vessels” should be restricted to those made from terrestrial material, to exclude anything coming from the sea (either from seaweed or fish skin). R. Jehudah reads כֵּלִים as כְּלֵי יַם “objects from the sea” (explanation of J. N. Epstein). E. Z. Melamed also reports there that J. N. Epstein, in his personal copy of the Yerushalmi, emended the parallel reading in <i>Šebuot</i>, כלי חרס “clay vessels”, into כלי חרם “fisherman’s netting”, which might have been made from material grown in the sea. (The reading כלי חרס makes no sense, since clay vessels are always understood if “vessel” is used without qualifier in biblical texts and do not need to be defined through additions. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 22a" href="/Zevachim.22a">Babli, <i>Zebaḥim</i> 22a</a>, anything grown in water is considered water.) “All kinds of vessels” includes vessels made from marine material.</i>. Samuel said, if he claimed from him two needles and he admitted to one, he is guilty<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 40b" href="/Shevuot.40b">Babli, <i>Šebuot</i> 40b</a>, points out that Samuel must hold that the “vessels” quoted in the verse refer to any vessels, irrespective of their value.</i>. Rebbi Ḥinena said, only if they are worth two <i>perutot</i>, that the claim should be about a <i>perutah</i>’s worth and the confession about a <i>perutah’s</i> worth<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Since otherwise the court could not take cognizance of the case, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:20" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.20">Note 91</a> (quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 39b" href="/Shevuot.39b">Tosephot <i>Šebuot</i> 39b, <i>s. v.</i>מה</a>).</i>. This follows the House of Shammai who do not learn money’s worth from “vessels”. But following the House of Hillel who learn money’s worth from “vessels”, since “vessels” are two, also “money” is two. Similarly, since “money” means two obols, also “vessels” means two oboli’s worth.
<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">The text of this paragraph and the one following is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.6"><i>Yebamot</i> 1:6, Notes 243–261</a>(<b>א</b>). It seems that the <i>Yebamot</i> text is the original.</i>“Even though the House of Shammai and the House of Hillel disagreed about co-wives, and sisters, and an old bill of divorce, and a woman doubtfully married, and someone who gives <i>qiddushin</i> in the value of a <i>peruṭa</i>, and someone who divorces his wife and spends the night with her in a hostelry, and a woman who receives <i>qiddushin</i> for at least a <i>denar</i> or the value of a <i>denar</i>, the House of Shammai did not refrain from marrying women from the House of Hillel or the House of Hillel from the House of Shammai but they behave truthfully and in peace, as it is said: ‘Love truth and peace.’ ” Bastardy is between them and you say so? How is that? If a first man gives her <i>qiddushin</i> for a <i>peruṭa</i> and a second [gives her afterwards)] for a <i>denar</i>, in the opinion of the House of Shammai she is betrothed to the second and her child by the first is a bastard. In the opinion of the House of Hillel she is betrothed to the first and her child by the second is a bastard. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The House of Shammai concede to the House of Hillel as a restriction. In that case, the House of Shammai should marry women from the House of Hillel since they concede to them. But the House of Hillel should not marry women from the House of Shammai since they do not concede to them. Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: They all followed the same practice. If they followed the same practice, in this case we stated: “The House of Shammai sent and diminished it, since the House of Shammai say unless most of it is missing?” Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, the House of Shammai acted before the question came before the House of Hillel. After a question came before the House of Hillel, the House of Shammai were not touching it. Rebbi Abba Mari said, that is correct. Did we not state: “They declared impure all purities from before”, but not in the future. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, Rav and Samuel disagreed. One said both acted according to valid practice; the other said, each party followed their own practice. Bastardy is between them and you say so? The Omnipresent watched and no case ever happened.
<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">The text of this paragraph and the one following is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.6"><i>Yebamot</i> 1:6, Notes 243–261</a>(<b>א</b>). It seems that the <i>Yebamot</i> text is the original.</i> In this matter, it was stated: “About anybody who wanted to take upon himself the stringencies both of the House of Shammai and the House of Hillel it was said: ‘The silly one walks in darkness’. The leniencies of both of them, he is called wicked. Either following the words of the House of Shammai in their leniencies and stringencies, or following the words of the House of Hillel in their leniencies and stringencies.” That is, before there came the disembodied voice. But after the disembodied voice was heard, “practice follows the House of Hillel forever.” And any who transgress the words of the House of Hillel are deserving of death. It was stated: There came the disembodied voice and said: Both of them are the words of God, but practice follows the House of Hillel. Where was the disembodied voice heard? Rebbi Bebai in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The disembodied voice was heard at Yabneh.
“How much is a <i>peruṭah</i>? One eighth of an Italic <i>as</i>.” It was stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Babli <i>Baba meṣia‘</i> 44b, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 5:11" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).5.11">Tosephta <i>Baba batra</i> 5:11</a>.</i> The <i>as</i> is<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup>/<sub>24</sub> of a silver denar; the silver denar is<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup>/<sub>24</sub> of a gold denar<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">In Imperial coinage, the gold denar was usually counted for 25 silver denarii.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: “A tetradrachma is four <i>denarii</i>. Six silver <i>oboli</i> are in a denar. Two <i>dupondii</i> are an obolus. An <i>obolus</i><sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">This has to read: “a <i>dupondius</i>”.</i> is two <i>as</i>. A <i>pondius</i><sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">This has to read: “an <i>as</i>.”</i> is two <i>semisses</i>. An <i>as</i><sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">This has to read: “a <i>semis</i>”.</i> is two <i>quadrantes</i><sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κοδράντης, equivalent of Roman <i>quadrans</i>, ¼ of an <i>as</i>..</i>. A <i>semis</i><sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">This has to read: “a <i>quadrans</i>”. The final list reads: 1 denar = 6 <i>oboli</i> = 12 <i>dupondii</i> = 24 <i>as</i> = 48 <i>semisses =</i> 96 <i>quadrantes</i> = 192 <i>peruṭot</i> (confirming the tannaïtic standard of the <i>peruṭah</i> as <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup>/<sub>8</sub> of an <i>as</i>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Eduyot 4:7" href="/Mishnah_Eduyot.4.7">Mishnah <i>Idiut</i> 4:7</a>). In the Roman system, the obolus appears as a weight, rather than a coin: one-sixth of a denar of 3.6 g.</i> is two <i>peruṭot</i>. It turns out that a <i>quadrans</i> is<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup>/<sub>32</sub> of the above<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">This has to read: “A <i>peruṭah</i> is <sup>1</sup>/<sub>32</sub> of an obolus.” In the entire list, the first entry in each sentence has to be replaced by the next smaller unit and למעלה by מעה.</i>.”
Rebbi Ze‘ira said, in the days of Rebbi Simai<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Of the generation of transition between Tannaïm and Amoraïm. “Our teachers” therefore refers to Rebbi.</i> and our teachers they made it<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>peruṭah</i>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup>/<sub>24</sub> of an obolus, as it was stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Babli <i>Baba meṣia‘</i> 44b, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 5:11" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).5.11">Tosephta <i>Baba batra</i> 5:11</a>.</i>“Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, three <i>dodrantes</i><sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Latin for ¾ of any measure.</i> are an obolus, two <i>besses</i><sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Latin for <sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup>/<sub>3</sub> of any measure.</i> are a <i>dodrans</i>, two <i>semisses</i> are a <i>bes</i>, two <i>peruṭot</i> a <i>semis</i>. It results in<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup>/<sub>24</sub> of an obolus<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">D. Sperber, <i>Roman Palestine</i>200-400,<i>Money and Prices</i>, Ramat Gan 1974, pp. 78–80, conjectures that the change in computation of a <i>peruṭah</i> from <sup>1</sup>/<sub>32</sub> to <sup>1</sup>/<sub>24</sub> of an obolus was a rabbinic response to Caracalla’s monetary reform which increased the ratio of silver to gold from 12:1 to 9:1. The argument is purely theoretical since the <i>peruṭah</i> as a coin disappeared with the Jewish Commonwealth.</i>.”
Rebbi Ḥanina and Rebbi Mana<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">They probably are R. Ḥanania of Sepphoris and R. Mana II, of the last Amoraïm. After Diocletian’s reform, with the gold standard universally accepted, they discuss the legal standard in the inflationary period of the last Severans and the following military anarchy when the government paid its debt in adulterated silver coin but required taxes to be paid in genuine silver coin.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanina says, bronze remained in its place<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., is the monetary standard.</i>, silver increased or decreased in value. Rebbi Mana said, silver remained in its place<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., is the monetary standard.</i>, bronze increased or decreased in value. In Rebbi Ḥanina’s opinion, with one <i>as</i> six women always can contract a preliminary marriage. In Rebbi Mana’s opinion, sometimes six, sometimes eight<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">It is assumed that a man can deliver a coin to a goup of women as payment for his preliminary marriage to all of them (Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 2:6–7). In R. Mana’s opinion, the groom has to deliver merchandise (including copper coin considered as merchandise) in the actual value of a <i>peruṭah</i>. In terms of the silver <i>as</i>, not in circulation after Nero’s time, he can use one silver coin for as many women as the <i>as</i> can buy <i>peruṭot</i>. In R. Ḥanina’s opinion, since women cannot be expected to be cognizant of the current value of silver on the metals exchange, the women can be sure that the <i>as</i> represents the value promised them only at the historical minimum value of silver. (The explanations of the classical commentaries have to be rejected since they require a switching of the names in addition to emending the text. The relation of coins to the mint standard, whether they are treated as money or merchandise, is the topic of <i>Baba meṣi‘a</i>, Chapter 4.)</i>.
Ḥilfai said<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">At many places, he is quoted as opposing the use of <i>baraitot</i> in talmudic discussions; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 6:7:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.6.7.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 6:7, Note 113</a>.</i>: Bring me to the river’s edge. If I cannot derive Rebbi Ḥiyya the Elder’s <i>baraita</i><sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">A paradigm for the later Tosephta, in the present case the two preceding <i>baraitot</i>.</i> from the Mishnah, throw me into the river. They said to him, did not Rebbi Ḥiyya state: “A tetradrachma are four denar.” He said to them, we also have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 4:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.4.4.1">Mishnah <i>Baba Meṣi‘a</i> 4:4</a>.</i>: “By how much can a tetradrachma be deficient without being fraudulent? Rebbi Meïr said four <i>as</i>, one <i>as</i> per denar<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">He accepts a coin as of full weight if it deviates from the standard by no more than <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup>/<sub>24</sub>, i. e. 4.167%, in contrast to commercial contracts which are fraudulent only if the customer was overcharged by more than <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup>/<sub>6</sub>, 16.667%. In any case, the Mishnah states clearly that the deficiency admissible for a tetradrachma is four times that of a denar. {Cf. Trimalchio’s remark that the profession of banker is one of the most difficult since he must be able to recognize the inferior metal of a coin beneath its silver coating (E. G.)}</i>.” They said to him, did not Rebbi Ḥiyya state: “Six silver obols are a denar.” He said to him, I also have stated it<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.4.3.1">Mishnah <i>Baba Meṣi‘a</i> 4:3</a>. Since “silver coin” means “smallest silver coin”, i. e., an obolus, and it was established that a denar was a quarter tetradrachma, it follows that a silver denar was 6 obols. These and smaller denominations had long disappeared from circulation in Ḥilfai’s time, towards the middle of the Third Century. (Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:20" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.20">Note 85</a>.)</i>: “Fraud<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">Overcharging or underpaying in commercial contracts.</i> is four silver coins for twenty-four silver coins, a tetradrachma, one sixth of the selling price.” They said to him, did not Rebbi Ḥiyya state: “Two <i>dupondii</i> are an obolus.” He said to them, we also have stated thus<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>‘Arakhin</i> 7:1. If somebody dedicates his field to the Temple, Scripture prescribes that he redeem it proportional to the numbers of years to the next Jubilee. The full rate, for the entire 49 yars of a Jubilee cycle, is 50 <i>šeqel</i> for a field on which a <i>ḥomer</i> (or <i>kur</i>, 30 <i>se’ah</i>) of barley can be sown (75’000 square cubits). Talmudic theory identifies the biblical <i>šeqel</i> with the Roman tetradrachma, which is slightly more than the standard <i>šeqel</i> documented from First Kingdom times, 11.4 g. Since in the vernacular the <i>zūz</i>, the Babylonian half-<i>šeqel</i>, was identified with the Roman <i>denarius</i>, the “sheqel” was identified as two denar. This gave rise to the theory that the biblical “holy <i>šeqel</i>”, which simply was “the king’s weight” and still the Tyrian <i>šeqel</i> in Roman times, was twice the profane <i>šeqel</i>. The rate to be paid for the standard field per year therefore was <sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup>/<sub>49</sub> = 1 <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup>/<sub>49</sub> tetradrachma. The rate stated in the Mishnah is 1 <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup>/<sub>48</sub> tetradrachma. The small difference, <sup>1</sup>/<sub>48</sub> - <sup>1</sup>/<sub>49</sub> is <i>qolbon</i> (κόλλυβος, Latin <i>collybus</i>,<i>collubus</i>, “exchange of coins; rate of exchange”), the small coin paid as agio to the money-changer to convert the sum into actual coin. The Mishnah shows that a <i>dupondius</i> was <sup>1</sup>/<sub>48</sub> of a tetradrachma or half an obolus.</i>: “He gives a tetradrachma and a <i>dupondius</i> per year.” They said to him, did not Rebbi Ḥiyya state: “Two <i>as</i> are a <i>dupondius</i>”. He said to them, we also have stated thus<sup>123</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ma‘aśer Šeni</i> 4:8, Note 130.</i>: “He who put aside an <i>as</i> and ate for half of its worth, then went to another place where it is worth a <i>dupondius</i>, has to eat another <i>as</i> for it.” They said to him, did not Rebbi Ḥiyya state: “Two <i>semisses</i> are an <i>as</i>, two <i>quadrantes</i> are a <i>semis</i>, two <i>peruṭot</i> are a <i>quadrans</i>.” He said to them, we also have stated thus<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.1">Mishnah 1:1</a>. While the Mishnah does not state anything about the intermediate denominations, it clearly defines the relation between <i>peruṭah</i> and <i>as</i>.</i>: “How much is a <i>peruṭah</i>? One eighth of an Italic <i>as</i>.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel but different discussion is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 13b" href="/Kiddushin.13b">Babli, 13b</a>.</i>“She regains her autonomy by a bill of divorce,” as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>. The part not quoted and v. 2 make it clear that the divorcee is free to contract another marriage.</i>: “He shall write her a bill of divorce.” “Or the husband’s death,” as it is written: “Or if the latter husband dies.<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:3" href="/Deuteronomy.24.3"><i>Deut</i>. 24:3</a>. Since the widow cannot remarry her first husband, it is clear that she can marry any other man.</i>” So far, if the latter one dies, [what about] the death of the former<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Which is not mentioned in the verse.</i>? Since the latter’s death releases her, even though his death does not cause a complete release<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">The former husband becomes forbidden. He was permitted after his divorce and before her second marriage.</i>, it is only logical that the former’s death should release her since his death will cause a complete release<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">The divorcee is free to marry any man not forbidden to her by incest or holiness prohibitions.</i>. Rebbi Ḥuna said, the verse implies that death releases, as it is written: “If brothers live together and one of them dies childless, etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:5" href="/Deuteronomy.25.5"><i>Deut</i>. 25:5</a>. Since the verse prohibits remarriage of the widow outside the family, it implies that the widow of a man who had children is free to marry according to her wishes.</i>” Therefore, if he had a child, death releases. Rebbi Yose bar Abun said, if you would say that death does not release, how could we find a widow for a High priest, a divorcee or one who has received <i>ḥalîṣah</i> for a common priest<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">The statement about the common priest implies that a widow is permitted to a common priest even if he is not of her first husband’s family.</i>? Rebbi Joḥanan bar Marius said, explain it for a sister-in-law<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">If a brother of a High Priest dies childless, his widow is forbidden to the High Priest. The prohibition has to be stated even if in general a widow were not permitted to remarry.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">The text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 2:1:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.2.1.2-6"><i>Yebamot</i>, Chapter 2:1</a> (<b>ב</b>), explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:3-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.3-8">Notes 12–20</a>, and Chapter 5:1 (<b>ה</b>).</i>“Her levir shall come upon her”, that is cohabitation. “And take her as a wife for himself”, that is “bespeaking”. I might think that just as cohabitation is final so “bespeaking” is final; the verse says “and take her in levirate.” This directs the entire paragraph towards levirate. Cohabitation is final, “bespeaking” is not final. Then what is “bespeaking” good for? To forbid her to his brothers.
Rebbi Simeon says, “bespeaking” either acquires or does not acquire. What is the argument of Rebbi Simeon? “Her levir shall come upon her”, that is cohabitation. “And take her for himself”, that is “bespeaking”. Just as cohabitation is final so “bespeaking” is final. Or “her levir shall come upon her”, then she has been acquired by him and “bespeaking” was of no use to her.
Rebbi Eleazar ben Arakh says, “bespeaking” acquires a sister-in-law completely. What is the argument of Rebbi Eleazar ben Arakh? “And take her as a wife for himself”, the same expression is used as for the preliminary marriage of a woman<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Simeon bar Ioḥai</i> (p. 167) the argument is inverted: Since in general a woman may be acquired by a document, so Hebrew slave girls may be acquired by a document.</i>. Just as preliminary marriage acquires completely, so “bespeaking” acquires a sister-in-law completely. What is the formula for “bespeaking” a sister-in-law? “You are betrothed to me by money or money’s worth.”
Rebbi Isaac asked: Why do we not say: “either by her own <i>ḥalîṣah</i> or that of her co-wife?”<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last sentence in the Mishnah, that the widow of the childless man can regain her autonomy by <i>ḥalîṣah</i>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.4.1.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 4:1</a> states that for the House of Hillel the levirate marriage or <i>ḥalîṣah</i> of one wife of the deceased childless brother forbids all her co-wives to the levir’s brothers and, therefore, frees the co-wives to marry any other man.</i> He turned around and said: Did we state “by her own <i>ḥalîṣah</i>”? Not “by her own <i>ḥalîṣah</i>”<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">It is only stated “by <i>ḥalîṣah</i>,” not indicating who performs it; it could be a co-wife.</i>! But here either by her own <i>ḥalîṣah</i> or that of her co-wife. But did we not state, “by intercourse”? Can you say, either by her own intercourse or the intercourse of her cowife<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">One has to assume that the Mishnah is formulated in a uniform style. It is clear that a man cannot marry a group of women by sleeping with one of them, even though he could marry all women in the group by giving valuables to one of them if the others empower her to act for them (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:25" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.25">Note 115</a>). If “intercourse” in the first case means a personal act, then <i>ḥalîṣah</i> in the last case also means a personal act.</i>? The Mishnah speaks of one sister-in-law; the problem arises for two sisters-in-law.
Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac asked: How can a <i>ḥăruphah</i> slave girl<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:20-22" href="/Leviticus.19.20-22"><i>Lev</i>. 19:20–22</a>. The slave girl has a status approximating that of married woman but sleeps with another man. By rabbinic interpretation, the girl is whipped and the man has to bring a reparation sacrifice. The exact meaning of the root חרף is in doubt, in view of the multiple meanings of Arabic حرف or خرف; rabbinic interpretations are given at the end of this paragraph and the next. If she were an unattached slave, she could not marry and, therefore, could have guiltless sex with any man of her choosing. The Jewish man who slept with her would be guilty of violating <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:18" href="/Deuteronomy.23.18"><i>Deut</i>. 23:18</a> for which he could be whipped if the act was observed by two witnesses, but no sacrifice would be due.</i> acquire her autonomy in such a way that she would be freed from flogging, and [free] the one who had intercourse with her from a reparation sacrifice? It is obvious that she cannot leave by a bill of divorce since Rebbi Ḥiyya said in Rebbi Joḥanan’s name: If a person who is half slave and half free entered a preliminary marriage, one does not consider his marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.5.2"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 4:5, Notes 140–141</a>. In this interpretation the girl is partially manumitted and engaged to be married by a free Jewish man after her complete manumission. Since in her present state she cannot be legally married, neither can she be divorced.</i>. In parallel, if he divorced one does not consider his divorce. It is obvious that she can leave by a bill of divorce at least since (Rebbi Ḥiyya said in Rebbi Joḥanan’s name: If a person who is half slave and half free entered a preliminary marriage, one does not consider his marriage. In parallel, if he divorced one does not consider his divorce. It is obvious that she can leave by a bill of divorce at least)<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of this sentence is unclear. The text in parentheses is dittography inserted by the corrector.</i>. Rebbi Yose said in Rebbi Joḥanan’s name: Aquila the proselyte translated before Rebbi Aqiba “and she is a slave girl נֶחֱרֶפֶת to a man”<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:20" href="/Leviticus.19.20"><i>Lev</i>. 19:20</a>.</i>, by “one who was pounded before a man”<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">She was betrothed by intercourse.</i>, as you say, “she spread morsels on it<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 17:19" href="/II_Samuel.17.19"><i>2S</i>. 17:19</a>. The identification of ה and ח is routine in rabbinic derivations but etymologically unfounded. Modern dictionaries treat הריפות, הרפות as derived from a hypothetical root ריף, not הרף.</i>.” Rebbi Ḥiyya said in Rebbi Joḥanan’s name: So Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon explained it before the Sages. “And she is a slave girl נֶחֱרֶפֶת to a man”, by “one who was pounded before a man”, as you say, “in morsels by a pestle.<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 17:19" href="/Proverbs.17.19"><i>Pr</i>. 17:19</a>. Aquila translates בְּתוֹךְ הָרִיפוֹת by ἐν μέσῳ ἐμπτισσομένων “in the midst of pounded things”. Both verses are quoted in the same sense in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 11a" href="/Keritot.11a">Babli, <i>Keritut</i> 11a</a>, that a man who sleeps with a <i>ḥaruphah</i> slave girl is guilty only if the girl had previously slept with her assignee, not if she is a virgin. (This agrees with the derivation of נחרפת, accepted by Gesenius, from Accadic <i>ẖarāpu</i>, Arabic خرف “to pluck, to harvest”, that the slave girl “had been plucked by a man,” <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:20" href="/Leviticus.19.20"><i>Lev</i>. 19:20</a>.) The tradent there is R. Isaac; Aquila is not mentioned [since in the Babli’s tradition he did not translate following R. Aqiba but R. Eliezer and R. Joshua, one generation earlier (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 3a" href="/Megillah.3a"><i>Megillah</i> 3a</a>).] {One is tempted to find in ח̇רפ the root of Greek Χάρυβδις, a dangerous whirlpool between Italy and Sicily, personified as a dangerous female being who “plucks men” (E. G.).}</i>”
Can she<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>ḥăruphah</i> slave girl, to be able to sleep with whomever she pleases.</i> regain her autonomy through her master’s death or by the end of six [years]? What is the problem? Following Rebbi Aqiba? But Rebbi Aqiba said that the verse speaks about a woman half slave and half free engaged to be married by a free man<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 11a" href="/Keritot.11a">Babli <i>Keritut</i> 11a</a>. Her situation can be remedied only by her full manumission, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.5.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 4:5</a>.</i>. Therefore, his problem is following Rebbi Ismael, since Rebbi Ismael said that the verse speaks about a Canaanite<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Meaning: originally Gentile.</i> slave girl married to a Hebrew slave, since this is marriage sanctioned by the Torah: “If his master give him a wife.<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:4" href="/Exodus.21.4"><i>Ex</i>. 21:4</a>.
