Since the children born from this union are slaves, the woman cannot be a Hebrew slave girl. Since the woman is called “wife”, she is required to be faithful by biblical standards. The question is whether this kind of marriage is terminated automatically, without any formality, at the moment the Hebrew slave regains his freedom. At the same moment, the slave woman becomes forbidden to him since any sexual relations between people who could not legally be married is qualified as <i>qādēš</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:18" href="/Deuteronomy.23.18"><i>Deut</i>. 23:18</a>).</i>” But his problem is whether she regains her autonomy through her master’s death or by the end of six [years] for him who says that the Hebrew slave does not serve the heir<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the next Halakhah. The question is not answered since it can never arise in the future (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.2.1">Note 152</a>).</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A Hebrew slave<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">The rules are based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:2-6" href="/Exodus.21.2-6"><i>Ex</i>. 21:2–6</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:39-43" href="/Leviticus.25.39-43"><i>Lev</i>. 25:39–43</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:12-18" href="/Deuteronomy.15.12-18"><i>Deut</i>. 15:12–18</a>. The <i>Lev</i>. source clearly refers to the person who sells himself as indentured servant because he cannot fend for himself. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:2" href="/Exodus.22.2"><i>Ex</i>. 22:2</a> provides for judicial sale of the thief who cannot pay the required double restitution.<br>Separate rules for the Hebrew slave acquired by a Gentile are given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:47-54" href="/Leviticus.25.47-54"><i>Lev</i>. 25:47–54</a>. In that case, the slave regains his freedom automatically only in the Jubilee year but the family can force his redemption by repaying the portion of his buying price not amortized by the time passed (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:50" href="/Leviticus.25.50"><i>Lev</i>. 25:50</a>).</i> is acquired by money or a document; he regains his autonomy by years<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">The maximum time of a contract of an indentured servant was 6 years; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:2" href="/Exodus.21.2"><i>Ex</i>. 21:2</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:12" href="/Deuteronomy.15.12"><i>Deut</i>. 15:12</a>.</i>, or the Jubilee<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:40" href="/Leviticus.25.40"><i>Lev</i>. 25:40</a>. Since the Jubilee brings back everybody to his ancestral land, everybody is presumed to be able to feed himself. The dependence of the rules of the Hebrew slave on the Jubilee implies that Hebrew slavery disappeared with the Jubilee; it never existed during the Second Commonwealth and is not to be re-instituted in the times of the Messiah (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 10:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.10.3"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 10:3, Notes 83–88</a>; <i>Giṭṭin</i> 4:3, Note 65). The discussion about the rules of Hebrew male slavery are a purely theoretical reconstruction of the past whose interest is not in statements but in the rules of inference.</i>, or reduction of the amount<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">If the slave or his family buy his freedom, the master is required to accept payment proportional to the time not yet served.</i>. The Hebrew slave girl<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">While the rules for the Hebrew slave girl are not tied to the Jubilee, the verses <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:12" href="/Deuteronomy.15.12"><i>Deut</i>. 15:12</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:17" href="/Deuteronomy.15.17">17</a> equate the rules for male and female slaves. Therefore, all the detailed rules developed in this Halakhah are purely theoretical interpretations of the biblical text; they never were operational within the rabbinic framework.</i> in addition regains her autonomy by indicators of puberty<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">While a father can forcibly marry off his daughter until she has reached adulthood (12 years six months and one day) he cannot sell her into slavery beyond adolescence (12 years and a day if she shows signs of puberty). He cannot sell her to work for longer than he had the right to sell.</i>. The pierced slave is acquired by piercing<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">The slave who does not want to regain his freedom after six years, whose earlobe is pierced (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:6" href="/Exodus.21.6"><i>Ex</i>. 21:6</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:17" href="/Deuteronomy.15.17"><i>Deut</i>. 15:17</a>).</i>; he regains his autonomy in the Jubilee or by the master’s death<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:5" href="/Exodus.21.5"><i>Ex</i>. 21:5</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:16" href="/Deuteronomy.15.16"><i>Deut</i>. 15:16</a> make it clear that the relationship of the “pierced” slave to his master is a personal one, not transferable to his heirs.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A Hebrew slave is acquired by money,” etc. It is written: “If your Hebrew brother or sister be sold to you,” he bracketed the male Hebrew with the female<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:12" href="/Deuteronomy.15.12"><i>Deut</i>. 15:12</a>. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 14b" href="/Kiddushin.14b">Babli, 14b</a>, points out that the verse only covers persons sold by others, the girl by her father and the man by the court, not the person who sells himself. <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 118 disputes the entire argument and holds that the female had to be mentioned separately from the male since the rules of her acquisition and liberation are distinct from those of the male. (E. g., the female cannot sell herself; the adult female cannot be a Hebrew slave.)</i>. Since the female Hebrew can be acquired by money or contract, also the male Hebrew can be acquired by money or contract. One understands money since it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:11" href="/Exodus.21.11"><i>Ex</i>. 21:11</a>. Since after six years, or after she reached adulthood, she leaves without payment, it follows that her work was worth money; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.19">Note 18</a>.</i>: “She leaves gratis, without money [due].” From where by contract? The female Hebrew is inferred from the free woman<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:10" href="/Exodus.21.10"><i>Ex</i>. 21:10</a> equates the marriage of the Hebrew slave girl to her master to the latter’s marriage with another, free, woman. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 16a" href="/Kiddushin.16a">Babli 16a</a>; <i>Mechilta dR</i>. <i>Šimon b</i>.<i>Jochai</i>, ed. Epstein-Melamed, p. 167).</i>, and the male Hebrew from the female. This is a combination of inferences. That follows Rebbi Aqiba who accepts combination of inferences<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">Except for texts based on <i>Lev</i>. For the intricacies of his position, cf. H. Guggenheimer, <i>Über ein bemerkenswertes logisches System aus der Antike</i>, Methodos 1951, pp. 150–164; <i>Logical Problems in Jewish Tradition</i>, in: Confrontations with Judaism, ed. Ph. Longworth, London 1966, pp. 171–196.<br>The problem before the rabbis of the second and third generations of Tannaïm was to derive a consistent legal system which intellectually could compete with the Roman law from the scattered remarks offered in the Biblical text. R. Ismael allows no more than one intermediate step between biblical text and legal statement.</i>. Following Rebbi Ismael who rejects combination of inferences? It was found that Rebbi Ismael stated: In the case of the word חֻפְשָׁה, “free” is inferred from חֻפְשָׁה<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">The expression חפשי is used in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:2" href="/Exodus.21.2"><i>Ex</i>. 21:2</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:5" href="/Exodus.21.5">5</a> in reference to the male Hebrew slave. In R. Ismael’s opinion, the <i>ḥâruphah</i> slave girl is a former Gentile who needs manumission (Notes 147,148) and חֻפְשָׁה mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:20" href="/Leviticus.19.20"><i>Lev</i>. 19:20</a> is her bill of manumission. Since the Gentile slave girl can be manumitted by a document, it is concluded that the Hebrew slave can be acquired by a document. As noted later, this is a <i>non sequitur</i>.</i>. Everywhere Rebbi Ismael rejects combination of inferences and here, he accepts combination of inferences? Rebbi Ismael stated it in the name of the Sages<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">This translation follows the sentence structure of A.</i>. From where is it derived for Rebbi Ismael? “Sending away, sending away.” Since “sending away” mentioned there means by a contract, also “sending away” here means by a contract<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">Divorce, which requires a written document, is called “sending away” (of the wife) in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>. Therefore, the “sending away” of the Hebrew slave (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:12" href="/Deuteronomy.15.12"><i>Deut</i>. 15:12</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:13" href="/Deuteronomy.15.13">13</a>) also refers to a written document. As noted immediately, this only would prove that the master can terminate the indenture by a written document, not that he could acquire the Hebrew’s services by a document.</i>. But it does not compare. There it is to gain autonomy, here it is to be acquired by others! Rebbi Mattaniah<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">In A: “Rebbi said, a <i>baraita</i>.” This reading is preferable since a fifth-generation Amora is unlikely to appear in a Tannaïtic discussion.</i> said, “sale, sale”<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:42" href="/Leviticus.25.42"><i>Lev</i>. 25:42</a> it is stated that a Hebrew “shall not be sold as in the sale of a [Gentile] slave.” In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:12" href="/Deuteronomy.15.12"><i>Deut</i>. 15:12</a> it says, “If your Hebrew brother or sister be sold to you.” It is an accepted principle (<i>Sifry Deut</i>. 72, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 8:4:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.8.4.2-4">Yerushalmi <i>Baba batra</i> 8:5</a> (16b), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 5a" href="/Temurah.5a">Babli <i>Temurah</i>5a</a>) that if the verse notes that “something shall not be done,” if it is done it is sinful but legally valid since an invalid action does not have to be forbidden. Since the Gentile slave can be bought by contract (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>), the Hebrew slave also can be bought by contract. The question remains whether he can be bought by contract without the buyer sinning.</i>. Since “sale” mentioned there means by a contract, “sale” here also means by a contract. But since there<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">Since the laws regarding Gentile slaves in general follow the rules of real estate, undisturbed possession for three years accompanied by a claim of rightful acquisition legally establishes ownership. Nobody claims that the same holds for Hebrew slaves.</i> it is possible by possession, then here it should be possible by possession. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">A: R. Ḥiyya bar Abba (an Amora of the third generation). The reading of L is the only one acceptable, referring to R. Ḥiyya bar Ada I, of the generation of transition between Tannaïm and Amoraïm.</i> said, the male Hebrew is like the female Hebrew<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">This essentially is the argument given at the start of this paragraph, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.2.2">Note 159</a>. The reason for the female to be mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:12" href="/Deuteronomy.15.12"><i>Deut</i>. 15:12</a> at the start of rules mostly dealing only with the male, is to transfer the rules of acquisition from the female to the male.</i>.
“By money”. “The money of his sale price shall be;”<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:50" href="/Leviticus.25.50"><i>Lev</i>. 25:50</a>, speaking of the redemption of a Hebrew slave from his Gentile master by his family.</i> he can be redeemed by money, not by produce nor by vessels<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 8a" href="/Kiddushin.8a">Babli 8a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Behar, Chapter 8 4" href="/Sifra,_Behar,_Chapter_8.4"><i>Sifra Behar Pereq</i> 8(4</a>).</i>. Everywhere you treat money’s worth like money, but here you do not treat money’s worth like money. Rebbi Abba Mari said, there is a difference, because the verse repeated “money, of money”<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">The first כסף is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:50" href="/Leviticus.25.50"><i>Lev</i>. 25:50</a>, the second (read מכסף for בכסף) <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:52" href="/Leviticus.25.52"><i>Lev</i>. 25:52</a>, about the same subject. The repetition shows that the redemption can be forced only by payment in cash.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada said<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">Text of A: א״ר חייא בר אדא, preferred over L: “Even R. Ḥiyya bar Ada will agree.”</i>, but if he wants to reduce, he can leave for the reduced amount even for produce, even for vessels<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">The cash requirement applies only to redemption by his family. If the slave himself buys his freedom, he can offer valuables instead of money.</i>. Rebbi Yudan, Rebbi Mattaniah’s father, said that this is only if he did not give them their money’s worth<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">The cash requirement applies only to redemption by his family. If the slave himself buys his freedom, he can offer valuables instead of money.</i>. But if he gave them their money’s worth, it is like money.
“By a document.” Rebbi Abbahu said, by a money document<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">A written contract which promises the slave a fixed sum of money, presumably at the end of his period of service.</i>; therefore not by a gift document<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">The slave agrees to serve without remuneration.</i>, since maybe he would renege. But then, could he not renege even with a money document? He said to him<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">R. Abbahu to the person raising the question.</i>, maybe there comes a year of famine and his master reneges<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">It is not a problem of the prospective slave’s reneging, but his master’s. If in a year of famine the cost of the slave’s upkeep exceeds the value of his work, the master might be tempted to get rid of the slave. But if there is an IOU in the slave’s hand, he can be sure to be kept for the full period. Therefore, with a money document the slave has the intent from the start to be acquired by the master.</i>.
The order of sale<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">The required text in a contractual sale or marriage of an underage girl by her father. This is the only relevant case possible in law after the disestablishment of the Jubilee.</i> is the following: I X sold my daughter to Y. The order of preliminary marriage is the following: I X gave my daughter to Y in preliminary marriage. Rebbi Ḥaggai asked before Rebbi Yose: If he switched and said, I Y acquired X’s daughter, I Y took X’s daughter in preliminary marriage? He said to him, what is the difference<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">The marriage of the underage girl is purely a matter of contract between two adults; the girl is not involved and it does not matter which party writes the contract. This is in contrast to the marriage of an adult woman where a text “I, X, am taking you, Y, as preliminarily wedded wife” is valid, but “I, Y, am agreeing to be your, X’s, preliminarily wedded wife” is invalid.</i>? But if he changed the language of sale to that of preliminary marriage or vice-versa, he did not do anything.
It is written: “Six years he shall work<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:2" href="/Exodus.21.2"><i>Ex</i>. 21:2</a>.</i>.” How much does he work? I could think that he leaves at the end of six [years]. The verse says, “and in the seventh he shall leave<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:2" href="/Exodus.21.2"><i>Ex</i>. 21:2</a>.</i>.” I could think that he leave at the end of seven [years]<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Šim‘on b</i>.<i>Jochai</i>(ed. Epstein-Melamed) p. 160 the argument is made that the slave should leave only at the end of the year since debts are remitted only at the end of the Sabbatical. A similar argument is presupposed here.</i>. The verse says, “six years he shall work.” How is that? He works the entire six and leaves at the start of the seventh. “And in the seventh he shall leave.” The seventh from the sale and not the Seventh of the world<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">The Sabbatical year. <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Ismael</i> (ed. Horovitz-Rabin p. 249).</i>. Do you say, the seventh from the sale and not the Seventh of the world? Since it says, “six years he shall work,” this spells out six years. How can I confirm “and in the seventh he shall leave?” The seventh from the sale and not the Seventh of the world. Say it is the opposite. Rebbi Ze‘ira said in the name of Rav Huna: It is written: “and in the seventh,” “and in the seventh<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the correct interpretation is the one given by <i>Qorban Ha‘edah</i>: One has to read the word וּבַשְּׁבִעִית as if it were written twice with two different meanings. “Six years he shall work, including the Sabbatical; but in the seventh he shall leave.”</i>”. Rebbi Ḥuna said, if you say, in the Seventh of the world, what would the Jubilee free<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">Since the 49th year of a cycle is a Sabbatical, if the slaves left then, nobody would be freed in the 50th, the Jubilee.</i>? Rebbi Joḥanan bar Marius said, this follows him who said that the Jubilee does not become part of a Sabbatical period<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">The Sabbatical period following the first Jubilee starts only in year 51; there is a Jubilee in every year which is a multiple of 50 in the Jubilee calendar; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 61a" href="/Nedarim.61a">Babli <i>Nedarim</i> 61a</a>. This is the calendar also underlying the Saducee Jubilee.</i>. But following him who said that the Jubilee becomes part of a Sabbatical period, sometimes it falls in the middle of a Sabbatical period<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">Since year 49 was a Sabbatical, the next Sabbatical is in year 55, and the next Jubilee in year 100 is in year 2 of the Sabbatical cycle. The Jubilee in year <i>n</i>∙50 falls on year <i>n</i> (mod 7) of a Sabbatical cycle, it will eventually fall on any of the years of the cycle.</i>. The rabbis of Caesarea say, even following him who said that the Jubilee becomes part of a Sabbatical period, we could deduce from here that the Sabbatical year frees the slaves and the Jubilee the pierced ones<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">The preceding argument cannot be used to prove that the “seventh’ mentioned in the verse is not the Sabbatical; the argument at the start of the paragraph is the only valid one. (The emendation of this sentence by the classical commentaries has to be rejected since the text is confirmed by A.)</i>.
From where even if he fell ill<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">He could not work the entire six years. Nevertheless, he leaves on time. The same argument in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 17a" href="/Kiddushin.17a">Babli, 17a</a>. In <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Šim‘on b</i>.<i>Jochai</i>, p. 161, the word “gratis” is interpreted to mean that the master has no regress on the slave for his medical costs.</i>? The verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:2" href="/Exodus.21.2"><i>Ex</i>. 21:2</a>.</i>, “in the seventh year he shall leave into freedom gratis.” I could think, even if he fled; the verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:2" href="/Exodus.21.2"><i>Ex</i>. 21:2</a>.</i>, “six years he shall work.<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 16b" href="/Kiddushin.16b">Babli 16b</a>; <i>Massekhet ‘Avadim</i> 1.</i>” What did you see to include this and to exclude that? After the verse included, it excluded. I am including the one who remains in his power and am exluding the one who is not in his power. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said that Rav Hoshaia asked: I understand that if he fell ill and fled afterwards, he has to make up [the time lost]. If he fled and then fell ill? Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada said, let us hear from the following: “If a woman rebels against her husband, one writes him a bill of rebellion on her <i>ketubah</i>.”<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">A rewording of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Ketubot 5:9" href="/Mishnah_Ketubot.5.9">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 5:9, Notes 199–100</a>. The wife refuses marital relations with her husband; the court deducts from her future claim of <i>ketubah</i>.</i> And Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: One writes a bill of rebellion on the <i>ketubah</i> of a menstruating woman or a sick one, a preliminarily married one and one waiting for her levir<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:8:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.8.2-5"><i>Ketubot</i> 5:10</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 63b" href="/Ketubot.63b">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 63b</a>.</i>. Where do we hold? If she rebelled against him while she was menstruating, the Torah instructed her to rebel<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">Since sexual relations with menstruating women are forbidden.</i>. But we must hold that she rebelled when she was not menstruating and then started menstruating. Here she is unable to rebel, nevertheless you say, one writes<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">Once she refused marital relations, one fines her by reducing the sum due from her <i>ketubah</i> the entire time she had no relations, irrespective of the cause. By analogy, the runaway slave has to make up the time lost, including the time he was ill and could not have worked while in his master’s house.</i>. Here also, if he rebelled and then fell ill he has to make up. For he can say to him, if you had stayed with me, you would not have become sick. Rebbi Ḥinena said, that argument even works in the first case. If he fell ill and fled afterwards<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">If he was ill when he fled, he has to make up even the times of his sickness.</i>, he has to make up, since he can say to him, if you had stayed with me, you would have recuperated sooner.
“He regains his autonomy by years.” Some <i>Tannaїm</i> state: He may be sold for less than six [years], but he cannot be sold for more than six. But some <i>Tannaïm</i> state: He may not be sold for less than six [years] or more than to [year] seven. Rebbi Jeremiah said, the reason of that Tanna: Sometimes he is sold two or three years before the Jubilee; then the Jubilee arrives and frees him automatically<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">Even though formally he was sold for six years. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 15a" href="/Kiddushin.15a">Babli 15a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Behar, Chapter 7 4" href="/Sifra,_Behar,_Chapter_7.4"><i>Sifra Behar Pereq</i> 7(4</a>) in the name of the Tanna R. Eliezer ben Jacob.</i>.
“Or the Jubilee,” as it is written, “in the Jubilee he shall leave.”<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:31" href="/Leviticus.25.31"><i>Lev</i>. 25:31</a>. This quote does not make sense since it refers to real estate transactions. The correct quotes are <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:40" href="/Leviticus.25.40"><i>Lev</i>. 25:40</a> for the slave sold to an Israel and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:53" href="/Leviticus.25.53"><i>Lev</i>. 25:53</a> for the one sold to a Gentile. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 16a" href="/Kiddushin.16a">Babli 16a</a>.</i> “Or reduction of the amount,” as it is written: “If there still are many years<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:51-52" href="/Leviticus.25.51-52"><i>Lev</i>. 25:51–52</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Behar, Chapter 8 5" href="/Sifra,_Behar,_Chapter_8.5"><i>Sifra Behar Pereq</i>8(5</a>). The biblical text is unusually wordy, stating that the redemption price is high if many years of service remain and low if few years remain.</i>.” Do we not know that if there are many there are not few, and if there are few there are not many? Rebbi Hila said, sometimes the years are worth more than the wages, sometimes the wages are worth more than the years. How do you explain the following? If he was sold for a <i>mina</i><sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">The Greek Μνᾶ of 100 drachmas each, not the Babylonian מָנֶה of 60 <i>šeqel</i>.</i> each and improved so that now he is worth 200 each, from where that one computes for him only by a <i>mina</i> each? The verse says, “according to the years he shall compute his redemption<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:52" href="/Leviticus.25.52"><i>Lev</i>. 25:52</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 20a" href="/Kiddushin.20a">Babli 20a</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Arakhin 30a" href="/Arakhin.30a"><i>‘Arakhin</i> 30a</a>, the proof is from v. 51: “He shall compute the amount of his redemption from the amount for which he was bought.” There is an additional text: From where that if he was sold for 200 denars each but now he is worth only a <i>mina</i>, one computes a <i>mina</i> per year, the verse says “according to the years he shall compute his redemption.” If this passage also was in the Yerushalmi text, it must have disappeared already from the common source of A and L.</i>.” We learned that one sold to a Gentile is advantaged<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">In that his redemption price is determined by law from the original sale price; there can be no haggling over the amount.</i> when he comes to be redeemed. From where that one sold to an Israel is advantaged when he comes to be redeemed? “Hireling, hireling”<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">The slave has to be treated like a hireling; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:53" href="/Leviticus.25.53"><i>Lev</i>. 25:53</a> for the one sold to a Gentile, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:40" href="/Leviticus.25.40"><i>Lev</i>. 25:40</a> for the one sold to an Israel. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Behar, Chapter 8 6" href="/Sifra,_Behar,_Chapter_8.6"><i>Sifra Behar Pereq</i> 8(6</a>).</i> for an equal cut. Since the hireling is advantaged in redemption when he is sold to a Gentile, so too the hireling is advantaged in redemption when he is sold to an Israel. Rebbi says, why does the verse say “he shall be redeemed, he shall be redeemed,” three times? To add all redemptions that they have to follow this order<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Behar, Chapter 8 3" href="/Sifra,_Behar,_Chapter_8.3"><i>Sifra Behar Pereq</i> 8(3</a>), an anonymous statement.</i>.
“If he be not redeemed by these<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:54" href="/Leviticus.25.54"><i>Lev</i>. 25:54</a>.</i>.” <sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 15b" href="/Kiddushin.15b">Babli 15b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Behar, Chapter 9 1" href="/Sifra,_Behar,_Chapter_9.1"><i>Sifra Behar Pereq</i>9(1</a>).</i> Rebbi Yose the Galilean says, by these to freedom, by all others to servitude<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">“These” are the family members mentioned in vv. 48–49, who are required to buy him back from the Gentile. If he is bought from the Gentile by an unrelated Jew, he has to serve as a Hebrew slave either for six years or until the Jubilee,</i>. Rebbi Aqiba says, by these to servitude, by all others to freedom. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Both interpret the same verse: “Unless he be redeemed by these.” Rebbi Yose the Galilean interprets: “Unless he be redeemed by these” but by others he is made to serve before he leaves<sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">The verse reads: “Unless he be redeemed by these, he leaves in the Jubilee year.” If “these” are the modalities of redemption detailed in vv. 48–52, then the meaning is that the Hebrew slave of the Gentile leaves his service in the Jubilee year. But if “these” are persons, then there must be a case when the slave is redeemed from the Gentile and nevertheless has to serve until the Jubilee.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba interprets: “Unless he be redeemed by these”, by these he completes<sup class="footnote-marker">213a</sup><i class="footnote">The translation follows A.</i> before he leaves. The words of the Sages? Rebbi Yasa<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli: R. Ḥiyya bar Abba.</i> in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: In both cases to freedom<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote">They read “these” as the modalities of his redemption, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:2:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.2.10">Note 213</a>.</i>. It was stated thus: “If his hand achieves,<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:26" href="/Leviticus.25.26"><i>Lev</i>. 25:26</a>, referring to the right of a farmer to buy back his ancestral land for the prorated price the buyer had paid.</i>” his own hand<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Behar, Chapter 5 2" href="/Sifra,_Behar,_Chapter_5.2"><i>Sifra Behar Pereq</i> 5(2</a>).</i>. “Or he found for <i>his</i> redemption<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:26" href="/Leviticus.25.26"><i>Lev</i>. 25:26</a>, referring to the right of a farmer to buy back his ancestral land for the prorated price the buyer had paid.</i>.” Since his own hand achieves for himself, what others achieve also is for himself.
Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Following him, who said “for servitude”<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote">Either R. Yose the Galilean or R. Aqiba.</i>, he makes up before he leaves<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote">He serves until the Jubilee or until he has served a total of six years.</i>. But was it not stated: After he redeemed him it is as if he were sold to him, he enslaves him before he leaves<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote">This seems to mean that he has to serve the Jew who buys him a full six years, irrespective of how long he served the Gentile.</i>. Rebbi Abba Mari said, there is no “enslaves him before he leaves”, only “he makes up before he leaves.” If his relatives want to redeem him from the first [master’s], they redeem. If his relatives want to redeem him<sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote">It would be reasonable to vocalize active לִגְאֹל but ms. A clearly reads <i>niph‘al</i> להגאל. Yerushalmi Hebrew usually avoids <i>qal</i>, replacing it in general by <i>pi‘el</i>, sometimes by <i>niph‘al</i>.</i> from the second [master’s], they cannot redeem<sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote">There is no verse authorizing the premature release of a Hebrew slave from a Hebrew master.</i>. Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: That follows him<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose the Galilean; but the authorized interpretation is that of the Sages.</i> who said: “by these to freedom, by all others to servitude.”
Samuel bar Abba asked before Rebbi Yose: There it is written “he computes” and here it is written “he computes”<sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote">The person who sells of his ancestral property until the Jubilee “computes the years of sale” when he buys the property back (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:27" href="/Leviticus.25.27"><i>Lev</i>. 25:27</a>). Since sale of agricultural property is a sale of harvests (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:15" href="/Leviticus.25.15"><i>Lev</i>. 25:15</a>) it is clear that the right of redemption can be exercised only between harvest and the next sowing. Only full years are counted. But the redemption of the Hebrew slave of a Gentile is possible at all times and, therefore, the amount due changes every month.</i>. Here you compute months and years so he may leave, there you do not compute months and years so he may leave. He said to him, the difference is that the Torah compared him to the hireling<sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:53" href="/Leviticus.25.53"><i>Lev</i>. 25:53</a>, even though the verse refers to “a hireling on a yearly contract.”</i>. Since the latter computes months and years and leaves, so this one computes months and years and leaves.
<sup class="footnote-marker">226</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Ismael</i>,<i>Neziqin</i> 3; <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Šim‘on b</i>.<i>Jochai</i> p. 168; <i>Midrash Wehizhir Mišpaṭim</i> p. 130; alluded to in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 4a" href="/Kiddushin.4a">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i> 4a</a>.</i>“She leaves gratis,<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:11" href="/Exodus.21.11"><i>Ex</i>. 21:11</a>. Here starts the discussion of the rules for the girl sold by her father.</i>” these are the days of adulthood<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote">The indicators of adolescence are two pubic hairs after the girl has reached the age of 12 years and one day (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 10:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.10.2.1"><i>Nedarim</i> 10:1, Note 1</a>.) An adolescent is legally responsible for her actions but still under her father’s authority (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:2:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.2.10"><i>Yebamot</i> 1:2, Note 159</a>). She becomes an independent adult six months after becoming an adolescent. (A male becomes an adult only at age 13 years and one day if he did grow two pubic hairs.) Since the adult girl is not under her father’s authority, she cannot be under the authority of a master to whom she was given by her father even though she remains legally married by biblical standards if she was married off by her father when underage or adolescent. The double expression in the verse is taken to mean that the master’s authority ends when she reaches the stage of adolescent.</i>; “without money<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:11" href="/Exodus.21.11"><i>Ex</i>. 21:11</a>. Here starts the discussion of the rules for the girl sold by her father.</i>,” these are the indicators [of adolescence.] Could He not have mentioned only one of them<sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote">When the text of the laws was given to Moses.</i>? If only one of them were mentioned, would I not have said that if she leaves with indicators [of adolescence], then certainly in the days of adulthood? But in that case I would have said that they are identical with the days of adulthood. Would this not be logical? She is emancipated from the power of the father and she is emancipated from the power of the master; since she is emancipated from the power of the father by some indicators [of adulthood]<sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote">It would be reasonable to emend סימנין to בגר, to keep unity of style <i>(Qorban Ha‘edah)</i>, but A confirms the text as it stands.</i>, she is emancipated from the power of the master by the same indicators. Therefore, it was necessary to say, “she leaves gratis,” these are the days of adulthood, “without money,” these are the indicators [of adolescence.] Or maybe the other way around? Rebbi Tanḥuma in the name of Rebbi Ḥuna: “Without money.” At a place where there is money for the father, there is no money for the master<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 46b" href="/Ketubot.46b">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 46b</a>, as proof that the father retains the right to marry off his daughter and pocket the valuables offered for the preliminary marriage, even though the master’s authority already expired at the girl’s adolescence.</i>.
“If he did not allot her, he must let her be redeemed<sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:8" href="/Exodus.21.8"><i>Ex</i>. 21:8</a>. <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Šim‘on b</i>.<i>Jochai</i> p. 166; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 19a" href="/Kiddushin.19a">Babli 19a</a>. “Allotment” is the legal term for the preliminary marriage of the slave girl to her master or his son.</i>.” This teaches that he cannot allot her for himself unless there is time for her handywork to be worth a <i>peruṭah</i> and that a <i>peruṭah</i> be left to be deducted, the words of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">233</sup><i class="footnote">If he wants to marry her but first extract the maximum of work from the girl, he must divide the money he paid by the number of days of her prospective servitude and marry her as long as at least a <i>peruṭah</i> is not worked off by her. (Since she works at most 6 years, at most 2190 days, if the buying price was at least 11.5 denars he can marry her early on the last day of her servitude.)</i>. But the Sages say, he allots any time until sundown<sup class="footnote-marker">234</sup><i class="footnote">On the last day of her servitude; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 19b" href="/Kiddushin.19b">Babli 19b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada<sup class="footnote-marker">235</sup><i class="footnote">In A: “Abba”. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.2.2">Note 169</a>.</i> said, everybody agrees that for a Hebrew slave only if a <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth is left. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah says it correctly, what is the rabbis’ reason? There is no money left, there is no handiwork left? Rebbi Ze‘ira said, he allots her by speech<sup class="footnote-marker">236</sup><i class="footnote">The money he paid to the father was <i>not</i> given for preliminary marriage but to acquire the right to make her his wife by declaration (Interpretation of Maimonides; cf. S. Z. Brauda, בגדרי יעוד, <i>Moriah</i> 23 fasc. 6–7, 2000, p. 100–107.)</i>.
Rebbi Hoshaia stated: How does he allot her? He tells her in front of two [witnesses]: “You are allotted to me.<sup class="footnote-marker">237</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 19b" href="/Kiddushin.19b">Babli 19b</a>, an anonymous tannaїtic statement. Since “allotment” legally is preliminary marriage, it has to follow the latter’s formal rules.</i>” In Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah’s opinion, he gives her<sup class="footnote-marker">238</sup><i class="footnote">This word, לָהּ, is correctly missing in A and in the quote of this passage in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 19b" href="/Kiddushin.19b">Rashba’s <i>Novellae</i> to <i>Qiddušin</i>, 19b</a>. The money was not given to her but to her father.</i> money for the allotment at the end. In the rabbis’ opinion, the money for allotment was given at the first hour<sup class="footnote-marker">239</sup><i class="footnote">At the moment of the original sale.</i>. What is the difference between them? Her earning power. For him who says, he gives her money for the allotment at the end, her earning power belongs to her husband<sup class="footnote-marker">240</sup><i class="footnote">From the moment of “allotment”, she has to be treated as fiancee and can no longer be used as a slave. But anything she completed before this moment rightfully belongs to her master who now is her husband, since he paid for it.</i>. For him who says, the money for allotment was given at the first hour, her earning power belongs to her father<sup class="footnote-marker">241</sup><i class="footnote">Since it turns out that the money was not given for her work but for <i>qiddušin</i>, the father should have the right to claim the proceeds of anything she earned during her years of servitude, since the earnings of the daughter belong to the father until either she is married or becomes an adult (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 4:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.4.2.1"><i>Ketubot</i> 4:2, Note 30</a>). That would create an impossible situation.</i>. Even for him who says, the money for allotment was given at the first hour, her earning power belongs to her husband. It is as if he said: “You are preliminarily married to me on condition that your earning power belong to me.<sup class="footnote-marker">242</sup><i class="footnote">The father, by agreeing to the sale, agreed to the stipulation.</i>”
If he was married to her sister who died<sup class="footnote-marker">243</sup><i class="footnote">At the moment of sale, the future master could not possibly think of allotting her for himself since she was forbidden to him, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18:18" href="/Leviticus.18.18"><i>Lev</i>. 18:18</a>. Therefore, the money was given only for her services as a slave.</i>. For him who says, he gives money for the allotment at the end, she needs additional money. For him who says, the money for allotment was given at the first hour, she does not need additional money. Even for him who says, the money for allotment was given at the first hour, she needs additional money. Why? Everybody agrees that preliminary marriage is impossible in an incestuous relationship<sup class="footnote-marker">244</sup><i class="footnote">The master could not have acquired the right of allotment from the father while the girl was forbidden to him by an incest prohibition; he has to buy this right after the death of his first wife.</i>.
Is Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah of the opinion that the “allotment” discussed here is preliminary marriage by biblical standards<sup class="footnote-marker">245</sup><i class="footnote">Does R. Yose ben R. Jehudah require that a <i>peruṭah</i> be left in the end because he holds that “allotment” not only <i>is</i> preliminary marriage but also must follow all rules of preliminary marriages? Then it is difficult to understand why separate biblical rules are given in this case.</i>? Rebbi Abun said, it turns out that Rebbi Yose ben Rebi Jehudah agrees with Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar. As it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">246</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 47a" href="/Kiddushin.47a">Babli 47a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kiddushin (Lieberman) 3:1" href="/Tosefta_Kiddushin_(Lieberman).3.1">Tosephta 3:1</a>.</i>: By the deposit you hold for me<sup class="footnote-marker">247</sup><i class="footnote">There should be an initial clause: “You are preliminarily married to me for the value of …” It is assumed that the deposit was given on condition that she would be responsible only if she misused it, not if it was lost without her fault or stolen. If nothing was left, she received nothing and could not possibly be married.</i>; if she went and found that it was stolen or lost, if there was a <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth left, she is preliminarily married; otherwise, she is not preliminarily married. By the loan you are holding from me, if she went and found that it was stolen or lost, even if there was no <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth left, she is preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">248</sup><i class="footnote">Since she does not have to repay the loan, she received value and is married.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon says in the name of Rebbi Meïr, a loan is like a deposit: If there was a <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth left, she is preliminarily married; otherwise, she is not preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">249</sup><i class="footnote">As the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 47a" href="/Kiddushin.47a">Babli explains, 47a</a>, a loan is given to be spent. For R. Meïr, nobody can marry except by offering some tangible value.</i>. Just as Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon equates loan and deposit, so Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah equates “allotment” and loan<sup class="footnote-marker">250</sup><i class="footnote">He considers the purchase price as a loan to the father which is going to be repaid by the daughter’s work. Therefore she cannot be married if she is not freed from a <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth of work by the “allotment”.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">252</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following paragraphs are paralleled in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 10:6:8-7:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.10.6.8-7.8"><i>Yebamot</i> 10:14</a> (<b>י</b>), explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:2:4-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.2.4-10">Notes 178–210</a>.</i>“If he allots her to his son.” He allots her to his son but may not allot her to his brother. He should be able to allot her to his brother by an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i>! If he may allot her to his son who may not take his place for <i>ḥalîṣah</i> and levirate, should it not be logical that he may allot her to his brother who may take his place for <i>ḥalîṣah</i> and levirate? No. If you would accept the son since he will take [the father’s] place in a field of inheritance, what can you say about his brother who will not take his place in a field of inheritance? Would it be reasonable that he may allot her to him? The verse says, “if he allots her to his son.” He allots her to his son but may not allot her to his brother.
“If he allots her to his son;” but he may not allot her to his grandson. Samuel bar Abba asked before Rebbi Ze‘ira: In matters of inheritance you recognize the grandson like a son but here you do not recognize the grandson like a son? Rebbi Ze‘ira said, if anybody explains this matter, I shall pour him spiced wine. Rebbi Naḥum answered: In matters of inheritance where the brother can take the place of a son and all others can take the place of the son, the grandson takes the place of a son. But here, where neither the brother nor any other relative can take the place of a son, the grandson cannot take the place of a son. The rabbis of Caesarea answered: In matters of impurity you consider a brother like a son and all [near] relatives like a son but not a grandson like a son. They said, there went the spiced wine.
“If he allots her to his son;” with [the girl’s] agreement. Rebbi Joḥanan said, there is no agreement here. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said so, the statement follows Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah. Rebbi Samuel bar Eudaimon said, even if you say the statement follows Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah, is he not a minor? “If he allots her to his son;” with [the latter’s] agreement. Rebbi Joḥanan said, he allots her to his minor or adult son, with or without the latter’s agreement. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, he allots her only to his adult son with the latter’s agreement.
“A male nine years and one day old makes a widow for the High Priest or a divorcee or one who performed <i>ḥalîṣah</i> for a common priest.” In the opinion of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish who must explain this for a marriage, she should also be free from levirate or <i>ḥalîṣah</i>, as we have stated: “If he married a woman, that one is free from levirate or <i>ḥalîṣah</i>.” Rebbi Abin said, following Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish it comes following Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah, as it was stated so: If a male nine years and one day old up to twelve years and one day old grew two pubic hairs, they are warts. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah said, they are indicators [of puberty]. Rebbi Jacob ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: Only if they were still there at the time of indicators.
Rebbi Yose asked: When they remained until the time of indicators, does he retroactively become a man or from that time onward? For Rebbi Abun it is obvious that he becomes a man retroactively, so certainly also for the future, for he explains the position of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">253</sup><i class="footnote">Here ends fragment A.</i> by Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah. Why does Rebbi Yose not explain the position of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish by Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah since he asked, when they remained until the time of indicators, does he retroactively become a man or from that time onward? Rebbi Mana said, because he had difficulty. Rebbi Yose asked before me: When they remained until the time of indicators, does he retroactively become a man or from that time onward? One understands the case of a widow. But a divorcee? Explain it if he copulated with her after he became an adult and divorced her. <i>Ḥalîṣah</i>? Explain it if he copulated with her, died, and the [adult] brothers performed <i>ḥalîṣah</i>; she had <i>ḥalîṣah</i> because of him. In that case, even if he was younger than nine [years]! Rebbi Samuel ben Eudaimon said, that is correct. But because in all cases one stated “nine”, one stated this with them.
Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: Rebbi Yose ben Jehudah learned from Aḥaz, as it was stated: Aḥaz produced a child at age nine, Haran at age six, and Caleb at age ten following him who identifies Caleb ben Ḥeṣron with Caleb ben Yephuneh.
If he<sup class="footnote-marker">254</sup><i class="footnote">The father. The same statement is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 19b" href="/Kiddushin.19b">Babli, 19b</a>.</i> sold her to one and gave her in preliminary marriage to another, the father played with the master<sup class="footnote-marker">255</sup><i class="footnote">She is preliminarily married to the other man and the master lost his right of allotment.</i>, the words of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah; but the Sages say that the father did not play with the master<sup class="footnote-marker">256</sup><i class="footnote">The master did not lose his right of allotment.</i>. It is as if somebody said to a woman, “you are preliminarily married to me after 30 days,” since if somebody said to a woman, “you are preliminarily married to me after 30 days,” and another came and married her preliminarily (after)<sup class="footnote-marker">257</sup><i class="footnote">This should read: “Within”.</i> 30 days, is she not preliminarily married to the second man<sup class="footnote-marker">258</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.1.1">Mishnah 3:1</a>. Since R. Yose ben R. Jehudah holds that the money paid for the services of the girl is not given for preliminary marriage but only to acquire the right of allotment, the girl was not married at the time the second man gave money for preliminary marriage. Since nobody can marry an already married woman, the master has lost his right of allotment.</i>? “From now after 30 days.” Since if somebody said to a woman, “you are preliminarily married to me from now after 30 days,” and another came and married her preliminarily within the 30 days, is she not preliminarily married to both of them?<sup class="footnote-marker">259</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.1.1">Mishnah 3:1</a>. The Sages hold that the original price was given for preliminary marriage at the time chosen by the master. If another man gives her money for preliminary marriage before the master exercises his right of allotment, both have equal rights to her and neither can marry her without the other writing a bill of divorce. A third man can marry her only if both divorce her.</i> From then when he wants<sup class="footnote-marker">260</sup><i class="footnote">It is the Sages’ interpretation of the sale contract, that the buyer can activate his right of allotment any time he chooses.</i>. Everybody agrees that if he definitively married her off, the father played with the master<sup class="footnote-marker">261</sup><i class="footnote">If the second man combined preliminary and definitive marriage at the same time, nothing can undo a definitive marriage except divorce or death. However, this statement is problematic both for R. Yose ben R. Jehudah and for the Sages.<br>A reasonable interpretation of the position of R. Yose ben R. Jehudah would be that the sales contract, by which the master acquires the right of allotment, implies that the father surrendered his right to marry off his daughter. One must assume that the contract explicitly excluded this interpretation.<br>For the Sages it really should not make any difference whether preliminary and definitive marriages are performed together or not; the potential preliminary marriage of the master in any case precedes any action by the second man. One must assume that the Yerushalmi holds that in addition to the cases mentioned in the Mishnah, definitive marriage frees the girl from the ownership of her master (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:2:27" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.2.27">Note 279</a>). In that case, even though the act of preliminary marriage created the impossible situation described in the text, the definitive marriage solved the problem by eliminating the master’s ownership and with it his right of allotment. The only recourse the master has is to sue the father for the value of services lost. (Explanation of S. Z. Brauda, <i>loc</i>.<i>cit</i>. Note 236).</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">262</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 19b" href="/Kiddushin.19b">Babli 19b</a>; <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Šim‘on b</i>,<i>Jochai</i>, p. 165.</i>“As a slave girl.<sup class="footnote-marker">263</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:7" href="/Exodus.21.7"><i>Ex</i>. 21:7</a></i>” Only as a slave girl: this teaches that he may sell her on condition that he not have the right of allotment over her, the words of Rebbi Meïr. But the Sages say, he did not do anything, for he stipulated against what is written in the Torah, “and the stipulation of anybody who stipulates against what is written in the Torah is invalid.”<sup class="footnote-marker">264</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Baba meṣi‘a</i> 7:14.</i> But does Rebbi Meïr not agree that “the stipulation of anybody who stipulates against what is written in the Torah is invalid”<sup class="footnote-marker">265</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah is anonymous and is based on R. Meïr’s compilation, it is assumed that it represents R. Meïr’s position.</i>? He accepts it if it is impossible to keep the stipulation in the end, but here it is possible to keep the stipulation in the end<sup class="footnote-marker">266</sup><i class="footnote">This is explicitly stated in <i>Baba meṣi‘a</i> 7:14. Since the master is not required to marry his servant, he will not infringe on any biblical obligation by agreeing not to marry her. Everybody will agree that if a man marries a woman on condition that she be freed from levirate or <i>ḥalîṣah</i> if he should die childless, the stipulation is invalid since it infringes on obligatory biblical law.</i>. Do the rabbis not agree about a stipulation which it is possible to keep in the end? They agree to it in money matters<sup class="footnote-marker">267</sup><i class="footnote">For which it is formulated in Mishnah <i>Baba meṣi‘a</i> 7:14; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:8:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.8.5"><i>Ketubot</i>5:10 Note 227</a> where all references are given.</i>, but this is a personal matter. But did we not state: A person may marry a woman and stipulate with her that she have no claim on him for food, clothing, or marital relations<sup class="footnote-marker">268</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 19b" href="/Kiddushin.19b">Babli, 19b</a>, it reads: “A person may marry a woman and stipulate with her that she have no claim on him for food, clothing, or marital relations; she is married and the stipulations are void, the words of R. Meïr; R. Jehudah says, in money matters his stipulation is valid.” As noted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:8:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.8.5"><i>Ketubot</i> 5:10 Note 227</a>, R. Jehudah’s opinion is accepted in the Yerushalmi as generally valid.</i>? One understands food and clothing. But are marital relations not a personal matter? Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada said, explain it for an underage girl<sup class="footnote-marker">269</sup><i class="footnote">Who does not expect sexual relations.</i>. How can that Tanna explain “as a slave girl”<sup class="footnote-marker">270</sup><i class="footnote">R. Meïr who denies the ability of the father to exclude allotment.</i>? He may sell her as a widow to the High Priest, as a divorcee or one who received <i>ḥălîṣah</i> to a common priest<sup class="footnote-marker">271</sup><i class="footnote">The father may sell her to a person unable to marry her by biblical law (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 19b" href="/Kiddushin.19b">Babli 19b</a>; <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Šim‘on b</i>,<i>Jochai</i>, p. 165).</i>. How can that Tanna explain “as a servant”? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, explain it by a widow from preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">272</sup><i class="footnote">Since an underage girl is emancipated from her father by definitive marriage (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.4.3.1"><i>Ketubot</i> 4:3, Note 42</a>), the girl sold as a widow or divorcee must be a widow or divorcee after preliminary marriage only. The mention of <i>ḥăliṣah</i> is a slip of the pen because of the usual combination of the terms; for the House of Hillel <i>ḥălîṣah</i> is possible only after definitive marriage (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.6.2"><i>Yebamot</i> 1:6, Note 192</a>).</i>. But was it not stated: A person may sell his daugher into marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">273</sup><i class="footnote">Preliminary, not definitive.</i> and repeat, into servitude and repeat, into marriage after servitude, but not into servitude after marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">274</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 18a" href="/Kiddushin.18a">Babli 18a</a>; <i>Massekhet ‘Avadim</i> 1.</i>? Rebbi Joḥanan said, these are different Tannaïm. He who states “a widow to the High Priest” agrees to servitude after marriage; he who does not state “a widow to the High Priest” does not agree to servitude after marriage. How does the latter Tanna explain “as a servant”<sup class="footnote-marker">275</sup><i class="footnote">As servant only, excluding the possibility of marriage; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:2:16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.2.16">Note 243</a>.</i>? Rebbi Mattaniah said, explain it if [the master] was married to her sister<sup class="footnote-marker">271</sup><i class="footnote">The father may sell her to a person unable to marry her by biblical law (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 19b" href="/Kiddushin.19b">Babli 19b</a>; <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Šim‘on b</i>,<i>Jochai</i>, p. 165).</i>.
Rebbi Simeon ben Yoḥai stated: Just as he cannot sell her into servitude after marriage, so not into servitude after servitude<sup class="footnote-marker">276</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 18b" href="/Kiddushin.18b">Babli 18b</a>. In <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Ismael</i>,<i>Neziqin</i>3, this is ascribed to R. Yose the Galilean and derived from the singular used in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:7" href="/Exodus.21.7"><i>Ex</i>. 21:7</a>.</i>. What is Rebbi Simeon ben Ioḥai’s reason? “By his being unfaithful to her.<sup class="footnote-marker">277</sup><i class="footnote">He reads בְּבִגְדוֹ as verbal form derived from the root בגד “to be unfaithful, traitorous”, referred to the father. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 18b" href="/Kiddushin.18b">Babli, 18b</a>, this is R. Aqiba’s opinion.</i>” Once he can be unfaithful to her, he cannot be unfaithful to her a second time. How do the rabbis explain Rebbi Simeon ben Ioḥai’s reason? “She being in his garment<sup class="footnote-marker">278</sup><i class="footnote">He reads בְּבִגְדּוֹ as nominal form derived from בגד “garment”, referring to the master as husband. They hold that the girl sold into servitude is emancipated even by preliminary marriage. In the Babli this is the position taken by R. Eliezer; anonymous in <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Šim‘on b</i>,<i>Jochai</i>, p. 166.</i>;” from the moment that [her master] spread his garment on her, her father no longer has any power over her.
Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish asked before Rebbi Joḥanan: Should not the Hebrew slave girl leave by definitive marriage, by an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i>? Since indications [of puberty] which do not emancipate her from her father’s power, free her from her master’s power<sup class="footnote-marker">279</sup><i class="footnote">An adolescent girl remains in her father’s power for six months after her formal puberty (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 10:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.10.2.1"><i>Nedarim</i> 10:1, Note 1</a>) but leaves servitude at the actual onset of puberty.</i>, is it not logical that definitive marriage, which emancipates her from her father’s power, shall free her from her master’s power? He said to him, I have only the Mishnah: “The Hebrew slave girl in addition regains her autonomy by indicators of puberty<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">While a father can forcibly marry off his daughter until she has reached adulthood (12 years six months and one day) he cannot sell her into slavery beyond adolescence (12 years and a day if she shows signs of puberty). He cannot sell her to work for longer than he had the right to sell.</i>.” Bar Pedaiah<sup class="footnote-marker">280</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 17b" href="/Kiddushin.17b">Babli, 17b</a>, “R. Pada”.</i> said, the Hebrew slave girl leaves at the death of her master. What is the reason? “Also your slave girl you shall treat in the same way;” and it is written: “He shall be <i>for you</i> a permanent slave.<sup class="footnote-marker">281</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:17" href="/Deuteronomy.15.17"><i>Deut</i>. 15:17</a>, making it clear that the servitude of the “pierced slave” is personally to his master; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.2.1">Note 157</a>. By consensus of all sources, the final clause of that verse, speaking of the Hebrew slave girl, cannot refer to the piercing of the earlobe since this would contradict <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:5" href="/Exodus.21.5"><i>Ex</i>. 21:5</a>.</i>” He bracketed the Hebrew slave girl with the “pierced slave”. Since the “pierced slave” leaves at his master’s death, the Hebrew slave girl also leaves at her master’s death<sup class="footnote-marker">282</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 17b" href="/Kiddushin.17b">Babli 17b</a>; <i>Sifry Deut</i>. #122; <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Ismael</i>,<i>Neziqin</i> 2; <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Šim‘on b</i>.<i>Jochai</i> p. 164.</i>. It turns out that Bar Pedaiah parallels the following Tanna, as it was stated: The Hebrew slave<sup class="footnote-marker">283</sup><i class="footnote">The one indentured for six years, not the “pierced slave” (parallel sources cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:2:27" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.2.27">Note 282</a>).</i> serves the son but not the daughter; the Hebrew slave girl serves the daughter but not the son<sup class="footnote-marker">284</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 17b" href="/Kiddushin.17b">Babli 17b</a>: Neither son nor daughter.</i>. Some Tannaїm state: The Hebrew slave girl as well as the Hebrew slave<sup class="footnote-marker">285</sup><i class="footnote">The “pierced slave”, not the one indentured for six years. This is Bar Pedaiah’s statement.</i> serve neither the son nor the daughter. How does the Tanna confirm “your slave girl you also shall treat in the same way”? He explains it about the allowance<sup class="footnote-marker">286</sup><i class="footnote">A supply of produce, required to be given the departing slave by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:14" href="/Deuteronomy.15.14"><i>Deut</i>. 15:14</a>. This is the standard interpretation (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 17b" href="/Kiddushin.17b">Babli 17b</a>; <i>Sifry Deut</i>. #122).</i>, as it was stated: The following are given allowances: The one who leaves after six years or in the Jubilee, and the Hebrew slave girl who becomes autonomous by indicators of puberty. But one does not give allowances to one who leaves by the reduction of the amount<sup class="footnote-marker">287</sup><i class="footnote">Who buys his freedom by repaying the amount given for his services reduced proportionally to the time served.</i> or following the master’s death<sup class="footnote-marker">288</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 16b" href="/Kiddushin.16b">Babli, 16b</a>, and <i>Sifry Deut</i>. #119, the person leaving at his master’s death is entitled to an allowance. The Yerushalmi restricts allowances to persons serving a predetermined period. (In <i>Masekhet ‘Avadim</i> 2, allowances are given only to those sold by the court.)</i>.
It is written: “His master shall bring him to the judges, or he shall bring him to the door<sup class="footnote-marker">289</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ex</i>.. 21:6.</i>.” How is that? The one who was sold by the court, “his master shall bring him to the judges.” The one who sold himself, “or he shall bring him to the door.”<sup class="footnote-marker">290</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Ismael Neziqin</i> 2; but in <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Šim‘on b</i>.<i>Jochai</i> p. 163 (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:17" href="/Deuteronomy.15.17"><i>Midrash Haggadol Deut</i>. 15:17</a>) even the private act of piercing needs a public act in court.</i> Rebbi Immi asked: It is obvious that the court writes the contract for him who was sold by the court. Who is writing the contract for him who sells himself?<sup class="footnote-marker">291</sup><i class="footnote">No answer is given since in a private transaction any one of the parties may write the contract.</i> It is written: “It shall not be hard in your eyes to send him away free from you; for twice the hire of a hireling he served you for six years.” The hired hand works during the day; he does not work in the night. The Hebrew slave works both day and night. It is written: “He shall not treat him harshly before your eyes<sup class="footnote-marker">292</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:53" href="/Leviticus.25.53"><i>Lev</i>. 25:53</a>.</i>,” and you say so<sup class="footnote-marker">293</sup><i class="footnote">It certainly is forbidden to ask more than a full day’s work from any slave, whether Hebrew or not.</i>? Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: His master marries him to a Canaanite slave; it turns out that he serves him day and night<sup class="footnote-marker">294</sup><i class="footnote">Since in the night he produces slave children for his master.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Mamai asked before Rebbi Immi: Think of it, if he was buying a Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">295</sup><i class="footnote">Since a Cohen may not marry a divorcee, certainly a slave woman is forbidden to him.</i>! He answered him: Would the case of an Israel not also be permission of something prohibited following Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">296</sup><i class="footnote">The slave woman is not more strictly forbidden to the Cohen than she is to the Israel. According to R. Simeon, children of a Gentile father and a Jewish mother have no family relationship with their father; children of a Jewish father and Gentile mother have no family relationship with their father; the latter statement includes slave women who as yet are not fully Jewish (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 4:15:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.4.15.2-4"><i>Yebamot</i> 4:15</a>,7:6 Note 135). Therefore, if the slave woman is temporarily permitted to the Israel, she also is temporarily permitted to the Cohen slave. The woman is not married to the slave in legal terms since at the moment when the slave regains his freedom she automatically becomes forbidden to him and permitted to others without a bill of divorce.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 21b" href="/Kiddushin.21b">Babli, 21b</a>, only Rav holds that the Canaanite woman was permitted to the Cohen while Samuel disagrees.</i>? Rebbi Abba bar Mamai here changed his mind.
Rebbi Jehudah ben Rebbi Abun<sup class="footnote-marker">297</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 21b" href="/Kiddushin.21b">Babli, 21b</a>, and <i>Sifry Deut</i>. #122, he seems to be identified as R. Jehudah (bar Ilai); explicitly so in <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Ismael Neziqin</i> 2. The opinion ascribed here to R. Meïr there is that of the anonymous Sages.</i> preached: The earlobe was pierced, lest a Cohen become disqualified. Rebbi Meïr says, he was pierced at his cartilage. Therefore, Rebbi Meïr says that a Cohen cannot be pierced lest he become blemished and be disqualified for service<sup class="footnote-marker">298</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Bekhorot 6:1" href="/Mishnah_Bekhorot.6.1">Mishnah <i>Bekhorot</i> 6:1</a> notes that both sacrificial animal and priest are disqualified for Divine service if the cartilage of their ears be punctured in the size of a vetch seed.</i>. Could not the cartilage be pierced less than the size of a vetch seed? Maybe it would result in the size of a vetch seed. Let it be the size of a vetch! The Torah said, “he shall return to his inheritance<sup class="footnote-marker">299</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:27" href="/Leviticus.25.27"><i>Lev</i>. 25:27</a>. This is the wrong quote since it refers to real estate returned to its original owner in the Jubilee. The verse referring to the Hebrew slave released in the Jubilee is v. 41, “he shall return to his family, to his forefathers’ inheritance he shall return.” The inheritance of a priest is the Divine service (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:20" href="/Numbers.18.20"><i>Num</i>. 18:20</a>).</i>,” as he was. He cannot be pierced unless he had a wife and children<sup class="footnote-marker">300</sup><i class="footnote">A Jewish wife and children, whom he is unable to support by himself. <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Ismael Neziqin</i>2, <i>dR</i>.<i>Šim‘on b</i>.<i>Jochai</i> p. 163.</i>. “By an awl”<sup class="footnote-marker">301</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:6" href="/Exodus.21.6"><i>Ex</i>. 21:6</a>.</i>. Not only an awl, from where even a buck-thorn, even a thorn, even glass? The verse says, “he pierces”<sup class="footnote-marker">302</sup><i class="footnote">This interpretation seems to be the reason for the masoretic accents which introduce a dividing accent: “he shall pierce his ear, with an awl”, taking “as an awl” as an afterthought. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 21b" href="/Kiddushin.21b">Babli, 21b</a>, refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:17" href="/Deuteronomy.15.17"><i>Deut</i> 15:17</a>: “You shall take the awl,” anything that can be used to serve as an awl.</i>. This follows Rebbi Aqiba.
Following Rebbi Ismael? Rebbi Ismael stated<sup class="footnote-marker">303</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 16b" href="/Sotah.16b">Babli <i>Soṭah</i> 16b</a>; <i>Sifry Deut</i>. #122.</i>: At three places teaching circumvents Scripture and at another place the interpretation.The Torah said, “in a scroll”<sup class="footnote-marker">304</sup><i class="footnote">This is a wrong quote, referring to Num. 6:23, the text of the incantations required in the rite of the wife suspected of infidelity. But that text has to be written on a scroll; cf. Soṭah 2:4, Notes 143-144. Here, it should say סֵפֶר "book", referring to the divorce document mentioned in Deut. 24:1 which can be written on anything not connected to the ground; Mishnah Giṭṭin 2:3. The quote is correct in the sources quoted in the preceding Note.</i>, but practice said on anything separated from the ground. The Torah said, “in dust”<sup class="footnote-marker">305</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 17:13" href="/Leviticus.17.13"><i>Lev</i>. 17:13</a>. The blood of slaughtered wild animals or birds has to be covered “in dust”.</i>, but practice said in anything on which plants grow<sup class="footnote-marker">306</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Chullin 6:6" href="/Mishnah_Chullin.6.6">Mishnah <i>Ḥulin</i> 6:6</a>.</i>. The Torah said, “with an awl”, but practice said, even a buck-thorn, even a thorn, even glass. And at one place the interpretation<sup class="footnote-marker">307</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ismael’s own hermeneutical rules.</i>: Rebbi Ismael stated: “It shall be on the seventh day that he shave all his hair<sup class="footnote-marker">308</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:9" href="/Leviticus.14.9"><i>Lev</i>. 14:9</a>, speaking of the ritual purification of the healed sufferer from skin disease. All the quotes are from this verse.</i>,” inclusion. “His head, his beard, and his eyebrows,” detail. Since it continues “and all his hair he shall shave,” it repeats inclusion. Inclusion, detail, and inclusion is judged only by what is similar to the detail<sup class="footnote-marker">309</sup><i class="footnote">By the seventh hermeneutical rule one has to try to find an intensional definition of the properties common to the examples given as detail; these then are the properties referred to by the inclusions.</i>. Since the detail is explained as place of bunching and exposed, it should refer only [hair growing] in bunches at exposed places. But practice is that he shaves to be like a gourd<sup class="footnote-marker">310</sup><i class="footnote">Shaving completely every exposed hair; Mishnah <i>Nega‘im</i> 14:4. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Metzora, Chapter 2" href="/Sifra,_Metzora,_Chapter_2"><i>Sifra Meṣora‘ Pereq</i> 2</a> disagrees with the <i>baraita</i> here and the Mishnah and holds that the insistence on the shaving of <i>all</i> hair in both inclusions requires that any single one of the properties mentioned in the analysis of the detail, hair growing in bunches <i>or</i> visible, has to be shaved but nothing else. The Tanna of <i>Sifra</i> holds that practice follows interpretation closely.)</i>. “With an awl”, since an awl is made of metal, so anything made of metal<sup class="footnote-marker">311</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonian sources, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 21b" href="/Kiddushin.21b">Babli 21b</a>, <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Ismael Neziqin</i> 2, <i>Sifry Deut #</i> 122, this is an argument of Rebbi, in <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Šim‘on b</i>.<i>Jochai</i>, of R. Yose ben Jehudah: Any metal implement which can be used for piercing is called “awl”. The most detailed analysis of the verse is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 21b" href="/Kiddushin.21b">Babli, 21b</a>, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:17" href="/Deuteronomy.15.17"><i>Midrash Haggadol Deut</i>. 15:17</a>) where the inclusion-exclusion methodology of R. Ismael is shown to lead to the admissibility of any metal piercing instrument and the addition-subtraction methodology of R. Aqiba to the inclusion of all mechanical and the exclusion of chemical means.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, this is a large drill<sup class="footnote-marker">312</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sifry Deut #</i> 122, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:17" href="/Deuteronomy.15.17"><i>Midrash Haggadol Deut</i>. on <i>Deut</i>. 15:17</a>: This is the large awl. The Yerushalmi text seems to be the original.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah says, that is the engraving-knife. “He shall bring him to the door.<sup class="footnote-marker">313</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:6" href="/Exodus.21.6"><i>Ex</i>. 21:6</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:17" href="/Deuteronomy.15.17"><i>Deut</i>. 15:17</a>.</i>” I could think, even if it was lying flat. The verse says, “or to the door-post”.
