Since the door-post is upright, so also the door has to be upright<sup class="footnote-marker">314</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 22b" href="/Kiddushin.22b">Babli 22b</a>, <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Ismael Neziqin</i> 2, <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Šim‘on b</i>.<i>Jochai</i> p. 163.</i>: a shame to him and to his family<sup class="footnote-marker">315</sup><i class="footnote">They violated their obligation to support their relative when he could not fend for himself.</i>.
It was stated: Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob says, why at the door? Because by the door they went from servitude to freedom<sup class="footnote-marker">316</sup><i class="footnote">By the blood of the Passover sacrifice on the doorposts in Egypt, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:7" href="/Exodus.12.7"><i>Ex</i>. 12:7</a>.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">317</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 22b" href="/Kiddushin.22b">Babli 22b</a>; <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Ismael Neziqin</i> 2.</i> The students asked Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai: Why should the slave be pierced in his ear rather than in any of his other body parts? He said to them, the ear which heard at Mount Sinai: “You shall have no other powers besides Me,” and removed from himself the yoke of the Kingdom of Heaven but accepted the yoke of flesh and blood; the ear which heard before Mount Sinai: “For the Children of Israel are My servants,” and went and acquired another master; therefore the ear should come and be pierced for he<sup class="footnote-marker">317a</sup><i class="footnote">The slave.</i> did not follow what his ear heard.
“His ear<sup class="footnote-marker">318</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:6" href="/Exodus.21.6"><i>Ex</i>. 21:6</a>.</i>.” Since “his ear” mentioned there<sup class="footnote-marker">319</sup><i class="footnote">This may refer either to the induction ceremony of the priests (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 8:23" href="/Leviticus.8.23"><i>Lev</i>. 8:23</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 8:24" href="/Leviticus.8.24">24</a>) or the purification of the sufferer from skin disease (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:14" href="/Leviticus.14.14"><i>Lev</i>. 14:14</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:17" href="/Leviticus.14.17">17</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:25" href="/Leviticus.14.25">25</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:28" href="/Leviticus.14.28">28</a>). In both cases, it is spelled out that blood was given on the <i>right</i> ear’s cartilage.</i> means the right ear, also here the right ear<sup class="footnote-marker">320</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Ismael</i>,<i>Neziqin</i> 2; <i>Sifry Deut</i>. #122.</i>. “If the slave says saying<sup class="footnote-marker">321</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:5" href="/Exodus.21.5"><i>Ex</i>. 21:5</a>. The interpretation of the emphatic infinitive construction as additional condition is characteristic of R. Aqiba’s interpretation; it is rejected by R. Ismael who considers it a regular feature of biblical syntax.</i>”, two sayings, one at the end of the sixth year and one at the start of the seventh year<sup class="footnote-marker">322</sup><i class="footnote">The slave has to give notice of his intention to remain in his servile state while he still is indentured and has to repeat it at the moment he should become free again. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 22a" href="/Kiddushin.22a">Babli 22a</a>, <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Ismael</i>,<i>Mišpaṭim</i> 2; <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Šim‘on b</i>.<i>Jochai</i> p. 162; <i>Sifry Deut</i>. #121.</i>. One at the end of the sixth year, while he still is in his servitude and one at the start of the seventh year, “I will not go free.<sup class="footnote-marker">323</sup><i class="footnote">This is the statement required at the time of his planned release.</i>” “I love my master, my wife, and my children.<sup class="footnote-marker">324</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:5" href="/Exodus.21.5"><i>Ex</i>. 21:5</a>.</i>” This teaches that he cannot be pierced unless he have a wife and children, unless he love his master and his master love him; unless the property be blessed because of him<sup class="footnote-marker">325</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Ismael</i>,<i>Mišpaṭim</i>2; <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Šim‘on b</i>.<i>Jochai</i> p. 162; <i>Sifry Deut</i>. #121; <i>Midrash Tannaïm</i> preserved in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:17" href="/Deuteronomy.15.17"><i>Midrash Haggadol Deut</i>. 15:17</a>. The last source is the only one which explicitly refers the mention of “the wife” to the Canaanite slave given to him by his master (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:5" href="/Exodus.21.5">Rashi to <i>Ex</i>. 21:5</a>), not to the Jewish wife and her children who also have to be sustained by his master (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:3" href="/Exodus.21.3"><i>Ex</i>. 21:3</a>).</i> as it is said: “For he feels well with you<sup class="footnote-marker">324</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:5" href="/Exodus.21.5"><i>Ex</i>. 21:5</a>.</i>.”
“He regains his autonomy in the Jubilee,” as it is written: “He leaves in the Jubilee together with his children<sup class="footnote-marker">326</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:54" href="/Leviticus.25.54"><i>Lev</i>. 25:54</a>. The text is misquoted; it reads וְיָצָא בִּשְׁנַת הַיֹּבֵל “he leaves in the Jubilee year.”</i>.” “Or at the master’s death,” as it is written: “He shall be for you a perpetual slave,<sup class="footnote-marker">327</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:17" href="/Deuteronomy.15.17"><i>Deut</i>. 15:17</a>. In contrast to the slave indentured for six years, the “pierced slave” does not serve the son. <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Ismael Neziqin</i> 2, end; <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Šim‘on b</i>.<i>Jochai</i> p. 164; <i>Sifry Deut</i>. #121.</i>” all the days of his master’s lifetime.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A Canaanite slave<sup class="footnote-marker">328</sup><i class="footnote">Any Gentile slave is called “Canaanite” since Canaan was called “a slave of slaves to his fellow men” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 9:25" href="/Genesis.9.25"><i>Gen</i>. 9:25</a>).</i> is acquired by money, or contract, or possession<sup class="footnote-marker">329</sup><i class="footnote">A slave who becomes ownerless by the death of his intestate owner who dies without heirs can be acquired by anybody who makes him perform a slave’s duties for himself. Also, a person who claims to have bought a slave from another party will have his ownership confirmed by the court if he can show three years of uncontested ownership. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.4.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 5:5, Note 100</a>.</i>. He gains his autonomy by money through a third party<sup class="footnote-marker">330</sup><i class="footnote">R. Meïr holds that any property of the slave’s is his master’s. If the slave receives monies from other people on condition that they not be his master’s property, the condition is not operative. The slave cannot buy his freedom with his master’s money.</i> or a document by himself<sup class="footnote-marker">331</sup><i class="footnote">By manumission, the slave (who had been circumcized as a Jew’s slave) becomes a full Jew. As such, he is permitted to marry a Jewish partner but is prohibited promiscuity. R. Meïr holds that people prefer promiscuity to the discipline of marriage; for him, manumission has detrimental aspects. Since nobody can legally act to another person’s detriment without that person’s authorization, a third party cannot accept a document of manumission without the slave’s authorization.</i>, the words of Rebbi Meïr. But the Sages say, by money also by himself<sup class="footnote-marker">332</sup><i class="footnote">If a slave receives money on condition that it not be his master’s, the condition is valid. The slave may use that money to buy his freedom.</i> or a document through a third party<sup class="footnote-marker">333</sup><i class="footnote">They hold that the prohibition of promiscuity is a small price to pay for freedom and the possibility of marriage. Since a person can legally act to another person’s benefit without that person’s authorization, a third party can accept a document of manumission without the slave’s authorization.</i>, if only the money comes from a third party.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A Canaanite slave is acquired by money,” etc. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">334</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:46" href="/Leviticus.25.46"><i>Lev</i>. 25:46</a>, speaking of Gentile slaves. The same argument in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 22b" href="/Kiddushin.22b">Babli, 22b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 13a" href="/Bekhorot.13a"><i>Bekhorot</i> 13a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Behar, Section 6 4" href="/Sifra,_Behar,_Section_6.4"><i>Sifra Behar Parašah</i> 6(4</a>).</i>: “They shall be inherited by your children after you, to be possessed as inheritance” This brackets slaves with landed property. Since real estate can be acquired by money, or contract, or possession, so a Canaanite slave can be acquired by money, or contract, or possession.
<sup class="footnote-marker">335</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 26a" href="/Kiddushin.26a">Babli 26a</a>.<br>A parallel text for this and the following paragraphs is in Halakhah 5 (Note 479); variants are noted there.</i> From where that landed property is bought by money, or contract, or possession? “Fields will be bought by money, writing on a scroll and signing, and testifying of witnesses.<sup class="footnote-marker">336</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 32:44" href="/Jeremiah.32.44"><i>Jer</i>. 32:44</a>. A prophetic text cannot really serve as legal source but it can demonstrate practice. Since the speaker in this verse is God, the practice is accepted by Heaven.</i>” “And signing”, these are the witnesses on the contract. “And testifying of witnesses,” these are the witnesses to possession. Or maybe these are only the witnesses on the contract? These are already written: “Writing on a scroll and signing.”
Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Mana<sup class="footnote-marker">337</sup><i class="footnote">R. Mana I.</i>; Rebbi Tanḥum, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Real estate cannot be bought for less than a <i>peruṭah</i>. What is the reason? “Fields will be bought by money<sup class="footnote-marker">338</sup><i class="footnote">Anything less than the smallest coin is not money.</i>.” This disagrees with what Rebbi Ḥanina said<sup class="footnote-marker">339</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 50a" href="/Bekhorot.50a">Babli <i>Bekhorot</i> 50a</a>. There, the statement of R. Jehudah bar Pazi is ascribed to R. Ḥanina. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 58:9" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.58.9"><i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i>58(9</a>), the statement of R. Jehudah bar Pazi is in the name of R. Yudan.</i>: Any <i>šeqalim</i> mentioned in the Torah are tetradrachmas<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>‘Arakhin</i> 7:1. If somebody dedicates his field to the Temple, Scripture prescribes that he redeem it proportional to the numbers of years to the next Jubilee. The full rate, for the entire 49 yars of a Jubilee cycle, is 50 <i>šeqel</i> for a field on which a <i>ḥomer</i> (or <i>kur</i>, 30 <i>se’ah</i>) of barley can be sown (75’000 square cubits). Talmudic theory identifies the biblical <i>šeqel</i> with the Roman tetradrachma, which is slightly more than the standard <i>šeqel</i> documented from First Kingdom times, 11.4 g. Since in the vernacular the <i>zūz</i>, the Babylonian half-<i>šeqel</i>, was identified with the Roman <i>denarius</i>, the “sheqel” was identified as two denar. This gave rise to the theory that the biblical “holy <i>šeqel</i>”, which simply was “the king’s weight” and still the Tyrian <i>šeqel</i> in Roman times, was twice the profane <i>šeqel</i>. The rate to be paid for the standard field per year therefore was <sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup>/<sub>49</sub> = 1 <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup>/<sub>49</sub> tetradrachma. The rate stated in the Mishnah is 1 <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup>/<sub>48</sub> tetradrachma. The small difference, <sup>1</sup>/<sub>48</sub> - <sup>1</sup>/<sub>49</sub> is <i>qolbon</i> (κόλλυβος, Latin <i>collybus</i>,<i>collubus</i>, “exchange of coins; rate of exchange”), the small coin paid as agio to the money-changer to convert the sum into actual coin. The Mishnah shows that a <i>dupondius</i> was <sup>1</sup>/<sub>48</sub> of a tetradrachma or half an obolus.</i>, in the Prophets pounds<sup>340</sup><i class="footnote">A Roman <i>libra</i>, Greek λίτρα, of 96 <i>denarii</i> or 24 tetradrachmas.</i>, and in the Hagiographs <i>centenarii</i><sup class="footnote-marker">341</sup><i class="footnote">A noun, meaning “100 pieces” formed in vulgar Latin from the classical adjective <i>centenarius</i>, “consisting of a hundred”, in this case 100 <i>librae</i>. The same meaning, 100 lb., is found in Middle High German <i>zëntenaere</i>,<i>zëntner</i>.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi said, except for the <i>šeqalim</i> paid to Ephron<sup class="footnote-marker">342</sup><i class="footnote">Paid by Abraham for the Makhpela cave, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 23:9" href="/Genesis.23.9"><i>Gen</i>. 23:9</a>.</i>, which were <i>centenarii</i>. What is the reason? “For full money he shall give it to me.” There is no comparison since here it is written “money” but there “<i>šeqalim</i>”. They objected, is there not the rapist<sup class="footnote-marker">343</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Deut</i>. 23:29; the extra fine the rapist of a virgin pays is “50 silver pieces”; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.3.1.1"><i>Ketubot</i> 3:1, Note 1</a>.</i>, for whom “money” is written and you say “<i>šeqalim</i>”?
This does not follow Rebbi Eliezer, for Rebbi Eliezer said that walking acquires, as it was stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">344</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 100a" href="/Bava_Batra.100a">Babli <i>Baba batra</i> 100a</a>. One assumes either that the field is ownerless or that the acquirer has a claim of possession that would give him squatter’s rights; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.4.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 5:5, Note 100</a>.<br>In R. Eliezer’s opinion, the rules of acquisition of a Canaanite slave are not identical with those for real estate; he will reject deriving legal rules from a verse in the Prophets.</i> If one walked across a field in length and breadth, he acquired up to the place he walked to, the words of Rebbi Eliezer. But the Sages say that he did not acquire up to the moment he acted in possession<sup class="footnote-marker">345</sup><i class="footnote">He performed some agricultural work on the property.</i>. Everybody agrees that if somebody sells a path to another, when the latter went, he acquired. What is the reason? “Arise, walk in the Land in length and breadth, for I shall give it to you.<sup class="footnote-marker">346</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 13:17" href="/Genesis.13.17"><i>Gen</i>. 13:17</a>.</i>”
A Mishnah says that slaves follow the rules of real estate. A <i>baraita</i> states, the rules of movables. A <i>baraita</i> states, neither the rules of realestate nor the rules of movables. A Mishnah says that slaves follow the rules of real estate, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">347</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.3.1.1">Mishnah <i>Baba batra</i> 3:1</a>. The Mishnah discusses how to establish title in the absence of a deed.</i>: “Possession of houses, cisterns, ditches, and caves, bath houses, and dovecots, olive presses, and irrigated fields, and slaves.” A <i>baraita</i> states, slaves do not follow the rules of movables. As it is stated: What is possession of a slave<sup class="footnote-marker">30,</sup><i class="footnote">His proof that arguments <i>de minore ad majus</i> are inappropriate here is explained at length in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.4.3"><i>Ketubot</i>5:5, Note 100</a>.<br>As a legal term, חֲזָקָה may have two very different meanings. What seems to be intended here is that ownerless property can be acquired by active actual possession (i. e., possession combined with use.) The idea seems to be that a woman, performing a wife’s duty in a man’s house, by this act should become his wife. The comparison is to a slave woman who belonged to a proselyte who failed to start a Jewish family and dies without heirs. Any Jew who gets hold of her and lets her perform a servile job for himself has acquired her in law.<br>A second meaning of חֲזָקָה is “permanence of the <i>status quo ante</i>,” cf. <i>Giṭṭin</i> 3:3, Notes 81,89; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 9:2:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.9.2.8"><i>Nazir</i> 9:2, Note 90</a>. This meaning is referred to, somewhat incongruously, in the verse quoted at the end; property is inherited by the permanence of the state of “belonging to”.<br>A subcategory of “permanence of the <i>status quo ante</i>” is the validation of squatter’s rights after three years of undisturbed possession, if accompanied by a claim of rightful acquisition.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">348</sup><i class="footnote">Babli 22b; Tosephta 1:5. Movables are acquired by actually taking them. Similarly, ownerless slaves are acquired by having them carry the new master's property.</i>? He tied his shoe for him, or untied his shoe, carried his things to the bath. Rebbi Simeon says, if he lifted him, that is possession. If he lifted his master, there is no stronger possession than this. A statement of the rabbis says that slaves follow the rules of movables. For Rebbi Yose said in the name of rabbis, there is no lien for a gift<sup class="footnote-marker">349</sup><i class="footnote">This statement has no connection with the remainder of the text. If real estate is sold, the seller accepts responsibility to indemnify the buyer if the latter should lose the real estate because of foreclosure of a pre-existing lien, whether or not this is spelled out in the sale contract. For a gift of real estate, the donor accepts responsibility only if this is so stated in the deed. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 128a" href="/Bava_Batra.128a">Babli, <i>Baba batra</i> 128a</a>, this is a matter in dispute; the ultimate authority Rav Naḥman agrees with the ruling of the Yerushalmi.</i>; one does not collect from slaves as from real estate<sup class="footnote-marker">350</sup><i class="footnote">While slaves can be given in chattel mortgage, they are not subject to blanket liens which cover all the debtor’s real estate. In particular, at places where a woman’s <i>ketubah</i> is a lien only on her husband’s real estate, the <i>ketubah</i> cannot be collected by the widow or divorcee by taking slave girls from the estate.</i>. Rebbi Mana asked Rebbi Shammai, who are those rabbis? He told him, Rebbi Isaac and Rebbi Immi. <sup class="footnote-marker">351</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.3.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 9:3, Notes 111–112</a>.</i> A widow took a slave girl. The case came before Rebbi Isaac who said, what she took, she took. Rebbi Immi took her away, for she thought that she was hers but she was not hers. Not of real estate, as it was stated: Real estate and slaves<sup class="footnote-marker">352</sup><i class="footnote">Like Ptolemaic and Roman laws, rabbinic civil law is based on the distinction between ownership and possession. Paying for an acquisition conveys ownership but the transfer of property rights and liabilities requires an act of possession (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:4:2-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.4.2-14">Halakhot 4</a>,5). Taking possession by use (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.9">Note 30</a>) of a piece of real estate establishes possession of all real estate and movables included in one sale contract. Similarly, movables are taken into possession by moving one piece; this simultaneously gives possession of all items included in the sale. Since taking possession of slaves is by individual action only, in this respect they follow neither the rules of real estate nor those of movables. Taking possession of slaves does not induce possession of either real estate or movables covered by the same sales contract; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 12a" href="/Bava_Kamma.12a">Babli <i>Baba qama</i> 12a</a>.</i>; he took possession of the real estate. If you say that slaves follow the rules of real estate, when he took possession of the real estate he would have taken possession of the slaves, since Rebbi Yasa said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If he had two fields, one in Judea and one in Galilee. If he took possession of the one in Judea with intention to take possession also of that in Galilee, or of the one in Galilee with intention to take possession also of that in Judea, he acquired<sup class="footnote-marker">353</sup>. Not of movables. If you say that slaves follow the rules of movables, when he took possession of the movables he would have taken possessionof the slaves, since we did state there: “For property that is not guaranteed<sup class="footnote-marker">354</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., movables which have no title guarantee.</i> will cause guaranteed properties to be objects of swearing about them.<sup class="footnote-marker">355</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.5.1">Mishnah 1:5</a>; <i>Šebuot</i> 6:5. Property claims about real estate and slaves, in contrast to movables, cannot be adjudicated by having the parties swear about ownership and responsibility.</i>”
“By money.<sup class="footnote-marker">356</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the manumission of a slave according to R. Meïr.</i>” Rebbi Jeremiah said, from another person to his master, therefore not from his master to another person<sup class="footnote-marker">357</sup><i class="footnote">A third party gives money to the master to buy the slave’s freedom. This works even if the transaction is purely symbolic and only a <i>peruṭah</i> changes hands. But the master cannot give a <i>peruṭah</i> to a third person as a symbol of the slave’s manumission.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, even from his master to another person. What brings this? From his master to himself<sup class="footnote-marker">358</sup><i class="footnote">The result of the transaction between master and third party is the freedom of the ostensibly uninvolved slave.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah asked before Rebbi Ze‘ira: “Here you have money that your field shall be unencumbered<sup class="footnote-marker">359</sup><i class="footnote">A third party can give money to a creditor to satisfy the mortgage on a field since even R. Meïr will agree that a person can legally act to another person’s benefit without that person’s authorization.<br>Some commentators are emending “field” to “slave” in the entire paragraph.</i>.” He answered, it is unencumbered. “That it be ownerless<sup class="footnote-marker">360</sup><i class="footnote">A person gives money to the owner of a piece of real estate for the latter to renounce his ownership. It is clear that if the former owner renounces his ownership by explicit declaration, the land becomes ownerless. Here the money is given without the owner’s saying a word.</i>”? He answered, it is not ownerless<sup class="footnote-marker">361</sup><i class="footnote">The action is not valid in law.</i>. What is the difference between the cases? Here he advantaged a thinking person, there he did not advantage any thinking person. Think of it, if he<sup class="footnote-marker">362</sup><i class="footnote">The beneficiary of a sane person’s action.</i> was deaf-mute<sup class="footnote-marker">363</sup><i class="footnote">Who is unable to act in law; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 1:2:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.1.2.5"><i>Ketubot</i> 1:2 Note 134</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 14:1:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.14.1.2-5"><i>Yebamot</i> 14:1</a>.</i>? He said to him, “a man.<sup class="footnote-marker">364</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:17" href="/Leviticus.25.17"><i>Lev</i>. 25:17</a>, the word “man” is used to describe the agent in a real estate transaction. The deaf-mute is a man even if he is unable to act in law.</i>” Think of it, if he<sup class="footnote-marker">362</sup><i class="footnote">The beneficiary of a sane person’s action.</i> was underage? He said to him, one usually grows up<sup class="footnote-marker">365</sup><i class="footnote">Only a minority of children die before reaching puberty. (The Babli would reject this argument since it insists that R. Meïr requires rules also to be valid for a minority. For the rabbis who dispute R. Meïr’s position, a minor can acquire but not renounce ownership; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 46b" href="/Sukkah.46b"><i>Sukkah</i> 46b</a>.)</i>. Rebbi Jonah said, Rebbi Ze‘ira follows Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar, since Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar says in the name of Rebbi Meïr: Even with a contract through others, not by himself<sup class="footnote-marker">366</sup><i class="footnote">He disagrees with the Mishnah. Since R. Meïr states that the hand of the slave is the hand of his master (see below and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 6:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.6.1.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 6:1, Note 10</a>), a document delivered by the master to the slave is a document delivered by the master to himself and cannot have any validity. The bill of manumission must be delivered through the agency of a third person whose acceptance of the bill sets the slave free. The Tanna of the Mishnah holds that “he receives simultaneously his hand and his freedom.” The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 24a" href="/Kiddushin.24a">Babli, 24a</a>, reports a similar opinion of R. Simeon bar Eleazar in the name of R. Meïr regarding a wife’s ability to contract without her husband.</i>. Does not his master deliver a document to others that his slave regain his freedom? Here also, his master gives money to others that his field become ownerless<sup class="footnote-marker">367</sup><i class="footnote">This justifies R. Ze‘ira’s earlier remark that a slave can be manumitted by his master’s giving a coin to a third party.</i>.
Rebbi Abun said, it<sup class="footnote-marker">368</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of R. Simeon ben Eleazar that R. Meïr requires the intervention of a third party in the manumission of a slave by document.</i> follows Rebbi, since Rebbi said that a person can free half of his slave<sup class="footnote-marker">369</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 23b" href="/Gittin.23b">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 23b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 41b" href="/Gittin.41b">41b</a>; in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 25b" href="/Temurah.25b"><i>Temurah</i> 25b</a> ascribed to R. Meïr. It is clear that “half of his slave” means “any part of his slave.” Why would anybody want to partially emancipate his slave? By <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.5.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 4:5</a>, the master is then forced to emancipate the slave completely and the slave has to write an IOU for the part of him that was not manumitted. If the slave is not worth his upkeep, the slave has no interest in being freed but his master is interested in getting rid of him and even having the chance of collecting some money from the IOU. (In Roman law, partial emancipation is accepted by Ulpian I 18 but rejected by most other earlier authorities. The Mishnaic law also appears in Egyptian sources and is incorporated in Justinian’s Code; cf. R. Taubenschlag, <i>The Law of Greco-Roman Egypt in the Light of the Papyri</i>, New York 1944, p. 75.)</i>. But do the rabbis not hold that a person can free half of his slave? They admit it for a slave held in partnership<sup class="footnote-marker">370</sup><i class="footnote">Since the partial owner eliminates all of his ownership in the slave.</i>. But there is a difference for a slave who entirely belongs to him, for it is as if he gave something from his right into his left hand<sup class="footnote-marker">371</sup><i class="footnote">Since for R. Meïr the slave’s hand is his master’s hand.</i>. Does Rebbi hold that it is not as if he gave something from his right into his left hand? He admits it if he gave to him through a third party. Do the rabbis not hold that he may give to him through a third party? The rabbis hold that one who can acquire by himself can acquire through a third party; one who cannot acquire by himself cannot acquire through a third party. Rebbi says, even though he cannot acquire by himself, he can acquire through third parties.
If he<sup class="footnote-marker">372</sup><i class="footnote">The slave picks up something that was lost in the public domain and, therefore, is ownerless. By the act of lifting, the finder acquires as property (Mishnah <i>Baba meṣia‘</i> 1:1.)</i> picked up a find and said, for the purpose that I own it rather than my master. Does he and his master own it automatically<sup class="footnote-marker">373</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Baba meṣia‘</i> 1:5 gives the master ownership of the finds of his Cannanite slave. The question is whether this is always the case automatically or whether it is only the default option, valid unless the slave stipulates that it be his own property before he picks up the object. The question has to be asked for the anonymous majority who hold that a third party may give something to a slave on condition that his master have no part in it.</i> or does he own it but not his master? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">374</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah and Halakhah <i>Nedarim</i>11:5 (<b>נ</b>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:18" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.18">Notes 69–70</a>.</i>: “If a person is by a vow prevented from benefitting his son-in-law but wants to give money to his daughter, he says to her: These coins are given to you as a gift on condition that your husband have no claim to them, except what you put into your mouth.” It was stated: “Neither do you<sup class="footnote-marker">375</sup><i class="footnote">Everything remains the father’s property until the food is eaten. But for the rabbis, a wife can acquire independent from her husband; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 23b" href="/Kiddushin.23b">Babli 23b</a>.</i>”. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, who stated “neither do you”? Rebbi Meïr since Rebbi Meïr makes the hand of a slave the hand of his master. For a gift [we follow] Rebbi Meïr that the wife’s hand is her husband’s hand<sup class="footnote-marker">376</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah is formulated anonymously, it represents practice.</i>; for a find [do we follow] the rabbis? Rebbi Ze‘ira<sup class="footnote-marker">377</sup><i class="footnote">The name probably should be “Ezra”.</i> said before Rebbi Mana, is there not a difference, for he is as if he acquired by somebody else’s opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">378</sup><i class="footnote">If the person who lost the article realizes his loss and gives up hope of recovery, he implicitly agrees to transfer his property rights to the finder.</i>? He said to him, is it not a case <i>a fortiori</i>? Since in her case, when she would acquire by somebody else’s opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">379</sup><i class="footnote">A conscious transfer of property by the father.</i>, you say that what a wife acquired, her husband acquired: here where he acquires by his own opinion, <i>a fortiori</i> what the slave acquired his master must acquire.
Why did we not state: By important limbs that do not regenerate<sup class="footnote-marker">380</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:26-27" href="/Exodus.21.26-27"><i>Ex</i>. 21:26–27</a> prescribes that a slave who lost an eye or a permanent tooth by his master’s action must be given his freedom. “Tooth and eye” is generalized as “permanenent deficiency involving a visible essential limb.” (<i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Ismael Neziqin</i> 9).</i>? Rebbi Joḥanan ben Marius said, because of the disagreement. Some Tannaīm<sup class="footnote-marker">381</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ismael in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 42b" href="/Gittin.42b">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 42b</a>, R. Eliezer in <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Ismael Neziqin</i>9; an anonymous Tanna in <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Šim‘on b</i>.<i>Jochai</i>, p. 177. For this Tanna, the freedom of the injured slave is “freedom by document”, covered by the Mishnah. The omission in the Mishnah is an implicit indication that practice requires a bill of manumission.</i> state that he needs a bill of manumission; some Tannaīm state that he does not need a bill of manumission<sup class="footnote-marker">382</sup><i class="footnote">R. Meïr in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 42b" href="/Gittin.42b">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 42b</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.4.5"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 4:4, Note 100</a>.</i>.
It is obvious that a slave may accept a gift by one third party to another<sup class="footnote-marker">383</sup><i class="footnote">Since gifts can be given without the recipient’s knowledge, the master A could accept B’s gift to C without asking C. Therefore, the slave can do the same in his master’s stead.</i>, by a third party to his master, [but] not from his master to himself<sup class="footnote-marker">384</sup><i class="footnote">A direct gift from the master to his slave is “a gift from the master’s right to his left hand” (Note 371).</i>. From a third party to himself is in dispute between Rebbi Meïr and the Sages. If one said: Here [I am giving] you this money on condition that your master have no power over it, the slave acquired it and with him his master, the words of Rebbi Meïr. But the Sages say, the slave acquired it [but not]<sup class="footnote-marker">385</sup><i class="footnote">Word missing in ms., corrected in <i>editio princeps</i>.</i> his master<sup class="footnote-marker">386</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted but in the end rejected in the Babli, 23b. The only gift which the Babli removes from the master is one given to the slave for the purpose of buying the latter’s freedom. Since the slave did not acquire the money, his master has no claim.</i>. What is the problem? From his master to a third party. Since the slave may receive from a third party for his master, may he receive from his master for a third party? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">387</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Baba meṣia‘</i> 8:4. The borrower of livestock is responsible if the animal is injured or dies while in his custody (<i>Ex</i>. 22:13). The responsibility of the borrower starts at the moment when he takes control of the animal. As long as the animal is driven by the owner’s slave, it is in the owner’s hand and the borrower is not responsible. But (as stated in the part of the Mishnah not quoted here) if the borrower said, send it to me through your slave and something happens on the way, the borrower is responsible.</i>: <sup class="footnote-marker">388</sup><i class="footnote">The text from here to the end of the paragraph is from <i>Ma‘aśer Šeni</i> 4:4 (<b>מ</b>, Notes 91–97), <i>Erubin</i> 7, <b>ע</b>.</i>“If somebody borrowed a cow and [the lender] sent her to him through his slave, his son, or his agent.” Does this not mean that the slave is able to transfer rights from his master to a third party? Rebbi Eleazar said, explain it for a Hebrew slave. Rebbi Joḥanan said, you have to explain if for a Canaanite slave; explain it that he said, open [the gate] for her and she will come by herself, as we have stated: “If he led her, drew her to himself, called her and she followed him, he is responsible as a borrower.” Rebbi Zeïra understood it from here: “But he cannot make them acquire through his underage son or daughter or his Canaanite male or female slave, because their hand is not like his hand.” Does this not imply that a slave is not able to transfer rights from his master to another person? Explain it following Rebbi Meïr since Rebbi Meïr makes the hand of the slave the hand of his master. But did we not state “his wife”? Rebbi Meïr holds that the hand of the wife is the hand of her husband! Rebbi Ḥananiah said in the name of Rebbi Phineas, explain it following the Tanna who stated: “His wife cannot redeem Second Tithe for him. Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar says in the name of Rebbi Meïr, his wife can redeem Second Tithe for him.” For that Tanna, Rebbi Meïr makes the hand of the slave the hand of his master but not the hand of the wife the hand of her husband!
Rebbi Ze‘ira and Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: It is reasonable that a slave be able to accept a bill of manumission<sup class="footnote-marker">391</sup><i class="footnote">The bill of manumission of another owner’s slave, even following R. Meïr. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 23b" href="/Gittin.23b">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 23b</a>.</i> since a bill of manumission applies to him. But he should not be able to accept a bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">392</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 2:6, Notes 129,130; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 23b" href="/Kiddushin.23b">Babli 23b</a>.</i> since a bill of divorce does not apply to him. If you say, is that not a <i>baraita</i>? “Your are a slave but your child is free; if she was pregnant, she acquired for him.” They considered her fetus as one of her limbs<sup class="footnote-marker">393</sup><i class="footnote">As explained below, this rule follows Rebbi who permits partial manumission, but R. Joḥanan claims that his statement is accepted by everybody. Therefore, the <i>baraita</i> is no support for the first statement of R. Joḥanan; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 23b" href="/Gittin.23b">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 23b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 25a" href="/Temurah.25a"><i>Temurah</i> 25a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">394</sup><i class="footnote">He presents an alternate version of what R. Joḥanan said.</i>: It is reasonable that a slave be able to accept a bill of manumission but he should not be able to accept a bill of his equal<sup class="footnote-marker">395</sup><i class="footnote">A slave cannot accept the bill of manumission of a fellow slave of the same master since his hand is his master’s hand. It is necessary to emphasize that the slave can validly receive only the bill of another master’s slave.</i>. If you say, the preceding <i>baraita</i>? “You are a slave but your child is free; if she was pregnant, she acquired for him.” They considered her fetus as one of her limbs.
“For my slave girl X I want a document that she should not be enslaved.<sup class="footnote-marker">396</sup><i class="footnote">A man writes in his will that the girl who cared for him cannot be used for servile tasks by his heirs. The heirs have to support her but cannot require any work from her. (The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 40a" href="/Gittin.40a">Babli, <i>Giṭṭin</i>40a</a>, disagrees and holds that the slave girl must be freed by the heirs. Only if the testator said that “she should be treated well”, she remains a slave but cannot be forced to do any work she dislikes.)</i>” Rebbi Eleazar and Rebbi Simeon ben Yaqim brought a case before Rebbi Joḥanan. He said, he<sup class="footnote-marker">397</sup><i class="footnote">The heir.</i> has no power to enslave. What are her children<sup class="footnote-marker">398</sup><i class="footnote">Born after her original master’s death. The Babli, <i>loc</i>.<i>cit</i>., agrees.</i>? Slaves. What did he retain for her? Her earnings<sup class="footnote-marker">399</sup><i class="footnote">She pockets all she earns while being supported by the heir.</i>. Rebbi Abba and Rebbi Yose both said, this follows Rebbi who said that a person frees half of his slave. If he wrote all his possessions jointly to two of his slaves, both are free and both have to free one another<sup class="footnote-marker">400</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 42a" href="/Gittin.42a">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 42a</a>, which notes that if the properties were not given jointly but half to one and half to the other, they would remain slaves and get nothing since they could not free one another.</i>. Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: This follows Rebbi who said that a person frees half of his slave. Rebbi Ze‘ira said to Rebbi Abba: Does this not imply that one slave accepts from his master for another one<sup class="footnote-marker">401</sup><i class="footnote">If the two slaves have to free one another, it seems to imply that by accepting the will each of them was instrumental in freeing half of the other. This would contradict the earlier statement that a slave cannot be part of the manumission of another slave of the same master.</i>? He answered, what do you think? Since they acquired the properties they went free? No, simultaneously slaves and properties went free<sup class="footnote-marker">402</sup><i class="footnote">The argument proposed in the previous Note is impossible since a half-free slave is unable to act in law. As the Babli formulates it, “his bill of manumission and his hand are simultaneous.” The <i>baraita</i> can only be satisfactorily explained following the rabbis who oppose R. Meïr and hold that a slave can accept his own bill since by the bill he is able to act in law.</i>.
“You are free but your child<sup class="footnote-marker">403</sup><i class="footnote">The unborn child.</i> will be a slave,” her child is like her<sup class="footnote-marker">404</sup><i class="footnote">In this version (the only one quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 25b" href="/Temurah.25b">Babli, <i>Temurah</i> 25b</a>), R. Yose the Galilean disagrees with Rebbi and holds that a slave partially freed is totally freed. He also holds that the fetus is part of the mother; the opposing view, that a fetus has a life of its own, is only found in the Babli (<i>loc</i>.<i>cit</i>. Note 393).</i>, the words of Rebbi Yose the Galilean. But the Sages say, he did not do anything. Rebbi Eleazar said, so did Rebbi Hoshaia, the father of the Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">405</sup><i class="footnote">He reputedly was one of the first collectors of the Tosephta, illustrating the Mishnah.</i>, explain: Both are free. Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Both are slaves. According to Rebbi Joḥanan, it is understandable; there is a disagreement. What is the disagreement following Rebbi Eleazar? But it must be so<sup class="footnote-marker">406</sup><i class="footnote">Corrected version of the <i>baraita</i>.</i>: His words stand, the words of Rebbi Yose the Galilean<sup class="footnote-marker">407</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned at the end of the paragraph, he follows Rebbi and admits freeing a slave limb by limb.</i>. But the Sages say, he did not do anything. What means “he did not do anything”? Rebbi Eleazar said, so did Rebbi Hoshaia, the father of the Mishnah, explain: Both are free. Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Both are slaves<sup class="footnote-marker">408</sup><i class="footnote">Following the Sages who admit partial manumission only in the case of a slave jointly owned by several persons, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.8">Note 370</a>.</i>. Following Rebbi, since Rebbi said that a person can free half of his slave<sup class="footnote-marker">407</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned at the end of the paragraph, he follows Rebbi and admits freeing a slave limb by limb.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Large animals by handing over<sup class="footnote-marker">409</sup><i class="footnote">Actual possession of cattle or horses is obtained by the buyer grasping either the animal’s hair or any saddle, bridle, or belt tied to it. Talmudic civil law, in parallel to Ptolemaic law in Egypt, is based on the distinction between ownership and possession. Ownership can be acquired by a monetary transaction but possession, including transfer of responsibility, requires a separate act. While acquisition, as a private act between consenting adults, is essentially unregulated, taking possession must follow specific rules.</i> and small<sup class="footnote-marker">410</sup><i class="footnote">Sheep and goats.</i> by lifting, the words of Rebbi Meïr and Rebbi Eleazar. But the Sages say, small animals are taken possession of by drawing near<sup class="footnote-marker">411</sup><i class="footnote">By making the animal walk according to the buyer’s will, but also by lifting the animal.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Large animals are taken possession of by handing over,” etc. Rebbi Huna said, the halter gives possession only for property of the proselyte<sup class="footnote-marker">412</sup><i class="footnote">Who failed to start a Jewish family and whose property becomes ownerless at his death; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.9">Note 30</a>. In the Babli, <i>Baba meṣia</i>‘ 8b, <i>Rav</i> Huna states: that “a halter from another person takes possession except in case of a find and property of a proselyte.” On basis of this quote, all commentators and editors delete אֶלָּא “only” in the present text. The deletion is unjustified; the texts in Yerushalmi and Babli are not comparable.<br>In the Babli, the formulation makes it clear that the buyer either receives the animal’s halter from the seller or is instructed to take it. Since both a lost article and the estate of an intestate heirless person are ownerless, there is nobody who could hand over the halter or authorize its use. Therefore, only actual moving of the animal can be an act of simultaneous acquisition and taking in possession. While the text of the Babli is beyond doubt, confirmed by Rashi’s translation of מוסירה by <i>chevêtre</i>, it is quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Bava Batra 75b:13:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Bava_Batra.75b.13.1">Tosaphot <i>Baba batra</i> 75b, <i>s. v.</i> אחזה</a>, as מְסִירָה מֵחֲבֵירוֹ, “handing over by another person”, which is correct in meaning if not in text. By contrast, neither handing over nor third person is mentioned in the Yerushalmi. The Yerushalmi asserts that in acquiring and taking possession of ownerless property, taking up the halter is enough, but not in course of formal taking possession of property acquired from a prior owner.</i>. Rav Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Abba: Rebbi Eleazar asked, if there were ten camels tied one to another and he handed him the halter of one of them. Did he take possession of all of them or only of the one of which he handed him [the halter]<sup class="footnote-marker">413</sup><i class="footnote">It is assumed that even if the first camel took a step under the influence of its new owner, the other nine did not move.</i>? Rav Ḥisda said: “Draw<sup class="footnote-marker">414</sup><i class="footnote">The seller instructs the buyer to proceed to take possession of the animal he bought.</i> this animal to take possession of it, he took possession. To take possession of its offspring, he did not take possession<sup class="footnote-marker">415</sup><i class="footnote">Moving an animal which he did not buy does not give possession. In the parallel <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 3:1:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.3.1.2-7"><i>Baba batra</i> 3:1,</a> fol. 13d 1. 66, “he took possession.” The reading of <i>Baba batra</i> is incomprehensible.</i>. To take possession of it together with its offspring, he took possession<sup class="footnote-marker">416</sup><i class="footnote">In taking possession of a group of animals, it is enough to move one of them. This answers R. Eleazar’s question. In <i>Baba batra</i>: “He did not take possession,” cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.4.2">Note 415</a>.</i>.” If somebody said to another person, draw this animal close to take possession of its load, would he not take possession<sup class="footnote-marker">417</sup><i class="footnote">If he also bought the animal, moving it moves the load and all is taken in possession. The Babli, <i>Baba meṣia</i>‘ 9b, disagrees.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, that is, if the animal was not pregnant<sup class="footnote-marker">418</sup><i class="footnote">If the offspring are already born, they are independent animals which must be acquired by themselves.</i>. But if it was pregnant, they considered it as if carrying a load<sup class="footnote-marker">419</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Baba batra</i> (Note 415), R. Nasa notes that a fetus is part of his mother’s body (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.14">Note 404</a>).</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">420</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel is in ‘<i>Avodah zarah</i>5:10 (<b>ז</b>).</i> Rebbi Abba, Rav Huna in the name of Rav: One who handles a wine-skin<sup class="footnote-marker">421</sup><i class="footnote">When no price had been agreed upon. Therefore, handling of the wine skin by the prospective buyer cannot be taking possession.</i> and it splits in his hand is not responsible for it. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun, it is necessary even if he takes it in the store when he had no other intention than that no other person should take it<sup class="footnote-marker">422</sup><i class="footnote">It was handled in preparation of a buy.</i>. Can he take possession at a minimal rate<sup class="footnote-marker">423</sup><i class="footnote">Is it possible to take possession now of a commodity whose price will be determined at a future rate, e. g., the lowest price at which the commodity will be traded in the market (the commodities exchange) during the next 30 days. This is a futures contract which cannot be enforced (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.5.3"><i>Ketubot</i>5:5, Note 116</a>.</i>? Rebbi Ḥaggai said in the name of Rebbi Yose: A Mishnah says that it cannot become his possession at a minimal rate, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">424</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah ‘<i>Avodah zarah</i> 5:10.</i>: “If somebody sells his wine to a Non-Jew<sup class="footnote-marker">425</sup><i class="footnote">The Non-Jew is assumed to use his wine for libations, among other things. Since a libation makes the wine an instrument of pagan worship, it will be forbidden to a Jew and all proceeds received for it are forbidden (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 13:18" href="/Deuteronomy.13.18"><i>Deut</i>. 13:18</a>).</i>. If he fixed the price before he measured, the proceeds are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">426</sup><i class="footnote">The Non-Jew owed the price to the Jew before he went and took possession of the wine. What the Jew sold was wine, not an article of pagan worship.</i>; if he measured before he fixed the price, the proceeds are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">427</sup><i class="footnote">If the Non-Jew handled the wine before the price was fixed, the Jew sold an article of pagan worship.</i>.” If you say that it can come into his possession at a minimal rate<sup class="footnote-marker">428</sup><i class="footnote">Or any rate to be determined in the future.</i>, even if he measured before he fixed the price, it should be as if he fixed the price before he measured<sup class="footnote-marker">429</sup><i class="footnote">Since he could have transferred possession to the Non-Jew before the latter handled the wine. The Babli, ‘<i>Avodah zarah</i> 72a, rejects the argument since it may be that the seriousness of the prohibition of pagan worship might override rules of contracts.</i> and the proceeds would be permitted.
Samuel said, if somebody take a small gold vessel and it be snatched, who is responsible for it<sup class="footnote-marker">430</sup><i class="footnote">If the person handling the vessel was not the owner.</i>? Rebbi Samuel ben Eudaimon said, that is at Tyre or a similar place, at Caesarea<sup class="footnote-marker">431</sup><i class="footnote">Big cities where thieves easily find fences.</i> or a similar place. But here, so far it usually would be recovered.
Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish in the name of Rebbi Yannai: If somebody sold a flock<sup class="footnote-marker">432</sup><i class="footnote">Of sheep or goats.</i> to another person, when [the seller] delivered the <i>maškokît</i>, [the buyer] took possession. What is <i>maškokît</i>? Some say, the shepherd’s staff. But some say, a whistle. And some say, the bell-wether<sup class="footnote-marker">433</sup><i class="footnote">It is difficult to explain the feminine form chosen for “bell-wether”. In the parallel <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 3:1:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.3.1.2-7"><i>Baba batra</i> 3:1</a> (13d 1. 43), the bell-wether is called תַּייָשָׁא רַבָּא “the large ram”, and instead of “whistle” one has πανδοῦρα, Latin <i>pandura</i>, a three-stringed musical instrument, invented by Pan. [Also compare Latin <i>pandus</i>, -<i>a</i>,<i>-um</i> “bent, crooked, curved” as reference to shephard’s crook? (E. G.)] In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 52a" href="/Bava_Kamma.52a">Babli, <i>Baba qama</i> 52a</a>, only tambourine and bell-wether (“the ram at the head of the flock”) are offered as explanations of <i>maškokît</i>.</i>.
Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: If somebody sell a cistern, when he handed over its pail [the buyer] took possession<sup class="footnote-marker">434</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 51b" href="/Bava_Kamma.51b">Babli, <i>Baba qama</i> 51b</a>, requires a declaration by the seller that the pail is handed over for possession.</i>. Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If somebody sell a house, when [the buyer] stored there, he took possession. Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi asked, what if he handed over the key<sup class="footnote-marker">435</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 51b-52a" href="/Bava_Kamma.51b-52a">Babli, <i>Baba qama</i> 51b/52a</a>, handing over the key as sign of transfer of possession is undeniably a valid action.</i>? Rebbi Zachariah, son-in-law of Rebbi Levi, said: This is a disagreement between Rebbi Simeon and the Sages, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">436</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tahorot 7:1" href="/Mishnah_Tahorot.7.1">Mishnah <i>Ṭahorot</i> 7:1</a>.</i>: “If somebody hand over the key to his house to an ‘<i>am ha</i>’<i>areṣ</i><sup class="footnote-marker">437</sup><i class="footnote">A person who does not keep the rules of ritual purity. If he did enter the house, any movables (food and vessels) he touches there would become impure.</i>, the house is pure since he handed to him only the guarding of the key.” It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">438</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Tahorot 8:1" href="/Tosefta_Tahorot.8.1">Tosephta <i>Ṭahorot</i> 8:1</a>. He implies, against the othet Tanna, that handing over the key implies permission to use the house.</i>: “Rebbi Simeon declares impure.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">439</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is from <i>Demay</i>6:3 (<b>ד</b>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.19">Notes 79–83</a>, which has an intelligible text.</i>“Rebbi Abbahu said in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: He who sells the tithes of his field to another person did not do anything; the future children of his slave girl to another person<sup class="footnote-marker">440</sup><i class="footnote">Translation of the text in <i>Demay</i>. The text here reads: “The children of another person’s slave girl” which does not make sense.</i>, he did not do anything; the fetus of his animal to another person, he did not do anything; the airspace of his dry land to another person, he did not do anything. But he may sell him a field and reserve the tithes for himself; a slave girl and reserve her children for himself; an animal and reserve her fetus for himself. Is it not impossible for a person to sell the airspace of his dry land to another person? Explain it if he tells him, tear out some grasses from that dry land to acquire its airspace<sup class="footnote-marker">441</sup><i class="footnote">Translation of the text in <i>Demay</i>. The text here reads: “A tenth of its surface area”, which is inappropriate.</i>. And here there is real estate before him and he says, pluck something from the ground so that one tenth of it be acquired by you.
<sup class="footnote-marker">442</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.1.4"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 8:1, Notes 17–21</a>.</i> Rebbi Samuel, Rebbi Ze‘ira, Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ashi said in the name of Rav: Drawing close does not acquire in a courtyard which belongs to neither of them. But Rebbi Ḥiyya stated in disagreement: “Under which circumstances did they say that movables are acquired by drawing close? In the public domain or in a courtyard which belongs to neither of them. But in the buyer’s domain he acquired the moment he accepted it. In the seller’s domain he does not acquire unless either he lifts it up or moves it out of its prior owner’s domain. In a domain where it was deposited he does not acquire unless he either is explicitly empowered or he leases its place.”
“But the Sages say, small animals are taken possession of by drawing near.” What is the reason of the rabbis? “Draw near and take for yourselves small animals for your families<sup class="footnote-marker">443</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:21" href="/Exodus.12.21"><i>Ex</i>. 12:21</a>.</i>.” As Rav Jehudah sent to ask<sup class="footnote-marker">444</sup><i class="footnote">As the later text shows, probably one should read here: “To ask R. Eleazar.” According to the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 11b" href="/Bava_Kamma.11b">Babli, <i>Baba qama</i> 11b</a>, the messenger was Ulla.</i>: By what means is possession of large animals established? He answered, by handing over; and asked, is that not the Mishnah? There are Tannaïm who switch the attributions<sup class="footnote-marker">445</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously, Rav Jehudah was supposed to have studied the Mishnah. But he was not sure about the references to large and small domesticated animals and asked for the correct text from the Babylonian R. Eleazar in Galilee where the latter had access to the traditions of the school of Rebbi.</i>. Rav Jehudah asked Rebbi Eleazar: If a firstborn became “torn” within thirty days<sup class="footnote-marker">446</sup><i class="footnote">A firstborn son has to be redeemed (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:13" href="/Exodus.13.13"><i>Ex</i>. 13:13</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:20" href="/Exodus.34.20">34:20</a>); this is specified in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 3:40" href="/Numbers.3.40"><i>Num</i>. 3:40</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 3:47" href="/Numbers.3.47">47</a> to apply to firstborn sons above the age of one month and the redemption money, 5 biblical <i>šeqalim</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.4">Note 122</a>), has to be given to a priest. A human who develops a defect which makes his long-term survival impossible is called “torn”, a term used in parallel with the talmudic interpretation of “torn” animals forbidden as food (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:30" href="/Exodus.22.30"><i>Ex</i>. 22:30</a>).</i>? He answered, he is as if dead<sup class="footnote-marker">447</sup><i class="footnote">This parallels the rabbinic theory that a person who kills a “torn” human cannot be convicted of first-degree murder (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 78a" href="/Sanhedrin.78a">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 78a</a>). In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 37" href="/Menachot.37">Babli, <i>Menaḥot</i> 37a/b</a>, the rule is derived from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:15" href="/Numbers.18.15"><i>Num</i>. 18:15</a> (cf. also <i>Sifry Num</i>. #118.)</i> and free from the five tetradrachmas of a son. <sup class="footnote-marker">448</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Niddah 3:4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Niddah.3.4.5"><i>Niddah</i> 3:4, Notes 103–104</a>; the parallel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 11a" href="/Bava_Kamma.11a">Babli is <i>Baba qama</i> 11a</a>.</i> Rav Jehudah sent and asked Rebbi Eleazar: A placenta which was delivered partially today and partially tomorrow? He answered, for pure blood she counts from the first day, for impure blood from the second day. Rebbi Mattaniah said, that is, if no child was produced with it. But if there was a child, both for pure and impure blood she counts only from the moment of birth of the child.
Rav Jehudah sent to Rebbi Eleazar: If a keeper give to another keeper<sup class="footnote-marker">449</sup><i class="footnote">And something happens to the object to be kept, who is responsible?</i>. He answered, the first is responsible. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the second<sup class="footnote-marker">450</sup><i class="footnote">This must read: “The first”, as shown by the last statement in the paragraph and shown in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.5.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 9:5, Note 144</a>.</i> is responsible. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, the second is responsible. <sup class="footnote-marker">451</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the paragraph, the text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.5.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 9:5, Notes 128–146</a> (variant readings given there).</i> There, we have stated: “If somebody leases a cow from another person and lends it to a third party; if it died naturally, the lessee has to swear that it died naturally and the borrower has to pay to the lessee. Rebbi Yose said, how can this person make a deal with another’s cow? But the cow’s [price] has to be restituted to the owner.” Rebbi Hila in the name of Rebbi Yannai: Only if he gave permission to lend to others. Also Rebbi Ḥiyya stated thus: “The borrower cannot lend, nor the lessee lease, nor the borrower lease, nor the lessee lend, nor the trustee give to another unless they received permission from the owners. And all who changed the titles without the owners’ knowledge are liable. But the borrower is liable even if he did not change.” But it is because we stated: “An unpaid watchman can stipulate to be exempt from an oath and the borrower may stipulate that he not have to pay.” This means that even if he stipulated to be exempt he is obligated. What if he wanted to let the borrower swear? Let us hear<sup class="footnote-marker">452</sup><i class="footnote">Translation of נשמעינה in the <i>Ketubot</i> text, instead of נשבע “been made to swear” which does not fit here.</i> from the following: “If he wrote to her: ‘I have no vow or oath against you,’ he cannot make her swear, but he can ask an oath from the heirs or her business associates.” This implies that if he wanted to make the borrower swear, he can force him to swear. The second case teaches about the first, that if he wants to force the wife to swear, he can make her swear. Rebbi Ḥanina said, it is not necessary to derive the first case from the second, and what does one learn for the second case from the first? Following what Rebbi Hila said in the name of Rebbi Yannai: Only if he gave permission to lend to others. And here, only if he gave permission to let her sons be stewards. Rebbi Yose said, he has to pay the lease the entire time it is leased to him. Rebbi Ze‘ira asked Rebbi Abinna: If the owners borrowed it and it died? He said to him, we hold that they ate it. Rebbi Yose bar Abun said, if they ate it, they ate their own property. Rebbi Ze‘ira asked before Rebbi Yasa: How does one act? He said to him, there are two against four; one does not act following the argument. He said to him, they are two against two! Rebbi Eleazar is the Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya’s student; Rebbi Joḥanan is Rebbi Yannai’s student.
Rav Jehudah sent asking from Rebbi Eleazar: If brothers split [the estate] and then one of them entered a levirate marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">453</sup><i class="footnote">The father died and then one of the brothers died childless. The brother who married the widow inherits the childless brother’s property. If the deceased brother was not married, it is clear that the father’s inheritance is distributed among the surviving brothers. The problem is whether the brother who marries the widow only gets the deceased brother’s property or also has a claim to the share the brother would have had if he had not died.</i>? He answered, the property of the deceased is like common property<sup class="footnote-marker">454</sup><i class="footnote">The estate of the deceased brother has no claim on the father’s inheritance.</i>. Ulla bar Ismael said, it is found that in Rebbi Eleazar’s opinion there is no difference whether they split and then one of them entered a levirate marriage or one of them entered a levirate marriage and then they split, but all of them share<sup class="footnote-marker">455</sup><i class="footnote">Equally.</i> in the property of the deceased. Why did he not say so? He answered what was asked of him. And why did he not ask? Because of what Rebbi Abinna said in the name of Rebbi Assi<sup class="footnote-marker">456</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 126a" href="/Bava_Batra.126a">Babli <i>Baba batra</i> 126a</a>; Yerushalmi <i>Ketubot</i> 4.<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.13">13, Notes 257–258</a>.</i>: If a first-born shared as a common son, it is <i>prima facie</i> evidence that he renounced his prerogative<sup class="footnote-marker">457</sup><i class="footnote">The claim to a double portion. Similarly, if the brother who married the widow did not put forth his deceased brother’s claim at the moment of distribution, he is presumed to have waived his claimt.</i>.
Rav Jehudah sent and asked Rebbi Eleazar: Brothers who split<sup class="footnote-marker">458</sup><i class="footnote">Brothers who all were living and working together on the father’s estate. During their father’s lifetime, they had no independent income. Sometime after the father’s death they decided to split the estate.</i>? He answered, they divide what they are wearing but not what their sons and daughters are wearing<sup class="footnote-marker">459</sup><i class="footnote">Since all they have came from the father’s estate, even what they are wearing is part of the estate. However, their children’s clothing is property of the children and cannot be counted in the estate. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 11b" href="/Bava_Kamma.11b">Babli <i>Baba qama</i> 11b</a> agrees. It notes that if the oldest brother had been made to wear more distinguished clothing in order to represent the entire family before the authorities, the extra expenditure was made on behalf of the estate and cannot be debited to the brother.</i>. Rebbi Immi said, if one makes an appraisal<sup class="footnote-marker">460</sup><i class="footnote">It is difficult to understand what is meant. The word שום has two meanings, (1) appraisal (of real estate, of dowry, etc.), (2) mole (on the skin). Neither of the meanings is appropriate here. It seems that meaning (1) is derived from Accadic <i>šāmum</i> “buy, object of acquisition; price of object”. Following the first meaning of the Accadic word, one speaks of clothing which one of the brothers bought for his wife with his father’s money, as distinct from clothing made by home manufacture. In making an inventory of the estate, these clothes are debited to the brother.</i> for his wife, this is counted for himself and becomes part of the common estate to be distributed. If one makes a necklace<sup class="footnote-marker">460a</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>catella</i>.</i> for his wife, this is not of the common estate to be distributed if it is to be used on weekdays<sup class="footnote-marker">461</sup><i class="footnote">Small jewellery objects become property of the women and are not to be counted.</i>. But if it is made to be used on holidays, it becomes part of the common estate to be distributed. Rebbi Mana said, holiday dresses are distributed<sup class="footnote-marker">462</sup><i class="footnote">They are not distributed but are counted as part of the husband’s part in the estate.</i>. Sabbath dresses are problematic. It is obvious for Rebbi Abin that both holiday and Sabbath dresses become part of the common estate and are distributed. Rebbi Ze‘ira<sup class="footnote-marker">377</sup><i class="footnote">The name probably should be “Ezra”.</i> asked before Rebbi Mana: What about spherules<sup class="footnote-marker">463</sup><i class="footnote">Greek βῶλοι, Latin <i>boloe</i>, “spherules” of glass or metal beads, used for jewellery, also kind of precious stones.</i>? He answered, you understand this since you own many spherules. He said to him, they become part of the common estate to be distributed.
Rav Jehudah sent and asked Rebbi Eleazar: May one foreclose slaves with real estate? He answered, one may foreclose slaves with real estate<sup class="footnote-marker">464</sup><i class="footnote">Since mortgages usually were written as liens on the entire real estate of the debtor (the Byzantine mortgage <i>omnium bonorum</i>, derived from Ptolemaic Egyptian practice), not on a specific parcel, the question arises whether slaves can be treated as real estate since for them the rules of possession are derived from the rules of real estate. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 128a" href="/Bava_Batra.128a">Babli, <i>Baba batra</i>128a</a>, the Babylonian Rav Naḥman rules against the Yerushalmi rule, represented there by the Galilean Rebbi Abba. (The Galilean rule could not be based on Egyptian precedents since mortgages in Egypt had to be registered with the registrar of deeds, restricted to real estate.)</i>. Rebbi Eleazar instructed those from the House of Rebbi Yannai to foreclose slaves with real estate<sup class="footnote-marker">465</sup><i class="footnote">His ruling was in practice, not only theoretical.</i>.
Rav Jehudah sent and asked Rebbi Eleazar: The extortionist, and the robber, and the thief<sup class="footnote-marker">466</sup><i class="footnote">The owner obtained a court order of restitution for anything taken unlawfully. If the thing was broken in the meantime or if it was livestock it died (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:35" href="/Exodus.21.35"><i>Ex</i>. 21:35</a>), is the thief or robber obligated to pay for the entire damage or may he return the damaged object and pay only the difference between the original value and the amount recoverable from sale of the damaged object?</i>? He answered, it is assumed that the owners do not deal with the dead animal<sup class="footnote-marker">467</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 11a" href="/Bava_Kamma.11a">Babli, <i>Baba qama</i> 11a</a>, Ulla in the name of R. Eleazar represents the opposite opinion. The opinion expressed here is accepted by the Babylonian authorities as judicial practice. The paragraph is discussed in Tosaphot <i>Baba meṣia</i>‘ 96b/97a, <i>s. v.</i> זיל.</i>. From where that the owners do not deal with the dead animal? Rebbi Abba bar Mamal said, “alive, he shall pay double<sup class="footnote-marker">468</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:3" href="/Exodus.22.3"><i>Ex</i>. 22:3</a>, speaking of the thief of livestock.</i>,” but not dead animals. That refers to theft; for robbery? Rebbi Abun said, “he shall return the robbed object which he robbed<sup class="footnote-marker">469</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:23" href="/Leviticus.5.23"><i>Lev</i>. 5:23</a>.</i>,” as it was.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Guaranteed properties<sup class="footnote-marker">470</sup><i class="footnote">Real estate which normally is sold with a title guarantee.</i> can be acquired by money, or contract, or possession<sup class="footnote-marker">329</sup><i class="footnote">A slave who becomes ownerless by the death of his intestate owner who dies without heirs can be acquired by anybody who makes him perform a slave’s duties for himself. Also, a person who claims to have bought a slave from another party will have his ownership confirmed by the court if he can show three years of uncontested ownership. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.4.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 5:5, Note 100</a>.</i>. Not guaranteed properties<sup class="footnote-marker">471</sup><i class="footnote">Movables.</i> can be acquired only by drawing close<sup class="footnote-marker">472</sup><i class="footnote">For this Tanna, there is no transfer of possession of movables except by delivery.</i>. Not guaranteed properties can be acquired together with guaranteed properties by money, or contract, or possession<sup class="footnote-marker">473</sup><i class="footnote">Taking possession of a piece of real estate transfers possession of all things included in the sale.</i>. Not guaranteed properties can force [the owner of] guaranteed properties to swear about them<sup class="footnote-marker">474</sup><i class="footnote">Monetary claims which cannot be proven or disproved are settled by an oath taken by the defendent. The verse, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:8</a>, specifies that the oath is required for “ox, donkey, sheep, garment, or anything lost,” excluding real estate (Mishnah <i>Šebuot</i> 6:7). But if the dispute was about both movables and real estate, since one party has to swear about the movables, it can also be made to include a statement about the real estate.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Guaranteed properties,” etc. In earlier times, transfer of possession was effected by taking off a shoe. That is what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">475</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ruth 4:7" href="/Ruth.4.7"><i>Ruth</i> 4:7</a>.</i>: “This was earlier in Israel about redemption and exchange, a person would take off his shoe.” Who took it off? There, they say Rav and Levi<sup class="footnote-marker">476</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, <i>Baba meṣia</i>‘ 47a, the first opinion is attributed to Rav, the second to Levi. In the Babli, the transaction is not considered as archaic but as current practice, meaning that claims can be validly transferred following Rav when the transferrer moves some of the acquirer’s movable property.</i>, one said the acquirer, and one said the transferer. It turns out that this disagreement parallels the following disagreement which was stated: Boaz was giving to the redeemer; Rebbi Jehudah said that the redeemer gave to Boaz.
Then they changed it to clipping. <sup class="footnote-marker">478</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:10:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.10.8"><i>Ketubot</i>2:11</a> (<b>כ</b>), Notes 201–203.</i> What is clipping? If a man sold his inherited field, his relatives brought amphoras, filled them with roasted kernels and nuts and broke them before children. The children were collecting them and saying, X was clipped from his inheritance. When he brought it back, they were doing the same and saying, X returned to his inheritance. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, also if a man married an unsuitable woman, his relatives brought amphoras, filled them with roasted kernels and nuts and broke them before children. The children were collecting them and saying, X was clipped from his family. When he divorced her, they were doing the same and saying, X returned to his family.
Then they changed it to acquisition by money, by document, or by possession. By money: <sup class="footnote-marker">479</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following two paragraphs are from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3">Halakhah 3 (ק), Notes 335–346</a>.</i>“Fields will be bought by money,” that is money. “Writing on a scroll and signing”, these are the witnesses on the contract. “And testifying of witnesses,” these are the witnesses to possession. Or maybe these are only the witnesses to the contract? These are already written: “Writing on a scroll and signing.”
Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Mana bar Tanḥum, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Real estate cannot be bought for less than a <i>peruṭah</i>. What is the reason? “Fields will be bought by money.” This disagrees with what Rebbi Ḥanina said: All <i>šeqalim</i> mentioned in the Torah are tetradrachmas, in the Prophets pounds, and in the Hagiographs <i>centenarii</i>, except for the <i>šeqalim</i> paid to Ephron. What is the reason? “For full money he shall give it to me.” There is no comparison since here it is written “money” but there “<i>šeqalim</i>”. They objected, is there not the rapist, for whom “money” is written and you say “<i>šeqalim</i>”.
This does not follow Rebbi Eliezer, for Rebbi Eliezer said that walking acquires. If one walked across a field in length and breadth, he acquired up to the place he walked, the words of Rebbi Eliezer. But the Sages say that he did not acquire up to the moment he acted in possession. Everybody agrees that if somebody sells a path to another, when the latter went, he acquired. What is the reason? “Arise, walk in the Land, etc.”
By document. Rebbi Jeremiah thought to say, by a contract without obligating money<sup class="footnote-marker">480</sup><i class="footnote">The deed of a gift transfers possession but a sales contract does not.
