The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 26a" href="/Kiddushin.26a">Babli, 26a</a>, endorses this opinion in the name of Samuel.</i>, but with a contract obligating money he did not acquire until he paid. Rebbi Jonah and Rebbi Yose both said, he acquired even if he did not pay<sup class="footnote-marker">481</sup><i class="footnote">If the document mentions the transfer of possession; cf. <i>Sefer Ha</i>‘<i>iṭṭur</i> I, p. 13a, Note 7.</i>. A <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Jonah and Rebbi Yose: <sup class="footnote-marker">482</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 27a" href="/Kiddushin.27a">Babli 27a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 2:12" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).2.12">Tosephta <i>Baba batra</i>2:12</a>. These texts do not refer to a sale document in the Greek style.</i>“If he sold him ten fields together, when he took possession of one of them, he took possession of all of them. But if he only paid the amount for one <i>or</i> wrote the deed<sup class="footnote-marker">483</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ὠνή, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.6.3"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 4:6, Note 167</a>. The Hellenistic ὠνή was a transfer of title; cf. Taubenschlag (Note 369), pp. 155, 206. It is asserted here that if the transfer was made conditional upon payment, the transfer of title was restricted to the amount paid.</i> only for one of them he acquired only the one which was sold to him.” Does there exist a deed without money<sup class="footnote-marker">484</sup><i class="footnote">There does, in the transfer of real estate from living parents to children in the form of a fictitious sale; cf. Taubenschlag p. 155. This practice is discussed in the next paragraph.</i>? If you say, we hold that he paid for all of them, did it not say: “the amount of one”? It must be one or the other<sup class="footnote-marker">485</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>or</i> in the second sentence is an exclusive “or”; the second clause is independent of the first. It follows that if the contract was written for all properties, it transfers possession of all of them.</i>.
“By money.” Rebbi Abba was of the opinion, by money if there was no intention to write a deed. But if money was given with the intention to write a deed for him, possession was transferred only when the deed was written<sup class="footnote-marker">486</sup><i class="footnote">Since proving title is difficult without a written deed, the buyer has no intention of accepting title without the deed. Therefore, transfer of possession is possible only by delivery of the deed unless one explicitly stipulates otherwise. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 26a" href="/Kiddushin.26a">Babli, 26a</a>, this is a statement of Rav (Rebbi Abba bar Ayvo).</i>. Rebbi Jonah and Rebbi Yose disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">487</sup><i class="footnote">Since they are the collectors of the material in the Yerushalmi, their opinion is accepted as practice; it is not mentioned in the Babli for whom Samuel is the deciding authority in civil law.</i>. It follows that Rebbi Abba parallels Samuel and Rav Huna Rebbi Joḥanan. Rebbi Abba parallels Samuel. Samuel asked Rav Ḥuna: He was slaughtering sacrifices while being occupied<sup class="footnote-marker">488</sup><i class="footnote">He was occupied in slaughtering profane animals when he also slaughtered an animal dedicated as sacrifice without realizing it. Is this valid slaughter? The scenario is really impossible since the slaughter of profane animals is forbidden in the holy precinct, as is the slaughter of sacrifices outside. A Temple slaughterer has to learn his trade outside the holy precinct.</i>? He answered, “for your intention<sup class="footnote-marker">489</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:19" href="/Leviticus.22.19"><i>Lev</i>. 22:19</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 13a" href="/Chullin.13a">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 13a</a></i>,” this excludes one otherwise occupied. If one wrote a gift in the language of a sale<sup class="footnote-marker">490</sup><i class="footnote">Is a deed valid if it formulates a gift in the language of a fictitious sale? Starting here there is a shortened version, formulated differently, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 8:4:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.8.4.2-4"><i>Baba batra</i> 8:5</a>.</i>? He answered, he made it ride on two racing horses; Rebbi Abba<sup class="footnote-marker">491</sup><i class="footnote">Since Rav was Rav Huna’s teacher, this reference must be to Rav, in whose name the expression is quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 152a" href="/Bava_Batra.152a">Babli <i>Baba batra</i> 152a</a>.</i> said this; Samuel did not accept it. What means “he made it ride on two shining racing horses”? They wanted to say, one brings two crazy horses and makes it ride on both. One goes in one direction and the other in another; he has nothing in his hand<sup class="footnote-marker">492</sup><i class="footnote">Since sale and gift follow different rules, a gift formulated as sale is invalid.</i>. Rebbi Yose from Mamelia said, he doubly empowered him, for there is a title guarantee in a sale but none for a gift<sup class="footnote-marker">493</sup><i class="footnote">The seller is required to indemnify the buyer if the latter loses his land because of foreclosure of a mortgage owed by the seller. A gift is given without such guarantee.</i>; the seller did not sell everything but the giver of a gift gave everything<sup class="footnote-marker">494</sup><i class="footnote">A sale document is interpreted minimally: the seller of a parcel is not presumed to have sold a cistern or any other building on the parcel unless it be mentioned in the deed (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 4:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.4.2.1">Mishnah <i>Baba batra</i> 4:2</a>; cf <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Kiddushin 26a:9:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Kiddushin.26a.9.1">Tosaphot <i>Qiddušin</i>26a <i>s. v.</i> ולמה</a>), but a gift document is interpreted maximally. In this interpretation of Rav, confirmed by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 152a" href="/Bava_Batra.152a">Babli <i>Baba batra</i> 152a</a>, the holder of the deed is doubly privileged. In the Babli, Samuel is quoted as being unable to decide whether the deed is judged as sale or gift. Since Samuel is quoted as disagreeing, he must hold with R. Abba that if a deed is intended, only the deed will transfer possession.</i>. And Rav Ḥuna parallels Rebbi Joḥanan. A person, when he lay dying, said: my property should be given to X. Afterwards he said: write and deliver. Rebbi Eleazar and Rebbi Simeon bar Yaqim brought the case before Rebbi Joḥanan who said: If he said to transfer to him, everybody agrees that he entered into possession<sup class="footnote-marker">495</sup><i class="footnote">If the first time he stated that the person designated should be his heir, the latter will inherit if the dying person made it clear that the deed will only be written as proof of title, not as means of transfer of property.</i>. If the transfer was to be in writing, everybody agrees that nobody transfers rights in writing after his death<sup class="footnote-marker">496</sup><i class="footnote">If the document was a deed, not a will, intended to convey possession but not delivered during the donor’s lifetime, it cannot be delivered after death (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 135b" href="/Bava_Batra.135b">Babli <i>Baba batra</i> 135b</a>).</i>. Who would notify the greatness<sup class="footnote-marker">497</sup><i class="footnote">If the donor intended his orally conveyed gift to convey possession, how can the recipient prove his title?</i>? Explain it if witnesses know. Think of it, if no witnesses know it? Rebbi Yose said, the field remains in the hand of its proprietors<sup class="footnote-marker">498</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, the legal heirs.</i>; the burden of proof is on the claimant<sup class="footnote-marker">499</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 5:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.5.1.2"><i>Baba qama</i> 5:1</a> (21b 1. 70), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 27b" href="/Bava_Kamma.27b">Babli <i>Baba qama</i> 27b</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 46b" href="/Bava_Kamma.46b">46b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 92b" href="/Bava_Batra.92b"><i>Baba batra</i> 92b</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">500</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph and the following are from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 3:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.3.8"><i>Peah</i> 3:8, Notes 134–147</a> (<b>פ</b>). The second paragraph has no connection to the topics discussed here; this shows that the original place of the texts was in <i>Peah</i>.</i> From where that non-guaranteed property may be acquired together with guaranteed property, by money, document, or by possession? Rebbi Yose in the name of Ḥizqiah; Rebbi Jonah, Rebbi Ḥanina Tortaya in the name of Ḥizqiah, it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 21:3" href="/II_Chronicles.21.3"><i>2Chr</i>. 21:3</a>): “Their father gave them many gifts, silver and gold and delicacies, with fortified cities in Jehudah.” So far real estate and movables were at the same place. If real estate was at one place and movables elsewhere? Rebbi Abin bar Ḥiyya said, let us hear from the following: Rebbi Eliezer said to them, it happened that a man from Meron was dwelling in Jerusalem, who was rich in movables. He wanted to distribute them, to give them as gifts. They said to him, you cannot do that except if you acquire real estate. What did he do? He went and bought a rock near Jerusalem and said: The Northern part I give to X with a hundred amphoras of wine, the Southern part I give to Y with a hundred amphoras of oil. The matter came before the Sages and they upheld his words. Rebbi Ḥananiah said before Rebbi Mana: But was he not bedridden? For in general a person might give property rights only in writing, and here even orally. In general, a person might give only if real estate and movables are at the same place; here, however, the real estate was at one place and the movables elsewhere. He said to him: But is there a difference for Rebbi Eliezer? Is not the sick person for Rebbi Eliezer like the sick person for the rabbis? He said to him: The sick person for Rebbi Eliezer is like the healthy person for the rabbis.
There, we have stated: “Any real estate is subject to <i>peah</i> and first fruits, the words of Rebbi Aqiba.” What is the use of “any” real estate? Rebbi Mattaniah said, explain it if it had space for one stalk but a pearl was hidden in it.
Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If two fields were involved<sup class="footnote-marker">502</sup><i class="footnote">In a real estate transaction; the seller expects the buyer to take possession of both fields; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.5">Note 345</a>.</i>, one in Judea and one in Galilee. If he took possession of the one in Judea with the intent of taking possession of the one in Galilee, or the one in Galilee with the intent of taking possession of the one in Judea, he took possession. But in [the case of] property of a proselyte<sup class="footnote-marker">503</sup><i class="footnote">Who dies intestate without Jewish heirs and whose property now is ownerless. The rules of taking possession of ownerless property are more strict than those of taking possession of what was bought since the agreement of the seller to the transfer of the property is missing.</i> he did not acquire even the boundary strip between them<sup class="footnote-marker">504</sup><i class="footnote">If the proselyte owned two adjacent fields; the Jew took possession of both of them by doing some agricultural work on both but he did not walk the boundary strip with the intent of acquiring it; the strip is not automatically acquired with the fields since it is not subject to agricultural activities such as ploughing, weeding, or harvesting; cf. Note 353.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira asked before Rebbi Yasa: If he intended to acquire starting from the boundary strip and below<sup class="footnote-marker">505</sup><i class="footnote">The question is not answered; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 2:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.2.1.5"><i>Peah</i> 2:1, Note 16</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 52b" href="/Bava_Batra.52b">Babli, <i>Baba batra</i> 52b</a>, the question is undecided, i. e., anybody laying claim to the strip would have to prove his claim by other means.</i>? Rav Ḥisda said, property of a proselyte which he took possession of in the North with the intent of also acquiring that in the South, or in the South with the intent of acquiring also that in the North, but did not have the intent of acquiring the strip in the middle<sup class="footnote-marker">506</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., the boundary strip in between.</i>, he did not acquire unless he also intended to acquire the middle part<sup class="footnote-marker">507</sup><i class="footnote">Also quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 3:1:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.3.1.2-7"><i>Baba batra</i> 3:1</a> (13d 1. 55). In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 55a" href="/Bava_Batra.55a">Babli, <i>Baba batra</i>55a</a>, R. Joḥanan states that the boundary strip separates properties of the intestate proselyte. He also states Rav Ḥisda’s position even for commercial real estate transactions (<i>Baba batra</i>53a/b).</i>. A <i>baraita</i><sup class="footnote-marker">508</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 2:10" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).2.10">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 2:10</a>.</i> disagrees with Rav Ḥisda: If a proselyte died and Jews plundered his property, if one takes possession of the ground he is obligated for everything<sup class="footnote-marker">509</sup><i class="footnote">Tithes and all obligatory gifts to the poor.</i>. If he took possession of standing grain, he is obligated for fallen stalks, forgotten sheaves, and <i>peah</i> but freed from tithes. Does not the air separate between stalks<sup class="footnote-marker">510</sup><i class="footnote">Since single stalks can be picked up by the owner, not subject to the law of gleanings reserved for the poor, if the air separates the ownerless stalks the entire crop would be exempt from that law. This contradicts Rav Ḥisda who holds that any ground not taken in possession separates properties of the intestate proselyte.</i>? There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">511</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 5:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.5.4.1">Mishnah <i>Baba batra</i> 5:9</a>, speaking of a transaction involving a field of flax.</i>: “If he tore off anything connected to the ground, he took possession.” Samuel said, he took possession only of that leaf. But did we not state: “If it was connected to the ground and he tore off anything, he took possession”? Rebbi Yose said, Rebbi Eudaimon the emigrant<sup class="footnote-marker">512</sup><i class="footnote">He emigrated from Galilee to Babylonia.</i> confirmed it: Samuel will agree, for proselyte’s property<sup class="footnote-marker">513</sup><i class="footnote">A proselyte’s standing crop cannot be acquired by harvesting a single stalk, but only by taking the entire crop. Otherwise, it can be acquired only by taking possession of the land and paying taxes on it. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 87a" href="/Bava_Batra.87a">Babli, <i>Baba batra</i> 87a</a>, Rav Sheshet explains the problem away by postulating that the seller of the crop also sold a <i>peruṭah’</i> s worth of ground and that the movable crop was taken as possession together with the fictitious real estate.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan asked: Can movables be taken into possession by dragging? Rebbi Abba bar Mamal said, what is his problem? With hard hides<sup class="footnote-marker">514</sup><i class="footnote">Which are too heavy to be lifted.</i>. But soft hides are not taken into posession until lifted<sup class="footnote-marker">515</sup><i class="footnote">Movables which can be lifted are taken into possession only by being picked up and then moved; heavy movables can be taken into possession simply by being moved on the ground.</i>. A <i>baraita</i> disagrees with Rebbi Abba bar Mamal: If somebody steals another person’s pouch and removes it on the Sabbath, he is obligated since he already is obligated for the theft of the wallet before he comes in conflict with the holiness of the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">516</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 9:19" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).9.19">Tosephta <i>Baba qama</i> 9:19</a>. A person committing two crimes simultaneously can be prosecuted only for the more serious crime even if this prosecution is impossible because of external circumstances (for example, if the rabbinic court has only civil but no criminal jurisdiction, or if the civil offense can be proven by the standards of civil procedure but the criminal act cannot be proven by the stricter standards of criminal law; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 7:1:11-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.7.1.11-14">Terumot 7:1, Notes 51–71</a>, paralleled in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 3:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.3.1.7"><i>Ketubot</i>3:1, Note 30</a>.)<br>Since the thief incurred the liabilities for his theft the moment he took the pouch in his victim’s room but he violated the Sabbath prohibition only when he left and transported the pouch from a private to the public domain, the capital crime of violating the Sabbath is not connected with the theft. Paralleled in Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 91a, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 31a" href="/Ketubot.31a"><i>Ketubot</i>31a</a>, where some unconnected problems are raised about the Sabbath prohibition of moving between domains; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 86a" href="/Bava_Batra.86a"><i>Baba batra</i> 86a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 72a" href="/Sanhedrin.72a"><i>Sanhedrin</i>72a</a>; a different version in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 3:4:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.3.4.2-5"><i>Baba qama</i>3:4</a> (3c 1. 58ff.).</i>. But if he was dragging it until he left, he is free since the obligations of capital crime and restitution fall on him simultaneously<sup class="footnote-marker">517</sup><i class="footnote">Dragging a person’s pouch in that person’s domain does not transfer possession to the person dragging.</i>. Therefore, if the obligations of capital crime and restitution do not fall on him simultaneously he is obligated<sup class="footnote-marker">518</sup><i class="footnote">If the theft happened on a weekday and the thief only dragged, never lifted, the pouch, he nevertheless is obligated for double restitution. He cannot be liable for double restitution unless he took possession. This seems to contradict R. Abba bar Mamal.</i>. Rebbi Mattania said, explain it with large pouches which usually are dragged<sup class="footnote-marker">519</sup><i class="footnote">And therefore acquired by dragging. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 86a" href="/Bava_Batra.86a"><i>Baba batra</i> 86a</a>, in the name of Rav Ada bar Mattanah.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">520</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph and the next are from <i>Soṭah</i> 2:5 (<b>ס</b>, Notes 166–174). In the Babli, the parallel is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 27b" href="/Kiddushin.27b"><i>Qiddušin</i> 27b</a>.</i> From where did they learn rollover of oaths? From the suspected wife: Amen from this man, Amen from any other man. That refers to subjects he is able to make her swear about. What about subjects he is not able to make her swear about? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, let us hear from the following: “Amen that I was not deviant preliminarily married and married, waiting for the levir and taken in.” Can he make her swear when she is preliminarily married or waiting for the levir? Nevertheless one rolls over.
Some Tannaïm state: Since this one is subject to curse and oath, so also all who have to swear are under curse and oath. Some Tannaïm state: This one is subject to curse and oath, but no others who have to swear are under curse and oath. They wanted to say, the one who said, since this one is subject to curse and oath, so also all who have to swear are under curse and oath, is understandable. The one who said, this one is subject to curse and oath, but no others who have to swear are under curse and oath, can you infer for rollover only but not for oath? Some Tannaïm state: Since this one is subject to Amen, Amen, so also all who have to swear are under Amen, Amen. Some Tannaïm state: This one is subject to Amen, Amen, but no others who have to swear are under Amen, Amen. They wanted to say, the one who said, since this one is subject to Amen, Amen, so also all who have to swear are under Amen, Amen, is understandable. The one who said, this one is subject to Amen, Amen, but no others who have to swear are under Amen, Amen, did one not infer rollover of oaths from the suspected wife? Can you infer for rollover only but not for Amen, Amen?

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If anything is given instead of money, if one entered in possession, the other is obligated for its exchange<sup class="footnote-marker">521</sup><i class="footnote">If property is acquired by payment in kind, only one of the parties has to execute an act of taking possession. The other party then automatically is in possession and also assumes liability. This is not simple barter but exchange of money’s worth against money’s worth.</i>. How is this? If an ox was exchanged for a cow, or a donkey for an ox, if one entered in possession, the other is obligated for its exchange. Possession of Heaven is by money<sup class="footnote-marker">522</sup><i class="footnote">For the Temple, possession comes together with ownership by payment; it does not need a separate act on entering into possession.</i>, possession of an individual by taking hold. A promise to Heaven is like delivery to an individual<sup class="footnote-marker">523</sup><i class="footnote">Anything vowed to Heaven is as if delivered; the promise cannot be retracted.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If anything is given instead of money,” etc. There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">524</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Baba meṣia</i>‘ 4:1. Legal tender coin is not “movables” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 28" href="/Kiddushin.28">Babli 28a/b</a>); payment transfers ownership but not possession. The exchange of movables transfers both ownership and possession.</i>: “This is the principle: All movables acquire one another.” Rebbi Abba, Rav Ḥuna in the name of Rav: Even heaps among heaps<sup class="footnote-marker">525</sup><i class="footnote">A heap of grain at one place may be exchanged for a heap of grain at another place even if it is impossible to assign an exact price to either of them.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said to him, we have stated only: “If anything is given <i>instead of money</i>,” anything that needs estimation. It turns out that Rebbi Abba follows Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Eleazar his own opinion, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">526</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Šeqalim</i> 1:7, <i>Ḥulin</i> 1:7, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Bekhorot 9:3" href="/Mishnah_Bekhorot.9.3"><i>Bekhorot</i> 9:3</a>. Tradition held that firstlings are sanctified only if they belong to a single owner. The same is stated for animal tithe (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:32" href="/Leviticus.27.32"><i>Lev</i>. 27:32</a>); it is due only if the herd belongs to a single individual.</i>: “Brothers having common property who are obligated for agio<sup class="footnote-marker">527</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.4">Note 122</a>. The annual Temple tax, half a <i>holy šeqel</i> (cf. Note 353) per person, had to be given as a coin (<i>Šeqalim</i> 1:6, 46 1. 32; cf. all sources quoted in <i>Tanḥuma Buber Ky Tiśśa</i> 7, Note 46.) If payment was not made in the correct coin, an agio had to be paid to the Temple’s collectors. Only one agio was due per household, when the father paid the tax for himself and all adult males in his house. (Liddell and Scott compare the Greek word κόλλυβος to Hebrew חלף, “exchange, substitution”.)</i> are free from animal tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">528</sup><i class="footnote">If brothers are obligated to pay separate agios, they do not form a household. The flock they own as inheritance from their father therefore is held in partnership and is free from the duty of animal tithe.</i>, but if they are obligated for animal tithe they are free from agio<sup class="footnote-marker">529</sup><i class="footnote">If their father’s inheritance was not distributed, they still form their father’s family, pay the Temple tax together, and are obligated for animal tithe.</i>.” Rebbi Eleazar said, only if they exchanged kid goats against rams and rams against kid goats. But if they exchanged kid goats against kid goats and rams against rams, that was [the recipient’s] part from the first moment<sup class="footnote-marker">530</sup><i class="footnote">It turns out that it was his from the moment of the father’s death. Then the animals never had multiple owners. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 52b" href="/Bekhorot.52b">Babli, <i>Bekhorot</i> 52b</a>, the opinion attributed here to R. Eleazar is ascribed to Rav Anan; R. Eleazar there requires that not all brothers receive the same number of animals.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, even if they exchanged kid goats against kid goats and rams against rams, they are buyers<sup class="footnote-marker">531</sup><i class="footnote">In all cases of liquidation of an inheritance with multiple heirs. This principle is accepted in the Babli in R. Joḥanan’s name (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 37b" href="/Beitzah.37b"><i>Beṣah</i> 37b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 25a" href="/Gittin.25a"><i>Giṭṭin</i>25a</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 48a" href="/Gittin.48a">48a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 69b" href="/Bava_Kamma.69b"><i>Baba qama</i> 69b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 52b" href="/Bekhorot.52b"><i>Bekhorot</i>52b</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 57a" href="/Bekhorot.57a">57a</a>).</i>. But we have stated there: “The buyer and the recipient of a gift are free from animal tithe. Brothers having common property who are obligated for agio are free from animal tithe.” When they split and then joined again. “But if they are obligated for animal tithe they are free from agio.” If they never split.
<sup class="footnote-marker">532</sup><i class="footnote">Shortened versions of this paragraph are in <i>Baba meṣia</i>‘ 4:1 (9c 1. 36), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 67" href="/Ketubot.67">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 67a/b</a>.</i> Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel: One has a cow and one a donkey. They exchanged one for the other. The donkey’s owner took the cow<sup class="footnote-marker">533</sup><i class="footnote">Then automatically the donkey became the possession of its new owner.</i>. When the cow’s owner came to take the donkey and found that it had died, the donkey’s owner has to bring proof that his donkey was alive at the moment he took the cow. For the burden of proof is on the claimant<sup class="footnote-marker">499</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 5:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.5.1.2"><i>Baba qama</i> 5:1</a> (21b 1. 70), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 27b" href="/Bava_Kamma.27b">Babli <i>Baba qama</i> 27b</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 46b" href="/Bava_Kamma.46b">46b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 92b" href="/Bava_Batra.92b"><i>Baba batra</i> 92b</a>.</i> except for barter<sup class="footnote-marker">534</sup><i class="footnote">In case one party had no opportunity to inspect the object given in barter before the transfer of possession.</i>. And anybody who does not agree to this does not know anything about civil law. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, I do not agree<sup class="footnote-marker">535</sup><i class="footnote">For him, the burden of proof is always on the claimant, without exception. In <i>Baba meṣia</i>‘, he is reported to note that apparently he does not understand civil law.</i>. Rebbi Abba said to Rebbi Ze‘ira: A Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">536</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 7:7:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.7.7.2-6"><i>Ketubot</i> 7:9</a>. The husband divorces his wife because of bodily defects without paying the <i>ketubah</i>. If the defects are discovered between preliminary and definitive marriages, the father (of the underage bride) has to prove that at the moment of preliminary marriage the girl was without blemish in order to collect the <i>ketubah</i>. After definitive marriage, the husband has to prove that she was defective even before the preliminary marriage in order to avoid paying the <i>ketubah</i>.</i> disagrees with Samuel. “If she had bodily defects, as long as she is in her father’s house, the father has to prove.” Not the husband has to prove to regain the [money given] for preliminary marriage from the father<sup class="footnote-marker">537</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli, <i>loc</i>.<i>cit</i>., notes that it is not obvious that money given for preliminary marriage can be recovered, even if the marriage was entered into under false premises.</i>. The students of Rebbi Jonah said, explain it if little money was involved<sup class="footnote-marker">538</sup><i class="footnote">Not worth the bother of a court battle. Then only the father’s claim on the husband for the <i>ketubah</i> money remains; the father is the claimant who has to bring proof.</i>. An example: Rebbi Ḥuna, Rebbi Phineas, and Rebbi Ḥizqiah went to visit Rebbi Yosef<sup class="footnote-marker">539</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Baba qama</i>: R. Yose.</i> to learn from him. They said to him, the Mishnah supports Samuel: “Once she entered the husband’s domain, the husband has to prove.” Would not the father have to prove, to extract the <i>ketubah</i> from the husband’s possession<sup class="footnote-marker">540</sup><i class="footnote">The question is, who is considered the claimant? The husband, who wants to avoid paying the <i>ketubah</i> which became due at the moment of divorce, or the father who would have to collect the money.</i>? He answered them, does not Samuel agree that if the cow’s owner took possession of the donkey, he has to prove that it was defective before it came into his possession<sup class="footnote-marker">541</sup><i class="footnote">Since possession implies not only rights but also liabilities, the act of taking possession eliminates the liabilities of the previous owner. The owner of the cow should have inspected the donkey before moving the cow; afterwards he certainly is the claimant.</i>? And this one, when he took her in it is as if he drew close<sup class="footnote-marker">542</sup><i class="footnote">In this respect, definitive marriage of a woman entails transfer of liabilities to the husband, parallel to the transfer of liabilities for livestock by taking possession.</i>.
Rebbi Abba, Rav Hamnuna, Rav Ada bar Aḥawa in the name of Rav<sup class="footnote-marker">543</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, <i>Baba meṣi</i>‘<i>a</i> 47a, Rav Ada bar Ahava has a similar statement which however refers to true barter.</i>: If he<sup class="footnote-marker">544</sup><i class="footnote">The seller of the cow.</i> sold a cow for money, then grabbed him<sup class="footnote-marker">545</sup><i class="footnote">The buyer of the cow.</i> and said, give me that money. He<sup class="footnote-marker">545</sup><i class="footnote">The buyer of the cow.</i> answered, for what do you need it? He said, to buy a donkey for him[self]<sup class="footnote-marker">546</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously, something is missing in this sentence. The seller stated that he needed the money to buy a donkey, the buyer sold him a donkey for the money he owed him. Then sale of the cow and purchase of the donkey are two unrelated operations.</i>. If the owner of the cow took possession of the donkey, the cow was not taken into possession<sup class="footnote-marker">547</sup><i class="footnote">If the deal had be agreed upon but was not yet executed.</i>. Was the donkey taken into possession<sup class="footnote-marker">548</sup><i class="footnote">If the buyer of the cow took possession of it.</i>? Rebbi Abba said, it was taken into possession. Rebbi Yose said, it was not taken into possession. Rebbi Abba thought that this was barter, but it was not barter. Rebbi Mana in the name of Rebbi Yose: Sometimes it is a new deal for one party and a new deal for the other party. How is that? He<sup class="footnote-marker">544</sup><i class="footnote">The seller of the cow.</i> sold him<sup class="footnote-marker">545</sup><i class="footnote">The buyer of the cow.</i> a cow for money; he left the money standing with him. The next day, he<sup class="footnote-marker">545</sup><i class="footnote">The buyer of the cow.</i> found him<sup class="footnote-marker">544</sup><i class="footnote">The seller of the cow.</i> standing there and said, what are you doing here? He<sup class="footnote-marker">544</sup><i class="footnote">The seller of the cow.</i> said, I want to collect the money. He<sup class="footnote-marker">545</sup><i class="footnote">The buyer of the cow.</i> answered, for what do you want it? He<sup class="footnote-marker">544</sup><i class="footnote">The seller of the cow.</i> said, to buy myself a donkey. He<sup class="footnote-marker">545</sup><i class="footnote">The buyer of the cow.</i> answered, there is a donkey before you. If either one of them drew<sup class="footnote-marker">549</sup><i class="footnote">He drew the animal close to himself in order to take possession. Since there are two separate deals, it needs two separate actions to take possession.</i>, the other one did not enter into possession; each one has to take possession separately.
The Mishnah: “Possession of Heaven is by money, possession of any individual by taking hold.” How? If the treasurer paid with Temple money for movables, they are in the Temple’s possession anywhere they are<sup class="footnote-marker">550</sup><i class="footnote">Without a separate act of taking possession. The seller cannot retract the sale. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 28b" href="/Kiddushin.28b">Babli 28b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kiddushin (Lieberman) 1:9" href="/Tosefta_Kiddushin_(Lieberman).1.9">Tosephta <i>Qiddušin</i>1:9</a>, <i>Baba qama</i> 4:3, ‘<i>Arakhin</i> 4:4.</i>, for it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">551</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 24:1" href="/Psalms.24.1"><i>Ps</i>. 24:1</a>.</i>: “The Eternal’s are the earth and what fills it, dry land and its inhabitants.”
“A promise to Heaven is like delivery to an individual.” How? If he bought a cow from the Temple for 200. If he did not have occasion to pay the 200 until the going rate was a <i>mina</i>, he has to pay 200. That means, a promise to Heaven is like delivery to an individual. A cow from the Temple for a <i>mina</i>. If he did not have occasion to pay the <i>mina</i> until the going rate was 200, he has to pay 200. That is what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">552</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:19" href="/Leviticus.27.19"><i>Lev</i>. 27:19</a></i>: “He has to add a fifth of its estimated value, then it is delivered to him.” Therefore, if he adds, it is delivered to him; if he does not add, it is not delivered to him. <sup class="footnote-marker">553</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 28b" href="/Kiddushin.28b">Babli 28b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kiddushin (Lieberman) 1:9" href="/Tosefta_Kiddushin_(Lieberman).1.9">Tosephta 1:9</a>.</i>“ ‘This steer as elevation offering, or this house as a <i>qorbān</i>,’ they are in the Temple’s possession wherever they are, for it is written: “The Eternal’s are the earth and what fills it,” etc. But among private persons one obtains possession only be taking hold.”

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> For any commandment a father has to fulfill towards his son, men are obligated but women are free. For any commandment the son has to fulfill towards his father, men and women are equally obligated. For any positive commandment activated by time<sup class="footnote-marker">554</sup><i class="footnote">Which either depends on the time of day or the calendar date. This rule admits of quite a few exceptions.</i>, men are obligated but women are free. For any positive commandment not activated by time<sup class="footnote-marker">555</sup><i class="footnote">E. g., the obligation to give charity and do charitable work.</i>, men and women are equally obligated. For any prohibition, whether or not activated by time, men and women are equally obligated except for “do not destroy”, “do not shear,”<sup class="footnote-marker">556</sup><i class="footnote">Not to destroy one’s beard or shear the sideburns, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:27" href="/Leviticus.19.27"><i>Lev</i>. 19:27</a>, inappropriate for women.</i> and “do not become impure for the dead.<sup class="footnote-marker">557</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:1" href="/Leviticus.21.1"><i>Lev</i>. 21:1</a>. In general, holiness prohibitions valid for priests do not apply to their daughters and only partially to their wives.</i>”<br>Leanings<sup class="footnote-marker">558</sup><i class="footnote">The obligatory leaning on the head of the sacrifice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:4" href="/Leviticus.1.4"><i>Lev</i>. 1:4</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 3:2" href="/Leviticus.3.2">3:2</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:8" href="/Leviticus.1.8">8</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:13" href="/Leviticus.1.13">13</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 4:4:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.4.4.2-3">4:4</a>,15,24,29,33).</i> and weavings<sup class="footnote-marker">559</sup><i class="footnote">The prescribed motions for selected sacrifices of purification (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 8:27" href="/Leviticus.8.27"><i>Lev</i>. 8:27</a>; 14:12,24; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:25" href="/Numbers.5.25"><i>Num</i>. 5:25</a>; 6:19).</i>, presentations<sup class="footnote-marker">560</sup><i class="footnote">Presentations to the altar, mainly of flour offerings (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2:6" href="/Leviticus.2.6"><i>Lev</i>. 2:6</a>; 6:7).</i>, taking of fistfuls<sup class="footnote-marker">561</sup><i class="footnote">The fistful burned on the altar of flour offerings; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:8" href="/Leviticus.6.8"><i>Lev</i>. 6:8</a>.</i>, burnings<sup class="footnote-marker">562</sup><i class="footnote">Bringing meat, flour, or incense and putting it in the fire on the altar.</i> and neck breakings<sup class="footnote-marker">563</sup><i class="footnote">Of bird sacrifices; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:15" href="/Leviticus.1.15"><i>Lev</i>. 1:15</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:8" href="/Leviticus.5.8">5:8</a>.</i>, sprinklings<sup class="footnote-marker">564</sup><i class="footnote">Of blood on the walls of the altar, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:5" href="/Leviticus.1.5"><i>Lev</i>. 1:5</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:11" href="/Leviticus.1.11">11</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:2:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.2.2-7">3:2</a>,8,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:11:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.11.2">13</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 4:6:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.4.6.2-7">4:6</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 4:7:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.4.7.2-3">7</a>,17,18, 25,30,34; 5:9.</i> and receptions<sup class="footnote-marker">565</sup><i class="footnote">Receiving the blood of slaughtered sacrifices in a sanctified vessel. All these actions are restricted to male Aharonides except for leanings and weavings, which are prescribed for the public.</i> apply to men but not to women except for the flour offerings of the suspected wife and the <i>nezirah</i>; those do weave.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Any commandment the father has to fulfill towards his son,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">566</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 29a" href="/Kiddushin.29a">Babli 29a</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 30b" href="/Kiddushin.30b">30b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kiddushin (Lieberman) 1:11" href="/Tosefta_Kiddushin_(Lieberman).1.11">Tosephta 1:11</a>; <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Ismael</i>,<i>Pisḥa</i> 18.</i>“To circumcise him, to redeem him, to teach him Torah, to teach him a trade, and to marry him to a wife. Rebbi Aqiba says, also to teach him to swim in water.” To circumcise him, “on the eighth day, he shall circumcise the flesh of his foreskin.<sup class="footnote-marker">567</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 12:3" href="/Leviticus.12.3"><i>Lev</i>. 12:3</a>. The masculine form of the verb implies that the father, who otherwise is not mentioned in the paragraph, is obligated to circumcise his son.</i>” “To redeem him,” as is written: “Any firstborn human among your sons you shall redeem.<sup class="footnote-marker">568</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:13" href="/Exodus.13.13"><i>Ex</i>. 13:13</a>.</i>” To teach him Torah, “you shall teach them to your sons.<sup class="footnote-marker">569</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 11:19" href="/Deuteronomy.11.19"><i>Deut</i>. 11:19</a>. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 2:3:2-19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.2.3.2-19"><i>Berakhot</i> 2:3</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:24" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.24">Note 110</a>, where the verse is quoted to show that daughters do not have to be instructed in Torah (Babli 29b).</i>” To teach him a trade, Rebbi Ismael stated: “Choose life<sup class="footnote-marker">570</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 30:19" href="/Deuteronomy.30.19"><i>Deut</i>. 30:19</a>.</i>,” that is a trade. To marry him to a wife, “make it known to your children and grandchildren<sup class="footnote-marker">571</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 4:9" href="/Deuteronomy.4.9"><i>Deut</i>. 4:9</a>.</i>.” When do you have children and grandchildren: if you marry off your sons when young. Rebbi Aqiba says, also to swim in water. “That you shall live together with your descendants<sup class="footnote-marker">570</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 30:19" href="/Deuteronomy.30.19"><i>Deut</i>. 30:19</a>.</i>.” How is this? Is it a commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">572</sup><i class="footnote">A meritorious deed.</i> or is it obligatory? Let us hear from the following: Bar Tarima came to Rebbi Immi and said to him, talk to my father that he should marry me to a woman. He talked to [the father], but the latter refused. That means it is a commandment, for if it were obligatory, he should have forced him. From where that if his father did not do these things for him, he is required to do them himself? The verse says a human you shall redeem<sup class="footnote-marker">568</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:13" href="/Exodus.13.13"><i>Ex</i>. 13:13</a>.</i>”, “you shall circumcise” them<sup class="footnote-marker">573</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 17:11" href="/Genesis.17.11"><i>Gen</i>. 17:11</a>.</i>, “you shall study them<sup class="footnote-marker">574</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 4:9" href="/Deuteronomy.4.9"><i>Deut</i>. 4:9</a>; reading וְלָמַדְתֶּם instead of וְלִמַּדְתֶּם.</i>”, “you shall know<sup class="footnote-marker">571</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 4:9" href="/Deuteronomy.4.9"><i>Deut</i>. 4:9</a>.</i>” yourself, “that you shall live<sup class="footnote-marker">570</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 30:19" href="/Deuteronomy.30.19"><i>Deut</i>. 30:19</a>.</i>.”<sup class="footnote-marker">575</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 29a-30a" href="/Kiddushin.29a-30a">Babli, 29a–30a</a>, in most cases quotes different verses to the same effects.</i>
There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">576</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Eduyot 2:9" href="/Mishnah_Eduyot.2.9">Mishnah <i>Idiut</i> 2:9</a>.</i>: “The father bestows on his son beauty, strength, riches, wisdom, and years.” From where beauty? “May Your deeds appear on Your servants, and Your glory on their sons.<sup class="footnote-marker">577</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 90:16" href="/Psalms.90.16"><i>Ps</i>. 90:16</a>.</i>” Strength, “strong on earth will be his descendants.<sup class="footnote-marker">578</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 112:2" href="/Psalms.112.2"><i>Ps</i>. 112:2</a>.</i>” Riches, “I was young and became old, but never saw a just man abandoned and his descendants in need of bread.<sup class="footnote-marker">579</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 37:25" href="/Psalms.37.25"><i>Ps</i>. 37:25</a>.</i>” Wisdom, “you shall teach your sons to argue about them.<sup class="footnote-marker">569</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 11:19" href="/Deuteronomy.11.19"><i>Deut</i>. 11:19</a>. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 2:3:2-19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.2.3.2-19"><i>Berakhot</i> 2:3</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:24" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.24">Note 110</a>, where the verse is quoted to show that daughters do not have to be instructed in Torah (Babli 29b).</i>” Years, “that your and your sons’ days be many.<sup class="footnote-marker">580</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 11:21" href="/Deuteronomy.11.21"><i>Deut</i>. 11:21</a>.</i>” And just as he inherits five qualities, so he owes him the following five things. He feeds him, he gives him to drink, he clothes him, he puts on his shoes, he leads him<sup class="footnote-marker">581</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 1:1:28" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.1.1.28"><i>Peah</i> 1:1, Note 119</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 31b" href="/Kiddushin.31b">Babli 31b</a>.</i>. That is what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">582</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 3:29" href="/II_Samuel.3.29"><i>2S</i>. 3:29</a>.</i>: “This should fall on Joab’s head: sufferer from flux and from skin disease, holding the distaff, falling by the sword, and senseless.<sup class="footnote-marker">583</sup><i class="footnote">This is a slip of the pen; later it is quoted in the language of the verse, “without bread”.</i>” Sufferer from flux, weak. From skin disease, abandoned<sup class="footnote-marker">584</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:46" href="/Leviticus.13.46"><i>Lev</i>. 13:46</a>.</i>. Holding the distaff, uneducated. Falling by the sword, short lived. Without bread, poor. <sup class="footnote-marker">585</sup><i class="footnote">The Babylonian version is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 48b" href="/Sanhedrin.48b">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 48b</a>, and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 23:13" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.23.13"><i>Num</i>.<i>rabba</i>23(13</a>).</i> When Solomon came to kill Joab, he said to him: Your father gave me five sentences; accept them and I can be killed. He accepted them and all of them came to pass on the House of David. Sufferer from flux was Rehabeam: “King Rehabeam with difficulty climbed on his chariot to flee to Jerusalem;<sup class="footnote-marker">586</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 12:18" href="/I_Kings.12.18"><i>1K</i>. 12:18</a>.</i>” some say, he suffered from flux; some say, he was weak. Suffering from skin disease was Uziahu: “King Uziahu suffered from skin disease until the day of his death<sup class="footnote-marker">587</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 26:21" href="/II_Chronicles.26.21"><i>2Chr</i>. 26:21</a>.</i>.” Holding the distaff was Joash: “They punished Joash<sup class="footnote-marker">588</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 24:24" href="/II_Chronicles.24.24"><i>2Chr</i>. 24:24</a>.</i>.” Rebbi Ismael stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">589</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Ismael</i>,<i>Amaleq</i> 1; <i>Tanḥuma Bešallaḥ</i> 25</i> This teaches that they gave him over to hardened hoodlums who had never known a woman and they raped him the way women are raped. That is what is written: “Israel’s pride will testify against it,<sup class="footnote-marker">590</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Hosea 5:5" href="/Hosea.5.5"><i>Hos</i>. 5:5</a>, repunctuating עָנָה to עִנָּה.</i>” Israel’s pride will be raped in its face. Falling by the sword, this is Josiah, as is written: “The archers shot at king Josia,<sup class="footnote-marker">591</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 35:23" href="/II_Chronicles.35.23"><i>2Chr</i>. 35:23</a>. In the text: הַיֹּרִים.</i>” and Rebbi Joḥanan said, this teaches that they made his body like a sieve<sup class="footnote-marker">592</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Moed Katan 28b" href="/Moed_Katan.28b">Babli <i>Mo‘ed qaṭan</i> 28b</a>; <i>Thr</i>.<i>rabbati</i> on 1:18.</i>. Rebbi Ismael stated: 300 arrows were shot at the Eternal’s anointed. Without bread, that is Jehoiachin: “And his meal, a permanent meal was given to him,<sup class="footnote-marker">593</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 25:30" href="/II_Kings.25.30"><i>2K</i>. 25:30</a>. He never had any money of his own. A completely different interpretation of the verse in <i>Midrash Shemuel</i> 18(5).</i>.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">594</sup><i class="footnote">This origin of this text is in <i>Šabbat</i>1, to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>.</i> There, we have stated: “If they started<sup class="footnote-marker">595</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah contains a list of things one should not do on Friday afternoon, but if one started one does not have to interrupt. One of these is going to the public bathhouse.</i>, one does not interrupt.” What is the start of bathing? Rebbi Zeriqan in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: When he opened his belt. Rav said, when he took off his shoe. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi used to hear the lesson<sup class="footnote-marker">596</sup><i class="footnote">The portion of the Torah to be read on the Sabbath.</i> of his grandson every Friday. Once he forgot and went bathing in the public baths of Tiberias; he was leaning on Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba’s shoulder. He remembered<sup class="footnote-marker">597</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Šabbat</i>: He remembered that he had not heard the lesson from his grandson, returned and left.</i> and left the public bath. <sup class="footnote-marker">598</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Šabbat</i>: How was it? Rebbi Derose said, he was anointing himself. Rebbi Eleazar …</i> He said, so it was: Rebbi Eleazar bar Yose said, he had taken off [his clothes]<sup class="footnote-marker">599</sup><i class="footnote">Fom <i>Šabbat</i>, instead of the unintelligible מטי written here.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said to him, did our teacher not teach us, “if they started, one does not interrupt”? He said to him, Ḥiyya my son, is that unimportant in your eyes? For anyone who hears the lesson from his grandson is as if he heard it from Mount Sinai. What is the reason? “<sup class="footnote-marker">600</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 4:10" href="/Deuteronomy.4.10"><i>Deut</i>. 4:10</a>.</i> You shall make them known to you sons and grandsons,” etc., <sup class="footnote-marker">601</sup><i class="footnote">Missing in <i>Šabbat</i>, probably correctly.</i>“the day when you stood before the Eternal, your God, at Horeb.” Like the day when you stood before the Eternal, your God, at Horeb. Rebbi Ḥizqiah bar<sup class="footnote-marker">602</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Šabbat</i>: R. Ḥizqiah, R. Jeremiah, R. Ḥiyya. This is the only correct text.</i> Jeremiah, Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If you can link the tradition back to Moses, link it. Otherwise take either the very first or the very last [source]<sup class="footnote-marker">603</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Šabbat</i> added: What is the reason? “The day when you stood,” Up to the day when you stood before the Eternal, your God, at Horeb. (Referring to the verse quoted earlier, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:7:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.7.4">Note 600</a>. Since Moses was speaking to the second generation, not present at Sinai, it proves that a person can be present at Sinai by connecting with the teachings from Sinai.)</i>. Giddul said, anybody who quotes somebody should consider it as if the author of the quote stood before him. What is the reason? “Only in image a man wanders.<sup class="footnote-marker">604</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 39:7" href="/Psalms.39.7"><i>Ps</i>. 39:7</a>.</i>” “Many a man professes good will, but where will you find one you can trust?” <sup class="footnote-marker">605</sup><i class="footnote">This is a <i>non sequitur</i>. The intelligible text is in <i>Šabbat</i>: “Many a man professes good will,” these are all other people, “but where will you find one you can trust?” That is R. Ze‘ira.</i> That is Rebbi Ze‘ira, as Rebbi Ze‘ira said, we do take the traditions of Rav Sheshet into account since he is blind. <sup class="footnote-marker">606</sup><i class="footnote">This text is unacceptable for reasons of chronology. The correct text is in <i>Šabbat</i>: “He said to him, R. Joḥanan said it in his name. R. Ze‘ira asked R. Abba bar Zavda: Did you know Rav (a chronological impossibility), that you are quoting in his name? He answered, Rav Ada bar Ahava said it in his name.”</i> And Rebbi Ze‘ira said to Rebbi Yasa, does the Rabbi know Bar Pedaiah, that you quote in his name? He said to him, Rav Ada bar Aḥawa said it in his name.
Commandments which the son is obligated to fulfill towards his father. <sup class="footnote-marker">607</sup><i class="footnote">This is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 1:1:28-29" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.1.1.28-29"><i>Peah</i> 1:1, Notes 117–124</a>. Leiden ms. text <b>פ</b>, Rome ms. R. This is a quote out of context, repeated in the following paragraphs which copy <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 1:1:22-32" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.1.1.22-32"><i>Peah</i> 1:1, Notes 98–137</a>.<br>A few sentences from <i>Peah</i> which are missing in the text have been given in brackets.</i> What is fear? He may not sit in his place and may not speak in his stead nor contradict his words. What is honor? He feeds and gives him to drink, clothes him, covers him, and puts on his shoes, leads him out and in. From whose money? Huna bar Ḥiyya said, from the old man’s. Some want to say, from his own. Did not Rebbi Abbahu say in the name of Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina: From where do we know that even if his father tells him to throw the wallet into the sea that he should obey him? That is, if he has another one and if it helps to quiet his father’s spirit. Both men and women. Only the man has the power in his hand, but the woman does not have the power in her own hand because others have disposition over her. If she is widowed or divorced, the power is in her own hand.
[They asked Rebbi Eliezer,] how far does honoring father and mother go? He said to them, you are asking me? Go and ask Dama ben Netinah. Dama ben Netinah was the head of the city council. Once his mother was slapping him in front of the entire council and a slipper fell from her hand, and he handed it back to her so that she should feel no inconvenience. Rebbi Ḥizqiah said: He was a Gentile from Ascalon and the head of the city council. He never sat on the stone on which his father used to sit and when his father died, he worshipped the stone. Once the jaspis of Benjamin was lost. They inquired, who would have one of similar quality? They were informed that Dama ben Netinah did. They went to him and agreed on 100 denars. He went to the upper floor to bring it and found his father sleeping. Some say that the key to the chest was in his father’s fingers; some say his father’s foot was resting on the chest. He descended to them and told them: I could not bring it to you. They thought, maybe he wants more money, and raised the price to two hundred, then to a thousand. When his father woke up from his sleep, he went up and brought it to them. They wanted to give him according to the amount last mentioned, but he refused. He said: Do I sell my father’s honor for money? I will not have any advantage from honoring my father. What reward did the Holy One, praise to Him, give him? Rebbi Yose bar Abun said: The following night, his cow gave birth to a red heifer and Israel gave him its weight in gold and took it. Rebbi Sabbatai said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 37:23" href="/Job.37.23"><i>Job</i> 37:23</a>), it is written: “Justice and much charity He will not suppress.” The Holy One, praise to Him, will not wait to give the reward for good deeds to the Gentiles.
Rebbi Tarphon’s mother went to promenade in her backyard on the Sabbath when her slipper broke. Rebbi Tarphon went and put his two hands under her feet and she walked on them until she got to her couch. Once the Sages came to visit him. She told them: Pray for my son Tarphon because he honors me too much. They asked her, what did he do for you? She told them what had happened. They said to her, even if he did this a million times, he did not yet reach even half of the honor that the Torah requires.
Rebbi Ismael’s mother came, spoke, and complained to our teachers about him. She said to them: Scold my son Ismael because he does not honor me! At that, the faces of our teachers became saffron colored. They said, is it possible that Rebbi Ismael does not honor his parents? They said to her, what did he do to you? She said, when he comes from the house of assembly, I want to wash his feet and drink from it, and he refuses. They said, since it is her wish it is her honor.
Rebbi Mana said: The millers are correct when they say that each and every person has in his chest that which is appropriate for him. Rebbi Tarphon’s mother said so and they answered her appropriately, Rebbi Ismael’s mother said so and they answered her appropriately. Rebbi Zeïra was sorry and said, if only I had father or mother that I could honor them and inherit paradise. When he heard these two instructions, he said: Praise to the All-Merciful that I have neither father nor mother. I could not have done as Rebbi Tarphon did and I could not accept what Rebbi Ismael accepted. Rebbi Abun said, I am free from honoring father and mother. They said, his father died when his mother was pregnant and she died in giving birth.
Some person might serve his father fattened meat and inherit hell. Some person might bind his father to the grindstone and inherit paradise. How does one serve his father fattened meat and inherit hell? A person used to serve his father fattened chickens. One day, his father said to him: My son, from where do you get the money for these? He said to him: Old man, old man, eat and shut up just as dogs eat and are silent. It turns out that he serves his father fattened meat and inherits hell. How does one bind his father to the grindstone and inherits paradise? A person was a miller at a grindstone; there came a requisition for millers. He said: Father, go and grind in my stead. If it should come to pass that anyone be degraded, it is better that it should happen to me rather than to you. If it should come to pass that anyone be beaten, it is better that it should happen to me rather than to you. It turns out that he binds his father to the grindstone and inherits paradise.
It is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:3" href="/Leviticus.19.3"><i>Lev</i>. 19:3</a>): “Everybody must fear his mother and his father,” and it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 6:13" href="/Deuteronomy.6.13"><i>Deut</i>. 6:13</a>): “You must fear the Eternal, your God, and serve Him.” This brackets the fear of father and mother with the fear of the Omnipresent. [It is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 20:12" href="/Exodus.20.12"><i>Ex</i>. 20:12</a>): “Honor your father and your mother,” and it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 3:9" href="/Proverbs.3.9"><i>Prov</i>. 3:9</a>): “Honor the Eternal with your property.” This brackets the honor of father and mother with the honor of the Omnipresent.] Is is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:17" href="/Exodus.21.17"><i>Ex</i>. 21:17</a>): “He who curses his father or his mother shall be put to death,” and it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 24:19" href="/Leviticus.24.19"><i>Lev</i>. 24:19</a>): “Everybody who curses his God must bear his sin.” This brackets cursing father and mother with cursing the Omnipresent. It is impossible to speak about hitting relative to the Deity. All this is logical since all three of them are partners in him.
<sup class="footnote-marker">608</sup><i class="footnote">This is a truncated repetition of the text at <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:7:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.7.5">Note 607</a>.</i> What is fear? He may not sit in his place and may not speak in his stead nor contradict his words. What is honor? He feeds and gives him to drink, clothes him and puts on his shoes, leads him out and in. From whose money? Huna bar Ḥiyya said.
The words of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba disagree, for Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said that Rebbi Yudan, son of Rebbi Simeon bar Ioḥai’s daughter, stated that Rebbi Simeon bar Ioḥai stated: Honoring father and mother is great because the Holy One, praise to Him, preferred it over His own honor. It is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 20:12" href="/Exodus.20.12"><i>Ex</i>. 20:12</a>): “Honor your father and your mother,” and it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 3:9" href="/Proverbs.3.9"><i>Prov</i>. 3:9</a>): “Honor the Eternal with your property.” How do you honor Him? From your property! <sup class="footnote-marker">609</sup><i class="footnote">From here on there exists a Genizah fragment of the text (G). It is fragmentary in this paragraph, continuous in the following.</i> One gives gleanings, forgotten sheaves, and <i>peah</i>, one gives <i>terumah</i>, the First and Second Tithes and the tithe for the poor, <i>ḥallah</i>, one makes a <i>sukkah</i>, and <i>lulav</i>,<i>shofar</i>, and <i>tefillin</i>, and <i>ẓiẓit</i>, one feeds the hungry and gives to drink to the thirsty. If you have the wherewhithal, you are obliged for all of these; if you have nothing, you are not obliged even for one of them. But when it comes to honoring father and mother, whether you own property or you do not, you must honor father and mother, even if you are a beggar at people’s doors.
Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Abba bar Cahana: It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 5:6" href="/Proverbs.5.6"><i>Prov</i>. 5:6</a>): “She does not smooth the way of life, her tracks deviate and you will not notice it.” The Holy One, praise to Him, moved the rewards of those who fulfill the commandments (to the future world) so that they should act in faith. Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Isaac: It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 4:23" href="/Proverbs.4.23"><i>Prov</i>. 4:23</a>): “Observe carefully everything which must be kept, for from it comes life,” observe carefully all the things you were told in the Torah, for you do not know from which of them life will come to you. Rebbi Abba bar Cahana said, the verse equals the easiest commandment with the most difficult one. The easiest commandment is sending away [the mother] from the nest. The most difficult one is honoring father and mother. For both of them it is written: “Your days will be lengthened.” Rebbi Abun said, if for anything that is repayment of debt it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 5:16" href="/Deuteronomy.5.16"><i>Deut</i>. 5:16</a>): “That you shall be well and that your days shall be lengthened,” so much more something that involves monetary loss and personal danger. Rebbi Levi said, this one is greater; repaying a debt is greater than fulfilling an obligation that does not involve repaying a debt. Rebbi Simeon ben Ioḥai stated: Just as their rewards are the same, so their punishments are identical. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 30:17" href="/Proverbs.30.17"><i>Prov</i>. 30:17</a>) “The eye that scoffs at the father and despises to obey the mother,” the eye that scoffs about honoring father and mother and despises the commandment not to take the mother with the chicks, “the river ravens should pick it out,” the cruel raven should come, pick it out, and not have any enjoyment from it, “the sons of the eagle should eat it,” the merciful eagle should come and enjoy it.
Rebbi Yannai and Rebbi Jonathan were sitting together. There came a man and kissed Rebbi Jonathan’s foot. Rebbi Yannai said to him: What kindness did he pay you back for from former days? He said to him: He once came and complained to me about his son that he should support him and I told him, go, assemble a congregation and humiliate him<sup class="footnote-marker">609a</sup><i class="footnote">S. Assaf (<i>Responsa Geonica</i>1942, p. 105; <i>Courts and their organization</i>, Jerusalem 1924, p. 25–26) reads: “Go, arrest the congregation and humiliate him.” He sees here a reference to the practice known from Galilean and Medieval Ashkenazic sources (rejected by Babylonian Geonim) that a person whose grievances are not addressed by the court may publicly request the congregation to stop their prayers until his complaint is heard (in Ashkenazic practice before the <i>Qaddiš</i> which introduces the main prayer.)</i>. He said to him, why did you not force him? He said to him, does one force him? He said to him, you still have questions about that? One said, Rebbi Jonathan changed his opinion and fixed it as a tradition in his own name. The result is: Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jonathan, one forces the son to feed his father. Rebbi Yose bar Abun said, if only all my traditions were so clear to me as this one: One forces the son to feed his father.
<sup class="footnote-marker">610</sup><i class="footnote">Here begins the discussion of the second half of the Mishnah. Except for a sentence which has disappeared from the Leiden ms. and <i>editio princeps</i>, this paragraph is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:3:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.3.8"><i>Berakhot</i> 3:3, Notes 143–146</a>. The ms. text there is noted <b>ת</b>, the Rome ms. R. The text in quotation marks is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kiddushin (Lieberman) 1:10" href="/Tosefta_Kiddushin_(Lieberman).1.10">Tosephta 1:10</a>.</i>“What is a positive commandment that is activated by time? For example <i>sukkah</i>, and <i>shofar</i>, and <i>lulab</i>, and <i>tefillin</i>. What is a positive commandment that is not activated by time? For example lost articles, sending away the [mother from the] nest, railing, and <i>ẓiẓit</i><sup class="footnote-marker">611</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 33b" href="/Kiddushin.33b">Babli, <i>Qiddušin</i> 33b</a>, does not mention <i>ẓiẓit</i>, cf. Berakhot, Note 146; it is quoted <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 43a" href="/Menachot.43a"><i>Menaḥot</i> 43a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Simeon exempts women from <i>ẓiẓit</i> because it is a positive commandment activated by time.” Rebbi Simeon said to them: Do you not agree with me that it is a positive commandment activated by time? Because a nightgown is exempt from <i>ẓiẓit</i>. Rebbi Hila said: The reason of the rebbis is that, if a garment is designated both for daytime and for night, it needs <i>ẓiẓit</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">612</sup><i class="footnote">The origin of this paragraph is <i>Pesaḥim</i> 8:1 (35d 1. 35 ff.), noted <b>ם</b>.</i> Rebbi Eleazar said: The Passover sacrifice of women is voluntary<sup class="footnote-marker">613</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence contradicts everything a student of the Babli would expect. Most commentators either emend the passage or explain it away. But since there are three witnesses to the text, emendation is not an acceptable option. In the Babli, 91b, R. Eleazar declares a woman to be obligated for Passover like men. However, this statement is obtained by an editorial re-interpretation of R. Eleazar’s statement.<br>In the Babli and its related texts, it is taken for granted that women present in Jerusalem on Passover eve are obligated to take part in the Passover meal, based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:4" href="/Exodus.12.4"><i>Ex</i>. 12:4</a> which designates the participants as “persons”, not “men” (<i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Ismael</i>,<i>Piṣha</i>3, <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Šim</i>‘<i>on b</i>.<i>Jochai</i> p. 10; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 8:1:1-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.8.1.1-10">Mishnah <i>Pesaḥim</i> 8:1</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 8:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.8.7.1">7</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 8:10" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).8.10">Tosephta <i>Pesaḥim</i> 8:10</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 91" href="/Pesachim.91">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 91a/b</a>.) But it is to be noted that missing the sacrificial meal is a deadly sin only for men (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 9:13" href="/Numbers.9.13"><i>Num</i>. 9:13</a>); only men are required (and permitted) to make up the missed sacrifice on the 14th of Iyar (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 9:13" href="/Numbers.9.13"><i>Num</i>. 9:13</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 8:10" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).8.10">Tosephta <i>Piṣha</i> 8:10</a>).<br>The statement means that an unaffiliated female, living neither in her father’s nor a husband’s house, is not obligated to seek a group with which to celebrate Passover. It would be considered most inappropriate if a single woman would join a group of men unrelated to her. But since she is included in the circle of celebrants of Passover by biblical law, if she alone or a group of similarly situated women want to organize their own Passover sacrifice and meal, they are authorized to do so.</i> but one pushes the Sabbath away for it<sup class="footnote-marker">614</sup><i class="footnote">If the 14th of Nisan is a Sabbath, the public Passover sacrifice has precedence over the Sabbath (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 6:1:1-18" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.6.1.1-18">Mishnah <i>Pesaḥim</i> 6:1</a>). Since women are biblically empowered to celebrate Passover, the rule applies also to groups of women.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: The Passover sacrifice by women and slaves<sup class="footnote-marker">615</sup><i class="footnote">Circumcised slaves are included in the group of Passover celebrants in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:44" href="/Exodus.12.44"><i>Ex</i>. 12:44</a>. The owner of uncircumcised slaves is prohibited from celebrating Passover.</i> is voluntary; certainly one does push the Sabbath away for it. What is their <i>maẓẓah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">616</sup><i class="footnote">Are women and slaves obligated to eat <i>maẓẓah</i>, a commandment triggered by the calendar?</i>? He said to him<sup class="footnote-marker">617</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eleazar (the Amora) to R. Jacob bar Aḥa.</i>, it is obligatory. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, this is in dispute. Rebbi Hila said, it is everybody’s [opinion]. A <i>baraita</i> supports the one and a <i>baraita</i> supports the other. A <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Ze‘ira: Lettuce, <i>maẓẓah</i> and Passover sacrifice are obligatory in the first night; on all other days they are voluntary<sup class="footnote-marker">618</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 2:22" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).2.22"><i>Pesaḥim</i> Tosephta 2:22</a>, Babli 91b. The Passover sacrifice is forbidden after the first night. The Tanna who holds that women are obligated for the sacrifice will hold that they are obligated to eat <i>maẓẓah</i>; he who holds that their Passover celebration is voluntary (R. Simeon in the Tosephta) must hold that their eating <i>maẓẓah</i> is voluntary.</i>. A <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Hila: It was said: “Do not eat leavened matter with it<sup class="footnote-marker">619</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 17:3" href="/Deuteronomy.17.3"><i>Deut</i>. 17:3</a>.</i>,” and it was said: “For seven days you shall eat with it <i>maẓẓot</i>, the bread of deprivation<sup class="footnote-marker">619</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 17:3" href="/Deuteronomy.17.3"><i>Deut</i>. 17:3</a>.</i>’<sup class="footnote-marker">620</sup><i class="footnote">Rules stated in the same verse must be of one and the same category.</i>.” Anybody under the prohibition of leavened matter is under the obligation to eat <i>maẓẓah</i>, and women, being under the prohibition of leavened matter, are under the obligation to eat <i>maẓẓah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">621</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, 91b, this is a statement of R. Eleazar, probably the Tanna. In <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 130, its contrapositive is a statement of R. Simeon.</i>. But did we not state: “For any positive commandment triggered by time, men are obligated but women are free”? Rebby Mana said, <sup class="footnote-marker">622</sup><i class="footnote">The other two sources add: It is more weighty since … That probably is the correct text.</i> it is a positive commandment which is implied by a prohibition<sup class="footnote-marker">623</sup><i class="footnote">And therefore follow the rules of prohibitions, obligatory for women; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:7:17" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.7.17">Note 620</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">624</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is not only in <i>Pesaḥim</i> (<b>ם</b>) but also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 4:5:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.4.5.7"><i>Ḥallah</i> 4:12, Notes 194–202</a> (<b>ח</b>) and refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 4:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.4.5.1">Mishnah <i>Ḥallah</i> 4:12</a> which states that a certain Joseph Hakohen was refused permission to celebrate the Second Passover with his wife and children.</i> Does it follow him who says the <i>Pesaḥ</i> of women is voluntary? It was stated: “A woman may make the First <i>Pesaḥ</i> by herself and the Second joining others, the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Yose says, a woman may make the Second <i>Pesaḥ</i> by herself, and certainly the First. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon says, a woman may make the First <i>Pesaḥ</i> joining others but does not make the Second.” What is the reason of Rebbi Meïr? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:3" href="/Exodus.12.3"><i>Ex</i>. 12:3</a>) “Every man a sheep for the family,” if they want “a sheep for the house.” What is the reason of Rebbi Yose, “Every man a sheep for the family,” <i>a fortiori</i> “a sheep for the house.” What is the reason of Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon? “Every man”, not woman. How do the rabbis uphold “man”? A man, not a minor. Rebbi Jonah said, even according to him who says it is an obligation, it is different here since the occasion was news, lest it become an obligation.
“Except ‘do not destroy’, ‘do not shear,<sup class="footnote-marker">556</sup><i class="footnote">Not to destroy one’s beard or shear the sideburns, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:27" href="/Leviticus.19.27"><i>Lev</i>. 19:27</a>, inappropriate for women.</i>’ and ‘do not become impure for the dead’.” Issy says, also because of “do not make a bald spot.<sup class="footnote-marker">626</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:1" href="/Deuteronomy.14.1"><i>Deut</i>. 14:1</a>: “You are children of the Eternal, your God; do not injure yourselves, do not make a bald spot between your eyes for a deceased.” The same argument in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 35b" href="/Kiddushin.35b">Babli, 35b</a>.</i>” What is the reason? “They shall not make a bald spot<sup class="footnote-marker">627</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:5" href="/Leviticus.21.5"><i>Lev</i>. 21:5</a>, speahing of Cohanim: “They may not make a bald spot on their head; the sideburn of their beard they shall not shave.”</i>.” One subject to the rules of the beard is subject to the rules of the bald spot; women who have no beard are free from the rules of the bald spot<sup class="footnote-marker">628</sup><i class="footnote">What is missing here is an argument why rules promulgated for Cohanim are valid for all Israelites; cf. <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 96; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Chapter 1 3" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Chapter_1.3"><i>Sifra Emor Pereq</i> 1(3</a>).</i>. And also from the following: “sons<sup class="footnote-marker">626</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:1" href="/Deuteronomy.14.1"><i>Deut</i>. 14:1</a>: “You are children of the Eternal, your God; do not injure yourselves, do not make a bald spot between your eyes for a deceased.” The same argument in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 35b" href="/Kiddushin.35b">Babli, 35b</a>.</i>,” not daughters. Rebbi Eleazar said, women are obligated by the rules of the bald spot.
