What is the reason? “For you are a holy people for the Eternal, your God.<sup class="footnote-marker">629</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:2" href="/Deuteronomy.14.2"><i>Deut</i>. 14:2</a>. The expression “people” applies to both men and women, even hermaphrodites and the sexless.</i>” Both men and women. How does Rebbi Eleazar explain “sons”? When they do the will of the Holy One, praise to Him, they are called sons, but when they do not do the will of the Holy One, praise to Him, they are not called sons<sup class="footnote-marker">630</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 36a" href="/Kiddushin.36a">Babli 36a</a>. In this interpretation, when Jesus insists on <i>his</i> being the Father’s son, he implies that the rest of the people do not obey God’s will.</i>. Rav was ordering his students, there came Rav Hamnuna ordering the colleagues: Order you wives not to tear out their hair when standing by a deceased, that they should not make a bald spot. What is a bald spot? There are Tannaïm who stated: Any size. There are Tannaïm who state: The size of a peeled grain. The one who says, any size, because the meaning of “bald spot” is of any size. The one who says, a peeled grain, for it is said here “to make a bald spot”, and it says there, a bald spot<sup class="footnote-marker">631</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:42" href="/Leviticus.13.42"><i>Lev</i>. 13:42</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:43" href="/Leviticus.13.43">43</a>. As with all skin diseases, the disease at a bald spot is diagnosed as such once it has reached the size of a peeled grain; Mishnah <i>Nega</i>‘<i>im</i> 4:5,6.</i>. Since “bald spot” mentioned there means in the size of a peeled grain, here also it means the size of a peeled grain. Rebbi Yose bar Mamal<sup class="footnote-marker">632</sup><i class="footnote">In the Genizah text, the rule is traced to R. Eleazar, in line with the other rules of this section.</i>: A Cohenet is permitted to leave the Land<sup class="footnote-marker">633</sup><i class="footnote">Since the “Land of the Gentiles” is impure, a Cohen is not permitted to leave the Land of Israel except in extraordinary circumstances. But the special laws for Cohanim promulgated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21" href="/Leviticus.21"><i>Lev</i>. 21</a> do not apply to their wives and daughters, who cannot serve in the Sanctuary.</i>. What is the reason? “Say to the Cohanim<sup class="footnote-marker">634</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:1" href="/Leviticus.21.1"><i>Lev</i>. 21:1</a>.</i>,” not to the Cohanot. If it were otherwise, what would we say? Since she is included in the decision<sup class="footnote-marker">635</sup><i class="footnote">It is difficult to know what this means; the word is missing in the Genizah text. Probably one should read גְּדִידָה “inflicting injury on oneself”, or in Rashi’s words <i>esgratiner</i>(<i>égratigner</i>), “to slit”, forbidden in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:1" href="/Deuteronomy.14.1"><i>Deut</i>. 14:1</a> to all of Israel.</i>, she may not leave<sup class="footnote-marker">636</sup><i class="footnote">Since the general prohibition of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:1" href="/Deuteronomy.14.1"><i>Deut</i>. 14:1</a>, applicable also to women, is also the particulat prohibition of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:5" href="/Leviticus.21.5"><i>Lev</i>. 21:5</a>, one might think that all special rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21" href="/Leviticus.21"><i>Lev</i>. 21</a> apply to everybody. Then the special position of Cohanim in rules of impurity would be abolished.</i>. If you say so, you push aside the paragraph of impurities.
<sup class="footnote-marker">637</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to Mishnah 8, speaking of the flour offering of the suspected wife. The text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:1:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.1.2-5"><i>Soṭah</i>3:1</a> (<b>ס</b>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:1-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.1-3">Notes 8–14</a>.</i>“The Cohen puts his hands under hers and performs the weave.” Does the Cohen put his hands under hers? Is that not objectionable? He brings a kerchief. Does that not separate? He brings an elderly Cohen. You may even say, a young man, since bad inclinations do not happen at that hour. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: It happens. If the suspected wife has no hands, two priests weave in her stead.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Any commandment which does not refer to the Land is obligatory both in the Land and outside the Land. But any commandment which does refer to the Land is obligatory only in the Land, except ‘<i>orlah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">638</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the commandment is formulated with a reference to the Land (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:23" href="/Leviticus.19.23"><i>Lev</i>. 19:23</a>), it has to be applied in a modified form outside the Land; cf. Mishnah <i>‘Orlah</i> 3:9.</i> and <i>kilaim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">639</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibitions of cross-breeding of animals and wearing a mixture of wool and linen (a prerogative of the priests serving in the Sanctuary) do not refer to the Land. The prohibition of <i>kilaim</i> in agriculture outside the Land is rabbinic in character.</i>. Rebbi Eliezer says, also new grain<sup class="footnote-marker">640</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibition of grain of the new harvest before the <i>‘Omer</i> sacrifice, while referring to the Land, is written in the holiday catalogue of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23" href="/Leviticus.23"><i>Lev</i>. 23</a> which is presumed not to be bound to the Land. In Mishnah <i>‘Orlah</i> 3:8, the obligation of <i>‘orlah</i> outside the Land is tradition, not Holy Writ.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Any commandment which does not refer to the Land,” etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">641</sup><i class="footnote">This entire Halakhah except the last paragraph is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 6:1:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.6.1.2-5"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 6:1 (Notes 4–20</a>); readings given by <b>שׁ</b>. The Genizah text is again indicated by G.</i> It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:1" href="/Deuteronomy.12.1"><i>Deut</i>. 12:1</a>): “These are the statutes and the rules of law which you will be required to follow in the Land.” In the Land you are required to follow them but not outside the Land. Still we say obligations depending on the Land only apply in the Land, but we might think that obligations not depending on the Land also should only apply in the Land. The verse says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 11:16-18" href="/Deuteronomy.11.16-18"><i>Deut</i>. 11:16–18</a>) “Be careful, lest your heart be seduced … and the Eternal’s rage be inflamed against you, etc. Put these words on your hearts,”. Even if you are exiled. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 11:18" href="/Deuteronomy.11.18"><i>Deut</i>. 11:18</a>) “Put these words on your hearts and your persons.” You have to say, for example <i>tefillin</i> and the study of Torah. Just as <i>tefillin</i> and the study of Torah do not depend on the Land and apply both in the Land and outside the Land, so everything not depending on the Land applies both in the Land and outside the Land.
If it is so, they should have been freed once they were redeemed. It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nehemiah 8:17" href="/Nehemiah.8.17"><i>Neh</i>. 8:17</a>): “All the congregation coming from captivity … since the days of Joshua bin Nun.” Why Joshua? Rebbi Hillel, the son of Samuel ben Naḥmani: The verse damaged the reputation of the just person in his grave for the reputation of the just person in his time; it compares their coming in the days of Ezra to the coming in the days of Joshua. As at the coming in the days of Joshua they had been free and became obligated, also at the coming in the days of Ezra they had been free and became obligated. How did they become obligated? Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, they became obligated by the words of the Torah; that is what is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 30:5" href="/Deuteronomy.30.5"><i>Deut</i>. 30:5</a>): “The Eternal, your God, will bring you to the Land that your fathers inherited and you will inherit it.” Just as the inheritance of your fathers was by the words of the Torah, so your inheritance is by the words of the Torah. “He will be good to you and give increase to you more than to your forefathers.” Your fathers had been free and became obligated, also you had been free and became obligated. On your fathers there was no yoke of government, but you [are obligated] even though there is on you the yoke of government. Your fathers did become obligated only after fourteen years, seven they conquered and seven they distributed, but you became obligated [immediately when you entered. Your fathers did become obligated only after they had acquired all, but you become obligated]<sup class="footnote-marker">642</sup><i class="footnote">The Genizah text shows that this argument from <i>Ševi‘it</i> belongs to the text here also. The text here is an abbreviation of the original from <i>Ševi‘it</i>.</i> for every single piece at the moment you acquire it.
Rebbi Eleazar said, they accepted the tithes voluntarily. What is the reason? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nehemiah 10:1" href="/Nehemiah.10.1"><i>Neh</i>. 10:1</a>) “In view of all this, we execute a contract and sign.” How does Rebbi Eleazar uphold (10:37): “The first-born of our cattle and flock”? Since they accepted upon themselves matters they were not obligated for, even matters they were obligated for the Omnipresent credited to them as if they were accepted voluntarily.
How does Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina explain “In view of all this, we execute a contract and sign”? Since they accepted [the obligations] with good grace, the verse credits it to them as if they had accepted it voluntarily. How does Rebbi Eleazar explain “more than your forefathers?” He explains it for the future since Rebbi Ḥelbo, Simeon bar Abba said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Your forefathers inherited the land of seven peoples, but you will in the future inherit the land of ten peoples. The three others are (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 15:19" href="/Genesis.15.19"><i>Gen</i>. 15:19</a>) “the Qenites, the Qenizites and the Qadmonites.” Rebbi Jehudah said, [these are] Arabia, Salmaia and Nabataea. Rebbi Simeon says, Asia, Spain, and Damascus. Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob says, Asia, Cartagena, and Thrace. Rebbi says, Edom, Moab, and the best of Ammon. “More than your forefathers”: Your forefathers, even though they were redeemed, were later subjugated again. But you when you are redeemed will not be subjugated again. What is the reason? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 30:6" href="/Jeremiah.30.6"><i>Jer</i>. 30:6</a>) “Inquire and see whether a male gives birth.” Just as a male cannot give birth so you, after you are redeemed, will not be subjugated again.
<sup class="footnote-marker">643</sup><i class="footnote">This text is from ‘<i>Orlah</i> 3:8 (62b, line 37) (<b>ל</b>); Notes 158–160.</i>“Rebbi Eliezer says, also new grain.” What is the reason of Rebbi Eliezer? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:14" href="/Leviticus.23.14"><i>Lev</i>. 23:14</a>) “In all your dwelling places,” both inside and outside the Land. How do the rabbis explain the reason of Rebbi Eliezer, “in all your dwelling places”? New grain from here which was exported. Rebbi Jonah asked: Why did we not state <i>ḥallah</i> with these? Rebbi Yose said to him, our Mishnah only deals with something which applies to Israel and the Gentiles. But <i>ḥallah</i> only applies to Israel, not to Gentiles. What is the reason? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:20" href="/Numbers.15.20"><i>Num</i>. 15:20</a>): The first of <i>your</i> dough,” not of Gentiles.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Everybody who keeps one commandment they<sup class="footnote-marker">644</sup><i class="footnote">The Heavenly court.</i> treat well and prolong his life; he will inherit the Land<sup class="footnote-marker">645</sup><i class="footnote">This probably is the Future World, in Psalms called “the Lands of Life.”</i>. But everybody who does not keep one commandment they do not treat well, do not prolong his life, and he does not inherit the Land. <sup class="footnote-marker">646</sup><i class="footnote">Of this part of the Mishnah there exist versions widely differing in text but identical in meaning.</i> Anybody who knows neither Scripture nor Mishnah nor a trade<sup class="footnote-marker">647</sup><i class="footnote">Which lets him earn an honest living.</i> is not part of civilization, but about one who holds on to these three, the verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">648</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 4:12" href="/Ecclesiastes.4.12"><i>Eccl</i>. 4:12</a>. The person studying Scripture and tradition, and earning an honest living, will not easily sin. This formulation is that of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kiddushin (Lieberman) 1:17" href="/Tosefta_Kiddushin_(Lieberman).1.17">Tosephta, 1:17</a>.</i>: “The triple cord will not easily break.”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Everybody who keeps one commandment they treat well,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">649</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Makkot 3:12:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Makkot.3.12.1">Mishnah <i>Makkot</i> 3:17</a>.</i>“Therefore anybody who is passive and refrains from sinning they reward like one who fulfills a commandment,” and you say so<sup class="footnote-marker">650</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Makkot</i> contradicts the Mishnah here since there one is rewarded for not sinning while here one is only rewarded for doing good.</i>? But we hold with one who is 50–50<sup class="footnote-marker">651</sup><i class="footnote">In the Heavenly accounting, he collected as many merits as demerits. If he now collects one merit, he is one of the Just and takes his place in Paradise; if he sins once he is of the sinners and has lost his share in the Future Life. If he does not do anything, his fate remains suspended; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 39b" href="/Kiddushin.39b">Babli 39b</a>.</i>. If he keeps one commandment, they treat him well and prolong his life; he will inherit the Land. But if he commits one sin, they do not treat him well, do not prolong his life, and he does not inherit the Land. There<sup class="footnote-marker">649</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Makkot 3:12:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Makkot.3.12.1">Mishnah <i>Makkot</i> 3:17</a>.</i>, we have stated: “Therefore anybody who is passive and refrains from sinning they reward like one who fulfills a commandement.” Rebbi Ze‘ira said, one who had occasion to commit a possible sin and did not act<sup class="footnote-marker">652</sup><i class="footnote">He refrained from acting out of religious scruple when there was a possibility that the intended act was permitted because possibly it was forbidden. His inaction is a very meritorious act.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, one who chose one commandment which he never violated in his lifetime. What kind? Mar Uqban said, for example the honor of father and mother<sup class="footnote-marker">653</sup><i class="footnote">Which is extremely difficult to keep at all times; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:7:2-20" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.7.2-20">Halakhah 7</a>.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, “Happy are those on a straight path, who walk in the Eternal’s Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">654</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 119:1" href="/Psalms.119.1"><i>Ps</i>. 119:1</a>. If they walk in the Eternal’s Torah, they certainly are on the right path. The verse can only mean that somebody choosing the right path, even if he is far from being perfect, is considered walking totally in the Eternal’s Torah. This gives a biblical source for the Mishnah.</i>,” as if they did walk in the Eternal’s Torah. Rebbi Abun said, “Also they did no evil, in His path they went,” as if they went in His path<sup class="footnote-marker">655</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 119:3" href="/Psalms.119.3"><i>Ps</i>. 119:3</a>. In order to be counted with those who walk in the Eternal’s way it is enough to refrain from evil; this explains the Mishnah in <i>Makkot</i>.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, what is written? “<sup class="footnote-marker">656</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 1:1" href="/Psalms.1.1"><i>Ps</i>. 1:1</a>. The argument is better in the Genizah text: “It does not say, ‘Happy the man who walks in the council of the just,’ but …”</i> Happy the man who did not walk in the council of the wicked;” since he did not walk in the council of the wicked it is as if he walked in the council of the just.
Ben Azai preached: “A dead fly will permanently spoil perfumer’s oil.<sup class="footnote-marker">658</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 10:1" href="/Ecclesiastes.10.1"><i>Eccl</i>. 10:1</a>; the translation follows Ibn Ezra.</i>” Does not one dead fly spoil perfumer’s oil? And this person<sup class="footnote-marker">659</sup><i class="footnote">Who has exactly as many merits as he has demerits. The Babylonian sources, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 40b" href="/Kiddushin.40b">Babli 40b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kiddushin (Lieberman) 1:14" href="/Tosefta_Kiddushin_(Lieberman).1.14">Tosephta 1:14</a>, base a formally similar agument on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 9:18" href="/Ecclesiastes.9.18"><i>Eccl</i>. 9:18</a>; but the Babli does not make the difference between the Worlds that are the main thrust of this paragraph.</i> by one sin spoiled all merits he had. Rebbi Aqiba preached: “<sup class="footnote-marker">660</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Is</i>.. 5:14.</i> So the abyss opened wide and its mouth swallowed the one without law.” It does not say “without laws”, but “without law”, him who has not a single merit that should help him on the scale of merit<sup class="footnote-marker">661</sup><i class="footnote">The image is always that the deeds of a person are weighed on scales in Heavenly court; the person is found innocent if the scale containing his merits tilts the balance in his favor. A different interpretation is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 111a" href="/Sanhedrin.111a">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 111a</a>.</i>. That is, for the Future World. But in this world, even if 999 angels plead for his conviction and one angel pleads for his acquittal, the Holy One, praise to Him, lends His weight to the scale of merit. What is the reason? “If there is for him one angel out of a thousand, to tell a man his straightness. He will act with him in grace and say, let him be redeemed, not go down to destruction; I found weregilt<sup class="footnote-marker">662</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 33:23-24" href="/Job.33.23-24"><i>Job</i> 33:23–24</a>.</i>.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, if you find a saying of Rebbi Eliezer, son of Rebbi Yose the Galilean, bend your ear like this funnel and listen, since Rebbi Joḥanan reported that Rebbi Eliezer, the son of Rebbi Yose the Galilean, said: Even if 999 angles plead for his conviction and one angel pleads for his acquittal, the Holy One, praise to Him, lends His weight to the scale of merit. Not only this entire angel, but if 999 aspects of that angel plead for conviction and one aspect for acquittal, the Holy One, praise to Him, lends His weight to the scale of merit. What is the reason? It does not say, “if there is for him one angel of a thousand,” but “one <i>out</i> of a thousand”, one aspect out of a thousand aspects of that angel. What is written afterwards? “He will act with him in grace and say, let him be hurt, not go down to destruction; I found weregilt.” “Let him be hurt,” by suffering<sup class="footnote-marker">663</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eliah Wilna points out that the Talmud reads פְּדָעֵהוּ as Aramaism, equivalent to Hebrew פְּצָעֵהוּ (cf. Onqelos and Pseudo-Jonathan to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:25" href="/Exodus.21.25"><i>Ex</i>. 21:25</a>).</i>. “I found weregilt,” he found means to redeem himself. That is, in this world. But in the Future World, if he has a majority of merits, he inherits paradise; a majority of sins, he inherits hell. If he was 50–50. Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, “he carries his iniquity.<sup class="footnote-marker">664</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:7" href="/Exodus.34.7"><i>Ex</i>. 34:7</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Micah 7:18" href="/Micah.7.18"><i>Micah</i> 7:18</a>. R. Yose ben R. Ḥanina holds that the person with an equal number of merits and demerits is found guilty. But in <i>Peah</i>1:1, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 10:1:2-26" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.10.1.2-26"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 10:1</a>, and the Babli, <i>Roš Haššanah</i>17a, R. Yose ben R. Ḥanina is credited with the opinion attributed here to R. Abbahu, who is not mentioned in the other sources. Everybody seems to agree that in the Heavenly Court, no more than one transgression will be forgiven.</i>” Rebbi Abbahu said, it is written: “He carries.” What does the Holy One, praise to Him, do? He grabs one of his demerits, and the merits decide<sup class="footnote-marker">665</sup><i class="footnote">R. Abbahu holds that the person with an equal number of merits and demerits is found not guilty.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">666</sup><i class="footnote">Babli <i>Roš Haššanah</i> 17b.</i> Rebbi Eleazar said: “You, Eternal, are acting in grace, for You will complete for everybody according to his deeds.<sup class="footnote-marker">667</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 62:13" href="/Psalms.62.13"><i>Ps</i>. 62:13</a>.</i>” And if he has none, You will give him from Yours. That is Rebbi Eleazar’s opinion, for Rebbi Eleazar said, “You, Eternal, are acting in grace,” this teaches that He turns to the aspect of grace<sup class="footnote-marker">668</sup><i class="footnote">He also holds that the person with an equal number of merits and demerits is found not guilty.</i>.
Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">669</sup><i class="footnote">For chronological reasons, this title from פן is the only correct one.</i> Jeremiah said that Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac asked about: “Justice watches over the one ambling in simplicity but evil falsifies sin<sup class="footnote-marker">670</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 13:6" href="/Proverbs.13.6"><i>Prov</i>. 13:6</a>. This and the following verses seem to imply that a person is just not by his own merit but only by God’s grace, whereas the wicked are destined to be wicked. This kind of predestination certainly contradicts the doctrine of personal responsibility which rabbinic interpretation finds in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 11:26-28" href="/Deuteronomy.11.26-28"><i>Deut</i>. 11:26–28</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 30:15-20" href="/Deuteronomy.30.15-20">30:15–20</a>.</i>.” “Evil pursues the sinners but He well rewards the just.”<sup class="footnote-marker">670</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 13:21" href="/Proverbs.13.21"><i>Prov</i>. 13:21</a>.</i> “He watches over the feet of His pious, but the wicked will be silenced in darkness, etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">671</sup><i class="footnote"><i>1S</i>. 2:9. This and the following verses are not in the Genizah fragment. In <i>Peah</i>, the order of the verses is slightly different.</i>” “If He scoffs with the scoffers, but to the meek He gives kindness.<sup class="footnote-marker">672</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 3:34" href="/Proverbs.3.34"><i>Prov</i>. 3:34</a>.</i>” “Sages inherit honor, but shame lifts the silly.<sup class="footnote-marker">673</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 3:35" href="/Proverbs.3.35"><i>Prov</i>. 3:35</a>.</i>” Fencing makes a fence, and doors entry<sup class="footnote-marker">674</sup><i class="footnote">A popular adage to the effect that a good fence warns away potential trespassers but an open door invites thieves. If the just are fenced in from the start but the wicked left open to sin, there is no personal merit in good behavior.</i>. And so it is, fencing makes a fence and a door entry. But Rebbi Jeremiah asked in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac: If somebody guards himself once, twice, and three times from a sin, from then on the Holy One, praise to Him, will guard him<sup class="footnote-marker">675</sup><i class="footnote">If a person from personal initiative guards himself a few times from sinning in a particular way, then it becomes an automatic habit and in the future will not require additional will power. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 86b" href="/Yoma.86b">Babli, <i>Yoma</i> 86b</a>, the Tanna R. Yose ben R. Jehudah is quoted as inferring that the same kind of sin is forgiven by the Heavenly Court a maximum of three times. <i>Šabbat</i> 104a, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 38b" href="/Yoma.38b"><i>Yoma</i> 38b</a>, R. Simeon ben Laqish quotes <a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 3:34" href="/Proverbs.3.34"><i>Prov</i>. 3:34</a> to the effect that Heaven will make it easy for the just to be just, for the wicked to be wicked.</i>. What is the reason? “Lo, all this God will work, twice or three times with a man.”
Rebbi Zeïra said, but only if the person does not revert<sup class="footnote-marker">678</sup><i class="footnote">Here begins the discussion of the quote at the end of the Mishnah</i>. What is the reason? It does not say “The triple thread will never snap” but rather “The triple thread will not quickly snap.” If you work on it, it will split. Rebbi Ḥuna said in the name of Rebbi Abbahu: There is no forgetting before the Holy One, praise to Him, but for Israel He becomes forgetful. What is the reason? It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">664</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:7" href="/Exodus.34.7"><i>Ex</i>. 34:7</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Micah 7:18" href="/Micah.7.18"><i>Micah</i> 7:18</a>. R. Yose ben R. Ḥanina holds that the person with an equal number of merits and demerits is found guilty. But in <i>Peah</i>1:1, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 10:1:2-26" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.10.1.2-26"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 10:1</a>, and the Babli, <i>Roš Haššanah</i>17a, R. Yose ben R. Ḥanina is credited with the opinion attributed here to R. Abbahu, who is not mentioned in the other sources. Everybody seems to agree that in the Heavenly Court, no more than one transgression will be forgiven.</i> “He <i>forgets</i><sup class="footnote-marker">679</sup><i class="footnote">Replacing נשׂא “to lift, carry” by נשׁה “to forget”.</i> sin.” And so David says<sup class="footnote-marker">680</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 85:3" href="/Psalms.85.3"><i>Ps</i>. 85:3</a>.</i>: “You <i>forgot</i> Your people’s sin, You covered up all their misdeeds, Selah.”

Chapter 2



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A man may contract a premininary marriage either in person or through an agent<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Since the groom is not permitted to live with the bride before the definitive marriage, the preliminary marriage is a legal, not a personal, act and may be executed either by the principal or by his duly appointed representative. For the same reason, a woman may appoint a representative to agree on her behalf to the stipulations proposed by the groom.</i>, and a woman may be taken in preliminary marriage either in person or through an agent. A man may give away his adolescent daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Who still can be married off by her father without necessarily being asked, while she is able to act in law in other respects; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:2:13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.2.13">Chapter 1, Note 228</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:2:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.2.10"><i>Yebamot</i> 1:2, Note 159</a>.</i> in preliminary marriage himself or through his agent. If a man say to a woman: Be married to me preliminarily by this date fruit, be preliminarily married by that one, she is preliminarily married if one of them was worth a <i>peruṭah</i>, otherwise she is not preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Two separate gifts of less than a <i>peruṭah</i> each do not add up to one gift of a <i>peruṭah</i>.</i>. By this and by that one, she is preliminarily married if together they are worth a <i>peruṭah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">If the entire gift was given, or at least promised, as a whole, it does not matter of how many individual pieces it is composed.</i>, but if she was eating immediately<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">If the dates were not promised as a bunch but given as “this and this and this” and the first date was already eaten by the time the second was received, they cannot be counted together since a nonexisting object cannot be counted together with an existing one. But if ten dates were promised at the outset in so many words and the prospective bride ate each one as soon as she received it, she is preliminarily married as soon as together they had represented a <i>peruṭah’</i> s worth.</i> what she received, she is not preliminarily married unless one of them was worth a <i>peruṭah</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A man may contract a premininary marriage either in person or through an agent,” etc. From where that a person’s agent may act in his stead<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Since preliminary marriage is an act prescribed by biblical standards, one has to ascertain that agency is admissible in the fulfilment of biblical decrees. {Agency always is direct representation as in Egyptian and Roman law (cf. R. Taubenschlag, <i>The Law of Greco-Roman Egypt in the Light of the Papyri</i>, New York 1944, §37). Indirect representation is authorized only if it unquestionably is to the benefit of the person represented; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 1:5:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.1.5.2-9"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 1:6</a>, Notel74.}</i>? Rebbi Eleazar said: “The entirety of the assembly of the congregation of Israel shall slaughter it in the evening.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:6" href="/Exodus.12.6"><i>Ex</i>. 12:6</a>.</i>” But do all of them slaughter<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Passover sacrifice is not an individual but a family affair, it is obvious that only one person can slaughter for his entire family.</i>? Does not one slaughter for the many? From here [it follows that] a person’s agent may act in his stead<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mekhilta Bo</i>,<i>Masekhet Depisḥa</i> Chap. 5 (ed. Horovitz-Rabin, p. 17) as tannaïtic statement; in the Babli attributed to R. Joshua ben Qorḥa. The parallel discussion in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 41a-42a" href="/Kiddushin.41a-42a">Babli, 41a–42a</a>, is a long-winded, possibly Saboraic, affair.</i>. And that would be the same as: “Everybody should take for himself a sheep for the family, a sheep to a house<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:3" href="/Exodus.12.3"><i>Ex</i>. 12:3</a>. The same argument applies as for the other verse. Then the question arises, at least for the school of R. Aqiba, why the same rule has to be expressed by two separate verses. This contradicts R. Aqiba’s axiom that the Torah text admits of no redundancies.</i>.” Did everyone take? Did not one take for the many<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Mekhilta Bo</i>,<i>Masekhet Depisḥa</i> Chap. 3 (ed. Horovitz-Rabin, p. 11) this also is a tannaïtic statement, not the subject of a discussion.</i>? From here [it follows that] a person’s agent may act in his stead. Rebbi Yose said, there is a difference, for a person may slaughter the Passover sacrifice of another without the latter’s knowledge<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The people participating in the Passover meal do not have to know who prepared it. Therefore, the slaughterer does not have to be the agent of every participant; the verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:6" href="/Exodus.12.6"><i>Ex</i>. 12:6</a> does not prove the thesis for which it was quoted.</i>. If you wanted to say that a person may separate the Passover sacrifice for another without the latter’s knowledge, you cannot say so, for Rebbi Ze‘ira said in the name of Rebbi Eleazar that a person may slaughter the Passover sacrifice of another without the latter’s knowledge, but nobody may separate the Passover sacrifice for another without the latter’s knowledge<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 8:1 (35d 1. 13), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 36a" href="/Nedarim.36a">Babli <i>Nedarim</i> 36a</a>. A person who was not inscribed beforehand in the list of participants cannot join the Passover celebration; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 5:8" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.5.8">Mishnah <i>Zebaḥim</i> 5:8</a>. Only <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:3" href="/Exodus.12.3"><i>Ex</i>. 12:3</a> is a proof; there is no redundancy.</i>.
The House of Shammai say, an agent may not testify as witness. But the House of Hillel say, an agent may testify as witness<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 43a" href="/Kiddushin.43a">Babli, 43a</a>, the Tannaïtic tradition is declared to be unclear. The problem is that if an agent acts instead of the principal, he should be disqualified from acting as a witness if the principal is unable to testify. Since relatives are disqualified as witnesses and everybody is a relative of himself, an agent should be disqualified in any suit involving the principal’s money.</i>. How is that? If he sent two<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">In the quote of this <i>baraita</i> in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 43a" href="/Kiddushin.43a">Babli, 43a</a>, “three”. <i>Qorban Ha</i>‘<i>edah</i> reads the statement as “two [in addition to the agent]”.</i>, in the opinion of the House of Shammai there is an agent and two witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Only one of the three may be appointed as agent to reserve the option of proving a case in court by witnesses.</i>. In the opinion of the House of Hillel, all three can be agents and all three can be witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">At the same time agents and witnesses.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said, the matter is reasonable if the preliminary marriage was executed by document. But if he executed the preliminary marriage by money, he became an interested party in his testimony<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">If he did not deliver the money to the prospective bride, he would have to pay back the consignor; his testimony is self-serving and therefore barred.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, since the Torah made him trustworthy<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">All commentators have great difficulty with this statement, from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Kiddushin 43b:1:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Kiddushin.43b.1.1"><i>Tosaphot</i> (43b <i>s. v.</i> וכן</a>) and Rashba (Novellae, 43a <i>s. v.</i> ירושלמי) to the commentators of Maimonides (<i>ad Iššut</i>3:16) and the standard commentators of the Yerushalmi (שיירי קרבן and מראה פנים <i>ad loc</i>.). In fact, R. Yose’s remark is incompatible with the Babli’s treatment of the subject in 43a/b. R. Yose seems to argue that the monetary aspect is irrelevant. The main consequence of preliminary marriage is to forbid the woman to any man other than her fiancé; this is not a matter of civil law. It is a principle in both Talmudim that in matters of prohibitions a single witness has standing by biblical law (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 6:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.6.2.3"><i>Soṭah</i> 6:2, Note 26</a>). Since the agent has standing in biblical law to force her to go to court if she wants to marry any other man, he also has standing to join another witness in the related financial case.</i>, even if he executed the preliminary marriage by money, he did not become an interested party in his testimony. Rebbi Abba said, there came a case before Rav who admitted the agent as witness<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Since the normal case is preliminary marriage by money, it must be assumed that the case before Rav was of this kind. Since Rav was the foremost authority in Babylonia, his decision was obligatory precedent in Babylonia.</i>. A person deposited his amphoras in another’s corral; [the latter] reneged<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">He said that all amphoras in his corral were his own property. This is a purely financial case, where R. Yose’s argument is irrelevant.</i>. The case came before Rebbi Jeremiah who admitted the agent as witness and made him swear [by the testimony of] the porter<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The person hired to deliver the amphoras to the corral was admitted as witness in court, even though his testimony was partly self-serving since he would have been liable as a thief had he failed to deliver the amphoras.<br>Since R. Jeremiah had a low opinion of everything Babylonian, it follows that the rule admitting an agent as witness is accepted both in Babylonia and in Galilee.</i>.
Some <i>baraita</i> implies that the agent of a person acts in his stead, and some <i>baraita</i> implies that the agent of a person does not act in his stead<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Everybody agrees that there exist situations in which an agent acts with the full authority of a principal and others where only the person himself can act. The question is, what is the normal case and what is the exception?</i>. “If he definitively will substitute<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">This seems to be a quote from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:10" href="/Leviticus.27.10"><i>Lev</i>. 27:10</a>, but there the masoretic text is הָמֵר יָמִיר. If the word יְמִירֶנּוּ is not a slip of the scribe’s pen, the reference might be to יַחֲליפֶנּוּ (“he himself may exchange it”) in the same verse. There is no explicit <i>baraita</i> in rabbinic literature which would invalidate substitution by agent; the double expression is always interpreted as an addition in R. Aqiba’s system. In <i>Sifra Beḥuqqotay Pereq</i> 9(6), the double expression is interpreted to include a woman for her own sacrifice and an heir for an inherited one. Since the agent is not mentioned, he is excluded. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 2a" href="/Temurah.2a">Babli, <i>Temurah</i> 2a</a>, the statement of <i>Sifra</i> is characterized as R. Meïr’s.</i>, if he himself will dissolve<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:14" href="/Numbers.30.14"><i>Num</i>. 30:14</a>. This does not refer to an infinitive construction but to the use of the word יְפֵרֶנּוּ instead of the simple יָפֵר. In R. Aqiba’s system, suffixes always carry a special meaning. The argument is explicit in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 12b" href="/Nazir.12b">Babli, <i>Nazir</i> 12b</a>, where the Tanna R. Joshia quotes <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:14" href="/Numbers.30.14"><i>Num</i>. 30:14</a> to prove that a husband cannot delegate his power over his wife’s vows to an attorney. R. Jonathan holds that an agent always can act for his principal.</i>.” We may hold that a person’s agent cannot act in his stead because Scripture excluded him. “He shall lean his hand,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:4" href="/Leviticus.1.4"><i>Lev</i>. 1:4</a>;<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:2:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.2.2-7">3:2</a>,8,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:11:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.11.2">13</a>;4:24,29,33, a necessary action to validate a sacrifice. The repetition of <i>his hand</i> in all these verses is taken in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 93b" href="/Menachot.93b">Babli, <i>Menaḥot</i>93b</a>, as proof that any agency is impossible for animal sacrifices.</i>” not the hand of his son nor the hand of his slave nor the hand of his agent<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">This formulation is in <i>Sifra Wayyiqra Pereq</i> 4(2).</i>. We may hold that a person’s agent can act in his stead but [in this case] Scripture excluded him. “His master shall pierce his ear with an awl<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:6" href="/Exodus.21.6"><i>Ex</i>. 21:6</a>, speaking of the Hebrew slave; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:2:2-33" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.2.2-33">Chapter 1:2</a>.</i>;” “his master” but not the latter’s son, “his master” but not the latter’s agent<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Ismael</i>,<i>Neziqin</i> 2 (ed. Horovitz-Rabin p. 253), <i>dR</i>.<i>Simeon ben Ioḥai Mišpaṭim</i> 6:6.</i>. We may hold that a person’s agent can act in his stead but Scripture excluded him. Some Tannaïm state: “He shall pierce”, to include the agent. This follows Rebbi Aqiba. Following Rebbi Ismael? “His master”, anybody acting on his master’s authority<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">This opinion is not found in any parallel source.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">From <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.2.3"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 6:2, Notes 55–69</a>. Variant readings are given there.</i> We have stated: “A preliminarily married adolescent girl, or her father, can accept her bill of divorce.” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, like the disagreement about divorce is the disagreement about preliminary marriage. Rebbi Joḥanan said, everybody agrees about preliminary marriage that her father contracts but not she herself. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish agrees about a definitive marriage that she is not empowered to marry herself off and to let her father lose her earnings. In the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan she has no legal standing relative to her father and cannot appoint an agent. In the opinion of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish she does have legal standing relative to her father and can appoint an agent. A Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan: “A man contracts a preliminary marriage either in person or through his agent and a woman contracts a preliminary marriage either in person or through her agent.” He explains it about an adult woman. But did we not state: “If an underage girl said, accept the bill of divorce for me, it is no valid bill of divorce until it reaches her hand.” He explains it about an orphan. But did we not state: “If her father said to [an agent]: Go and receive my daughter’s bill of divorce, if [the husband] wants to retract he cannot retract.” He explains that the Mishnah deals with cases; the first part about an orphan and the second part if she has a father. A Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. “A man may contract a preliminary marriage for his adolescent daughter either by himself or by his agent.” He explains that following Rebbi Jehudah, since Rebbi Jehudah said, “no two hands can acquire together.” Rebbi Yose said, a rabbi came out from the assembly and said, a case was decided following Rebbi Joḥanan. Can one rely on that? It was no question of relying on him since it was as from the sea to the frying pan.
Rav Hamnuna in the name of Rebbi Assi<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The Babylonian, Rav Assi in the Babli.</i>: If an underage girl married herself off preliminarily with two pubic hairs<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">This statement seems to be a contradiction in terms. The definition of an underage girl is a female who does not yet have two pubic hairs. The interpretation can be either that she contracted the marriage when she was underage but now, when the case comes to court, she already became an adolescent (R. Moses Margalit) or, preferably, that she is an adolescent (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:2:13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.2.13">Chapter 1, Note 228</a>) who, while legally responsible, still is under her father’s authority in marital matters (R. David Fraenckel). In either case it is presumed that the father was not informed of the preliminary marriage at the time it was contracted.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 46a" href="/Kiddushin.46a">Babli, 46a</a>, Rav Assi’s statement is that only the father has the right to annul her marriage but not she herself, since she unlawfully arrogated to herself the rights of an adult. In the Babli, practice is decided following Rav that both her father and herself have the right to unilaterally terminate the irregular preliminary marriage.</i>, her father repudiates the marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Repudiation of a marriage is possible only for an orphan girl who was married off by her mother or brothers, who can walk out of a marriage by a simple declaration as long as she is formally underage, i. e., did not grow two pubic hairs (<i>Yebamot</i> Chapter 13). In the present case, the expression “repudiates” would seem to be inappropriate but it is justified in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 46a" href="/Kiddushin.46a">Babli (46a</a>) by reference to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:16" href="/Exodus.22.16"><i>Ex</i>. 22:16</a> which uses the verb מאן to describe the father’s veto of the marriage of his daughter to her seducer.</i> but not she herself. This follows neither Rebbi Joḥanan nor Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. If following Rebbi Joḥanan, there is no place for repudiation<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Since only the father has the right to contract the marriage of his underage or adolescent daughter, there is no marriage and the transaction does not have to be nullified.</i>. If following Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, the marriage is valid by biblical standards<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since he holds that there is an opinion which gives the adolescent girl the right to marry on her own, he would have to require a valid bill of divorce to dissolve the marriage.</i>. It follows only Rebbi Joḥanan. Even though Rebbi Joḥanan says that everybody agrees about preliminary marriage that her father contracts but not she herself, he agrees in this case that the father repudiates on her behalf<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan agrees that the marriage contracted by an adolescent on her own is valid if it is not countermanded by her father. She herself cannot walk out of a union contracted by herself as an adolescent. Since this statement cannot hold for a marriage contracted by an underage girl without her father’s knowledge, it seems that the interpretation of R. Moses Margalit cannot be sustained.</i>.
Rebbi Abba bar Cahana and Rebbi Jacob bar Idi in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: If she<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">An underage or adolescent girl.</i> was preliminarily married with agreement<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The father’s agreement.</i> but definitively married without agreement: there was such a case and the father came and removed her from the bridal chamber<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Even an adolescent is emancipated from her father’s power only by definitive marriage, not by the preliminary. Therefore, she cannot contract the definitive marriage on her own.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">This name probably is corrupt.</i>: He<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">R. Abba bar Cahana justified himself for quoting R. Joshua ben Levi whom he could not have known by quoting an attached lemma to the effect that the latter ruled differently from what R. Simeon ben Laqish would expound a generation later.</i> had an indication that Rebbi Joshua ben Levi disagreed with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. But this does not disagree. Just as Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said there<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Two paragraphs earlier.</i>, she is not empowered to marry herself off and let her father lose her earnings, so Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said here, she is not empowered to marry herself off and let her father lose her earnings<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Her earnings are her father’s before and her husband’s after definitive marriage. Therefore, it is understandable that the father will want to give his adolescent daughter in preliminary marriage, for which he is paid, but refuse to let her enter definitive marriage before she reaches adulthood and he anyhow loses the right to her earnings.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Cahana and Rebbi Jacob bar Idi came in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: If she was preliminarily married without agreement and was being definitively married without agreement: there was such a case and the father came and annulled her preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Then automatically the definitive marriage cannot proceed.</i>.
If she<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">An underage or adolescent girl.</i> was preliminarily married with agreement<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The father’s agreement.</i> but definitively married without agreement, may she eat heave<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">If the bride was an Israel but the groom a Cohen. The wife of a Cohen is a member of his family and as such entitled to eat heave (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:11" href="/Numbers.18.11"><i>Num</i>. 18:11</a>).</i>? Rav said, she does eat; but Samuel said, she may not eat. Rebbi Mana said, Rav’s reason is that if she was preliminarily married with agreement, the permanence of the status quo implies the assumption that he definitively married her with agreement<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The father’s agreement to the preliminary marriage implies his acquiescence in a definitive marriage unless he explicitly objects. A definitive marriage without consent or objection by the father is marriage with consent. The treatment of this topic in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 45b" href="/Kiddushin.45b">Babli, 45b</a>, is not comparable to the argument here since the Babli distinguishes between a marriage in the presence of the father and one contracted when the father was on a far away trip. The Babli notes that Rav in a real case followed Samuel’s opinion, attributed in the Babli to Rav Assi.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, Rav’s reason is that by the earlier Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Only the preliminary marriage is an act of acquisition which brings the bride into the husband’s family; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.3.1"><i>Ketubot</i> 5:3, Note 66</a>. The denial of heave to the bride before the definitive marriage is a later rabbinic institution; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.4.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 5:4</a>.</i>, a preliminarily married daughter of an Israel may eat heave<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">By biblical standards, definitive marriage is irrelevant for the bride to be able to eat heave.</i>. What is the difference between them? Her earnings. According to Rebbi Mana, for Rav her earnings belong to her husband<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Since the earnings are the husband’s from the moment of definitive marriage which for R. Mana is valid unless vetoed explicitly.</i>; according to Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun, for Rav her earnings belong to her father<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">One would have to say that the father might have the right to the earnings, since R. Yose ben R. Abun does not say that the definitive marriage is invalid, only that its status is irrelevant.</i>. If she should die, who inherits from her? According to Rebbi Mana, for Rav her husband inherits; according to Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun, for Rav her father inherits if she dies<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">For the wife’s estate, the same rules apply as for the wife’s earnings.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun stated, for Rav her husband inherits if she dies<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The prior speculation about the difference between the two arguments attributed to Rav is false; R. Yose ben R. Abun also will agree that the definitive marriage is valid unless explicitly vetoed by the father. He only stated that the problem of validity of the definitive marriage is irrelevant for the problem of heave.</i>. If she was preliminarily married without agreement and definitively married without agreement, may she eat heave? Samuel said, she does eat<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 45b" href="/Kiddushin.45b">Babli, 45b</a>, this is attributed to Rav Huna.</i>. Come and see: If she was preliminarily married with agreement and definitively married without agreement, Samuel says that she may not eat; here he says she may eat? Samuel treats her as an orphan<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Since everything was done without consulting the father, one may assume that the latter would have indicated his disapproval had he disapproved. His silence therefore is considered as formal approval and the underage girl is emancipated from him; she becomes “an orphan during the father’s lifetime” (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.1.3"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 6:1, Note 20</a>). The same argument is given in the Babli.</i>.
Mishnah: “Be married to me preliminarily by this date fruit,” etc. The Mishnah [presents the case] that she said, I cannot accept this one, try that one; I cannot accept this one, try that one<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">In this interpretation, if the man said: Be preliminarily married to me by this, by that one, etc., she would be married if all dates together were worth a <i>peruṭah</i>. If the preliminary marriage must be mentioned every time, it must be that the proposal was rejected in between. Then not <i>any</i> date but specifically the last one must be worth a <i>peruṭah</i>.</i>. “By this and by that one and by that one.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The triple mention is the version of the Mishnah in the Babli and most independent Mishnah mss.</i>” Who is the Tanna of <i>Wawim</i>? Rebbi Jehudah. But for Rebbi Meïr, either this or this or that one<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The positions of Rebbis Jehudah and Meïr about the interpretation of conjunctions in multiple statements are discussed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.6.2"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 9:7, Notes 93–101</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Jeremiah<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Jeremiah, the colleague of Rav.</i> in the name of Rav: “ ‘Be preliminarily married to me by this tetradrachma after thirty [days].’ Even if she spent it within the thirty [days], she is preliminarily married; also Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Whose opinion will be discussed later in this paragraph.</i> will agree to this.” What is the difference between this and a loan<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">If the creditor forgives a loan to his prospective bride, she receives real value. Nevertheless, RR. Meïr and Simeon ben Eleazar hold that there is no marriage if nothing of the original loan is left in the fiancée’s hand. But if the money was given specifically for preliminary marriage, there is no restriction on its use. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 59a" href="/Kiddushin.59a">Babli agrees, 59a</a>, in the names of both Rav and Samuel. The Babli raises the additional problem, whether the woman may have second thoughts and return the money’s worth before the end of the thirty days if at the start she accepted it.</i>? A loan was not given for preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Forgiving the loan, while it might involve considerable value, is only invalidating a sheet of paper which in itself is not worth a <i>peruṭah</i>.</i>, while this was given from the start for preliminary marriage. But did we not state: “If she was eating right away she is not preliminarily married unless one of them was worth a <i>peruṭah</i>”? Then even if a <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth was not left, she should be preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">If all dates were given for the preliminary marriage and all were eaten, in what is this different from the tetradrachma which was spent before the marriage entered into force?</i>! He explains that as earlier<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">In the preceding paragraph; the woman explicitly rejected every gift as inadequate.</i>. If it refers to the earlier statement, did we not state: “If she was eating immediately she is not preliminarily married unless one of them was worth a <i>peruṭah</i>”<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the dates were referred to as given together.</i>? Rebbi Eleazar said, it<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah, while formulated anonymously, only represents the opinion of R. Simeon ben Eleazar.</i> follows Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar, as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.1">Halakhah 1:1, Notes 246–249</a>.</i>: “Be preliminarily married to me by the deposit you hold for me; if she went and found that it was stolen or lost, if there was a <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth left, she is preliminarily married; otherwise, she is not preliminarily married. By the loan you are holding from me, if she went and found that it was stolen or lost, even if there was no <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth left, she is preliminarily married. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon says in the name of Rebbi Meïr, a loan is like a deposit: If there was a <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth left, she is preliminarily married; otherwise, she is not preliminarily married.” Rebbi Eleazar said, Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar follows Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">In asserting that <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.1.1">Mishnah 2:1</a> follows R. Simeon ben Eleazar in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kiddushin (Lieberman) 3:1" href="/Tosefta_Kiddushin_(Lieberman).3.1">Tosephta 3:1</a>, R. Eleazar holds that both can be analyzed in the same way.</i>. Just as Rebbi Meïr said that anybody who deviates from the orders of the owner is called a robber<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Baba meṣi</i>‘<i>a</i> 6:3 (11a 1. 13), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 78" href="/Kiddushin.78">Babli 78a/b</a>. If raw wool was given to the dyer to be died red and he died it black, for R. Meïr the dyer acquired the wool as a robber and has to pay the value of the raw wool to his client. R. Jehudah holds that the wool remains the property of the consignor but the dyer is paid only an amount which protects the consignor from loss. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 9:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.9.5.1">Mishnah <i>Baba qama</i> 9:6</a>)</i>, so Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar says here, anybody who deviates from the orders of the owner is called a robber. For he explains it<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The Tosephta quoted from 1:2.</i> to be for a loan when the lender said<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">It would be natural to read the text as לוֹוֶה “the borrower”. This is the reading of the commentaries who are led to extraordinary intellectual acrobatics to make sense of the text. If לווה is read as a verbal noun derived from <i>pi</i>‘<i>el</i>, parallel to Babylonian מַלְוֶה, everything becomes simple.</i>, buy wheat for me, but he bought barley for him<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon ben Eleazar follows R. Meïr only in a very special case. He will agree in general with the anonymous majority that forgiving a loan represents enough monetary value to create a valid marriage. He only holds that if the borrower violated the terms of the loan, there no longer exists a loan but rather lender’s money in the borrower’s hand, i. e., a deposit.</i>. That is difficult: can one pay for a preliminary marriage with robbed money? Rebbi Yose asked: One understands that a person may invest a <i>peruṭah</i> to gain a denar; would he invest a <i>peruṭah</i> to gain a <i>perutah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">This question is asked against R. Meïr’s position in general. It is understandable that for the anonymous majority the woman is married, since the profit she gains from not having to repay the entire loan is certainly worth an extra <i>peruṭah</i>. Therefore, even if we note that a loan was given to be spent and cannot retroactively be given as a marriage present, the additional profit for the woman makes her marriage worthwhile. But for R. Meïr, who holds that only the unspent part of the loan can be a marriage gift, there is no additional profit for the woman and one may ask why R. Meïr does not have a general statement that forgiving a loan can never be a valid marriage present.</i>? Some Tannaïm state: One may preliminarily marry with a robbed item; some Tannaïm state: One cannot preliminarily marry with a robbed item. Rebbi Mana said, the one who said that one may preliminarily marry with a robbed item refers to an item which he has a chance to recoup from her hand. The one who said that one cannot preliminarily marry with a robbed item refers to an item which he has no chance to remove from her hand<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">He refers to the situation described by R. Eleazar, that the loan given to the woman turned into robbed property in her hand. If the prospective groom has documents to prove his case in court, the woman is a robber and he has a good chance to recoup his property in court, he offers her real value and the marriage is valid. But if all he has is a moral claim, that has no monetary value and cannot be used as a marriage present.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac: the one who said that one may preliminarily marry with a robbed item refers to an item which the owner gave up hope to recover. The one who said that one cannot preliminarily marry with a robbed item refers to an item which the owner did not give up hope to recover<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">This opinion does not disagree with R. Mana’s but refers to a different situation: The man is a robber and offers his prospective bride an object which he obtained by robbery. He can use the object for a preliminary marriage if and only if it is his both in ownership and in possession. The robber, who is in possession by the act of robbing, becomes owner at the moment the prior owners give up hope of recovery (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:4:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.4.6"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 4:4 Note 88, 5:6 Note 169</a>).</i>.
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The <i>baraita</i><sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The Tosephta quoted from 1:2.</i> if he said to her, by the deposit you are holding for me. But if he said, by <i>everything</i> which I deposited with you, she is not preliminarily married unless all of it is available<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Since he insists that everything shall be her property, she cannot be married if anything does not become her property because it is lost.</i>. What is the difference between this and a loan? A loan was given to be spent<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 47a" href="/Kiddushin.47a">Babli 47a</a>; <i>Mo</i>‘<i>ed qaṭan</i> 2:3 (81b 1. 27).</i>; this was not given to be spent<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">It is at the disposal of the depositor at any moment.</i>. But did we not state: “If she was eating right away she is not preliminarily married unless one of them was worth a <i>peruṭah</i>.” Then even if a <i>peruṭah’</i> s worth was left, she should not be preliminarily married unless all of them were available! He explains that as earlier<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">In the preceding paragraph; the woman explicitly rejected every gift as inadequate.</i>. If it refers to the earlier statement, did we not state: “If she was eating immediately she is not preliminarily married unless one of them was worth a <i>peruṭah</i>”<sup class="footnote-marker">66,</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the dates were referred to as given together.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">If the dates were stated as given together, the marriage should be possible if all of them come into the woman's hand together.</i>? Rebbi Abun said, were we not of the opinion to say, who is the Tanna of <i>Wawim</i>? Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The positions of Rebbis Jehudah and Meïr about the interpretation of conjunctions in multiple statements are discussed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.6.2"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 9:7, Notes 93–101</a>.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said: The words of Rebbi Jehudah imply comprehensive one sacrifice, in detail three sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 9:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.9.6.2"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 9:7</a>. If A sues B for wheat, barley, and spelt, and B denies falsely in court that he owes any, he has to atone by one sacrifice. But if he enters three separate denials, he owes three separate sacrifices.</i>. Just as he says there, comprehensive one sacrifice, in detail three sacrifices, so he says here, comprehensive one preliminary marriage, in detail three preliminary marriages<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">As explained earlier, the Mishnah is explained to refer to the case that the woman rejects all dates except the last. Then each is given as a separate gift; one cannot require that all of them should have been together.</i>.
“Be preliminarily married to me by this tetradrachma,” and she said, “throw it into the sea or the river,” she is not married.<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">A superficially similar text is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 8b" href="/Kiddushin.8b">Babli, 8b</a>, and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kiddushin (Lieberman) 2:8" href="/Tosefta_Kiddushin_(Lieberman).2.8">Tosephta, 2:8</a>. The big difference is that in the Babylonian texts the woman took the coin and threw it into the sea whereas in the Yerushalmi the woman never touched the coin. It is a recognized principle that actions speak louder than words; if a marriage gift is offered to a woman and she reacts by throwing it away, she certainly is not married. This has nothing to do with the problem treated in the present paragraph.</i> “Give it to a poor person,” she is married<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">From the moment the coin was given as charity.</i>. In either case nothing came into her hand; in one case you say, she is married; in the other case you say, she is not married! Rebbi Abbahu said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: She wants to be married and be seen as benefiting the poor. Rebbi Phineas said, this can be interpreted either following Rebbi Ze‘ira or Rebbi Ila.
<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">This text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 6:8:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.6.8.9"><i>Demay</i> 6:12, Notes 205–214</a> (<b>ד</b>). This insert given in the next paragraph shows that this is a genuine quote, not a copy.</i> As it was stated: “One may say to his worker: Here you have a denar, use it for food, here you have a denar, use it for drink, and not be worried about either the Sabbatical year, tithes, or wine for libations. But if he told him, go and buy yourself a loaf and I shall give you the money, go and buy yourself a <i>reviït</i> of wine and I shall give you the money, he has to worry because of the Sabbatical year, tithes, and wine used for libations.” Rebbi Ze‘ira said, the grocer becomes the agent of the employer to let the worker acquire. <sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Text missing here, added from <i>Demay</i>.</i>[Rebbi Hila said, the worker] acquired for the employer from the grocer and then acquires it himself.
Here also, in the opinion of Rebbi Ze‘ira the husband becomes the wife’s agent<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, the coin became the woman’s property in her future husband’s hand; the objection raised at the start is baseless.</i> to benefit the poor. Rebbi Hila said, the poor person acquired it for the wife from her husband<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">From the moment the coin was given as charity.</i> and afterwards acquired it for himself.
<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">This also is from <i>Demay</i>.</i> What is the difference between them? If the grocer was deaf-mute. According to Rebbi Ze‘ira, he does not have to worry because a deaf-mute cannot become an agent; according to Rebbi Hila, he does have to worry.
If the worker was deaf-mute. According to Rebbi Hila, he does not have to worry<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">But then the woman is not married if the coin is given to a deaf-mute beggar.</i> because a deaf-mute cannot acquire; according to Rebbi Ze‘ira, he does have to worry.
If he grabbed a tetradrachma from her hands, returned it to her, and at the moment of delivery said to her: “You are preliminarily married to me”, then she is preliminarily married. By what was she preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was her money, she did not receive anything. In addition, it is questionable whether one may use robbed property as marriage gift.</i>? Rebbi Ḥaggai in the name of Rebbi Pedat: She wants to be preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 13a" href="/Kiddushin.13a">Babli, 13a</a>, she is only married if she had previously agreed to be married to him.</i> to him and he would owe her a tetradrachma<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">She accepts the coin as a marriage gift, not as return of stolen goods. Therefore, the husband now owes her another tetradrachma.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, if this had been formulated for a vessel, it would be understandable. A tetradrachma can be exchanged<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Nobody insists that payment be made with a particular coin. He disputes that the husband owes another coin. The fact that she does not have to go to court to retrieve her coin is worth a <i>peruṭah</i>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kiddushin (Lieberman) 2:7-9" href="/Tosefta_Kiddushin_(Lieberman).2.7-9">Tosephta 2:7–9</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 12b" href="/Kiddushin.12b">Babli 12b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 13a" href="/Kiddushin.13a">13a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 8a" href="/Kiddushin.8a">8a</a>.</i>“ ‘Hold this tetradrachma for me’ and at the moment of delivery he said, ‘lo, you are preliminarily married to me,’ she is preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Unless she objects in word or deed. She is married if she remains silent.</i>. After delivery, if she agrees<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">But not if she remains silent.</i>, she is preliminarily married, otherwise she is not preliminarily married. ‘Here is the tetradrachma which I owe you,’ and at the moment of delivery he said, ‘lo, you are preliminarily married to me,’ if she agrees<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">But not if she remains silent.</i>, she is preliminarily married, otherwise she is not preliminarily married. If after delivery he said, ‘lo, you are preliminarily married to me,’ she is not preliminarily married even if both parties agree. What can he do? He should take the coin back, deliver it again and say, ‘lo, you are preliminarily married to me.’ ‘Be preliminarily married to me by this <i>mina</i>,’ if it turns out that a denar was missing, she is not preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Since there is no <i>mina</i> given to her.</i>. If one denar was bad<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Counterfeit or underweight.</i>, she is preliminarily married if he replaces it. If he was counting the coins into her hand, she can change her mind any time before he finishes.” Rebbi Eleazar said, she is preliminarily married by the first [coin]; the remainder is a condition<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 8a" href="/Kiddushin.8a">Babli 8a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 47a" href="/Kiddushin.47a">47a</a>. It is understood that he asked the woman to be married by <i>a mina</i>, not <i>this mina</i>. Then the first coin establishes the marriage, the remainder is the husband’s debt to be liquidated.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, only after he completes [the count.]<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">He seems to hold that the start of delivery is an obligation to finish it at that time. This opinion is not mentioned in the Babli since it is retracted here.</i> It was said that Rebbi Joḥanan changed his mind about this.
<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">A different version is in <i>Baba meṣi‘a</i> 4:2 (9c).</i> A person gave a pledge to another; the other reneged on it<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Baba meṣi‘a</i>: A person made a down payment, the seller then annulled the contract. Here also “reneged” does not mean that the seller claimed not to have received a pledge, but that he refused to honor the contract.</i>. Rebbi Abba, Rebbi Hila, and Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa all three say it was a deal about flax seed<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Baba meṣi‘a</i>: “silk”.</i> and he had given partial payment. The case came before Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Baba meṣi‘a</i>: Before R. Ḥiyya bar Joseph and R. Joḥanan. This also is required by the context here.</i>, who said: Either he delivers the entire contract or one may turn him over to “Him Who exacted retribution.<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">It was stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.5.1">Mishnah 1:5</a> that possession of movables is obtained only by an act of moving. The buyer does not acquire possession by paying the price for the object bought. It is possible for the seller to take the money but then to renege on the deal, not deliver the merchandise but return the money. The injured buyer cannot force the execution of the contract but Mishnah <i>Baba meṣi‘a</i> 4:2 states that he can go to court and have the court declare that “He Who exacted retribution from the generation of the Flood and of the generation of the Tower of Babylon will in the future exact retribution from him who does not keep his word.”</i>” Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Joseph said, either he delivers for the amount of the pledge or ome may turn him over to “Him Who exacted retribution.<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan here decides following R. Eleazar in the previous paragraph: Even a partial payment validates the entire deal. He should hold that the woman who starts to accept partial payment for the promised marriage gift is married. R. Ḥiyya bar Joseph represents the position first ascribed to R. Joḥanan, that financial deals are not completed until the entire debt be paid.</i>” Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Joseph agrees with Rebbi Joḥanan in a sale which is not usually split, such as of a cow or a stole<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">A down payment for a cow requires the delivery of the entire cow.</i>. Is a woman usually acquired split? Rebbi Ḥaggai and Rebbi Yudan both said, a sale usually may be split, the acquisition of a wife cannot be split<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">The position of R. Eleazar in the preceding paragraph is universally accepted.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “Be preliminarily married to me by this cup of wine,” but it turned out to be honey; “of honey” but it turned out to be wine; “by this silver denar,” but it turned out to be of gold, “gold denar” but it turned out to be of silver; “on condition that I be poor”, but he turned out to be rich; “rich” but he turned out to be poor; she is not preliminarily married.
