Rebbi Simeon said, if he tricked her by understatement she is preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">If he gave a gold denar pretending it was of silver or pretended to be poor when he was rich.</i>.<br>“On condition that I be a Cohen,” and he turned out to be a Levite; “a Levite” and he turned out to be a Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">A Cohen has many more rights than a Levite but a Cohen’s wife is subject to many more restrictions than a Levite’s.</i>; “a Gibeonite” and he turned out to be a bastard, “a bastard” and he turned out to be a Gibeonite<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">For Gibeonites, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 4:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.4.1.7">Chapter 4, Notes 48ff</a>.; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.2.4.1"><i>Yebamot</i> 2:4, Note 72</a>. Both Gibeonites and bastards (children of adulterous or incestual unions) are precluded from marrying in the congregation.</i>; “a villager<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">In rabbinic Hebrew, any unwalled place is called עִיר.</i>” and he turned out to be from a walled city; “from a walled city” and he turned out to be from a village; “on condition that my house be close to the public baths” and it turned out to be far, “far” and it turned out to be close; “on condition that I have a grown-up daughter or slave-girl<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">She can do the household chores. In some Mishnah mss. one reads גַדֶּלֶת, “hairdresser.”</i>” and it turned out that he had none, “on condition that I have not” and it turned out that he did have; “on condition that I have children” and it turned out that he had none, “on condition that I not have children” and it turned out that he did have<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband already has children, the second wife is not in danger of a levirate marriage. But the existence of children from a prior marriage may cause trouble.</i>. Il all these cases she is not preliminarily married even if she says, I had the intention to be preliminarily married to him regardless. The same holds if she misinformed him.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Be preliminarily married to me by this cup of wine,” etc. If this cup and its contents together have the value of a <i>peruṭah</i>, she is preliminarily married; otherwise she is not preliminarily married. “Acquire it and its contents,” if its contents are worth a <i>peruṭah</i>, she is preliminarily married; otherwise she is not preliminarily married; she acquired only its contents<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">This text seems to be elliptic. It probably should be understood following the text of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kiddushin (Lieberman) 2:3" href="/Tosefta_Kiddushin_(Lieberman).2.3">Tosephta 2:3</a>:<br>The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 48b" href="/Kiddushin.48b">Babli, 48b</a>, notes that every statement has to interpreted in context. If the cup was filled with water, it is understood that the cup was given. If wine, only the contents are intended. If any fluid used as spice, not intended to be used immediately, both cup and contents are given.</i>.
“Rebbi Simeon said, if he tricked her by understatement she is preliminarily married.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rebbi Simeon agrees that she is not preliminarily married if he tricked her by misstating his personal status<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 49a" href="/Kiddushin.49a">Babli, 49a</a>, and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kiddushin (Lieberman) 2:5" href="/Tosefta_Kiddushin_(Lieberman).2.5">Tosephta, 2:5</a>, this is a tannaïtic statement.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah says this: “ ‘On condition that I be a Cohen,’ and he turned out to be a Levite.<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon disagrees only in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>, about monetary affairs, not in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.2.1">Mishnah 3</a>, in matters of personal status.</i>”
One understands “Cohen and he turns out to be a Levite.<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">If he is less than he represented himself to be, there is no reason to validate the marriage.</i>” “Levite and he turns out to be a Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">Why can we not assume that normally a woman will be glad to get a husband of higher social standing than he represented himself to be.</i>”? She may say, I do not want one who is haughty towards me<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 49a" href="/Kiddushin.49a">Babli, 49a</a>, this argument is attributed to Ulla.</i>. One understands “from a walled city” and he turned out to be a villager; “from a village” and he turned out to be from a walled city? She may say, I want to be in a quiet place. “On condition that my house be close to the public baths” and it turned out to be far. One understands “close and it turned out to be far.” “Far and it turned out to be close”? She can say, I want to take a walk<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">This translation is pure speculation. It does not seem that this טרפס is identical to טפס as asserted by the dictionaries and commentators. In Arabic, طر فس means “look through almost closed eyelids; put on many garments; be dark (night)”. Perhaps cf. ظر ف “to be beautiful”.</i> going and returning. “On condition that I have a daughter or slave-girl hairdresser” and it turned out that he had none, that one understands. “On condition that I have” and it turned out that he did not have? Think of it, if he had one at the end of the world<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">What difference would it make to the woman?</i>! Rebbi Abba bar Mamal said, so is the Mishnah: “A daughter to dress your hair, and a slave-girl to serve you.<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 49a" href="/Kiddushin.49a">Babli, 49a</a>, notes that while this looks like a monetary stipulation, it is one of personal service and R. Simeon is correct in not objecting.</i>”

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Somebody said to his agent: “Go and marry me preliminarily to woman X at place Y”; if he went and married him preliminarily at another place, she is not preliminarily married. “She is at place Y;” if he married him preliminarily at another place, she is preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">The geographical information is not part of the instruction of agency but an indication where the agent may discharge his commission.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Somebody said to his agent: “Go and marry me preliminarily to woman X,” etc. The Mishnah does not follow Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">Both paragraphs parallel <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.3.2"><i>Giṭṭin</i>6:4, Notes 83–84</a>. The statement of the Tanna R. Eleazar is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.3.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i>6:5</a>.</i>. But following Rebbi Eleazar she is preliminarily married unless [the husband] tells [the agent]: Do not preliminarily marry me to her except at place X<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">This also is from <i>Demay</i>.</i> and he went and executed the preliminary marriage at another place.
“She is preliminarily married,” Rebbi Eleazar agrees that he only indicates a place; the rabbis agree that he only indicates a place<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 59a" href="/Kiddushin.59a">Babli agrees, 59a</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody performed preliminary marriage with a woman on condition that she had no obligation of vows on her and it turns out that she had vows to fulfill, she is not preliminarily married. If he married her definitively without inquiry and she had vows to fulfill, she should leave without <i>ketubah</i>. For all defects which disqualify a priest disqualify a woman<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">This Mishnah is severely truncated. It is identical to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Ketubot 7:8" href="/Mishnah_Ketubot.7.8">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 7:8 (Notes 101–105</a>). The missing part, which explains the last sentence, reads: “If somebody performed preliminary marriage with a woman on condition that she had no bodily defects and it turned out that she did have bodily defects, she is not preliminarily married. If he married her definitively without inquiry and she had bodily defects, she should leave without <i>ketubah</i>.” The following Halakhah is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 7:7:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.7.7.2-5"><i>Ketubot</i> 7:9, Notes 110–143</a>. The minor differences in readings are noted there.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody performed preliminary marriage with a woman etc. We have stated defects. Which vows? Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Yoṣadaq: If she vowed not to eat meat, or not to drink wine, or not to wear dyed garments. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, the fine linen garments which come from Bet She‘an have the status of dyed garments. Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah deals with the case that he said to her, “on condition that you have no obligation of vows on you.” But if he said to her, “on condition that you have no vow on you,” then even if she made a vow not to eat carob fruit it is a vow.
“If she went to an Elder and he dissolved her vow, she is preliminarily married. To a doctor and he healed her, she is not preliminarily married.” What is the difference between the Elder and the doctor? The Elder uproots the vow from its start; the doctor heals only for the future. Some Tannaïm state: Even if she went to an Elder and he dissolved her vow, she is not preliminarily married. The <i>baraita</i> follows Rebbi Eleazar, as we have stated there: “Rebbi Eleazar said, it would have been logical about a vow which has to be investigated by a Sage, because the Elder uproots the vow from its start. Why did they forbid a vow which has to be investigated by a Sage? Because of a vow which does not have to be investigated by a Sage. Some Tannaïm state: She is allowed to marry without a bill of divorce. Some Tannaïm state: She is forbidden to marry without a bill of divorce. They wanted to say that he who says, she is allowed to marry without a bill of divorce is Rebbi Eleazar, and he who says, she is forbidden to marry without a bill of divorce are the rabbis. Everything follows the rabbis. He who says, she is forbidden to marry without a bill of divorce, the rabbis. He who says, she is allowed to marry without a bill of divorce: Since she knows that if she went to an Elder, he would dissolve her vow, since she does not go therefore she can be married without a bill of divorce. What is the reason of him who says, she is forbidden to marry without a bill of divorce, that she should not go to an Elder who would dissolve her vow, then the preliminary marriage would become retroactively valid for her and it would turn out that her children became bastards. Therefore she is forbidden to marry without a bill of divorce.
“If he married her definitively without inquiry”. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, the Mishnah deals with the case that he married her preliminarily conditionally but definitively silently. But if he married her preliminarily silently and definitively silently, she can claim her <i>ketubah</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, even if he married her preliminarily silently and definitively silently, she has no <i>ketubah</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: But she needs a bill of divorce from him even if he married her preliminarily conditionally but definitively silently. Rebbi Ze‘ira asked before Rebbi Mana: In case he married her preliminarily silently and divorced her after the preliminary marriage, what does Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish say? Let us hear from the following: “If she had bodily defects, as long as she is in her father’s house, the father has to prove.” This implies that if he proved his case, she can claim her <i>ketubah</i>. What do you see to say, if he married her preliminarily silently and divorced her after the preliminary marriage! Where do we hold? Since afterwards [it is stated]: “When was this said? For hidden defects, but he has no claim about visible defects.” If he. made it conditional, could he not also claim for visible ones? But did not the colleagues say before Rebbi Yose: Should we say that the following statement disagrees with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish? “Once she entered her husband’s domain, the husband has to prove.” This implies that if the husband proved his case, she has no claim to <i>ketubah</i>. But by the statement of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, she has a claim to <i>ketubah</i>. Rebbi Cohen in the name of the rabbis of Caesarea: The Mishnah deals with the case that he definitively married her but did not sleep with her; Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish speaks about the case that he definitively married her and slept with her; I am saying that she was acceptable to him since he took her to bed.
“And all defects which disqualify a priest disqualify a woman. They added to them for women mouth odor, sweat odor, and a hairless mole.” Rebbi Ḥamai bar Uqba in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: They taught this about the skin of the face. But did we not state: “When was this said? For hidden defects, but he has no claim about visible defects.” Is this not of the visible defects? Explain it if she hid it under her headgear. It was stated: “A mole with hairs is a defect, whether it be large or small, whether it be on the body or on the face. Without a hair it is a defect on the face but not on the body. When has this been said? If it is small. But if it is large, it is a defect whether it be on the body or on the face. How big is large? Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, up to an Italic <i>as</i>.” Rebbi Eleazar bar Ḥanina said, for example such a Gordianic denar: its measure is half a ubiquitous gold piece. Rebbi Radifa, Rebbi Jonah: Rebbi Jeremiah asked: A bald woman with a row of hair going from one ear to the other? They wanted to understand it from the following: “They added to them for women mouth odor, sweat odor, and a hairless mole.” They did not mention baldness. They wanted to conclude that it is not a defect. There came Rebbi Samuel the son of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun: Rebbi Nasa said, it is a defect. It is listed only if it is ugly for both sexes. But something which is beautiful in one sex but ugly in the other, like a bald pate, even though it is a defect it was not listed. You should know that this is so because a beard is beautiful for a man and a defect for a woman, and we did not state: In addition, for a woman a beard. There are breasts which are beautiful for a woman and a defect for men, and we did not state: In addition, for a man women’s breasts.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Somebody who preliminarily marries two women with the value of a <i>peruṭah</i>, or one woman with the value of half a <i>peruṭah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">Even though it is possible to contract a multiple polygamous marriage with a group of women by giving the marriage gift to one of them who acts as the agent for all of them, the gift must be worth as many <i>peruṭot</i> as there are women to be married.</i>, is not preliminarily married even if afterwards he sent presents<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Worth much more than a <i>peruṭah</i>.</i>, since he sent them based on the earlier preliminary marriage. The same holds for an underage boy who contracted a preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">If he contracts the preliminary marriage when underage and sends presents to the woman after he came of age he is not preliminarily married.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody preliminarily marries two women with the value of a <i>peruṭah</i>,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kiddushin (Lieberman) 4:4" href="/Tosefta_Kiddushin_(Lieberman).4.4">Tosephta 4:4</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 73b" href="/Ketubot.73b">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 73b</a>.</i>: “In all these cases, if they had intercourse they acquired<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">The invalid marriage was validated retroactively by the couple living together.</i>. Rebbi Simeon [ben]<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of the parallel sources. The ms. tradition of the names in the mss. of the Babli is varied and quite uncertain.</i> Jehudah says in the name of Rebbi Simeon: In all these cases, if they had intercourse they did acquire<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babylonian sources (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.5.2">Note 132</a>): Did not acquire. Probably the text here should not be emended.</i> since his intercourse was predicated on the preceding preliminary marriage.<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Since preliminary marriage is a formal act of acquisition, it needs intention of the acquirer. If there is no intention because the acquirer wrongly thought that he already had acquired, there is no acquisition. This is the reasoning behind the Babylonian statements.</i>” What did they acquire<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">Since the argument of R. Simeon is based on generally accepted principles, it is difficult to see what he understands by “acquiring”.</i>? Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: They acquired for restrictions<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">A single woman can be married to any man not forbidden to her by incest prohibitions. A married woman cannot be married by any other man; if another man gives her a gift for preliminary marriage and she accepts it, these actions have no consequence whatsoever in law. But a woman preliminarily married for restrictions is not married in this sense. As explained later in the Halakhah, if another man performs preliminary marriage with her, his action is potentially valid in law; the woman is forbidden to both men until at least one of them validly divorces her. But for the anonymous Tanna the marriage is unconditionally validated by intercourse.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: With any condition which refers to a preceding action<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">If the preliminary marriage was performed unconditionally and then the husband added a condition, that condition is invalid, as stated later. But in marriage law, the condition is considered valid if it causes any trouble.</i>, she is preliminarily married for restrictions. Rebbi Mana asked before Rebbi Yudan: Who is the Tanna who stated: “Any condition which refers to a preceding action is invalid”<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Baba meṣi</i>‘<i>a</i> 7:14. In contract law, an action already completed cannot retroactively be made conditional. If someone marrieda woman and after the fact declares that the marriage shall be valid only if she will support him, the marriage is valid but she has no obligation to support her husband.</i>? This does not follow Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Since (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.5.2">Note 139</a>) he accepts legal consequences of conditions attached after the act.</i>. Rebbi Yudan in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: She is preliminarily married for restrictions. How is that: “I am marrying you preliminarily by intercourse on condition that rains should fall.<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">One has to wonder why this marriage should not be invalidated as an idolatrous act or at least as rainmaking by sympathetic magic.</i>” If rain fell, she is preliminarily married, otherwise she is not preliminarily married. Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: In matters of a preliminary marriage by a loan<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">Which is invalid for R. Meïr, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:1:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.1.10">Note 62</a>. It is decided here that a preliminary marriage for which nothing of value changes hands is valid only as a restriction (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.5.2">Note 138</a>).</i> for restrictions, for real estate it was not acquired<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">If a debt be liquidated by handing over real estate without a contract, the deal is invalid.</i>, for movables one does not deliver him to “Him Who exacted retribution.<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:23" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.23">Chapter 1, Note 106</a>. A contract for sale of movables for which no money changes hands and no action of acquisition is performed is unenforceable. (The entire statement with slight variations is quoted in <i>Sefer Ha</i>‘<i>iṭṭur</i> 1, לח b, Note 6.)</i>” If [the buyer] insists on the deal? Let us hear from the following: “You have wine to get from me, when he had no wine.<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to Mishnah <i>Baba meṣi‘a</i> 5:1: A person made a contract for later delivery of grain at the then going rate of 25 denars a <i>kur</i> (30 <i>se‘ah</i>). The buyer demanded delivery when the going rate was 30 denars a <i>kur</i>, in order to sell the grain and invest the proceeds in wine. The seller told him that he was ready to take the grain at 30 denars and to give him wine instead. If the seller actually has the wine, this is a valid deal. But if the seller has no wine at that moment, the deal would be an illegal circumvention of the prohibition of interest payments.</i>” Therefore, if he had wine he would be obligated to deliver. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated, if he has wine he is obligated to deliver. Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac did (not)<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">It seems best to delete this word. The emendation of the standard commentaries, to read שלח for שלא does not make sense since שלח אמר “he sent (by letter) said (in person)” is a contradiction in terms.</i> say there<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">“There” usually refers to Babylonia.</i>: You should know that Rebbi Ḥiyya instructed following Rebbi Joḥanan that a preliminary marriage by a loan is for restrictions. Rebbi Ze‘ira resented it. Why? Because they were restrictive in practice and he taught them leniency<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">They considered preliminary marriage by cancellation of a debt as invalid. R. Samuel ben Rav Isaac gave them a handle to validate the marriage.</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, they were lenient in practice<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">They considered preliminary marriage by cancellation of a debt as valid. R. Samuel ben Rav Isaac instructed them to consider the marriage only probationary; if another man interferred between preliminary and definitive marriages, a bill of divorce would be required from at least one of the men involved.</i> and he taught them to be restrictive, that if another man came and married her preliminarily, his marriage would hold. Rebbi Yudan ben Rebbi Ḥanan said, if there is a doubt between adultery and no adultery, decide on no adultery<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">This is the same statement as the preceding. In a case where it is doubtful whether a woman is married and therefore immune to all proposals of marriage by another man, or is not married and open to such proposals, one has to decide that she might be open to proposals and eventually require bills of divorce from all parties involved.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody preliminarily marries a woman and her daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">Forbidden in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18:17" href="/Leviticus.18.17"><i>Lev</i>. 18:17</a>.</i> or two sisters<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">Forbidden in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18:18" href="/Leviticus.18.18"><i>Lev</i>. 18:18</a>.</i> simultaneously<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">Forbidden in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18:18" href="/Leviticus.18.18"><i>Lev</i>. 18:18</a>.</i>, they are not preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">If the action was not simultaneous, the first marriage would be valid and the second meaningless because impossible.</i>. It happened to five women, among them two sisters, that a man collected a bag of figs from their own property in the Sabbatical year and said: All of you are preliminarily married to me by this bag, and one of them accepted for all of them. Then the Sages said, the sisters are not preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">The invalidity of the act for the two sisters has no influence on the validity for the others. Naturally the woman who acts as recipient cannot be one of the sisters.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody preliminarily marries a woman and her daughter,” etc. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, one understands from this five rules: One understands that five women can be preliminarily married simultaneously. That a woman may receive her own wedding gift and that of her companion. And one may use the spoils of robbery for a preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah stated that the figs were the property of the women being married. It also states that this happened in a Sabbatical year. Since the produce of a Sabbatical year is ownerless, the Mishnah seems to contradict itself. One will have to explain that the women collected the figs and acquired them by the act of collection. Then the man came and took them away from them. The Mishnah only implies that a woman may be preliminarily married by objects robbed from herself since by accepting marriage she forgives the robber. It does not imply that objects obtained by robbery can be used to marry an uninvolved woman.<br>The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 52a" href="/Kiddushin.52a">Babli, 52a</a>, comes to an opposite conclusion. It assumes that the figs grew in the women’s orchard and were collected as ownerless produce by the man. Then the conclusion is that in any other year the marriage would have been invalid, even involving the owners of the robbed object.</i>. And one may preliminarily marry using produce acquired by a prohibited act<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Sabbatical produce can only be used to be eaten under the rules of the Sabbatical. Using such produce for an act of acquisition is illicit. Nevertheless, the act remains valid.</i>. And preliminary marriage is impossible in incest situations.
Rebbi Eleazar said, sisters are not preliminarily married but in the case of purification sacrifices it atoned<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">If a person simultaneously slaughters two purification sacrifices for one transgression, he has fulfilled his obligation. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Meilah 1:2" href="/Mishnah_Meilah.1.2">Mishnah <i>Me‘ilah</i> 1:2</a> describes this situation: A person dedicated an animal as purification sacrifice. Then this animal was lost, another was dedicated as replacement, and then the first one was found before the second was sacrificed. Each of the animals becomes the replacement of the other.) This statement is nontrivial since as a general rule an animal dedicated as purification sacrifice but whose owner then used another animal for the same purpose can no longer be used for anything.</i>. How is that? If one slaughtered two purification offerings for one transgression<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">Simultaneously.</i>, the altar selects that which is appropriate<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Expression of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 9:1" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.9.1">Mishnah <i>Zebaḥim</i>9:1</a>. Since both sacrifices have equal standing, there is no reason to prefer one to the other. Only selected parts of the purification offering are given to the altar; the remainder of the meat has to be eaten by the priest (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:17-23" href="/Leviticus.6.17-23"><i>Lev</i>. 6:17–23</a>).</i>; both are forbidden to be eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">The priests are commanded to eat the meat of the animal which effects the purification. But in this case it is impossible to determine which animal effects the purification.</i>. If one slaughtered two reparation offerings for one damage, the altar selects that which is appropriate; both are forbidden to be eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">The rules of reparation sacrifices follow those of purification sacrifices; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:7" href="/Leviticus.7.7"><i>Lev</i>. 7:7</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If the first<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">This deals with a separate case, that the animals were slaughtered one after the other.</i> one was slaughtered not for its purpose but the second for its purpose, it did atone since the first, which was not for its purpose, becomes acceptable only through the purpose stated later<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 1:1" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.1.1">Mishnah <i>Zebaḥim</i> 1:1</a> states that both purification and Passover sacrifices which were slaughtered not for their stated purpose are invalid and cannot be offered to the altar. Normally, a purification offering which is invalidated at the time of slaughter is burned outside the Temple precinct and another sacrifice is required independent of the first. But if the second sacrifice is slaughtered correctly immediately after the first, when its flesh is still in the Temple precinct, then the correct slaughter of the second rehabilitates the first, both sacrifices have their selected parts offered on the altar, and both are forbidden as food to the priests.</i>. But if the first was slaughtered for its purpose but the second not for its purpose: if the first atoned for what may the second atone? For impurity which occured between the first and the second<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">This answer makes more sense in <i>Šebuot</i> 1:4 (33b 1.4) where the relative merit of the purification sacrifices on New Year’s day are discussed, one required for the New Moon and one for the holiday. If one sacrifice purifies, what is the use of the second? To atone for impurities which might have occurred in the meantime. In the case discussed here, the second sacrifice is invalid and useless.</i>. But for Passover sacrifices it did not atone<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">The Passover sacrifice does not atone. He holds that people who bring a Passover sacrifice which cannot be eaten did not fulfill their duty, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.4.1">Mishnah <i>Pesaḥim</i>7:4</a>.</i> since the Passover sacrifice is only for the meat to be eaten. This does not follow Rebbi Nathan since Rebbi Nathan said, one fulfills one’s duty by sprinkling [the blood] without eating<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:5:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.5.2-7"><i>Pesahim</i> 7:5</a> (34b 1. 45), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 78b" href="/Kiddushin.78b">Babli 78b</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody uses his portion either from most holy or simply holy sacrifices for preliminary marriage, she is not preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">The parts of most holy sacrifices (purification and reparation sacrifices) that are eaten are reserved for male Cohanian in the Temple precint, off limits to women. The Cohen’s part of simple sacrifices (well-being sacrifices) are eaten by the Cohen’s family in purity. Since a woman by preliminary marriage becomes “bought by the Cohen’smoney”, she might eat from these parts. But everybody agrees that sacrificial meat never is the Cohen’s property but that the Cohanim “eat from the Almighty’s table.” Since the Cohen’s part is not his property, it cannot be used in a marriage transaction. Cf. <i>Ma‘aśer Šeni</i> 1:2, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:1:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.1.11">Note 65</a>.</i>. From Second Tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">Which is eaten by the farmer’s family in Jerusalem; cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i>. (First Tithe is profane in the Levite’s hand.)</i> there is no preliminary marriage whether unintentionally<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">At the moment of the transaction neither man nor woman was aware that the gift was one of Second Tithe.</i> or intentionally, the words of Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that Second Tithe is Heaven’s peroperty in the hand of the farmer.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, intentionally he performed preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">In his opinion, Second Tithe is the farmer’s property but is reserved for sanctified use. If the farmer intentionally misused Second Tithe as marriage gift, he desecrated it. The tithe becomes profane and the farmer has to replace it in the statutory amount of 125%.</i>, unintentionally he did not perform preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">If the use was unintentional, the tithe is not desecrated but cannot be used.</i>. From Temple property, intentionally he performed preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">The intentional use of Temple property for private purposes is sacrilege and desecrates the property, which becomes profane.</i>, unintentionally he did not perform preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">In R. Meïr’s opinion, Temple property becomes profane only by intentional profanation.</i>, the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Jehudah says, unintentionally he performed preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">He extends the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:14-16" href="/Leviticus.5.14-16"><i>Lev</i>. 5:14–16</a> to all Temple property. The inadvertent use of Temple property constitutes larceny which requires a reparation offering and restitution in the amount of 125%, but the object taken becomes profane and, therefore, can be used as marriage gift.</i>, intentionally<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">Since intentional sin cannot be atoned for by sacrifice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:30" href="/Numbers.15.30"><i>Num</i>. 15:30</a>), the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:14-16" href="/Leviticus.5.14-16"><i>Lev</i>. 5:14–16</a> cannot apply.</i> he did not perform preliminary marriage.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Eleazar said, it is everybody’s opinion. Rebbi Joḥanan said, it is in dispute<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">The statement that the Cohen’s share of simple sacrifices may not be used as a marriage gift.</i>. There<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i> 1:2, Notes 43–44. The firstling becomes the Cohen’s property at the rancher’s corral, outside the Temple precinct.</i>, we have stated: “One may sell a firstling unblemished alive, blemished alive or slaughtered, and one may use it for preliminarily marrying a woman.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the paragraph, the text is from <i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i> 1:2, Notes 63–69 (<b>מ</b>). The only major addition is a quote of the Mishnah here.</i> Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: Alive, but not slaughtered. And Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi said, Rebbi Meïr learns all sacrifices from animal tithe. Just as one may not become betrothed to a woman with animal tithe, so no sacrifices may be used to become betrothed to a woman. Rebbi Jehudah learns all dedicated things from the firstling. Just as one may become betrothed to a woman with a firstling, so all sacrifices may be used to become betrothed to a woman. The opinion of Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi is inverted. There, he says, alive or slaughtered. But here, he says alive, but not slaughtered. There in his own name, here in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi. Even if you say there and here in his own name; if he becomes betrothed while it is still alive and with what is scheduled to fall to him. After slaughter, what is the reason of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:18" href="/Numbers.18.18"><i>Num</i>. 18:18</a>): “Their meat shall be for you, like the breast of weaving.” And what is the reason of Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi? “<i>Shall be for you</i>”<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">The text is misquoted here, correct in <i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i>.</i>, even after slaughtering. How does Rebbi Joshua ben Levi uphold “<i>shall be for you</i>”<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">The text is misquoted here, correct in <i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i>.</i>? He added another <i>being</i> that it should be eaten during two days and one night.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Demai 1:3" href="/Mishnah_Demai.1.3">Mishnah <i>Demay</i> 1:3, Notes 112–114</a>.</i>, we have stated: “<i>Ḥallah</i> of the <i>am haäreẓ</i>, food containing heave, food bought with money of the Second Tithe, and the remainders of flour sacrifices are free from <i>demay</i>.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">This text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 1:3:24" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.1.3.24"><i>Demay</i> 1:3, Notes 183–188</a>.</i> It was stated: “Concerning all of these, if he gave a name to their heave of the tithe or Second Tithe, what he did is done.” Rebbi Eleazar said, with the exception of the remainders of flour sacrifices. Rebbi Jeremiah said, the rest is in dispute. Rebbi Yose asked, what dispute? Where are we standing, if according to Rebbi Meïr, both for Second Tithe and for the remainders of flour sacrifices, he did not do anything. If according to Rebbi Jehudah, what he did is done. Rebbi Mana said, I went to Caesarea and heard Rebbi Ḥizqiah who was sitting there, stating: “If [a priest] preliminarily marries by means of his share in the holiest sacrifices or simple holy sacrifices, she is not married.” Rebbi Eleazar said, that is everybody’s opinion. Rebbi Joḥanan said, it is in dispute. I said to him, from whom did you hear this, and he said, from Rebbi Jeremiah. I said, this explains the matter! Rebbi Jeremiah, who heard that Rebbi Eleazar said, it is everybody’s opinion, he says it is in dispute. Rebbi Yose, who did not hear that, asked which dispute? If according to Rebbi Meïr, both for tithe and for the remainders of flour sacrifices, he did not do anything; if according to Rebbi Jehudah, what he did is done.
When Rebbi Meïr expired, Rebbi Jehudah decided and said, Rebbi Meïr’s students shall not enter here<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 52b" href="/Kiddushin.52b">Babli 52b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nazir 49b" href="/Nazir.49b"><i>Nazir</i> 49b</a>. In the version of the Babli, Symmachos quoted R. Meïr’s Mishnah in R. Jehudah’s school, R. Jehudah objected that most holy sacrifices are distributed at a place reserved to male Cohanim, and R. Yose gave the answer attributed here to R. Jehudah.<br>Since in the preceding paragraph it was established that R. Jehudah admits the possibility of contracting marriage by the Cohen’s part in the sacrifices, the question arises how this is possible for most holy meat. In R. Meïr’s Mishnah, most holy sacrifices could be mentioned together with simple sacrifices since all are forbidden.</i>. Symmachos squeezed by and entered. He said, “Most holy sacrifices for preliminary marriage,” how could a woman enter the Temple courtyard? He said to him, if he contracts preliminary marriage through an agent<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Babli, it also could be a transaction between an unmarried Cohen and the Cohen father of an underage girl. Then not even an agent is needed.</i>. By what does he contract preliminary marriage? Rebbi Eleazar said, by its body<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">The meat itself, given from one Cohen to another. Then the question arises whith whom a Cohen possibly could trade most holy meat.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, by the goodwill created<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">The goodwill created by a gift of meat might be worth a <i>peruṭah</i>. Goodwill is immaterial, not subject to the restrictions imposed on sancta.</i>. Or we might say that they do not disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">To avoid the question raised in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:7:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.7.6">Note 187</a>.</i>. When Rebbi Eleazar said, by its body, [he meant] by the expected goodwill from it. Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Bevai in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: One may contract preliminary marriage with a <i>peruṭah</i> from Second Tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">Second Tithe is agricultural produce. It may be redeemed by money, which transfers the sanctity from the produce to the coin (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:25" href="/Deuteronomy.14.25"><i>Deut</i>. 14:25</a>). But only the original produce is called “holy” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:13" href="/Deuteronomy.26.13"><i>Deut</i>. 26:13</a>); the replacement is functionally but not intrinsically holy. Therefore, the money may be given as marriage gift to a woman in Jerusalem who may buy food with it for consumption in purity.</i>.
“With Second Tithe.” Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: He did not intend to transgress the words of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">One has to explain why Second Tithe cannot be used as marriage gift in error according to R. Jehudah. If the groom did not intend to acquire the Second Tithe, he cannot give it away.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, in one case he did not intend, in one case she did not intend<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">The rules for both Temple property and Second Tithe can be explained in the same way. R. Jonah and R. Abin try to understand what R. Ze‘ira meant.</i>. Rebbi Jonah said, the sanctified he did not intend<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">Misappropriation of Temple property is characterized as a sin in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:15-16" href="/Leviticus.5.15-16"><i>Lev</i>. 5:15–16</a>. One cannot assume that a man intentionally wants to sin. Therefore, for R. Meïr one has to assume that the marriage is invalid, for R. Jehudah that it is valid. Both will agree that if the groom is interviewed and he insists that he intentionally misused Temple property, then for R. Meïr the marriage is valid, for R. Jehudah it is invalid (Notes 175,177).</i>, Second Tithe she did not intend<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">No woman wants to be married by produce which she will be obligated to bring to Jerusalem before it can be used. According to the undisputed opinion of R. Eleazar (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:7:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.7.6">Note 190</a>), the Mishnah must speak of produce which is Second Tithe, not of money for which the tithe was redeemed.</i>. Rebbi Abin said, the sanctified neither he nor she did intend<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">Nobody wants to start his marriage with a blatant sin.</i>; Second tithe he did intend but she did not<sup class="footnote-marker">194,</sup><i class="footnote">No woman wants to be married by produce which she will be obligated to bring to Jerusalem before it can be used. According to the undisputed opinion of R. Eleazar (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:7:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.7.6">Note 190</a>), the Mishnah must speak of produce which is Second Tithe, not of money for which the tithe was redeemed.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 53b" href="/Kiddushin.53b">Babli, 53b</a>, this opinion is attributed to R. Johanan.</i>.
Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Pedaiah: Intentionally desecrated Temple property becomes profane without redemption<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">This explains why for R. Meïr a marriage effected by intentional misuse of Temple property is valid.</i>. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra Wayyiqra Parašah</i> 11(8), (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 32b" href="/Pesachim.32b">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 32b</a>) referring to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:15" href="/Leviticus.5.15"><i>Lev</i>. 5:15</a> dealing with larceny committed with sacrifices or Temple property, explicitly restricted to unintentional sin.</i>: “In error,” that excludes intention? That you say for the additional fifth<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">He extends the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:14-16" href="/Leviticus.5.14-16"><i>Lev</i>. 5:14–16</a> to all Temple property. The inadvertent use of Temple property constitutes larceny which requires a reparation offering and restitution in the amount of 125%, but the object taken becomes profane and, therefore, can be used as marriage gift.</i> and the sacrifice, but profaned it is<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">Since intentional sin is excluded from expiation by sacrifice, the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:14-16" href="/Leviticus.5.14-16"><i>Lev</i>. 5:14–16</a> do not apply.</i>. Rebbi Ḥama bar Uqba in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: The excluded well-being offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">Well-being offerings, which remain the property of the donor and of which only a small part is given to the altar and the priests, is excluded from the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:14-16" href="/Leviticus.5.14-16"><i>Lev</i>. 5:14–16</a>, as will be explained immediately following.</i> become profane without redemption. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra Wayyiqra Pereq</i> 20(1), referring to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:15" href="/Leviticus.5.15"><i>Lev</i>. 5:15</a>, speaking of larceny committed “<i>from</i> the Eternal’s sancta”. In rabbinic interpretation, prefix מ or מִן always implies “some, not all”. In this case, the verse is taken to refer to “<i>the Eternal’s</i> sancta”, no part of which is private property. This excludes well-being and other simple sacrifices but includes sacrifices given to the priests since these offerings always remain Heaven’s property (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.7.1">Note 169</a>).</i>: “From the sancta,” not all sancta? That you say for the additional fifth and the sacrifice, but profaned it is<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">Since intentional sin is excluded from expiation by sacrifice, the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:14-16" href="/Leviticus.5.14-16"><i>Lev</i>. 5:14–16</a> do not apply.</i>.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 7:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.7.3.1">Mishnah <i>Šeqalim</i> 7:4</a>. The present paragraph has a parallel there in Halakhah 7:6 of which, however, it is not a direct copy.</i>, we have stated: “An animal that is found as from Jerusalem to Migdal-Eder in every direction<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">If an animal which is a potential sacrifice (cattle, sheep, or goats) is found ownerless near Jerusalem, one may assume that it was dedicated as sacrifice but escaped from its owner. Elevation offerings must be male (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:2" href="/Leviticus.1.2"><i>Lev</i>. 1:2</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:10" href="/Leviticus.1.10">10</a>); well-being sacrifices can be of either gender. Most well-being offerings were bought from redemption monies of Second Tithe. The Mishnah precribes that male animals be used for elevation offerings, females for well-being offerings.</i>.” The elder Rebbi Hoshaia said, this was taught for their monetary value<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">It is impossible to say that a male animal which was found ownerless should be taken as elevation offering since it might have been dedicated as well-being offering. The animal should be redeemed and the redemption money used for an elevation offering. The same argument is given in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 55a" href="/Kiddushin.55a">Babli, 55a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said to him, does one say to a person, go and commit larceny with sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">It is impossible to redeem an unblemished dedicated animal (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:10" href="/Leviticus.27.10"><i>Lev</i>. 27:10</a>). Any redemption is both sinful and ineffective. R. Hoshiah’s rule seems to be impossible.</i>? But in every case<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">The animal itself should be used for what was its most probable dedication.</i> they followed the majority of cases: If most are male, they are elevation offerings, if most are female, well-being offerings. But do not well-being offerings come from males and females? What does he do? He redeems them as profane and then turns them into elevation offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">One follows both R. Hoshaia and R. Joḥanan. The animal is first redeemed and then rededicated; this precludes the sin of freeing a dedicated animal and removes the prior specific dedication. (In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 55b" href="/Kiddushin.55b">Babli, 55b</a>, R. Joḥanan holds that the redemption of unblemished animals is never possible. He requires that the aninmal be put out to graze until it develops a blemish; then it can be redeemed and the proceeds used for another sacrifice.)</i>. This is difficult. Can a purification offering<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">This reading is unacceptable since purification offerings are female (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:28" href="/Leviticus.4.28"><i>Lev</i>. 4:28</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:32" href="/Leviticus.4.32">32</a>). One must read: “well-being offering.” This argument is missing in <i>Šeqalim</i>.</i> become an elevation offering? Rebbi Ḥanina said, it is a stipulation by the court that all redundant animals should be brought as elevation offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, it is part of every dedication of any male animal that, if not needed for its original purpose, it should be used as an elevation offering.</i>. Rebbi Yose said to Rebbi Jacob bar Ḥama, is that not intentional [misuse]? He said to him, since it is a stipulation by the court, it is not intentional [misuse]. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, as you say there, it is a stipulation by the court that lost animals should be brought as elevation offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">It is also part of every dedication of any male animal that, if lost and then found by another person, it should become an elevation offering.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a man performs preliminary marriage using ‘<i>orlah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">The fruit of a tree in the first three years after planting whose usufruct is forbidden (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:23-24" href="/Leviticus.19.23-24"><i>Lev</i>. 19:23–24</a>; cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>‘Orlah</i>.) The entire list consists of items forbidden for usufruct. Since marriage is to the man’s advantage, items forbidden for usufruct cannot be used as marriage gifts.</i>, or <i>kilaim</i> of a vineyard<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">While mixtures of seeds are always forbidden (cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Kilaim</i>), only foreign produce in a vineyard is forbidden for usufruct (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:9" href="/Deuteronomy.22.9"><i>Deut</i>. 22:9</a>).</i>, or an ox sentenced to be stoned<sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">An animal which killed a human (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:28" href="/Exodus.21.28"><i>Ex</i>. 21:28</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:29" href="/Exodus.21.29">29</a>).</i>, or a calf whose neck was broken<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote">To atone for an unsolved murder (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:1-9" href="/Deuteronomy.21.1-9"><i>Deut</i>. 21:1–9</a>); cf. Tractate <i>Soṭah</i>, Chapter 9.</i>, or the birds of a sufferer from skin disease<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote">The two birds the recovered sufferer from skin disease needs for his purification (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:1-7" href="/Leviticus.14.1-7"><i>Lev</i>. 14:1–7</a>).</i>, the hair of a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">Which must be burned when his sacrifice is cooked, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:18" href="/Numbers.6.18"><i>Num</i>. 6:18</a>.</i>, or the first-born of a she-ass<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote">This is forbidden for usufruct only before it was redeemed by a lamb, or whose neck broken. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:11-13" href="/Exodus.13.11-13"><i>Ex</i>. 13:11–13</a>. The question of the biblical root of the prohibition of usufruct is raised in the Halakhah.</i>, or meat cooked in milk<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:19" href="/Exodus.23.19"><i>Ex</i>. 23:19</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:26" href="/Exodus.34.26">34:26</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:21" href="/Deuteronomy.14.21"><i>Deut</i>. 14:21</a>.</i>, or profane meat slaughtered in the Temple precinct<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote">Forbidden for usufruct by rabbinic interpretation.</i>, [the woman] is not preliminarily married. If he sells any of these items<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote">While the sale is sinful, the coins received in payment are not forbidden.</i> and uses their proceeds for preliminary marriage, she is preliminarily married.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a man performs preliminary marriage using <i>‘orlah</i>, or <i>kilaim</i> of a vineyard,” etc. The first-born of a she-ass, Rebbi Eleazar said, may be used to preliminarily marry a woman; Rebbi Joḥanan said, it may not be used to preliminarily marry a woman. Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: The first-born of a she-ass may be used to preliminarily marry a woman by an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i>. Since you say that a firstling, whose prohibition cannot be lifted during its lifetime, can be used to preliminarily marry a woman, this one, whose prohibition can be lifted during its lifetime, not so much more<sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote">The firstlings of cattle, sheep, or goats (the animals admitted to the altar) must be given to a Cohen. If without blemish, the animal must be sacrificed and eaten by Cohanim. If blemished, it remains the property of the Cohen and may be eaten by any Jew. In both cases, the living animal cannot be used for any work. The only use permitted is as sanctified or profane food; as far as use goes it is forbidden during its lifetime and becomes permitted by slaughter. But the firstling of a she-ass is supposed to be redeemed by the gift of a lamb to a Cohen; by this act, the donkey becomes totally profane and can be put to work immediately.</i>? What is the difference between them<sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote">What additional differences does one find between Rabbis Eleazar and Joḥanan?</i>? If somebody transgressed and redeemed it without the knowledge of its owner<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote">A third party gave a lamb to a Cohen to redeem the firstborn of the she-ass, without knowledge of the latter’s owner.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, it is not redeemed<sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Eleazar the unredeemed donkey is his owner’s property; it cannot be redeemed without the owner’s knowledge. For R. Joḥanan the unredeemed donkey is not the owner’s money, being forbidden for the latter’s usufruct, but it can be redeemed by a third party.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, it is redeemed<sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Eleazar the unredeemed donkey is his owner’s property; it cannot be redeemed without the owner’s knowledge. For R. Joḥanan the unredeemed donkey is not the owner’s money, being forbidden for the latter’s usufruct, but it can be redeemed by a third party.</i>. A <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Eleazar and disagrees with him. A <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Eleazar: <sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 11a" href="/Bekhorot.11a">Babli, <i>Bekhorot</i>11a</a>. The translation follows the Babli in reading בּוֹ “in it” instead of בן “son” of the ms.</i> If somebody steals the first-born of another person’s she-ass, he has to pay double restitution<sup class="footnote-marker">226</sup><i class="footnote">This implies that the unredeemed firstling represents property value, agreeing with R. Eleazar.</i>. The end<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote">The second half of the <i>baraita</i> quoted, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:8:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.8.2">Note 225</a>.</i> disagrees with him: For even though he has no interest in it now he will later<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote">Since by giving a lamb to the Cohen the owner can establish ownership of the donkey. This agrees with R. Joḥanan’s opinion that the unredeemed firstling donkey is not his owner’s property.</i>. The Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Eleazar: One may not use the first-born of a she-ass to preliminarily marry a woman<sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote">A paraphrase of Mishnah 9.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar explained that, after breaking of [the foal’s] neck. For Rebbi Eleazar said, everybody agrees that after its neck was broken it cannot be redeemed<sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote">If the owner prefers to break the neck of his firstling donkey rather than give a lamb to a Cohen, R. Eleazar agrees that by this act he has lost his ownership and the carcass is forbidden for any usufruct. The Babli agrees in the name of Babylonian authorities, 57b; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 10b" href="/Bekhorot.10b"><i>Bekhorot</i> 10b</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote">This is a new <i>baraita</i>; in slightly different formulation it appears in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 10b-11a" href="/Bekhorot.10b-11a">Babli, <i>Bekhorot</i> 10b–11a</a>.</i>“One may not use the first-born of a she-ass to preliminarily marry a woman, but Rebbi Simeon permits it.” That disagrees with Rebbi Eleazar in two respects. If during its lifetime, it disagrees following the rabbis; if after breaking the neck, it disagrees following Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>baraita</i> does not specify the status of the firstling. It is clear that after redemption the firstling is profane and can be used as a marriage gift. If the <i>baraita</i> speaks of the living unredeemed firstling, it contradicts R. Eleazar’s statement that it can be given as marriage gift. If it refers to the dead firstling, the assertion that R. Simeon permits its use contradicts the statement that everybody prohibits the dead fistling.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanina in the name of Rebbi Yudan: Explain it that it died<sup class="footnote-marker">233</sup><i class="footnote">It died a natural death before being redeemed. In this version, R. Simeon will agree that usufruct is forbidden after its neck was broken. This is the Babli’s explanation, <i>loc</i>.<i>cit</i>. Note 230.</i>. If he broke its neck, it is forbidden for usufruct, but Rebbi Simeon permits it. <sup class="footnote-marker">234</sup><i class="footnote">A new <i>baraita</i>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 10b" href="/Bekhorot.10b">Babli <i>Bekhorot</i>10b</a>; <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Ismael Pisḥa</i> 18 (Horovitz-Rabin p. 71); <i>Mekhilta dR</i>.<i>Simeon ben Ioḥai</i> 13:13 (Epstein-Melamed p. 43). Then the comparison is between breaking the neck of the firstling and of the calf which atones for an unsolved murder. Since the calf has to be buried and its grave is forbidden for all usufruct (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:5:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.5.10"><i>Soṭah</i> 9:5, Note 132</a>) it is clear that the carcass itself is forbidden for all usufruct.</i>“Breaking the neck, breaking the neck.” Since “breaking the neck” mentioned there implies that one has to break its neck, bury it, and it is forbidden for usufruct, so “breaking of the neck” mentioned here implies that one has to break its neck, [bury it,]<sup class="footnote-marker">235</sup><i class="footnote">Added from all parallel sources and a Genizah fragment.</i> and it is forbidden for usufruct. Following Rebbi Eleazar it is understandable. He permits during its lifetime; he needs an argument to forbid it after the breaking of the neck. Following Rebbi Joḥanan it is difficult. If it is forbidden during its lifetime, not so much more after breaking of its neck? There came Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, Rebbi Ze‘ira in the name of the rabbis: It is needed following the rabbis, lest you say that since the Torah combined the breaking of its neck with its redemption<sup class="footnote-marker">236</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibition of usufruct of the firstling after its neck was broken is far from clear from the biblical text. Redemption by a lamb or breaking the neck are mentioned in the same verse (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:13" href="/Exodus.13.13"><i>Ex</i>. 13:13</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:20" href="/Exodus.34.20">34:20</a>); one might assume that both follow the same rule. The same explanation is given in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 10b" href="/Bekhorot.10b">Babli, <i>Bekhorot</i> 10b</a>.</i>, and its redemption makes it profane, so also the breaking of its neck should make it profane<sup class="footnote-marker">236a</sup><i class="footnote">A Genizah fragment adds here:<br>לפם כן צרך מימר עריפה ער' וגו'. ר' יעקב בר אחא בש' רב י.. ... שמעון את חכמין. <br> Therefore it was necessary to say "breaking the neck, breaking the neck." R. Jacob bar Aha in the name of Reb[bi] Y[ohanan].... one understands this for the Sages.<br>The text seems to be redundant; its omission in the Leiden text may be original.</i>. Do we find anything whose lifting makes profane and which is forbidden for usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">237</sup><i class="footnote">This challenges R. Joḥanan’s position that the living unredeemed firstling is forbidden for ususfruct. In comparable cases, untithed produce (<i>ṭevel)</i> or sacrificial animals which developed a blemish, redemption transfers holiness to the redeeming object. But in the case of the donkey both it and the redeeming lamb are totally profane.</i>? They objected: There is <i>ṭevel</i> containing the tithe of the poor<sup class="footnote-marker">238</sup><i class="footnote">In general, <i>ṭevel</i> is produce from which either the required heave or the tithe was not taken; its consumption is sinful. The tithe of the poor is due in the third and sixth years of a Sabbatical cycle, when no Second Tithe is due. Its only claim to biblical status is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:28" href="/Deuteronomy.14.28"><i>Deut</i>. 14:28</a>. Second Tithe, which must be eaten near the Sanctuary, has a status of holiness; produce from which Second Tithe was not taken is recognized as <i>ṭevel</i>(<i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i> 3:5, Notes 61ff.) But the tithe of the poor is totally profane; produce from which this tithe was not taken is referred to as <i>ṭevel</i> only here.</i> which becomes totally profane by its lifting; but Rebbi Abba, Rav Huna said in the name of Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">239</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that one should read “Rav” instead of “Rebbi”.</i>: A person eating produce which is <i>ṭevel</i> for the tithe of the poor is guilty of a deadly sin. The rabbis of Caesarea in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah: So the rabbis answer Rebbi Simeon: Do we find anything that needs redemption and is permitted for usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">240</sup><i class="footnote">Any dedicated animals which become disqualified as sacrifices are forbidden for all use before being redeemed.</i>? He replied to them: There is the first-born human who needs redemption and is permitted for usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">241</sup><i class="footnote">A first-born human male whose father did not redeem him by giving five <i>Šeqalim</i> to a Cohen must redeem himself when adult, but nowhere is there any restriction on his earning power before redemption.</i>.
“If he sells any of these items and uses their proceeds for preliminary marriage, he is married.” Rebbi Ḥaggai in the name of Rebbi Ze‘ira: Because it is not its proceeds. Rebbi Ḥanina said, this means that one cannot marry preliminary with [the proceeds of] robbery<sup class="footnote-marker">242</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is quoted by Rosh (<i>Qiddušin</i> 2, #31) as follows:<br>רִבִּי חַגַּי בְשֵׁם רְבִּי זְעִירי, שֶׁאֵין תוֹפְסִין דְּמֵיהֶן. אָמַר רַבִּי חֲנִינָא, זֹּאת אוֹמֶרֶת שֶׁמְּקַדְּשִׁין בְּגְזֵילָה . "Rebbi Haggai in the name of Rebbi Ze‘iri: Because (the prohibition of usufruct) is not transferred to its proceeds. Rebbi Hanina said, this means that one can marry preliminarly with (the proceeds of) robbery."<br>However, Ran (<i>Novellae Baba meṣi‘a</i>7:1), Rashba (<i>Novellae ad</i> 57b), Ritba (<i>Novellae ad</i> 56b), and Meïri (<i>Bet Habeḥira Qiddušin</i>, ed. A Sopher, p. 261) all read the statement of R. Ḥaggai in the version of the Leiden ms. but that of R. Ḥanina in the version of Rosh. [It seems that most authors did not have access to the Yerushalmi but copied from Ran (R. Nissim Gerondi); they cannot be counted as independent sources.] His arguments and those of the other medieval authorities are discussed in great detail and at length by J. Rosanes (<i>Mishneh Lammelekh</i>, Maimonides <i>Hilkhot Iššut</i> 5:7)]. In the opinion of the Babli (Rashi <i>Ḥulin</i> 4a, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 54a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.54a"><i>‘Avodah zarah</i> 54a</a>), the prohibition of usufruct is not transferred to the (illegitimate) proceeds except in the cases of idols and Sabbatical produce. However, our ms. sources (including the Constantinople print edition) do not support Ran’s reading of R. Ḥanina; one has to accept the ms. reading as it stands.<br>The statement of R. Ḥaggai is straightforward and was explained by Ran. Since objects forbidden for usufruct are worthless, the money received by the seller was not payment for the object. Since it was necessary to point out that the marriage was valid, it follows that if the money had been given in exchange for an object acquired unlawfully, the marriage would be invalid.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a man perform preliminary marriage using heaves<sup class="footnote-marker">243</sup><i class="footnote">Which can be consumed only by a Cohen.</i>, or tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">244</sup><i class="footnote">Which either are given to a Levite, or a poor person, or must be eaten in purity in Jerusalem.</i>, or gifts to priests<sup class="footnote-marker">245</sup><i class="footnote">From slaughtered profane animals, prescribed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 18:3" href="/Deuteronomy.18.3"><i>Deut</i>. 18:3</a>.</i>, or money for purification<sup class="footnote-marker">246</sup><i class="footnote">It is not clear what this money represents; probably the sums of restitution mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:16" href="/Leviticus.5.16"><i>Lev</i>. 5:16</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:8" href="/Numbers.5.8"><i>Num</i>. 5:8</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 12:17" href="/II_Kings.12.17"><i>2K</i>. 12:17</a>). In the Babli, most independent Mishnah mss., and in the Halakhah one reads מֵי חַטָּאת “purification water”, the water used in the purification rite of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num</i>. 19</a>.</i>, or the ashes of purification<sup class="footnote-marker">247</sup><i class="footnote">Ashes from the burning of the Red Cow, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num</i>. 19</a>. This is very precious material.</i>, she is married even if he is an Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">248</sup><i class="footnote">While an Israel and his wife have no use for most of these items, they can sell them to Cohanim. The items have value and can be used as marriage gifts.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">249</sup><i class="footnote">This text is from <i>Demay</i> 6:3, explained there in Notes 59–76 and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 11:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.11.3.3"><i>Nedarim</i> 11:3, Note 46</a>. It seems that the text in <i>Demay</i> is the original source.</i> Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina said, a person gives his tithes for the benefit of goodwill. What is the reason of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:10" href="/Numbers.5.10"><i>Num</i>. 5:10</a>) “Everybody shall be the owner of his holy things.” How does Rebbi Joḥanan explain this? He may give them to whomever he likes.
A Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥaninah: “A vow that no Cohanim or Levites should have any advantage from me: they should take against his will.” He explains it about a person who says, I cannot possibly give them <i>any</i> gifts. You should know that this is so, since we have stated: “<i>These</i> Cohanim and Levites should [not] have any advantage from me; let others take.” A <i>baraita</i> disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan: “One may say, here you have a tetradrachma and give this firstling to my daughter’s son, a Cohen.” He explains, if he already wanted to give it to two Cohanim and that daughter’s son was one of them; then one said, here you have a tetradrachma and give it whole to my daughter’s son, a Cohen.
They asked before Rebbi Zeïra: a Cohen to an Israel, Rebbi forbids. What does Rebbi Yose say? He did not respond. Rebbi Ḥizqiah in the name of Rebbi Aḥa said, so he said to them: In the opinion of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina, why is a Cohen to an Israel forbidden, not because it looks badly? Also Rebbi Joḥanan holds that from an Israel to an Israel it is forbidden because it looks badly. In addition, because of the following, as it was stated: “Cohanim and Levites who help at the threshing floor have no right either to heave or to tithe, and if the farmer gave, it is desecrated, as it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:15" href="/Leviticus.22.15"><i>Lev</i>. 22:15</a>): ‘They should not desecrate the sanctified things of the Children of Israel,’ but they do desecrate them! In addition, they said that their heave is no heave, their tithes are no tithes, their dedications are no dedications, and about them the verse says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Micah 3:11" href="/Micah.3.11"><i>Micha</i>3:11</a>): ‘Their heads judge for bribes, their priests instruct for hire, etc.’ The Omnipresent brings over them three catastrophes; that is what is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Micah 3:12" href="/Micah.3.12"><i>Micha</i> 3:12</a>): ‘Therefore, because of you Zion will be ploughed over as a field, Jerusalem will be desolate, and the Temple Mount a wooded hill.’
A Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan: “If a man performs preliminary marriage using heaves, or tithes, or gifts to priests, or water for purification, or the ashes of purification, she is married even if he is an Israel.” He explains it with heave he inherited from his maternal grandfather, a Cohen.

Chapter 3



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody said to another: go and marry me preliminarily to woman X, and [the agent] went and married her preliminarily himself, she is preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">A person who accepts agency does not by this act lose the ability to legally act in his own behalf. As long as the agent did not sign a contract which subjects him to a fine unless he execute what he promised, the principal may consider him a trickster but has no legal recourse.</i>. Also, if somebody said to a woman, you are preliminarily married to me after thirty days and another man married her preliminarily within these thirty days, she is preliminarily married to the second man<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">At the moment at which the second man married her, she was legally single; his marriage is valid. After thirty days, when the first man’s preliminary marriage should enter into force, she already is a married woman. The preliminary marriage of a married woman to another man is nonexistent in law. It is not forbidden; only actions which exist in law can be forbidden.</i>; as a daughter of an Israel married to a Cohen she may eat heave<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">If the groom is a Cohen, she is married to the Cohen; the existence of the first man is irrelevant. But if the first man gave her a wedding gift and said: you are preliminarily married to me now but the mutual legal obligations shall start only after 30 days (which would give the groom 13 months to arrange the definitive wedding), and if within these thirty days another man preliminarily married her unconditionally, neither of the two marriages can be unquestionably valid. Therefore, she needs valid bills of divorce from both men. [In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:3:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.3.6"><i>Giṭṭin</i>7:3 (Note 80</a>) Rebbi disagrees and holds that the preliminary marriage of the second man disappears at the end of 30 days; this opinion is adopted by Samuel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 59b" href="/Kiddushin.59b">Babli, 59b</a>, but not mentioned in the Yerushalmi <i>Qiddušin</i>.]</i>. From today and after thirty days, and another man married her preliminarily within these thirty days, she is and is not preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">If the groom is a Cohen, she is married to the Cohen; the existence of the first man is irrelevant. But if the first man gave her a wedding gift and said: you are preliminarily married to me now but the mutual legal obligations shall start only after 30 days (which would give the groom 13 months to arrange the definitive wedding), and if within these thirty days another man preliminarily married her unconditionally, neither of the two marriages can be unquestionably valid. Therefore, she needs valid bills of divorce from both men. [In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:3:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.3.6"><i>Giṭṭin</i>7:3 (Note 80</a>) Rebbi disagrees and holds that the preliminary marriage of the second man disappears at the end of 30 days; this opinion is adopted by Samuel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 59b" href="/Kiddushin.59b">Babli, 59b</a>, but not mentioned in the Yerushalmi <i>Qiddušin</i>.]</i>. As a daughter of an Israel married to a Cohen or daughter of a Cohen married to an Israel she may not eat heave<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The daughter of an Israel can eat heave only if she is certainly married to a Cohen; the daughter of a Cohen can eat heave only if she certainly is not married outside the clan.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody says to another: go and marry me preliminarily to woman X,” etc. He looks out for himself and is rewarded since his acquisition is valid, but he behaved treacherously<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 58b" href="/Kiddushin.58b">Babli 58b</a>.</i>. The same holds for monetary transactions. If somebody says to another: go and buy for me goods X, and [the agent] went and bought [the merchandise] for himself, he looks out for himself and is rewarded since his acquisition is valid, but he behaved treacherously<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 59a" href="/Kiddushin.59a">Babli, 59a</a>, agrees in general but points out that an agent must have some flexibility in warding off claims by third parties; it might be to the advantage of the principal if the agent acquires for himself rather than let the property fall into hostile hands.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira cursed those who saw another person trying to buy certain goods and outbid him.
