Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Ze‘ira: Also about one who organizes a group against another<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">S. Lieberman [<i>Tarbiz</i> 4 (1937), p. 379] explains that simply by having a group of people showing interest, even if no competing bid ensues, the hand of the seller is strengthened and the buyer forced to increase his bid.</i>, the rabbis quote: “A person who prevents a good thing coming to his neighbor abandons the fear of the Almighty.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 6:14" href="/Job.6.14"><i>Job</i> 6:14</a>, interpretation of Targum. (In a Genizah fragment: “The rabbis <i>of Caesarea</i> quote.”)</i>”
If the agent was not identified as such by the witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The witnesses to the preliminary marriage say that they did not know that the male was appointed as agent and, therefore, they did not listen carefully whether the male stated that he was marrying the woman for himself or for a third party.</i> and he says, I married her preliminarily for myself, but she says, to the first, the second is like one who says to a woman: I married you preliminarily but she says, you did not marry me preliminarily<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The case treated in Mishnah 11. The party which asserts the existence of the marriage is forbidden to marry the close relatives of the other party. The party which denies the existence of a marriage is not forbidden to marry any close relative of the other party.</i>. Also it is as if she said to the first, you married me preliminarily but he says, I did not marry you preliminarily. If she says, I do not know, the presumption is in favor of the second<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">If the ceremony was not announced as one of marriage by proxy and nobody noticed anything unusual in the formulas being used, it was not a marriage by proxy and the woman is certainly married to the agent.</i>. If the agent was identified as such by the witnesses and he says, I married her preliminarily for myself, but she says, to the first, the presumption is in favor of the first<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">If the agent was announced as a proxy and nobody noticed anything out of the ordinary, it was a marriage by proxy.</i>. If she said, I do not know, both of them give a bill of divorce or, if they so desire, one gives a bill of divorce and the other marries definitively.
Therefore<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Referring to the second part of the Mishnah, about the man who stipulated that the preliminary marriage be effective only after 30 days.</i>, if the second died within thirty days or divorced her, the preliminary marriage of the first is activated after thirty days. If he died after thirty [days] without having divorced her, the preliminary marriage of the first is not activated<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">If the second man was preliminarily married to her at the end of 30 days, the first preliminary marriage would be marriage to an already married woman; it is null and void. It cannot be reactivated even if the second man later dies.</i>. That is what Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: With any condition which was fulfilled<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">At the time of the activation of the preliminary marriage.</i>, even if later it becomes moot, she is preliminarily married. But with any condition which was not satisfied at the moment of preliminary marriage, even if later it became moot, she is not preliminarily married.
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: “This animal shall be an elevation sacrifice after thirty days.” If he sold it within the thirty days, it is sold; its dedication sanctifies<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Since nobody can dedicate anything which is not his, the first dedication was invalidated by the sale; the buyer may use the animal for any sacrifice, not just as elevation sacrifice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 2:9:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.2.9.4"><i>Nazir</i> 2:9, Note 120</a>).</i>. If he bought it back within the thirty days, the dedication as elevation sacrifice applies to it. After thirty days, the dedication as elevation sacrifice no longer applies<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Since at the end of 30 days the animal was neither his property nor in his possession.</i>. But an elevation sacrifice cannot be compared to a woman<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">As R. Abun bar Ḥiyya notes later in this paragraph, dedications follow rules quite different from acquisitions; it is difficult to understand why the dedicated animal could validly be sold.</i>! Could an elevation sacrifice be compared to a woman? Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada said, explain it that the second man died<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">He died childless. While the widow is not automatically married to the levir, she is prevented from marrying any other man unless she receives <i>ḥalîṣah</i> from him. Therefore, the preliminary marriage of the first is void even if the second man, whose preliminary marriage was valid immediately, dies within the thirty days.</i> but he had a brother. Since she is a candidate for levirate, the preliminary marriage of the first cannot be valid. Could an elevation sacrifice be compared to a woman? Rebbi Mattania said, explain it that he dedicated an animal with a permanent defect.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Then the dedication was not as elevation sacrifice but stipulated that the animal be sold and the money used as elevation sacrifice. In that case, it would not be necessary that the original owner buy the animal back but only that the buyer renounce his property rights before the end of 30 days.</i> Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked before Rebbi Ze‘ira: There you say, my promise to Heaven is like my delivery to a private person<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.6.1">Mishnah 1:6</a>. If the animal is immediately delivered to the Temple, it cannot be sold.</i>, and here, you say so? He answered him: there, immediately, but here if he says, after thirty days<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.6.1">Mishnah 1:6</a>. If the animal is immediately delivered to the Temple, it cannot be sold.</i>.
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: “This shall be an elevation sacrifice for thirty days,” it is an elevation sacrifice for thirty days; after thirty days it automatically becomes profane<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">A different version, describing an animal designated as (most holy) elevation offering for 30 days and afterwards as (simply holy) well-being offering, is quoted as tannaïtic text in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 29a" href="/Nedarim.29a">Babli, <i>Nedarim</i> 29a</a>.</i>. What is its status regarding larceny<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">An animal dedicated as sacrifice is forbidden for all use; it cannot be used for work, nor can it be shorn for its wool. The question is whether illicit use of an animal temporarily declared a sacrifice triggers the penalties spelled out in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:14-16" href="/Leviticus.5.14-16"><i>Lev</i>. 5:14–16</a>.</i>? Rebbi Joḥanan said, the larceny is clear<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Since during the 30 days it is a sacrifice, if it was used then all the penalties are due.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira, Rebbi Hila both say, the larceny is in doubt<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">If the animal was sacrificed during the thirty days, any prior use was larceny. But if the animal was not sacrificed, then it reverts to profane status and its use was not illegitimate; it would be forbidden to dedicate an animal to atone for the larceny. The prior use was an “evil that cannot be corrected.”</i>. “You are preliminarily married to me for thirty days,” she is preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Permanently, even after 30 days.</i>. What is the difference between dedication and woman? We find that a dedication can be eliminated without redemption but we do not find that a woman could leave without a divorce document. Where do we find that a dedication can be eliminated without redemption? Following Rebbi Simeon, since Rebbi Simeon says, “they enter without paying.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>‘Arakhin</i> 7:4. If a field donated to the Temple was not redeemed before the Jubilee year, it becomes profane property of the Cohanim (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:21" href="/Leviticus.27.21"><i>Lev</i>. 27:21</a>). In R. Simeon’s opinion, the reversion to profane status is automatic; the Cohanim to whom it is distributed do not have to redeem it from the Temple treasury.</i>” Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, explain it according to everybody regarding a bought field<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">A field which was not a family heirloom but bought from another owner automatically reverts to its hereditary owner in the Jubilee year. Even if in the meantime the field was given to the Temple by the buyer, it reverts without payment (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:24" href="/Leviticus.27.24"><i>Lev</i>. 27:24</a>).</i>. “This is your bill of divorce for thirty days”: it is no bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is implied that after 30 days there should be no divorce, it is invalid. A divorce must be irrevocable to be valid (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:8:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.8.3"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 4:8, Note 192</a>). A shortened version of the text is discussed by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ran on Nedarim 29a" href="/Ran_on_Nedarim.29a">Ran, <i>Nedarim</i> 29a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Isaac ben R. Eleazar said, when do you say that she is preliminarily married? If he effectuated the preliminary marriage by money. But if he effectuated the preliminary marriage by a document, since marriage documents were inferred from divorce documents<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:18" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.18">Chapter 1, Note 21</a>.</i> and in the case of a divorce she is not divorced, so in the case of a preliminary marriage she is not preliminarily married.
Rebbi Abbahu<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this name is incorrect since earlier R. Abbahu stated that the animal may be validly sold (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.1.5">Note 16</a>). Since the name tradition in the first case is confirmed by a parallel, the quote here is the one to be questioned.</i> in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: “This animal shall be an elevation sacrifice after thirty days;” if he sold it during the thirty days, it is not sold; its dedication does not sanctify<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">This Amora holds that even “a delayed promise to Heaven is like an immediate delivery to a private person” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.1.5">Note 21</a>).</i>. For which purpose did he say: “after thirty”? To reserve for himself shearing and work<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">For the first 30 days.</i>. “Pick from this field<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">This is authorization of transfer of ownership following <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.5.1">Mishnah 1:5</a>. Title to the field is transferred immediately even if actual possession by the buyer is deferred.</i> that it should be acquired by you after thirty days;” if he sold it within thirty days it is not sold, if he<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The seller.</i> dedicated it it is not sanctified. Why did he say, after thirty days? To reserve usufruct for himself<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The seller may harvest the field for the next 30 days.</i>. “Here you have this tetradrachma that your field should be mine after thirty days<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">While real estate may be acquired by money (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.5.1">Mishnah 1:5</a>), the language implies that both ownership and possession will be deferred for 30 days.</i>,” if he sold it within thirty days it is not sold, if he dedicated it it is not sanctified. What is the difference between this and a woman<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is stated in the Mishnah that money given now for a later acquisition of a woman in preliminary marriage does not prevent her from marrying another man in the meantime.</i>? But if he sold it, it is sold<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">To exclude any sale to a third party, either ownership or possession must have been transferred at the moment of transaction.</i>.
“Here you have money that your slave shall become acquired by me after thirty days.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in this paragraph, ownership is transferred immediately but possession is deferred for thirty days.</i>” Some Tannaïm stated: The first [owner] is subject to “a day or two”<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:20-21" href="/Exodus.21.20-21"><i>Ex</i>. 21:20–21</a>. Killing a slave is murder. But if the slave dies because of a punishment he receives from his owner and lives at least 24 hours after the punishment, the owner’s act is not prosecutable.</i>. But some Tannaïm stated: The second [owner] is subject to “a day or two”. Some Tannaïm stated: Neither the first nor the second [owners] are subject to “a day or two”. But some Tannaïm stated: Both first and second [owners] are subject to “a day or two”. He<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 50a" href="/Bava_Batra.50a">Babli, <i>Baba batra</i> 50a</a>, R. Meïr.</i> who says, the first [owner] is subject to “a day or two”, “if he dies in his possession<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:20" href="/Exodus.21.20"><i>Ex</i>. 21:20</a>, the verse which declares the owner guilty of murder if he kills his slave.</i>.” He<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, R. Jehudah.</i> who says, the second [owner] is subject to “a day or two”, “for he is his money<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:21" href="/Exodus.21.21"><i>Ex</i>. 21:21</a>, the verse which exempts the owner from prosecution if the slave lives for 24 hours. “His money” indicates ownership.</i>.” He<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, R. Yose.</i> who says, both first and second [owners] are subject to “a day or two”, the first because of “if he dies in his possession,” the second because of “for he is his money.” He<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, R. Eleazar.</i> who says, neither the first nor the second [owners] are subject to “a day or two”, for he is not the first’s money and does not die in the possession of the second.
“As daughter of an Israel married to a Cohen she may eat heave<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">If the first man wanted the preliminary marriage to be valid only after 30 days, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.1.1">Note 3</a>.</i>.” Some Tannaïm state: She may not eat heave. Rebbi Hila said, the one who says that she may eat heave refers to the case that the second man is a Cohen, but the one who says that she may not eat heave refers to the case that the second man is not a Cohen.
“From today and after 30 days.” Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Even preliminary marriages of a hundred men apply to her<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the case where a sequence of men said: “from now and after 30 days,” cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.1.1">Note 3</a>. In this case, she is married to none of them completely; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 3:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.3.4.3"><i>Yebamot</i> 3:5, Notes 102–105</a> (5:1, end; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 10:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.10.5.2"><i>Nedarim</i> 10:6 Note 59</a>); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 60a" href="/Kiddushin.60a">Babli 60a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, it was necessary [to state], even if the second preliminarily married her absolutely<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The interpretation of R. Abbahu is not the only one possible. Since the Mishnah mentions the condition “from now and after 30 days” only for the first man, it is quite possible that the second marries her unconditionally. Then no other man can join the list of suitors but nevertheless the woman is still “married and not married”.</i>. Rebbi Isaac bar Tebele asked before Rebbi Eleazar: As you take it<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">מַה נַפְשֵׁךְ is a technical term implying that a certain conclusion follows from two mutually exclusive premises.</i>; what the first acquired, he acquired; the remainder the second finished<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">This elliptic statement can be explained as follows (R. Moses Margalit): If the statement of the first groom, “from today and after 30 days”, means that he requires 30 days to make up his mind and might annul the preliminary marriage, then it is obvious that the second contracted a valid marriage and the first is eliminated. But if “from today and after 30 days” means that he wants to be married now but his obligations start only after 30 days, the first acquired the right to preliminarily marry the woman after 30 days. If now the second man marries her unconditionally within the thirty days, which the first cannot hinder, his acquisition should have eliminated the option which the first had acquired.</i>. He answered, is this kind of argument applicable to incest prohibitions<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Incest and adultery prohibitions are so serious that no kind of intellectual acrobatics is applicable to them.</i>? How is that? To any woman who is not acquired by one man only, even preliminary marriages of a hundred men apply.
A student asked before Rebbi Ze‘ira: If he gave it to her in the morning on condition that he divorce her in the afternoon<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">From the following it seems that the question was about a case in which A gave a wedding gift to a woman stating that she should be preliminarily married to him “from now after a specified time” but divorcing her before the end of the stated period. B preliminarily married the same woman after A’s conditional marriage but before the divorce. The Mishnah refers to the situation after the end of the 30 day period, when both preliminary marriages are in conflict. That situation may be rectified by at least one divorce. But in the situation described here, the question remains whether a divorce is possible for a marriage which has not yet started.</i>? A certain David was of the opinion that anything acquires<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, a divorce is possible also for a partial marriage.</i>; Rebbi Ze‘ira said that after thirty days he acquires absolutely<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">He questions the possibility of divorce of a not yet existing marriage.<br>A Genizah fragment adds a sentence which was lost by the Leiden scribe by homeoteleuton:<br>ניתגרשה מזה מותר לזה. נית׳ מזה מותר לזה. ניתגרשה משניהן מותר׳ לאיזו … בשלא הוסיף בה השיני<br>קניין גמור.<br>If she was divorced by the first, she is permitted to the second; if she was divorced by the second, she is permitted to the first. If she was divorced by both of them, she is permitted to anybody [she likes]. If the second did not add absolute acquisition;<br>The first two sentences are the conclusion of the previous text. The last sentence is the introduction to the following text. The text does not add anything to the understanding of the Leiden text.</i>. But if the second one added absolute acquisition, I am reading for her: “Her first husband who had sent her away cannot return to take her back.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:4" href="/Deuteronomy.24.4"><i>Deut</i>. 24:4</a>. If the second preliminary marriage was unconditional and the second man died, the first who had given a divorce whose validity may be suspect cannot marry the woman, since for the rule that a divorcee married to another man may not be taken back by her first husband, the divorce and the preliminary marriage to the second man was real.</i>”
If both were Cohanim or brothers, both are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts a discussion of the statement of the Mishnah that the situation can be resolved if one of the men writes a bill of divorce and the other marries the woman. There are cases in which both must divorce and neither of them can marry: A Cohen may not marry a divorcee; this includes a woman who is divorced without ever being really married. The wife of a brother is prohibited to her brother-in- law except in the case of a required levirate marriage. In the case of the Mishnah, if the two men are brothers, the woman is forbidden to either of them as the brother’s wife.</i>. If there were three brothers; two of them contracted a preliminary marriage with her and then died. That is what Rebbi Ḥiyya stated<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 3:10:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.3.10.2"><i>Yebamot</i> 3:10, Note 137</a>.</i>: The wife of one deceased contracts levirate marriage but not the wife of two deceased men. If they were brothers, both contracted a preliminary marriage with her and then one of them died. Is she permitted to the second? As you take it, what he had acquired, he had acquired; the rest he inherits from his brother<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The argument is that the surviving brother should be allowed to marry both in his own right and as levir.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi said, she is forbidden to him. Rebbi Yose said, the reason of Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi is that the aspect acquired in any sister-in-law who is not totally inside [the family] is considered to be under the incest prohibition and an incest prohibition frees the co-wife<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 2:2:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.2.2.5"><i>Yebamot</i> 2:2, Note 55</a>. The argument of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:1:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.1.12">Note 61</a> is invalid: Levirate is possible only if the brother’s marriage was undoubtedly valid.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanina said, Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi said it correctly. Does she not become his because of his brother? To his brother she was forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts a discussion of the statement of the Mishnah that the situation can be resolved if one of the men writes a bill of divorce and the other marries the woman. There are cases in which both must divorce and neither of them can marry: A Cohen may not marry a divorcee; this includes a woman who is divorced without ever being really married. The wife of a brother is prohibited to her brother-in- law except in the case of a required levirate marriage. In the case of the Mishnah, if the two men are brothers, the woman is forbidden to either of them as the brother’s wife.</i>; to him she should be permitted?
“A daughter of a Cohen married to an Israel may not eat heave.” This is a necessary statement, even in case the second man is a Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of the Mishnah seems to be trivial since the daughter of a Cohen married to an Israel is excluded from heave by biblical decree (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:12" href="/Leviticus.22.12"><i>Lev</i>. 22:12</a>). It is stated here that she is excluded even if the claim of the Israel on her is only one of partial marriage.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody says to a woman: You are preliminarily married to me on condition that I give you 200 <i>zuz</i><sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Accadic <i>zūzum</i>, the half-sheqel piece, identified with the Roman <i>denarius</i>.</i>, she is preliminarily married and he is obligated to give<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">It is understood that he also gave a <i>peruṭah’s</i> worth at the time he contracted the preliminary marriage. There is no time limit set for the liquidation of the claim. However, it seems that if she has to sue him in court for the 200 <i>zuz</i>, the preliminary marriage is invalidated.</i>. On condition that I give you within the next 30 days; if he gave her within 30 days she is preliminarily married; otherwise she is not preliminarily married. On condition that I owe 200 <i>zuz</i>, she is preliminarily married if he has it. On condition that I show you 200 <i>zuz</i>, she is preliminarily married once he shows it to her<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">He assures her that she is marrying a person of means; nevertheless it is not sufficient that he prove to her that he has the means; in order to be married she must be shown the actual coins.</i>; but if he showed it on his bank she is not preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">If he is a banker, handling his depositors’ money, he cannot show anything which is not his own capital invested in his business.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody says to a woman: You are preliminarily married to me,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">This is a reformulation of the first paragraph of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.5.2"><i>Giṭṭin</i>, Halakhah 7:5, Notes 116–118</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 60b" href="/Kiddushin.60b">Babli, 60b</a>, the rabbis who disagree with Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel hold that the conditional divorcee never is subject to <i>ḥalîṣah</i> or levirate marriage.</i> There, we have stated: “This is your bill of divorce on condition that you give me 200 <i>zuz</i>, she is divorced and has to pay. If she did not manage to pay before he died, it was stated: Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, she pays his father or his brother and is free from <i>ḥalîṣah</i> and levirate marriage. The same holds for preliminary marriage: “You are preliminarily married to me on condition that I give you 200 <i>zuz</i>;” if he did not manage to pay before he died, Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, his father or his brothers pay and she is subject to <i>ḥalîṣah</i> or levirate marriage.
<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">This text is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.6.3"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 7:6, Notes 132–139</a>; the contract text also in <i>Eruvin</i> 3 and later in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:3:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.3.2-4">Halakhah 3</a>. A. Gulak (<i>Tarbiz</i> 5, 1934, pp. 126–133) has discussed the text in detail.<br>The term σύμφωνον “agreement” appears in Egyptian papyri of the Byzantine period (after the middle of the fifth Century C. E.) and later in Justinian’s legislation to designate special stipulations within a written contract [R. Taubenschlag (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.8">Chapter 1, Note 369</a>) p. 224]. In earlier Greek sources one finds only the adjective σύμφωνος, α, ον “agreeing”. Since the authorities quoted in the Talmud belong to the third Century, the term must have been in use in the Hellenistic world long before it appeared in surviving papyri.<br>As a special stipulation, the <i>symphōn</i> contract has to be executed concurrent with the preliminary marriage; otherwise it could not undo the marriage without a divorce. This supplementary contract fulfills the same role as the Alexandrian <i>Ketubah</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 15:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.15.3.3"><i>Yebamot</i> 15:3, Note 34</a>, and the sources quoted there.)<br>On the other hand, the delivery of property by the groom as pledge first is documented in Hammurabi’s laws; it appears in Roman contracts as <i>arra</i> (or <i>arrha) sponsalicia (arra</i> = ἀρραβών = עֵרָבוֹן) only in Byzantine times, clearly under the influence of Eastern usage.</i> Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The following is the contract text: “I, X son of Y, contract a preliminary marriage with you, Z, daughter of U, on condition that I give you property A and definitively marry you by day B. If that day should pass without me having taken you in, I shall have no claim on you.” If anything intervened beyond his control? Rebbi Joḥanan said, matters beyond his control are as if he were inactive. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, matters outside his control are as if he had acted. In the opinion of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, what would be necessary? “If that day should pass without you having taken me in, I shall have no claim on you.” When Rebbi Joḥanan was dying, he told his daughters to act following Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. He said, sometime in the future there might be a court which follows him; then his descendants might be in danger of bastardy.
<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The interpretation essentially follows Gulak, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.2.3">Note 69</a>. The paragraph is also discussed by Nachmanides, Nissim Gerondi (Ran), and Yom Tov ben Abraham Išbili (Ritba) in their <i>Novellae</i> to 60a.</i> If the appointed time has arrived<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">And for some reason the engagement was dissolved.</i> and he says, I gave, but she says, I did not receive<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">The valuable object promised in the additional contract.</i>: Rebbi Abun said, since he tries to wrest the contract from the woman’s hand<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The text of the contract specifies that the valuable object remain the woman’s property even if the marriage be cancelled.</i>, the burden of proof is on him. Think of it, maybe there was no contract<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">If there is no written document in the woman’s hand, the burden of proof is on her, not him.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, since he tries to forbid her to anybody, he has to prove that they entered into the contract<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">If they were married, and therefore she is forbidden to any man in the world except her husband, he has to show that the conditions of the contract were adhered to.</i>. A case came before Rebbi Abbahu<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">In which the <i>symphōn</i> stipulations were not satisfied and the marriage did not take place.</i>; he said to him, go and deliver<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The woman stated that she did not receive the <i>symphōn</i> gift; absent proof to the contrary, she has to be believed.</i>. He answered, Rabbi, I did not acquire a wife and you say to me, go and deliver! Rebbi Abbahu said, it never happened to me that one got the better of me except this one. He rephrased and said: If he changed his mind, he shall deliver. If she changed her mind, she shall deliver<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">She has to return the gift or prove that she never received it. [Both Hammurabi’s codex (§§159–161) and the <i>Corpus Iuris</i> (V,l,5) double the value of the <i>arra</i> which has to be returned if the woman’s party decides not to proceed with the marriage. Talmudic law does not seem to have adopted this clause; it would be difficult to read it into R. Abbahu’s decision.]<br>While the Babli does not know the institution of <i>symphōn</i>, A. Gulak sees in the rules of <i>symphōn</i> the precursor of the Medieval contracts of promise of marriage.</i>. Is that not his former judgment<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">He still insists that the man has to deliver the valuable object if he cannot prove prior delivery.</i>? He rephrased to give it the status of a court document<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the next paragraph, the <i>symphōn</i> was drawn up by three witnesses who could act as a court. A court document whose genuinness was attested to cannot be attacked in court.</i>.
The following is the way of contracting: One witness for the groom and one witness for the bride; these two together select a third in order to have two witnesses for each side<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">We do not find in any other talmudic document that the witnesses testify for one party. The witnesses are supposed to testify to the genuineness of the document, equally for both sides. Each side chooses one representative and both together choose a third in the constitution of a court of arbitration (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.3.1.1">Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i> 3:1</a>). The procedure justifies R. Abbahu’s position in the preceding paragraph that every <i>symphōn</i> has the status of a court document.</i>. That is, if not both of them agree. But if both of them agree, both may invalidate the preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">If there is a <i>symphōn</i>, both parties can agree to call off the marriage. If there is a dispute, the document has to be submitted to the competent local court to determine that the relationship can be terminated without a divorce.</i>. As, e. g., <sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">This has nothing directly to do with the topic of <i>symphōn</i>. The reference is to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 13:14:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.13.14.3"><i>Yebamot</i> 13:16, Notes 133–134</a>: A levirate marriage can be contracted only by sexual relations. If the sister-in-law was living in the levir’s house but claims that he never slept with her, she might be able to force him to give her <i>ḥalîṣah</i>. If they both agree, the court must accept their statement even though it may seem unlikely that a man would not sleep with a woman available to him. Similarly in the present case, even though a court will be reluctant to free a preliminarily married woman without a bill of divorce, if both parties agree to the existence of a <i>symphōn</i>, the court will have to hold that the preliminary marriage be void since it was conditional and the condition was not fulfilled.</i>“if he said, I copulated, but she says, I was not copulated with. Even if he changed his story and then said, I did not copulate, he cannot be believed since from the start he had said, I copulated. But if from the start he said that he had not copulated, the two together can uproot the presumption.” If there was one witness for the groom, one witness for the bride, and the groom signed personally: Rebbi Abbahu said, it is a <i>symphōn</i><sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Since there are three signatures, as required.</i>, Rebbi Abba said, it is preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">The groom may not appear as judge in his own case. Since the formal conditions of <i>symphōn</i> are not satisfied, there is no <i>symphōn</i> and the preliminary marriage is unconditional.</i>, for I am saying that he did not really intend a <i>symphōn</i> but thought that it would permit her sister to him<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The groom might have wanted a document which would allow him to marry the bride’s sister if for some reason the preliminary marriage would end in divorce. But preliminary marriage triggers all incest prohibitions; a <i>symphōn</i> is valid only to cancel a preliminary marriage if all rules are carefully observed as required for a court document.</i>.
Only if it be with his and her agreement<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">While preliminary marriage essentially is a unilateral act by the groom where the woman only has a right of refusal but no active role, a <i>symphōn</i> is a contract between parties of equal standing.</i>. If it was <i>symphōn</i> in his opinion and preliminary marriage in her opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">As A. Gulak (<i>loc</i>.<i>cit</i>. Note 69) points out, since the disagreement is about the nature of the contract, not its contents, it is clear that even for the woman the preliminary marriage is conditional and will be void if any of the conditions were not satisfied. The difference in the status of the contract is that in case of preliminary marriage, the woman would have to return the <i>arra</i> and would have no right unilaterally to cancel the marriage.</i>, Rebbi Ḥanina<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">As shown subsequently in the paragraph, he really is the later R. Ḥananiah, not the earlier R. Ḥanina.</i> said, it is <i>symphōn</i>, Rebbi Ḥaggai in the name of Rebbi Ze‘ira said, it is preliminary marriage. Rebbi Ḥanina was taking up arms against Rebbi Ḥaggai, when Rebbi Hila said to him, accept Ḥaggai, for Ḥaggai is a thinking man; this is what Rebbi Ze‘ira said. In the first Adar<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">In an intercalary year, when the 12th month is reduplicated as “second Adar” to make sure that Passover fall after the spring equinox.</i>, Rebbi Hila died. In the second Adar, a case came before Rebbi Ḥananiah, the colleague of the rabbis, and he wanted to rule following Rebbi Ḥaggai. Rebbi Samuel bar Immi told him, Rebbi Hila was wrong when he told you, accept Ḥaggai, for Ḥaggai is a thinking man<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Ḥaggai reported R. Ze‘ira’s opinion, rather than his own.</i>. Rebbi Ḥaggai said, a Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.10.1">Mishnah 3:11</a>. R. Ḥaggai on his own shows that the Mishnah supports R. Ze‘ira since it shows that one party can accept the consequences of preliminary marriage without imposing the same consequences on the other party. Since (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.2.6">Note 89</a>) the preliminary marriage even in the woman’s opinion was conditional, the ability of the groom to unilaterally terminate the relationship is not impaired if one rules following R. Ze‘ira.</i> supports him: “ ‘You married me preliminarily’, and he says, ‘I did not marry you preliminarily.’ He is permitted her relatives but she is forbidden his relatives.” Rebbi Borqai asked before Rebbi Mana: Is it the same with <i>symphōn</i><sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Do the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>:11 also apply to <i>symphōn</i>?</i>? He answered, a <i>symphōn</i> is not doubtful but the rules are identical for <i>symphōn</i> and<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Reading או as (ה)אוּ, not אוֹ (A. Gulak; cf. also S. Lieberman, ו=או=הוא, <i>Tarbiz</i> 4, 1933, pp. 377–378.)</i> preliminary marriage.
If he performed a preliminary marriage during the validity of the <i>symphōn</i>, this is unconditional preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">An unnecessary procedure since based on the <i>symphōn</i> he could proceed immediately with definitive marriage, retroactively validating the <i>symphōn</i> as preliminary marriage. His action overrides the <i>symphōn</i>.</i>. If he divorced<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">During the period specified in the <i>symphōn</i>. This also is unnecessary since the contract permits each party to declare its unwillingness to proceed with the marriage, thereby eliminating the preliminary marriage.</i>, the students of Rebbi Jonah said, divorce touched her<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">She is forbidden to marry a Cohen.</i>; Rebbi Jonah said, divorce did not touch her<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">It is as if a man not married to a woman divorces her; she is not touched by that action and free to marry a Cohen.</i>. When Rebbi Jonah died, his students acted following him. They said, since after 30 [days]<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Or any other period specified in the <i>symphōn</i> as final term for the definitive marriage.</i> she would leave without a bill of divorce, how can you say that divorce touched her? But divorce did not touch her. If he died during the time stipulated in the <i>symphōn</i>, Rebbi Abbahu said, she is permitted to marry; Rebbi Abba said, she is not permitted to marry<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">This discussion presupposes the opinion of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.2.2">Note 68</a>) that the family of a man who dies after conditional preliminary marriage has the option to fulfill the conditions and force the widow to contract levirate marriage with the brother of the deceased. If a <i>symphōn</i> follows the rules of conditional preliminary marriage, the woman cannot marry another man without the family of the deceased giving up their option. The widow/bride naturally could exercise her option to invalidate the <i>symphōn</i>, but then she will have to return the <i>arra</i>.</i>. Rebbi Mana asked the family of Rebbi Yose’s father: How did your master usually act? He said to him, when it was a long time<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">If the end of the period specified in the <i>symphōn</i> was far off, to require the woman to wait would be an unreasonable burden on her.</i>, he said that she should go and marry immediately. If the time was short, he said, what is the hurt if she waits? If he sent things during the validity<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">In addition to the contractual <i>arra</i>, the groom sent additional gifts to his prospective bride. If these are also considered <i>arra</i>, the bride keeps them if the marriage does not take place by the action of the groom; if they are engagement presents they have to be returned.</i> of the <i>symphōn</i>, Rebbi Mana said, [it follows the rules of] <i>symphōn</i>; Rebbi Abba said, [it follows the rules of] preliminary marriage. The rabbis take note of Rebbi Mana’s opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">If the marriage does not take place, it would be incumbent upon the man to prove in court that the gifts were specifically designated as engagement presents, not <i>arra</i>.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “On condition<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">He gives the woman a <i>peruṭah</i> as marriage gift and specifies that he preliminarily marries her as owner of a <i>bet kor</i>, 30 <i>bet se‘ah</i> or 75’000 square cubits of agricultural land. If he actually is the owner of the land, she is married immediately; he can show her the title to his land later.</i> that I possess a <i>bet kor</i> of arable land,” she is preliminarily married if he owns it. “On condition that I possess it at place X”, she is preliminarily married if he owns it at place X, otherwise she is not preliminarily married. “On condition that I shall show you a <i>bet kor</i> of arable land,” she is preliminarily married and he has to show her<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Since he said “to show”, it means that she has to see it with her own eyes; it is not enough for him to prove his ownership to her.</i>, but if he showed it in the valley<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">בִּקְעָא everywhere means open agricultural land not easily accessible. In all these conditions, a claim of ownership is understood.</i> she is not preliminarily married. <sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">In the independent Mishnah mss. and later in the Halakhah here starts a new Mishnah 4.</i> Rebbi Meïr says, any condition which is not formulated in the manner of the condition imposed on the tribes of Gad and Reuben is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">In the independent Mishnah mss. and later in the Halakhah here starts a new Mishnah 4.</i>, as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 32:29-30" href="/Numbers.32.29-30"><i>Num</i>. 32:29–30</a>.</i>: “Moses said to them, if the tribes of Gad and Reuben cross over … but if they will not in arms cross over with you, etc.” Rebbi Ḥananiah ben Gamliel said, that formulation was necessary<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">The involved formulation of the deed of Transjordan to the tribes of Gad and Reuben was necessary under the circumstances. A simple condition is valid; it is not necessary for a man to say: “Be preliminarily married to me on condition that I possess a <i>bet kor</i> of arable land, but be not preliminarily married to me if I do not possess a <i>bet kor</i> of arable land.”</i> for otherwise one would have understood that even in the Land of Canaan they should not inherit.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “On condition that I possess a <i>bet kor</i> of arable land,” etc. Rebbi Ḥananiah the son of Rebbi Hillel asked<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">He asks against R. Ḥanaiah ben Gamliel.</i>: If somebody said, my son A should perform action X and receive property Y, and the rest of my sons shall inherit my properties; if he performs, he takes, but if he does not perform, he does not take<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">This does not disinherit A from sharing with his brothers.</i>. Or maybe it is a difference since it is written: “To give to Nine and a half tribes.<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 34:13" href="/Numbers.34.13"><i>Num</i>. 34:13</a>. This verse clearly excludes the Transjordan tribes from property in Canaan. Therefore, R. Ḥanaiah ben Gamliel’s argument is correct.</i>” It is as if he said, my son A should perform action X and receive property Y, and the rest of my sons shall inherit my properties; if he performs, he takes, but if he does not perform, he does not take from here or from there.
<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the Halakhah, the text is also in <i>Eruvin</i> 3, 21b line 1 ff.</i> There, we have stated: “A person can impose a condition of his ‘eruv.<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">The rabbinic interpretation of the biblical commandment that (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 16:29" href="/Exodus.16.29"><i>Ex</i>. 16:29</a>) “nobody shall leave his place of residence on the Seventh Day” is that on the Sabbath one may not go a distance greater than 2’000 cubits from the boundary of the built-up domain in which one resides. Since by tradition the diameter of the encampment of the Israelites in the desert was 12 <i>mil</i>, 24’000 cubits (<i>Shevi‘it</i> 6:1, Note 28), and certainly the Israelites were able to freely move in their camp on the Sabbath, it is clear that the restriction to 2’000 cubits is purely rabbinical. Therefore, it is open to manipulation. One of the accepted ways of manipulation is for a person to deposit some food, the ‘<i>eruv</i>, at the border of the permitted domain and declare that he intends to consider the place of that food as dwelling for the coming Sabbath. Then he still can move freely in the built-up domain, which is counted only as 4 cubits, but he has an additional 2’000 cubits on the other side of the ‘<i>eruv</i>, but nothing on the opposite side of the built-up area. Mishnah ‘<i>Eruvin</i> 3:5 permits a person to prepare a number of ‘<i>eruvin</i> which are mutually exclusive, and say: “If attackers appear in the East, my ‘<i>eruv</i> in the West shall be active; if they appear in the West, my <i>‘eruv</i> in the East shall be active; if there is no danger, I shall be like the other inhabitants of my place.”</i>” So is the Mishnah: “A person can impose a condition of his<i>‘eruvin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">Since there are at least two. This remark belongs to <i>‘Eruvin</i>, not to <i>Qiddušin</i>.</i>” Rebbi Eleazar said, who is the Tanna of “if they came”, “if they did not come”? Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">He will hold that even if there was only one <i>‘eruv</i>, it has to be spelled out that the ‘<i>eruv</i> shall be invalid if there is no danger; otherwise the person making the <i>‘eruv</i> can never keep the Sabbath with the other inhabitants of his place.</i>! Which Rebbi Meïr? The colleagues say, Rebbi Meïr of <i>Qiddušin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>‘Eruvin</i> 3:5 is a direct application of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.3.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 3:3</a> and the <i>baraita</i> quoted in the sequel.</i>. As it was stated: If somebody say to a woman: You are preliminarily married to me on condition that there will be rain<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">If a <i>peruṭah</i> changed hands and a definite time was given for the duration of the condition.</i>. If rain fell, she is preliminarily married, otherwise she is not preliminarily married. Rebbi Meïr says, she is preliminarily married whether or not rain fell unless he doubled his condition<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">If it was not stated explicitly that there was no marriage without rainfall, the condition is void and the marriage valid unconditionally.</i>. Everybody agrees that if he said, after rainfall<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">If the <i>peruṭah</i> is given with the understanding that it is counted as marriage gift when it rained before a fixed date, that explains the modalities of the preliminary marriage, it is not a condition.</i>, if it rained she is preliminarily married, otherwise she is not preliminarily married. Rebbi Yose said, it follows Rebbi Meïr in <i>Eruvin</i>, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>‘Eruvin</i> 3:4. Somebody put an <i>‘eruv</i> near the Sabbath boundary. If it was somehow moved and now it is not clear whether it is inside or outside the Sabbath boundary: If it is still within the boundary, the person making it can reach it on the Sabbath and it is valid. If it is outside, it cannot be reached and is invalid. If it is in doubt, according to rabbis Meïr and Jehudah, the person making it can only move in the domain permitted both if the ‘<i>eruv</i> is valid and if it is invalid.</i>: “If it be in doubt, Rebbi Meïr and Rebbi Jehudah say, he is a camel-donkey driver.<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">The donkey driver has to go behind his animal; it will not move otherwise. The camel driver has to go in front of his animal and draw it by its bridle; it will not move otherwise. The camel-donkey driver cannot function at all; the usual Babli expression “donkey-camel driver” describes one who cannot move from between the animals as similar to the person with an ‘<i>eruv</i> in doubt who cannot leave the four cubits at which he started the Sabbath.</i>” Rebbi Yose said, Rebbi Meïr said this only as a restriction<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">R. Meïr requires a double formulation of a condition only for marriage and divorce, not for ‘<i>eruvin</i>. While this is also the opinion of Rif, most interpreters of the Babli hold that a double formulation is always required. Cf. <i>Sefer</i> <i>Ha</i>’<i>iṭṭur</i> I, p. 74 ff.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, that is correct. He cannot acquire his ‘<i>eruv</i> since he is not able to enjoy it. [Why not] like the people of his place? If he had decided to leave his place<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that the person with the ‘<i>eruv</i> in doubt is a donkey-camel driver has nothing to do with the requirement of a double formulation. The ‘<i>eruv</i> cannot be valid if the person cannot reach it on the Sabbath; the person cannot have the domain available to the other residents since he had decided not to be part of the population for the coming Sabbath.</i>. And here<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">A hypothetical case in which a first man contracted a preliminary marriage conditioned upon the timely arrival of rains, when a second man contracted an unconditional preliminary marriage with the same woman before the arrival of rains. Since she was not married when the second man came, it is impossible to say that the latter’s preliminary marriage were invalid. It is also impossible to say that it were valid, since the first man’s condition was not stated in the double formulation required by R. Meïr; she needs divorces from both men.</i>, she is not preliminarily married to the first, since no rain came. She is not preliminarily married to the second, since [the first] did not double his condition. Rebbi Ḥaggai asked before Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">This question is directed against R. Ḥanaiah ben Gamliel. What was the necessity of the double formulation in the case of the tribes Gad and Reuben? The question should be asked not for the negative part but for the positive; was it necessary to state that the tribes will get the Transjordan territory if they come to fight in Canaan?</i>: Is this “if” not a reaction? He answered, there is a difference since the land was in their hand<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">They already were in possession; to make this possession conditional needed the involved formulation.</i> and he wanted to take it away from them.
Rebbi Jehudah bar Sisin<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">An Amora of this name is not otherwise documented in Talmudic literature. Probably the reading of <b>ע</b> “R. Jehudah ben Shalom” is correct.</i>, Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazy in the name of Rebbi Aḥa. They formulated the <i>symphōn</i> according to Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Since it states both that there is preliminary marriage if the conditions are satisfied but none if they are not satisfied.</i>. Rebbi Ḥananiah, the colleague of the rabbis, asked: Why does it have to follow Rebbi Meïr but not also the rabbis? Did not Rebbi Abbahu say in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The following is the contract text: “I, X son of Y, contract a preliminary marriage with you, Z, daughter of U, on condition that I shall give you property A and definitively marry you by day B. If that day should pass without me having taken you in, I shall have no claim on you.” Why can he not say “on condition” but not double his stipulation? If he did not double his stipulation, could this eliminate the preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">Even the rabbis must require an extra stipulation which will eliminate the preliminary marriage if the conditions are not satisfied.</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, everywhere Rebbi Meïr holds that from “no” you infer “yes”, except here<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 11a" href="/Nedarim.11a">Babli (<i>Nedarim</i> 11a</a>,13b; <i>Ševu</i>’<i>ot</i> 36a) emphatically holds that R. Meïr requires a double formulation since he does not infer a positive statement from a negative one. For example, a statement “this shall for me be not profane” implies a dedication for the rabbis but not for R. Meïr.</i>? Rebbi Mattaniah said, one is more restrictive in matters of incest and adultery.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a person contracts a preliminary marriage with a woman and then says, I was of the opinion that she was a Cohenet but she is a Levite, a Levite but she is a Cohenet, poor but she is rich, rich but she is poor, she is preliminarily married since she did not trick him<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">His relations with other people are not influenced by his erroneous opinions, as long as he alone is responsible for them.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">In the independent Mishnah mss. and later in the Halakhah here starts a new Mishnah 4.</i>“Rebbi Meïr says, any condition,” etc. Rebbi Eleazar said, she<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">A woman preliminarily married with a condition that was not expressed in double form and was not satisfied. He holds that for R. Meïr she is married unconditionally. In the opinion of Nachmanides (reported in <i>Sefer</i> <i>Hatterumot</i> 51:6) the statement does not refer to the preceding Mishnah, but to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>: Even if there are witnesses who can testify that the groom told them that he was going to marry a Cohenet, as long as she did nothing to suggest that idea to him, she is validly married.</i> is preliminarily married as if by witnesses. Rebbi Samuel in the name of Rebbi Ze‘ira: She is preliminarily married as a restriction<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">For him R. Meïr agrees that the woman cannot be married if the stipulation was not fulfilled. But he will hold that if a second man married her, she needs a bill of divorce from both of them since both of them have equal claim on her.</i>.
Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Immi: Reuben owes to Simeon; he transferred [the debt] to Levi<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">And Simeon accepted the assignment, thinking that Levi had good credit. The case belongs here because it frees Reuben from liability as long as he did nothing to inflate Levi’s credit in Simeon’s eyes.</i>. Levi became poor<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Levy and Kohut explain as Greek ἀπορέω, “to become poor”.</i>; then Reuben owes nothing to Simeon. That is, if it was not done in bad faith, but if it was done in bad faith, he is obligated.
Similarly, the pedlars directed the village wax chandlers to a merchant. The merchant absconded. The case came before Rebbi Mana, who said, if it is a matter of pennies, they were negligent<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">The wax-chandlers should have collected their debt expeditously; but for a large debt the pedlars remain obligated.</i>. If it is a matter of denars, it is the way of merchants to move around.
A person met another in the market place. He said to him, give me the linen which you hold for me. The other responded, give me the denar which you hold for me. The first said, give me the linen and deduct the denar. The case came before Rebbi Mana, who said to him, you admitted that you owe him a denar; he did not admit anything about the linen. Go and give him the denar! Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, Rebbi Immi<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">One is tempted to read “R. Mana” for “R. Immi”.</i> will agree that this is not the law but that he obligated him lest the other deny his liability for the linen.
A person owed another one 100 denars on a document<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Greek χάρτης, Latin <i>charta</i>, “papyrus, roll of papyrus”.</i>. He sent 50 with an agent and instructed him: If he does not surrender the document, do not give him anything. The case came before Rebbi Immi. He said, go and give it, for we know. Think of it, Rebbi Immi [might die]<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of the Geniza fragment. It makes more sense than the reading “who said” of L. The emendation of <i>Pene</i> <i>Moshe</i>, דמת instead of דמר, is confirmed by the Genizah text as to meaning but not as to formulation.</i>! One writes for him a proclamation of the court<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">The agent was not empowered to pay the 50 denars without getting the document; the creditor was not willing to give the document for less than full payment. R. Immi told him to deliver the document and receive a court document instead which documented the remaining claim.</i>. If one accepts for another person, the rabbis here say, what he accepted, he accepted for the claimant.
<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Text and translation are from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 1:5:6-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.1.5.6-7"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 1:6, Notes 181–199</a> (<b>ט</b>). The Genizah text simply refers to the <i>Giṭṭin</i> text, reserving less than one line for the entire quote.</i> Explanation: Rebbi Dositheos ben Rebbi Yannai and Rebbi Yose ben Kipper descended to collect there for the colleagues when they were slandered. They asked but were not given anything. Then they came and wanted to take it back from them. They answered, we already acquired it. They said, we wish for you to accept it in good faith; they answered, we are unpaid trustees. They approached Rebbi Dositheos ben Rebbi Yannai who told them, there it is, all of it. They took Rebbi Yose ben Kipper, whipped him with ropes, and took it from him. When they returned here, he went to his father and told him, look what your son did to me! He asked him, what did he do to you? He answered, if he had taken my position, they could not have taken anything from us. He asked him, why did you act in such a way? He said, I saw that they were a unanimous court, their hats were a cubit wide, they were directing blows, my brother Yose was bound and the whip ascended and descended. I said, does my father have another Dositheos? Rebbi Ḥaggai said, you say that for somebody who cannot give a warranty in good faith. But if somebody gives a warranty in good faith, one takes from this one and gives to the other. If somebody forgave a bond to another person: Rebbi Ḥanania and Rebbi Mamal: one said it is forgiven; the other said it is not forgiven unless he handed over the bond.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody say to a woman: You are preliminarily married to me after I convert, or after you are converted<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">While marriage exists for Gentiles, preliminary marriage is a uniquely Jewish institution. If one or both parties are Gentile, no preliminary marriage is possible.</i>, after I am freed, or after you are freed<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">A slave cannot marry. Therefore, if one or both parties are servile, no preliminary marriage is possible.</i>, after your husband has died<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">A married woman cannot marry another party. A preliminary marriage in anticipation of the husband’s death is impossible.</i>, or after your sister has died<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">If the man was once married to a woman, her sister is forbidden to him during the woman’s lifetime. This is the only incest prohibition of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18" href="/Leviticus.18"><i>Lev</i>. 18</a> for which no criminal penalty is indicated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 20" href="/Leviticus.20"><i>Lev</i>. 20</a>. But <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18:18" href="/Leviticus.18.18"><i>Lev</i>. 18:18</a> is read as: “A woman in addition to her sister you are unable take, to be co-wives, to uncover her nakedness in addition to her, during her lifetime.” The act of preliminary marriage is here defined as impossible during the sister’s lifetime.</i>, or after your levir has given you <i>ḥalîṣah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">This Tanna follows the majority interpretation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:5" href="/Deuteronomy.25.5"><i>Deut</i>. 25:5</a>, which reads: “If brothers dwell together and one of them die without children, the widow of the deceased <i>is</i> <i>unable</i> <i>to</i> <i>be</i> <i>an</i> <i>outside</i> <i>man’s</i>.” Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:1:21" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.1.21"><i>Yebamot</i> 1:1, Notes 93–103</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:5:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.5.6">Note 166</a> below.</i>, she is not preliminarily married. Similarly, if somebody say to another, if your wife have a daughter, she shall be preliminarily married to me, he did not say anything<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">A marriage contract cannot be concluded as a futures contract on anything which does not yet exist. The Mishnah in the Babli states in addition that if the wife of the prospective father-in-law was visibly pregnant, the contract is valid. The Halakhah shows that the Yerushalmi clearly holds that a woman is said to exist only from the moment of her birth, not while she still is in her mother’s womb.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody say to a woman,” etc. They asked before Rebbi Joḥanan: What is the difference between this and one who says, “this shall be heave for this standing produce after it has been cut”<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">It is impossible to give heave from standing grain since the obligation of heave starts only at the end of the harvesting process. Nevertheless, the farmer can set aside a certain amount for heave at the start of the harvest to cover everything he will harvest in the future. Then why is it impossible for somebody who at the moment is unable to contract a preliminary marriage to give a gift which is to become a marriage gift as soon as the obstacle to his contracting is removed? The same question is asked in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 62a" href="/Kiddushin.62a">Babli, 62a</a>.</i>? He said to them, there, it is in his power to cut; but here, it is not in his power to be freed<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">But it is uniquely in the power of the slave’s owner, or, in the case of Gentiles, in the power of the rabbinic court to whom they must apply for conversion.</i>. Rebbi Ephes asked, think of it, if she was his slave girl<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is in his own power to free her. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 62b" href="/Kiddushin.62b">Babli, 62b</a>, the question is attributed to R. Abba bar Mamal.</i>? Rebbi Abba bar Mamal said, “after you are freed”, she is made dependent on another person’s will<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of the owner marrying his own slave girl, it is purely a matter of formulation. By making it conditional, “if you be set free”, he abdicates his own power. The Yerushalmi will agree that if the owner simply marries his slave girl, without mentioning her need to be freed, by one action he sets her free and marries her (R. Moses Isserles in <i>Šulḥan</i> ‘<i>Arukh</i> <i>Yore</i> <i>Dea</i>’ 267:58). (The classical commentators of the Yerushalmi incorrectly read the Babli’s explanation into the Yerushalmi text.)</i>.
The elder Rebbi Hoshaia and Rebbi Jehudah Neśia were sitting together. They said, let us say something on the subject of preliminary marriage. If somebody say to his<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Translation follows the Genizah text.</i> wife, here you have a <i>peruṭaḣ</i> that you should be preliminarily married to me after I have divorced you, what is the rule? They laughed and got up<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 62b" href="/Kiddushin.62b">Babli, 62b</a>, takes R. Hoshaia’s question seriously. Naturally, the answer to the question as stated is simply that the action is invalid since a married woman cannot marry again while married, not even her own husband.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, why did they laugh? Did not Rebbi Abba bar Mamal say, “after you are freed”, she is made dependent on another person’s will; and here “after you are divorced,” she is made dependent on another’s will<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">In the opinion of the Babli, the reason is that while the husband can divorce her unilaterally, he cannot marry her again against her will. This is the “other will” which is involved here.</i>. If you want to find a difficulty. ask the following: A person went to preliminarily marry a woman. Another person overtook him and said to her, you should know that the man who is coming to preliminarily marry you has a bad mind. In the future he will abandon you. But here you have this <i>peruṭah</i> that you should be preliminarily married to me after he will have<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Translation follows the Genizah text.</i> divorced you<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">It was shown in 3:1 that if a man gives a gift for preliminary marriage stipulating that the marriage be valid only after 30 days, and another man came, married, and divorced her within those thirty days, the first man’s marriage is valid. The problem naturally is that “30 days” is an objective standard, not depending on another’s will; but the clause “if the other man will have divorced you” makes it dependent on another’s will and invalidates the preliminary marriage.</i>. What is the rule?
There<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 3:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.3.5.1">Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i> 3:6</a> and the relevant Halakhah (Notes 110–121). The text here does not make any sense and is not intended to make any sense. The Genizah fragment starts the quote of the Mishnah; the missing part of the line contained an instruction to continue the text in <i>Nedarim</i>. The same is intended here. We have a quote of the start of the Mishnah and a few words from the first paragraph of the Halakhah, ending with a complete copy of the last sentence, a sign that the copy now starts in earnest. It is clear that both scribes of the Genizah and the Leiden mss. considered the <i>Nedarim</i> text as the original for <i>Qiddušin</i>. For the reader’s convenience, here is the text omitted by the scribes:<br><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:6:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.6.2-3">Halakhah 6</a>: “These orchard trees shall be <i>qorbān</i>,” etc. If he saw the king’s cutting crew coming near, if he saw fire coming near, and he said: These orchard trees shall be <i>qorbān</i> if they are not cut, this garment shall be <i>qorbān</i> if it is not burned or torn. Were they sanctified retroactively or only for the future? What is the difference? If he used them. If you say that they are sanctified retroactively, he committed larceny. If you say that it refers to the future, he did not commit larceny.</i>, it was stated: “These orchard trees shall be <i>qorbān</i> if they are not cut, this garment shall be <i>qorbān</i> if it is not burned.” And it was burned. Were they sanctified retroactively? He committed larceny. If you say that it refers to the future, he did not commit larceny.
<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 3:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.3.5.3"><i>Nedarim</i> 3:6, Notes 114–121</a>.</i> Ḥizqiah said, if he redeemed them they revert to their sanctity. Rebbi Joḥanan said, if he redeemed them they are redeemed. The Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan: “These have no redemption.” He explains thus: After they were cut they do not need redemption. Rebbi Yose said, what Ḥizqiah said refers to the case that he himself redeemed them. But if another person redeemed them, the sanctity is removed from them. The argument of Rebbi Yose is switched: There, he says that if [another] redeemed them, the sanctity is removed from them, but here, he says that if she was married to another man the <i>qiddušin</i> [of the first] were not removed! All that is questionable for him is if he gave her two <i>peruṭot</i>, one for the moment and one for after he divorced her; what is the situation?
<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:1:2-26" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.1.2-26"><i>Yebamot</i> 1:1</a> (<b>א</b>), Notes 95–100. In the Genizah text, the entire paragraph again is only worth one line’s mention.</i> Rebbi Yannai said: More than 30 Elders voted: from where that <i>qiddushin</i> have no legal effect on a sister-in-law? The verse says, “the wife of the deceased may not belong to any outside unrelated man”, that she cannot have any existence with an outsider. Rebbi Joḥanan said to him, is that not a Mishnah? “Or after your levir will have performed <i>ḥalîṣah</i> with you, she is not preliminarily married.” And Rebbi Yannai praised him “those who pour out gold from the wallet,” “my son, they should not be removed from your eyes,” “get wise, my son, and make me happy, that I can answer those who insult me”, “give to the wise that he shall become wiser,” “let the wise listen that he increase in knowledge.” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, after all these praises I can explain it following Rebbi Aqiba since Rebbi Aqiba said that there exists a bastard from a sister-in-law!
<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the Mishnah dealing with the widow of a childless man. Samuel disagrees with the Mishnah; his position is accepted in the Babli as practice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 92b" href="/Yevamot.92b"><i>Yebamot</i> 92b</a>). While the marriage of the widow outside the family without <i>ḥalîṣah</i> certainly is sinful, it nowhere in the biblical text is punished as a criminal act; for Samuel this indicates that such a marriage should not be void by biblical standards.</i> Samuel said, in our poverty, she needs a bill of divorce from him. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, the Mishnah [said], “after your levir has given you <i>ḥalîṣah</i>”; but if he said, “after your levir has died,” she is preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that the Mishnah can be explained following Samuel. Even though the marriage by the widow of the childless man outside the family is valid, the marriage conditional on the action of the levir is invalid as explained by R. Abba bar Mamal. Therefore, the marriage conditioned on an act of God is valid, in particular also since after the death of the levir it no longer is sinful.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the Mishnah dealing with the newborn girl.</i> Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: A one-day-old girl can be preliminarily married by money; a girl of three years and one day can be preliminarily married by intercourse.” Rebbi Mana said, the Mishnah implies this: “Similarly, if somebody say to another, if your wife have a daughter, she shall be preliminarily married to me, he did not say anything,” because she does not exist. Therefore, if she does exist, she is preliminarily married.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody says to a woman, be preliminarily married to me on condition that I speak for you to the government, or work for you as a worker, she is preliminarily married if he did speak for her to the government or did work for her as a worker; otherwise she is not preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">As the Halakhah explains, there are two possible interpretations of this Mishnah, depending on whether or not a <i>peruṭah</i> changes hands at the moment. The problem does not arise in the Babli, which instead of “be preliminarily married to me” reads הרי את מקודשת לי “you are preliminarily married to me”; that language implies that (a) at least a <i>peruṭah</i> changes hands at the moment of engagement and (b) the marriage, if valid, is in effect from the moment of giving. Both implications are absent in the Yerushalmi version.</i>. On condition that my father agree; if the father agreed, she is preliminarily married, otherwise she is not preliminarily married. If the father died, she is preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">One interprets “on condition that my father agree” as “if my father does not object”. Since the father died, he can no longer object.</i>; if the son died, one instructs the father to say that he does not agree<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">If the father has other sons, one tells him to free the woman from the obligation of levirate by rejecting the preliminary marriage.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody says to a woman, be preliminarily married to me,” etc. Rav said, we deal with the case that he gave her a <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 63a" href="/Kiddushin.63a">Babli, 63a</a>, the uncontested opinion of R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i>.
