Rebbi Immi asked (before Rebbi)<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">This should be deleted with G.</i>: Do we not always deal with the case that he gave her a <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">In all preceding cases, if it says “be preliminarily married to me on condition”, it was understood that the proposal was accompanied by a marital gift. Then the present Mishnah would be unnecessary. But the new facet of this Mishnah is that preliminary marriage may be effected with lawyer’s or worker’s fees.</i>? But so it is: “By what I shall speak for you to the government; if he did speak for her to the government for a <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth she is preliminarily married, otherwise she is not preliminarily married.” “By what I shall work for you as a worker; if he did work for her as a worker for a <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth, she is preliminarily married, otherwise she is not preliminarily married.” Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav: Only if she set aside a tetradrachma in her chest for him<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">The lawyer or the worker have to present the bill for their work; if the bill amounts to at least a <i>peruṭah</i>, she is married by the forgiving of the bill if she had the money to pay. This formulation avoids the problem of the Babli, that marriage cannot be effectuated by forgiving a loan, and the fee due a professional might be considered a loan slowly accreting through delivery of his services.</i>. With what did he effectuate the preliminary marriage? As we have stated there: “If anything is bartered instead of money, if one party entered in possession, the other is obligated for its trade-in<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 1:6, Note 521. By writing the bill, the lawyer puts a monetary value on his services.</i>.”
“On condition that my father agree; if the father agreed, she is preliminarily married; if the father does not agree, she is not preliminarily married;” silently he does not agree<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">This is an example of the problem of the undistributed middle, frequently discussed in the Babli but rarely in the Yerushalmi. The Mishnah is silent on the status of the marriage if the father refuses to express an opinion. The formulation “if the father agreed” seems to imply that failure to give a positive reply is a rejection. If the formulation had been “unless the father objects,” the father’s silence would have signified assent.</i>. “If the father died, she is preliminarily married;” silently he agreed<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">According to the argument of the preceding Note, if the father died without expressing an opinion, the marriage should be invalid. The second and third clauses of the Mishnah seem to contradict the first.</i>. “If the son died, one instructs the father to say that he does not agree;” silently he agreed. Rebbi Joḥanan asked Rebbi Yannai, can the Mishnah say so? He answered him, do you ask wretched Yannai to explain <i>Qiddušin</i>? Rebbi Yannai returned and explained the Mishnah: “On condition that my father agree; if the father agreed, she is preliminarily married; otherwise she is not preliminarily married,” he said on condition that [the father] express his opinion but he did not<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">One has to read the first clause as explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.6.3">Note 178</a>, that there is no undistributed middle but the son made the validity of the marriage dependent on the explicit acceptation by his father. The father’s silence means rejection.</i>. “If the father died, she is preliminarily married;” as if he had given his opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">If the father died before he had an opportunity to react, the court may assume that he might have assented if he had lived; the woman is married to the extent that if another man married her preliminarily before the first one married her definitively, she would need a bill of divorce from both of them.</i>. “If the son died, one instructs the father to say that he does not agree;” as if he had given his opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the father had given his initial consent, if the son died before the definitive marriage the court will instruct the father to refuse to accept the marriage in order not to forbid the woman to a Cohen. This clause has nothing to do with the problem of the undistributed middle.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira said [before Rebbi Yasa]<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of G. The text of L is unacceptable; R. Ze‘ira cannot ask his student’s student R. Yose but he can ask his teacher and R. Simeon ben Laqish’s student R. Yasa.</i>: Can Rebbi Yannai explain the Mishnah as he wishes? He answered him, so says Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, Rebbi does not refrain from stating generality and detail simultaneously<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">The three clauses of this part of the Mishnah refer to three different situations. In the first clause, a very restrictive interpretation is appropriate, in the second a very general one, in the third even a prior acceptance is turned into a rejection. This agrees with the general style of the Mishnah.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah states thus: “On condition that I have a <i>bet</i> <i>kor</i> of arable land”, a generality; “at place X”, a detail<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.3.1">Mishnah 3:3</a>. The example does not quite fit the problem, but the general thrust is clear.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “I preliminarily married off my daughter but I do not know to whom I married her preliminarily,” and somebody comes and says, I married her preliminarily, he is believed<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">Since the father has the right to marry off his underage daughter, if he believes that the person claiming her is his son-in-law, he has the right to marry her to him.</i>. This one says, “I married her preliminarily,” and that one says, “I married her preliminarily,” both give a bill of divorce or, if they so wish, one gives a bill of divorce and the other marries her definitively.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 2:11:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.2.11.2-4"><i>Yebamot</i> 2:11</a> (<b>ב</b>) (Notes 184–195). G simply has a note: גרש בסוף פירקא תניינא דיבמות עד לא הכל … “One continues the end of the second Chapter of <i>Yebamot</i> as far as “he is not [trusted] in any way.”</i>“If one says, I preliminarily married off my daughter”, etc. About what is he trustworthy? Samuel says, he is trusted to give a bill of divorce. Assi said, he is trusted to marry. Rav Huna said in the name of Rav: He is trusted to marry. Rebbi Joḥanan said, he is trusted to marry but one does not infer anything for another case from this ruling. What means, one does not infer anything for another case from this ruling? I sold one of my fields but I do not remember to whom I sold it. If someone comes and says, I bought it, he is not trusted in any way. In preliminary marriage, the same holds true. “I betrothed one of my daughters but I do not remember to whom I betrothed her. If someone comes and says, I married her preliminarily,” he is not trusted in any way. The Mishnah disagrees with Rav: “If somebody brings a bill of divorce from overseas and says, it was written and signed in my presence, he shall not marry that woman.” There, she is considered by everybody to be a married woman. Here, she is considered to be a married woman only by two persons. One waits until these two come and say, this is the man who gave her <i>qiddushin</i>. The Mishnah disagrees with Samuel: “This one says, I married her preliminarily, and that one says, I married her preliminarily. Both of them give a bill of divorce, but if they agree, one gives a bill of divorce and the other one marries.” He explains this, if [the father] says, I did betrothe her to one of these two but I do not remember which one it was. Rebbi Ze‘ira, Rebbi Yasa, in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If one of them jumped the gun and married her, one does remove her from him. That is, if this one says, I married her preliminarily, and that one says, I married her preliminarily, and one of them jumped the gun and married her definitively. But if he said, I married her preliminarily and then married her definitively, and another one comes and says, I married her preliminarily, he is not trusted in any way. It was stated thus: If after the marriage another man comes and says, I married her preliminarily, he is not trusted in any way.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “I preliminarily married off my daughter, married her preliminarily and received her bill of divorce while she was underage,” and [now] she still is underage, he is believed<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">Since he has the right to marry his daughter to a man whose intercourse will make her unfit for the priesthood, he can also declare her unfit for the priesthood as divorcee without producing proof.</i>. “I preliminarily married off my daughter, married her preliminarily and received her bill of divorce while she was underage,” and [now] she is an adult, he is not believed<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">Since he does not have the right to marry off his adult daughter, he cannot declare her ineligible for the priesthood without producing proof.</i>. “She had been kidnapped<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">And presumed raped, which forbids her to a Cohen.</i> and I ransomed her,” he is not believed whether she is underage or adult. If somebody said when dying, I have children, he is believed<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">His childless widow is free to marry outside the family; she does not have to investigate whether his claim is true. The Torah empowers a father to recognize a son (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:17" href="/Deuteronomy.21.17">Deut. 21:17</a>); the court is not empowered to require additional proof (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 134b" href="/Bava_Batra.134b">Babli Baba Batra 134b</a>).</i>; I have brothers, he is not believed<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">If he dies childless and had married his wife without telling her that he had brothers, he cannot now subject her to levirate with a brother-in-law she might be unable to locate since her husband's uncorroborated statement cannot undo the <i>prima facie</i> presumption created at the time of his marriage (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 1:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.1.4.2">Ketubot 1:4, Note 177</a>; 5:5 Note 100; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.3.4">Gittin 3:4, Notes 92</a> ff.)</i>. If somebody preliminarily marries off his daughter without mentioning her name, the adult ones are not included<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">Since the father has no right to marry off his adult daughter. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 64b" href="/Kiddushin.64b">Babli, 64b</a>, points out that even if the adult daughter appoints her father as an agent to find her a husband, the father cannot contract the preliminary marriage without mentioning that he is an agent. Then the name of the principal automatically has to be stated.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “I preliminarily married off my younger daughter,” etc. “I preliminarily married off my daughter.” The underaged are included<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">Since Scripture bases the rules of a criminal procedure on the uncorroborated statement of a father who says “I gave my daughter to this man” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:16" href="/Deuteronomy.22.16"><i>Deut</i>. 22:16</a>), it is concluded that his statement concerning a daughter over which he has the power to give her away into marriage has to be accepted by the court without further documentation or corroboration.</i>, the adults are excluded<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">Since the father has no right to give her away into marriage.</i>. “My daughter was preliminarily married,” even the underaged are not included<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">The language seems to imply that the father was not involved in the marriage. But an underage girl cannot be legally wedded without her father arranging the marriage. For illegal acts, the father’s standing is no different from any other person in the world; one needs two independent witnesses. The father, being his daughter’s relative, is automatically disqualified as a witness.</i>. “I accepted my daughter’s bill of divorce,” the underaged are included, the adults are excluded. “My daughter was divorced,” even the underaged are not included. “I preliminarily married off my daughter to one of those disqualified for her,” he is believed. “I made her sleep with one of those disqualified for her,” he is not believed. “She had been kidnapped and I ransomed her,” whether she is underage or adult or had intercourse with one of those disqualified for her, he is not believed.
So is the Mishnah: “If somebody said when dying, I have children, he is believed; I have brothers, he is not believed;” Rav said, since it is in his hand to divorce, he is to be believed<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">One is not forced to say that the man married under false pretenses when he did not inform his bride of the existence of a brother; that now he cannot change her situation while maintaining the validity of his marriage, since he could conditionally divorce his wife, the divorce becoming active a moment before his death, and thereby protect her from the complications of levirate (in particular, in this case where the widow would be bound to an unknown brother-in-law whom she might be unable to locate).</i>. The word of Samuel agrees with this. A Persian when dying said to them: Give this man’s<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">His own.</i> wife a bill of divorce. They asked him, why? Because of your wretched brother? After he died, the case came before Samuel who said, since it was in his hand to divorce, he is to be believed<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">There is no reason to believe that the bill of divorce was not written and duly delivered. Samuel’s decision was that the estate, after payment of the widow’s claims, has to be held by a court-appointed trustee for the brother as next of kin.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, he is not to be believed. Ulla descended there<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia he proclaimed the Babylonian authorities Rav and Samuel to be in error.</i> and said this in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan; they<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">The Babylonian scholars.</i> ganged up on him. Think of it, if he was a Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">This is R. Joḥanan’s and Ulla’s argument against Rav and Samuel: Since a Cohen may not give his wife a conditional bill of divorce without forbidding her permanently to himself, and civil law does not make any difference in the personal standing of the parties, the Babylonian argument is invalid; the Mishnah is justified.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 134b-135a" href="/Bava_Batra.134b-135a">Babli, <i>Baba batra</i> 134b/135a</a>, R. Joḥanan holds that a man who says that he divorced his wife cannot be believed without corroborating evidence. Then he cannot be believed if unexpectedly he declares to have a brother.</i>?
Rav Sheshet objected<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">The language is unusual; how can one object if no statement was yet enunciated? But it is impossible to move the question after the statements of Rav and Samuel because of the concurrent testimony of G (which admittedly is fragmentary at this point.)</i>: Does not the Mishnah disagree? “You married me preliminarily but he says, I did not marry you preliminarily; he is permitted her relatives but she is forbidden his relatives.”<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 12; the order of arguments is that of the Mishnah in the Babli.<br>Rav Sheshet’s point is that the case treated in the Mishnah simply cannot happen. Either there are witnesses to the marriage, then there can be no dispute; or there are no witnesses, then there is no marriage.</i> They said, so did it happen: Simeon bar Abba brought a bill of divorce and delivered it in front of one witness. The case came before Rebbi Joḥanan, who said, a single witness in marital matters is nothing<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">There was no delivery, the woman is not yet divorced. The agent has to take the document back and deliver it anew in front of two witnesses.</i>. Did not Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Assi in the name of Assi<sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">Unfortunately, these names are missing in G; there is an outside chance that the reference is to Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi, student of Rav.</i> say: a single witness in marital matters is nothing? Rav said, one who performs a preliminary marriage in front of a single witness did not do anything<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 65a" href="/Kiddushin.65a">Babli, 65a</a>, this is a statement of Rav Naḥman in the name of Samuel; it is stated explicitly that this holds even if the parties agree.</i>. Samuel said, he who performs a preliminary marriage without prior negotiation is whipped, but the preliminary marriage holds. Mar Uqba in the name of Samuel said three things: He who performs a preliminary marriage without prior negotiation is whipped<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 12b" href="/Kiddushin.12b">Babli, 12b</a>, this is attributed to Rav and rejected as practice in Nahardea, Samuel’s place. Probably this means that a betrothal is possible at the second, but not the first, date.</i>; one who sleeps with his betrothed in his father-in-law’s house<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">As was the custom in Mishnaic times in Judea (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 1:5:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.1.5.2-3"><i>Ketubot</i> 1:5</a>; Mishnah and Note 208).</i> is whipped; one who insults the court bailiff<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote">Who acts in an official capacity.</i> is whipped.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody has two groups of daughters from two wives and he says, I preliminarily married off my older daughter, but he does not know<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">When he took the money or signed the contract, he did not mention her name and now he does not remember which girl he had in mind when he contracted the marriage.</i> whether the oldest of the older ones, or the oldest of the younger ones, or the youngest of the older ones who is older than the oldest of the younger ones, all are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">They are forbidden to marry. None of them can marry an outsider for she may already be married. She cannot marry the man her father contracted with for he may be her sister's husband. That man has to write a separate bill of divorce for every girl.</i> except the youngest of the younger ones, the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Yose says, all are permitted except the oldest of the older ones<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">Since only she is unequivocally determined by the predicate "old".</i>. I preliminarily married off my younger daughter, but he does not know whether the youngest of the younger ones, or the youngest of the older ones, or the oldest of the younger ones who is younger than the youngest of the older ones, all are forbidden except the oldest of the older ones, the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Yose says, all are permitted except the youngest of the younger ones.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody has two groups of daughters,” etc. Rav Ḥama said, two of two<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote">Even R. Meïr will agree that if one wife has more than two daughters, the intermediate ones will neither be called “old” or “young” but be specified by name. Therefore, the whole disagreement is about a man who has two daughters each from two wives.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, for example I, who have two of two. The Mishnah [speaks of] him who has two groups of daughters from two wives, but if he has only one group and he said, the older, he married off only the oldest; if he said, the younger, he married off only the youngest.
<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote">This is essentially <i>Nedarim</i>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.8">Halakhah 8:3 (Notes 36–45</a>). In G, the entire reference to this text seems to have been in one missing line.</i> There, we have stated: “ ‘Until before Passover’, Rebbi Meïr says, until it comes, Rebbi Yose says, until it passed.” Rebbi Jeremiah asked before Rebbi Ze‘ira: The opinion of Rebbi Meïr seems inverted; the opinion of Rebbi Yose seems to be inverted. The opinion of Rebbi Yose seems inverted: There, he says “until all the elder possibilities are exhausted, until all the younger possibilities are exhausted,” and here, he says so? He said to him: Since Ben Azai and Ben Zoma died, the perseverers disappeared; no perseverer was there until Jeremiah appeared. Rebbi Abba, son of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba, said, why does he needle him? Did not great mountains find this difficult, did not Rebbi Eleazar already ask before Rebbi Joḥanan, does not the opinion of Rebbi Meïr seem to be inverted? He said to him, it is not inverted, the Mishnah is inverted, for in the House of Rebbi they stated: “ ‘Until before Passover’, Rebbi Meïr says, until it passed, Rebbi Yose says, until it comes.” He said: We ask “until before”, and you say so? He said to him, this is a Nabatean expression, “much before Passover”. Rebbi Abin said, everybody agrees that he is permitted on Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:21" href="/Exodus.21.21"><i>Ex</i>. 21:21</a>, the verse which exempts the owner from prosecution if the slave lives for 24 hours. “His money” indicates ownership.</i>. Where do they disagree? The day before Passover. One of them says, until it passed, the other until it comes.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody says to a woman, I married you preliminarily, but she says, you did not marry me preliminarily<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">And there is no witness present to support either of the parties.</i>, he is forbidden her relatives<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">While in the absence of witnesses he cannot force the woman to be married to him, since he declares himself to be married to her he has to observe all incest prohibitions which would follow from the marriage if it were real.</i> but she is permitted his relatives. I she says, you married me preliminarily but he says, I did not marry you preliminarily, she is forbidden his relatives but he is permitted her relatives.<br>“I am preliminarily married to you,” but she says, you are only preliminarily married to my daughter; he is forbidden the relatives of the old woman<sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote">Since he claims marriage with her daughter, he is forbidden the relatives he could not marry had the claim been true (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 4:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.4.7.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i>4:7</a>). While all relatives of the mother’s are also relatives of the daughter’s, the lists of forbidden unions are different in the two cases.</i>, and the old woman is permitted his relatives; he is permitted the relatives of the young woman, and the young woman is permitted his relatives<sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote">She did not claim being party to a marriage; it was her mother who claimed for herself but the mother’s action cannot impair the daughter.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody says to a woman,” etc. Rebbi Yossi said, it is reasonable that Rebbi Meïr not disagree in this case<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the next Mishnaiot 12 and 13, where the question is whether the mother or a daughter was being married. There, R. Meïr’s name is not mentioned. R. Yose impies that the Mishnah is R. Meïr’s.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, the entire series implies that he disagrees. Since there, where they are all<sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote">Reading with G כּוּלָּם for כְּלוּם “nothing” of L.</i> older and younger<sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote">The case of two groups of girls from different mothers.</i>, you say that Rebbi Meïr disagrees, so here, where all are old compared to young or young compared to old, he certainly disagrees<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote">R. Mana seems to imply that also in Mishnah 11, and certainly in Mishnaiot 12 and 13, R. Meïr imposes incest restrictions on all parties, including those who deny being parties to a marriage.</i>.

Halakhah 11

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “I am preliminarily married to your daughter,” but she says, you are only preliminarily married to me; he is forbidden the relatives of the young woman<sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote">Since he claims marriage with her daughter, he is forbidden the relatives he could not marry had the claim been true (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 4:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.4.7.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i>4:7</a>). While all relatives of the mother’s are also relatives of the daughter’s, the lists of forbidden unions are different in the two cases.</i>, and the young woman is permitted his relatives; he is permitted the relatives of the old woman, and the old woman is forbidden his relatives.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “I am preliminarily married to your daughter,” etc. Rav Ḥuna said, one induces him to give a bill of divorce, and one forces him to pay a fine<sup class="footnote-marker">226</sup><i class="footnote">It is not clear from this text to which woman the bill of divorce has to be given. If sense is to be made of the text, one has to read it in the light of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 65a" href="/Kiddushin.65a">Babli, 65a</a>, where Samuel states that if a woman claims to be preliminarily married to a man, which claim he denies, one has to try to make him give her a bill of divorce to free the woman for marriage to another. In the case of the Mishnah, the bill of divorce would be for the mother; the daughter is not mentioned in the discussion. Rav, the teacher of R. Ḥuna, states that if he gave the bill of divorce on his own initiative, one can force him to pay the divorce settlement required by the law of <i>ketubah</i>.</i>. Think of it, if he was a Fellow and knew that he would be obligated for a bill of divorce and a fine; if he went and married her sister, would one force the divorce of the certain because of the doubtful<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Ḥuna’s statement is rejected as impractical. Assuming that the man either is reasonably well trained in the law or has access to legal advice, he can avoid being badgered into offering a bill of divorce and then forced to pay a hefty sum by threatening to marry the woman’s sister. Since the Mishnah permits him to marry the sister, one cannot ask him to give a divorce to his sister-in-law since by that act he would prohibit his prospective wife to himself. It is in the interest of the woman desiring the bill of divorce to make it clear that she has no monetary claims. (Explanation of D. Fraenckel.)<br>The last sentence cannot be read as declarative: “one forces the divorce of the certain because of the doubtful”, meaning that the man would be barred from marrying the sister because of the unproven claim of the woman, since this would contradict both the Mishnah which permits the woman’s relatives to the man, and the general principle that in marriage matters only those statements count which can be backed up by the testimony of two witnesses. The case cannot be compared to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 3:3:2-4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.3.3.2-4.3"><i>Yebamot</i> 3:4</a> where a man is forced to divorce his (rabbinically) preliminarily married wife and give <i>ḥalîṣah</i> to her sister.<br>“Fellow” is used here not in the technical sense of a person observing the laws of ritual purity (cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Demay</i>), but somewhat learned in the law.</i>?

Halakhah 12

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> In any case of a not sinful preliminary marriage, the child follows the father<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote">The child belongs to the same marriage class (detailed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.4.1.1">Mishnah 4:1</a>) as his father.</i>. Who is this? A priestly, or Levitic, or Israel woman married to a Cohen, a Levite, or an Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote">As the Halakhah points out, this list is far from complete; it contains only those cases in which there is absolutely no controversy.</i>. And in any case of a sinful preliminary marriage, the child follows the blemished part. Who is this? A widow [married to] a High priest, a divorcee or one who has received <i>ḥalîṣah</i> to a common priest<sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote">The divorcee is forbidden to any Cohen by biblical decree (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:7" href="/Leviticus.21.7"><i>Lev</i>. 21:7</a>); the woman having received <i>ḥalîṣah</i> is a divorcee only by common practice. The child is “desecrated” in the sense of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:7" href="/Leviticus.21.7"><i>Lev</i>. 21:7</a>; the marriage prohibition is transferred to future generations.</i>, a bastard<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote">Who is forbidden any endogamous marriage, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:3" href="/Deuteronomy.23.3"><i>Deut</i>. 23:3</a>.</i> or a Gibeonite<sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.4">Halakhah 4:1. Notes 46 – 71</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 3:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.3.1.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 3:1, Notes 16–19</a>.</i> to an Israel, the daughter of an Israel to a Gibeonite or a bastard.<br>In any case where a preliminary marriage with him is impossible but with others is possible, the child is a bastard. Who is this? This is one having sexual relations with any woman forbidden in the Torah because of incest or adultery<sup class="footnote-marker">233</sup><i class="footnote">The child is a bastard in all cases of adultery; in cases of incest only those mentioned as deadly sins in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18" href="/Leviticus.18"><i>Lev</i>. 18</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 20" href="/Leviticus.20">20</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 4:13:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.4.13.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 4:13</a>.</i>. And in any case where a preliminary marriage is impossible with anybody, the child follows the mother. Who is this? The child of a slave girl or a Gentile woman<sup class="footnote-marker">234</sup><i class="footnote">Since preliminary marriage is a uniquely Jewish institution, Gentiles are automatically excluded since for them only definitive marriage exists (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.10">Chapter 1, Notes 33–39</a>.) The Jewish father cannot have any family relationship with his Gentile child: The widow of a man having only had a child with a Gentile woman is the widow of a childless man and subject to levirate marriage: <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 2:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.2.5.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i>2:5</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “In any case of a not sinful preliminary marriage,” etc. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish asked before Rebbi Joḥanan: If a proselyte married a bastard girl, there is no sinful preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">236</sup><i class="footnote">Following the rabbis in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 4:1:2-16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.4.1.2-16">Halakhah 4:1</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 8:2:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.8.2.9"><i>Yebamot</i> 8:2, Note 151</a>), whose opinion is that of R. Yose in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 72b" href="/Kiddushin.72b">Babli, 72b</a>; for R. Jehudah the marriage would be sinful but not punishable, not included in the list of Mishnah 14 (Maimonides, <i>Issure Bi’ah</i> 15:7).</i>, but the child follows the blemished part? He answered, everywhere Rebbi taught, he explained: “Who is this? A priestly, or Levitic, or Israel woman married to a Cohen, a Levite, or an Israel.<sup class="footnote-marker">237</sup><i class="footnote">He pointedly avoids taking a position about marriages permitted to proselytes.</i>” Rebbi Abin understood this from the second part: (In any case of a not sinful preliminary marriage, the child follows the father.) [In any case of a sinful preliminary marriage, the child follows the blemished part.]<sup class="footnote-marker">238</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is from L, the one in brackets from G. It is obvious that the G text is correct.</i> Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish asked before Rebbi Joḥanan: If a proselyte married a bastard girl, there is no sinful preliminary marriage, but the child follows the blemished part? He answered, everywhere Rebbi taught, he explained: “Who is this? A widow [married to] a High priest, a divorcee or one who has received <i>ḥalîṣah</i> to a common priest, a bastard or a Gibeonite to an Israel, the daughter of an Israel to a bastard or a Gibeonite.<sup class="footnote-marker">238</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is from L, the one in brackets from G. It is obvious that the G text is correct.</i>” Could he not have objected to him: In the case of a disqualified priest married to the daughter of an Israel there is no sinful marriage but the child follows the blemished part<sup class="footnote-marker">239</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 4:6:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.4.6.2-7">Halakhah 4:6</a>. The question is not answered since the case presents a genuine inconsistency in the Mishnah.</i>? In the case of a man taking back his divorcee after she had been remarried, there is (no)<sup class="footnote-marker">240</sup><i class="footnote">Text of L; the interpretation has to follow G.</i> sinful marriage but the child is unblemished since Rebbi Ḥiyya said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The daughter of a man taking back his divorcee after she had been remarried is enabled for the priesthood. What is the reason? [For she is an abomination,]<sup class="footnote-marker">241</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:4" href="/Deuteronomy.24.4"><i>Deut</i>. 24:4</a>; quote missing in L; it is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 4:13:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.4.13.2"><i>Yebamot</i> 4:13, Note 207</a>.</i> she is an abomination but her children are not an abomination<sup class="footnote-marker">242</sup><i class="footnote">The marriage of the remarried divorcee is sinful but not incestuous; the formulation of the Mishnah correctly excludes this case.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">243</sup><i class="footnote">As noted in G, this paragraph is quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 9:1:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.9.1.2-3"><i>Yebamot</i> 9:1</a>, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:1:3-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.1.3-4">Notes 10–14</a>; a slightly different text.</i> Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rav: A proselyte, a freedman, and a disqualified priest are permitted priestly wives. What is the reason? Qualified men are warned against disqualified women and disqualified women against qualified men but not qualified women against disqualified men nor disqualified men against qualified women. Did we not state: “they should not take, should not take”, which teaches that the woman is warned through the man? They wanted to say, qualified women against disqualified men but it is only disqualified women against qualified men.
A proselyte came to Rebbi Yose and asked him, is it permitted to marry a bastard girl? He answered him, it is permitted. He continued and came to Rebbi Jehudah, who answered him, it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">244</sup><i class="footnote">The language is somewhat difficult since for R. Jehudah proselytes are part of the “Congregation of the Eternal” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:3" href="/Deuteronomy.23.3"><i>Deut</i>. 23:3</a>), to whom marriage with bastards is forbidden (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 8:2:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.8.2.9"><i>Yebamot</i>8:2, Note 151</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 4:1:16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.4.1.16"><i>Qiddušin</i> Halakhah 4:1, Note 105</a>). But even R. Jehudah will agree that marriage of a proselyte with a bastard is not punishable.</i> but you should know that the children of such a man would be bastards before Heaven<sup class="footnote-marker">245</sup><i class="footnote">From his point of view, he is obligated to point out the implicit punishment meted out by Heaven for such an illicit union.</i>. He returned to Rebbi Yose and told him, since you knew that, why did you permit it to me at the start? He replied, I answered you what you asked me<sup class="footnote-marker">246</sup><i class="footnote">Since he holds that proselytes are not part of the “Congregation of the Eternal”, he is not obligated to point out that the children of the proselyte will remain outside the congregation if he marries a bastard.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, the proselyte can be compared to cotton<sup class="footnote-marker">247</sup><i class="footnote">Which is neutral for the prohibition of <i>ša‘aṭnez</i>. While wool and linen cannot be mixed, either one may be mixed with cotton; cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Kilaim</i>.</i>. If one wants to use it with wool it is permitted, with linen it is permitted.
One understands “the daughter of an Israel to a bastard or a Gibeonite,” for it is written: “A bastard shall not come into the Eternal’s congregation.” Why does the verse say: “<i>of his</i> shall not come”? To disqualify<sup class="footnote-marker">248</sup><i class="footnote">Marrying a bastard, while sinful, is not punishable. Therefore, one might assume the children of a bastard not to be bastards; they are not conceived in deadly sin. But <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:3" href="/Deuteronomy.23.3"><i>Deut</i>. 23:3</a> disqualifies the children from marrying in the congegration: “A bastard shall not come into the Eternal’s congregation, even the tenth generation <i>of his</i> shall not come into the Eternal’s congregation.”<br>It seems that G had here another derivation, of which no trace is left in L and <i>editio princeps</i>.</i>. Rebbi Abbu said, what means ממזר? “A strange defect.”<sup class="footnote-marker">249</sup><i class="footnote">The word for “bastard” has no etymology in the Pentateuch (following J. Barth, the root is مذر “to be dirty”).</i> This disagrees with what Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi<sup class="footnote-marker">250</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 65:2" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.65.2"><i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i> 65(2</a>), this is attributed to the Tanna R. Jehudah (bar Ilai).</i> said, “God installs singles<sup class="footnote-marker">251</sup><i class="footnote">A single person in God’s eyes cannot be a bachelor who could find mates all around him; he must be an intrinsic single who has no known marriage partner. This is the bastard who cannot marry a Jewish partner, and as a Jew is unable to contract marriage with a Gentile (but cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:13:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.13.2-4">Halakhah 15</a>).</i> as heads of families,” even if there is a male bastard at one end of the world and a female bastard at the other, the Holy One, praise to Him, brings them and copulates them with one another. Rav Huna said, a bastard does not live more than thirty days.<sup class="footnote-marker">252</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is missing in G; it is superfluous here but will be quoted later in a fitting context.</i> In the days of Rebbi Berekhiah, a Babylonian immigrated here<sup class="footnote-marker">253</sup><i class="footnote">When at the end of the Fourth Century, Jews usually emigrated from the hostile Christian Roman empire to the more welcoming Persian empire.</i>; he was informed that this was a bastard. He<sup class="footnote-marker">254</sup><i class="footnote">The bastard.</i> said to him, support me<sup class="footnote-marker">255</sup><i class="footnote">R. Berekhiah.</i>. He<sup class="footnote-marker">255</sup><i class="footnote">R. Berekhiah.</i> told him, tomorrow you will stand up in the community and I shall look for an allowance for you. He came and sat during the sermon. When he<sup class="footnote-marker">245</sup><i class="footnote">From his point of view, he is obligated to point out the implicit punishment meted out by Heaven for such an illicit union.</i> finished the sermon, he said, our brothers, support this man for he is a bastard. After the congegration had left, he<sup class="footnote-marker">254</sup><i class="footnote">The bastard.</i> said to him, rabbi, I requested material support from you but you destroyed this man’s life. He<sup class="footnote-marker">255</sup><i class="footnote">R. Berekhiah.</i> answered, by your life, life<sup class="footnote-marker">256</sup><i class="footnote">Following the reading of G.</i> I gave you for Rebbi Abba, Rav Huna, said in the name of Rav: A bastard lives only thirty days. When? If he is not publicly known. But when he is publicly known, he survives.
<sup class="footnote-marker">257</sup><i class="footnote">This is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 8:3:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.8.3.9"><i>Yebamot</i> 8:3, Note 201</a>; an addition to Rav Huna’s statement. The text is missing in G.</i> When Rebbi Ze‘ira immigrated here, he heard voices call “he-bastard, she-bastard”. He said, what is this? There goes that of Rav Huna, since Rav Huna said, no bastard lives more than thirty days. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said to him, I was with you when Rebbi Abba, Rav Huna said in the name of Rav: No bastard lives more than thirty days, when? If he is not publicly known. Therefore, if he is publicly known, he lives.
It was stated: “If a Gentile or a slave had intercourse with a Jewish woman, the child is a bastard<sup class="footnote-marker">258</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 7:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.7.6.2"><i>Yebamot</i> 7:6 (Note 129</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 3:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.3.1.4"><i>Ketubot</i>3:1 (Note 22</a>), <i>Giṭṭin</i> (Note 123); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 45a" href="/Yevamot.45a">Babli <i>Yebamot</i> 45a</a>. The entire <i>baraita</i> in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kiddushin (Lieberman) 4:15" href="/Tosefta_Kiddushin_(Lieberman).4.15">Tosephta 4:15</a>.</i>; Rebbi Simeon ben Jehudah says in the name of Rebbi Simeon, the child is not a bastard, for a bastard is only from a woman forbidden to him by an incest prohibition for which the penalty is extirpation<sup class="footnote-marker">259</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 4:14:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.4.14.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 4:14</a>.</i>.” <sup class="footnote-marker">260</sup><i class="footnote">In G, the following is a statement by R. Abbahu in the name of R. Joḥanan.</i> Both of them explained the same verse, “nobody may take his father’s wife, etc.”<sup class="footnote-marker">261</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel discussion, among different Tannaïm, is reported in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 4:15:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.4.15.2-3"><i>Yebamot</i> 4:15 (Notes 211–218</a>). The prohibition of the father’s wife (who is not the mother) is repeated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:1" href="/Deuteronomy.23.1"><i>Deut</i>. 23:1</a> from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18:8" href="/Leviticus.18.8"><i>Lev</i>. 18:8</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 20:11" href="/Leviticus.20.11">20:11</a>, to be connected with the exclusion of the bastard from endogamous marriage. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:3" href="/Deuteronomy.23.3"><i>Deut</i>. 23:3</a>. It is understood that a man’s child from his father’s wife is a bastard. The problem is to discern the legal principle which implies that the child is a bastard.</i> Rebbi Meïr explains: Since his father’s wife is special in that he cannot have preliminary marriage with her, but she could have preliminary marriage with others, and the child is a bastard, so in all cases in which she cannot have preliminary marriage with him, the child is a bastard<sup class="footnote-marker">262</sup><i class="footnote">This text, adopted by the <i>editio princeps</i>, is the corrector’s text. The second clause and a logical connection are missing from the scribe’s original text which makes no sense as it stands:<br>רְבִּי מֵאִיר דָּרַשׁ. מָה אֵשֶׁת אָבִיו מְיוּחֶדֶת שֶׁאֵין לוֹ עָלֶיהָ קִידוּשִׁין. אֲבָל אִם הָיָה לָהּ עָלָיו קִידוּשִׁין הַוְלָד מַמְזֶר.<br>Rebbi Meir explains: Since his father's wife is special in that he cannot have preliminary marriage with her, but if she could have preliminary marriage with him, the child is a bastard.<br>Reading of G (lacunae in the text filled in brackets):<br>רְבִּי מֵאִיר דָּרַשׁ. מָה אֵשֶׁת אָב מְיוּחֶדֶת שֶׁאֵין לוֹ עָלֶיהָ [קִידוּשִׁין הַוְלַד מִמְזֵר. אֲבָל] כָּל־שֶׁאֵין לָהּ עָלָיו קִידוּשִׁין הַוְלָד מַמְזֶר.<br>Rebbi Meïr explains: Since his father's wife is special in that he cannot have preliminary marriage with her, so in any case in which she cannot have preliminary marriage with him, the child is a bastard.<br>It seems that the original text underlying L was the text of G.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Jehudah explains: Since his father’s wife is special in that she cannot have preliminary marriage with him, but she could have preliminary marriage with others, and the child is a bastard; this excludes the Gentile and the slave who cannot contract preliminary marriage with her or with others<sup class="footnote-marker">244</sup><i class="footnote">The language is somewhat difficult since for R. Jehudah proselytes are part of the “Congregation of the Eternal” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:3" href="/Deuteronomy.23.3"><i>Deut</i>. 23:3</a>), to whom marriage with bastards is forbidden (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 8:2:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.8.2.9"><i>Yebamot</i>8:2, Note 151</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 4:1:16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.4.1.16"><i>Qiddušin</i> Halakhah 4:1, Note 105</a>). But even R. Jehudah will agree that marriage of a proselyte with a bastard is not punishable.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Abba questions that first Tanna: The sister-in-law who whored cannot have preliminary marriage with him or with others<sup class="footnote-marker">263</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:1:21" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.1.21"><i>Yebamot</i> 1:1, Notes 93–94</a>. The widow of the childless man is obligated to definitively marry his brother in levirate; by biblical standards this requires no preliminary marriage. By the same token, she is unable to contract preliminary marriage with any other man before being released by <i>ḥalîṣah</i> (cf. Halakhah 5, Note 166). Any sexual relations she might have during the time she is waiting for her levir are sinful but neither adulterous nor punishable.</i>, but the child is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">264</sup><i class="footnote">To contract marriage “in the Eternal’s congregation.”</i>! Rebbi Yannai in the name of Rebbi: If a Gentile or a slave had intercourse with a Jewish woman, the child is a bastard. Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish both say, the child is a bastard. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Simeon bar Abba, Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, in the name of Rebbi (Yannai ben)<sup class="footnote-marker">265</sup><i class="footnote">There is no R. Yannai ben Rebbi. Later the statement is quoted in the name of R. Gamliel (IV) ben Rebbi. G is illegible at this point.</i> Rebbi: The child is neither qualified nor disqualified but dirtied<sup class="footnote-marker">266</sup><i class="footnote">If the child is female, she is not eligible as marriage partner of a Cohen.</i>. Rebbi Jonathan went with Rebbi Jehudah Neśia to the hot springs of Gadara. There, he instructed: The child is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">267</sup><i class="footnote">Even for a Cohen.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, this child improves as time goes by. Rebbi declared the child dirtied, his grandson declared him qualified. Rebbi Abba bar Zavda instructed in the name of all rabbis: The child is qualified. Rebbi Bevai said before Rebbi Ze‘ira in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: The child is qualified. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, if it is from that, one does not learn from a declaration. Rebbi Ḥizqiah said, I know the story from beginning to end. Rebbi Ḥama bar Ḥanina went up to the hot springs of Gadara. He visited his father who said to him: Be careful, because there are those who are disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">268</sup><i class="footnote">Children of Jewish women from Gentile fathers.</i>, lest you injure them.
<sup class="footnote-marker">269</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 4:15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.4.15"><i>Yebamot</i>4:15, Notes 215–218</a>. G only refers to that source.</i> Even though Rebbi Simeon ben Jehudah said in the name of Rebbi Simeon: If a Gentile or a slave came to a daughter of Israel, the child is acceptable; they agree that if she was female, she is blemished for the priesthood. Even though Rebbi Joshua said, the child of him who sleeps with his sister is acceptable, he agrees that if the child was female she would be blemished for the priesthood. A man came to Rav and said, since the children of this man’s mother are from an Aramean? He said, acceptable. Rav Ḥama bar Guria said to him, let your feet carry you away before Samuel comes and declares you blemished. Even though Rav said, if a Gentile or a slave came to a daughter of Israel, the child is acceptable, he agrees that if it was a female, she would be blemished for the priesthood.
Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said: There<sup class="footnote-marker">270</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 45b" href="/Yevamot.45b">Babli <i>Yebamot</i>45b</a>.</i>, they state that the one who disqualifies, disqualifies from an unmarried woman, and the one who qualifies, qualifies even from a married woman<sup class="footnote-marker">271</sup><i class="footnote">R. Meïr who declares the child of a Gentile and a Jewish woman a bastard, does so even if the woman was single. R. Simeon ben Jehudah who declares the child qualified does so even if the mother was a Jewish woman married to a Jew. Practice in the Babli is decided according to R. Simeon ben Jehudah.</i>. Rebbi Tanḥum bar Pappus sent and asked Rebbi Yose about two cases from Alexandria, one involving an unmarried woman, the other a married woman. About the married woman, he wrote him: “A bastard shall not come into the Eternal’s congregation.<sup class="footnote-marker">272</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:1" href="/Deuteronomy.23.1"><i>Deut</i>. 23:1</a>. He decided against the Babylonian practice.</i>” About the unmarried woman he wrote, be inventive<sup class="footnote-marker">273</sup><i class="footnote">The expression מבדין is a <i>hapax legomenon</i>. It can be derived from Aramaic בדי “to invent a story”, or from a multiplicity of Arabic roots. In G one reads “you should recognize that”, meaning that both the girl and her child can marry in the congregation. The only argument one has for the text of L is that it is <i>lectio difficilior</i>.</i>, for your are not forewarned about the holy daughters of Israel. He<sup class="footnote-marker">274</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi Yose (the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 45b" href="/Yevamot.45b">Babli, <i>Yebamot</i>45b</a>, is undecided whether it was R. Yose bar Abin or R. Yose bar Zavida) who declared the child of the married woman a bastard and that of the single woman qualified. He collected signatures from the members of his Academy to stress the importance of his decision.</i> said to Rebbi Mana, take and sign; he signed. He said to Rebbi Berekhiah, take and sign, but he did not agree. After they ended the session Rebbi Mana was standing with Rebbi Berekhiah and asked him, why did you not sign? He answered, did not Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa say that there, they state that the one who disqualifies, disqualifies from an unmarried woman, and the one who qualifies, qualifies even from a married woman<sup class="footnote-marker">275</sup><i class="footnote">How can you qualify the child of the unmarried woman if the child of the married woman is a bastard?</i>? He said, praised be the Merciful that I had not heard this doctrine, for if I had heard this doctrine I would not have signed and people would have said that the student<sup class="footnote-marker">276</sup><i class="footnote">R. Mana, the student of R. Yose.</i> disagrees with his teacher. Mar Uqban said, here, you say that the one who disqualifies, disqualifies from an unmarried woman, and the one who qualifies, qualifies from an unmarried woman, but about a married woman, nothing was said. The next morning he<sup class="footnote-marker">277</sup><i class="footnote">R. Berekhiah, who had heard from Mar Uqban that R. Yose decided correctly according to the Galilean tradition.</i> came and wanted to sign. He<sup class="footnote-marker">278</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose, who apparently was offended by R. Berekhiah’s behavior. R. Berekhiah was a great preacher but no great authority in law.</i> said to him, there is no space.
Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba went to Tyre. When he came before Rebbi Joḥanan, he asked him, what case did you handle? He said to him, a proselyte who had been circumcised but had not immersed himself<sup class="footnote-marker">279</sup><i class="footnote">From the context it seems clear that the proselyte had married a Jewish wife and R. Ḥiyya bar Abba declared the child qualified to marry in the congregation even though his teacher R. Joḥanan held that the child of a Gentile and a Jewish mother was a bastard.</i>. He asked him, why did you not injure him<sup class="footnote-marker">280</sup><i class="footnote">And declare the child a bastard since the father did not complete the process of conversion.</i>? Rebbi Joshua ben Levi<sup class="footnote-marker">281</sup><i class="footnote">Of the generation of R. Joḥanan’s teachers.</i> told him, leave him alone, he acted correctly not to injure him. The argument of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi seems inverted. There, Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Simeon bar Abba, Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, in the name of Rebbi Gamliel ben Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">255</sup><i class="footnote">R. Berekhiah.</i>: The child is neither qualified nor disqualified but dirtied<sup class="footnote-marker">282</sup><i class="footnote">Then at least R. Ḥiyya bar Abba should have declared any female child ineligible for the priesthood.</i>. And here, he says so? Did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">283</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of the <i>baraita</i> is missing in G.</i>: If a proselyte was circumcised but not immersed, immersed but not circumcised, everything depends on circumcision, the words of Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">284</sup><i class="footnote">Circumcision is mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:48" href="/Exodus.12.48"><i>Ex</i>. 12:48</a> as formal requirement for conversion, but nothing else.</i>. Rebbi Joshua says, immersion also is necessary<sup class="footnote-marker">285</sup><i class="footnote">Since all the Childen of Israel, when they were converted to the Jewish faith at Mount Sinai, had to immerse themselves, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 19:10" href="/Exodus.19.10"><i>Ex</i>. 19:10</a>.</i>. But Rebbi Joshua ben Levi follows what Bar Qappara stated, as Bar Qappara stated: A proselyte who was circumcised but not immersed is qualified since there is no proselyte who did not immerse himself for his emission of semen<sup class="footnote-marker">286</sup><i class="footnote">Which is required even for lawful intercourse, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:16" href="/Leviticus.15.16"><i>Lev</i>. 15:16</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:18" href="/Leviticus.15.18">18</a>.</i>. Is that not difficult? The cleansing from a minor impurity is counted for him for a major impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">287</sup><i class="footnote">The essence of immersion in water is not the fact of immersion but the intent of the action. The impurity of an emission is minor since it can be remedied immediately. The entrance of a proselyte into the Jewish system of impurity is a major undertaking. How can a routine immersion be the equivalent of a major ceremony?</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Abun said, since either one is for the holiness of Israel, it is counted for him<sup class="footnote-marker">288</sup><i class="footnote">The argument is quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 45b" href="/Yevamot.45b">Babli, <i>Yebamot</i> 45b</a>, in the name of R. Joshua ben Levi; a similar principle is promulgated by Rav Assi for a woman proselyte who follows the rules of family purity.</i>.
“Who is this? The child of a slave girl or a Gentile<sup class="footnote-marker">234</sup><i class="footnote">Since preliminary marriage is a uniquely Jewish institution, Gentiles are automatically excluded since for them only definitive marriage exists (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.10">Chapter 1, Notes 33–39</a>.) The Jewish father cannot have any family relationship with his Gentile child: The widow of a man having only had a child with a Gentile woman is the widow of a childless man and subject to levirate marriage: <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 2:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.2.5.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i>2:5</a>.</i>.” There<sup class="footnote-marker">289</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 2:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.2.6.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 2:6</a>. As long as the mother is Jewish, there is no difference between legitimate and illegitimate children; but children from a Gentile or servile mother are not their father’s children.</i>, we have stated: “Except what he has from a slave girl or a Gentile.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">290</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the Halakhah, the text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 2:6:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.2.6.2-5"><i>Yebamot</i> 2:6</a> (ב), Notes 140–146. G only has a note to continue the text in <i>Yebamot</i>, Chapter 2. The story about Jacob from Kefar Naboraia is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 7:2" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.7.2"><i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i>7(2</a>), <i>Num</i>.<i>rabba</i> 19, <i>Pesiqta rabbati</i> 14 ed. M. Friedmann 62b, <i>Pesiqta dR</i>.<i>Cahana</i> (Buber) <i>Parah</i> Note 99, <i>Tanḥuma Ḥuqqat</i> 6, <i>Tanḥuma Buber Ḥuqqat</i> 15; <i>Eccl</i>.<i>rabbati</i> 7(44).</i> A slave woman: “The woman and her children shall belong to her owner.” A Gentile woman. Rebbi Joḥanan [said] in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Ioḥai, it is written: “You shall not conclude marriage with them, your daughter you shall not give to his son”. And it is written: “For he will divert your son from following Me.” Your son from a Jewish woman is called your son; but your son from a Gentile woman is not called your son but her son.
Jacob from Kefar Naboraia went to Tyre. They came to ask him, may one circumcise the son of a Gentile woman on the Sabbath? He wanted to permit it from the verse: “They determined their genealogies for their families according to their fathers’ houses.” Rebbi Ḥaggai heard it and said, he shall come and be whipped. He said to him, for what reason do you whip me? He said, from what is written: “Now we shall make a covenant to send away all foreign women and those born to them, etc”. He said, you want to whip me based on tradition? He said, “and it should be done according to the Torah.” He said to him, from which teaching? He said, from that which Rebbi Joḥanan said in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Ioḥai, it is written: “You shall not conclude marriage with them,” etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">291</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted in the preceding paragraph.</i> He said to him, whip your whipping, because it is good to impress.

Halakhah 13

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Tarphon says, bastards can cleanse themselves. How is this? If the bastard married a slave girl, the child is a slave. When he is manumitted, the son is a free person<sup class="footnote-marker">292</sup><i class="footnote">Naturally, the son is not legally his father's son and heir; he cannot inherit except by a will. But he will be able to marry in the congregation.</i>. Rebbi Eliezer said, he is a slave and a bastard<sup class="footnote-marker">293</sup><i class="footnote">In his opinion, manumission of the slave will not remove the stigma of bastardy.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Rebbi Tarphon says, bastards can cleanse themselves,” etc. So is the Mishnah: A bastard is permitted to marry a slave girl<sup class="footnote-marker">294</sup><i class="footnote">This is not an emendation of the Mishnah, but a logical inference. In general, a Jew is not allowed to sleep with a slave girl since any sexual relations with a person whom he could not marry violate <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:18" href="/Deuteronomy.23.18"><i>Deut</i>. 23:18</a>. But R. Tarphon implies that the informal marriage of a bastard with a slave girl (or a free Gentile woman) has rabbinic blessing since 1) the bastard is precluded from contracting a marriage with members of the congregation and 2) having the possibility of sexual relations is a basic human right (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 1:28" href="/Genesis.1.28"><i>Gen</i>. 1:28</a>). The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 69a" href="/Kiddushin.69a">Babli agrees, 69a</a>.</i>. Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel: Practice follows Rebbi Tarphon<sup class="footnote-marker">295</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 69a" href="/Kiddushin.69a">Babli, 69a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Simlai instructed in Antiochia<sup class="footnote-marker">296</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Babli, his landlord was a bastard; he counseled him how to have marriageable children.</i>, Rebbi Simai instructed in Kefar Seforaia<sup class="footnote-marker">297</sup><i class="footnote">The name of the locality cannot be determined either from L (different versions of scribe and corrector) or from G.</i>: Practice follows Rebbi Tarphon.
<sup class="footnote-marker">298</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kiddushin (Lieberman) 5:11-12" href="/Tosefta_Kiddushin_(Lieberman).5.11-12">Tosephta 5:11–12</a>; in slightly different form <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 99a" href="/Yevamot.99a">Babli <i>Yebamot</i> 99a</a>.</i>“It was stated in the name of Rebbi Meïr: It can happen that a man and a woman give birth to five nations<sup class="footnote-marker">299</sup><i class="footnote">A nation is defined here as a group of people following identical marriage rules.</i>. How is this? [A Gentile]<sup class="footnote-marker">300</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of G and Tosephta; implied by L.</i> had a male and a female slave who had children: these are Gentiles<sup class="footnote-marker">301</sup><i class="footnote">As far as Jewish marriage laws are concerned, there is no difference between a Gentile free person and a Gentile slave.</i>. If one of them<sup class="footnote-marker">302</sup><i class="footnote">One of the children.</i> became Jewish, one is a proselyte and one a Gentile. If their master converted and converted them<sup class="footnote-marker">303</sup><i class="footnote">If the parents become slaves in a Jewish household, the children will be slaves under Jewish law.</i> as slaves, [any children] are slaves.” Rebbi Ze‘ira said, this implies that if a Gentile had intercourse with a slave girl, the child is a slave<sup class="footnote-marker">304</sup><i class="footnote">This rule is presupposed everywhere in the Yerushalmi. Since the slave girl cannot marry and is forbidden as sex partner to any free Jew by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:18" href="/Deuteronomy.23.18"><i>Deut</i>. 23:18</a>, she has the right to freely associate both with slaves and Gentiles. The children of the slave mother always inherit their mother’s status; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:13:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.13.1">Mishnah 3:15</a>.</i>. “If he manumitted the slave girl and she had a son, the child is a bastard<sup class="footnote-marker">305</sup><i class="footnote">Once the woman is freed, if she continuous to live with the slave who was her de-facto husband as a slave, the child is a bastard for R. Meïr (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:12:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.12.5">Note 252</a>).</i>. If afterwards he manumitted the slave and he begat a child, he is a freed slave.<sup class="footnote-marker">306</sup><i class="footnote">Following <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:13:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.13.1">Mishnah 3:15</a>, there is marriage without sin; the child obtains the status of his father.</i>” <sup class="footnote-marker">307</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kiddushin (Lieberman) 5:13" href="/Tosefta_Kiddushin_(Lieberman).5.13">Tosephta 5:13</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 99a" href="/Yevamot.99a">Babli <i>Yebamot</i>99a</a>.</i>“It can happen that a man sells his father to pay his mother’s <i>ketubah</i>. How is this? Somebody has a male and a female slave and they have a son. He manumitted the female slave, married her, and willed all his property to her son. That is one who sells his father to pay his mother’s <i>ketubah</i>.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">308</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kiddushin (Lieberman) 5:4" href="/Tosefta_Kiddushin_(Lieberman).5.4">Tosephta 5:4</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 72b" href="/Kiddushin.72b">Babli 72b</a>, in different formulation.</i>“Rebbi Meïr says, bastards will not be purified in the future, since it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">309</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Za</i>. 9:6.</i>: ‘The bastard will dwell in Ashdod’; one carried garbage to garbage and deviant to deviant. Rebbi Yose says, bastards will be purified in the future, since it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">310</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 36:25" href="/Ezekiel.36.25"><i>Ez</i>. 36:25</a>.</i>: “I shall pour upon you pure water [and purify you]<sup class="footnote-marker">283</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of the <i>baraita</i> is missing in G.</i>.” Rebbi Meïr said to him, but it says only “from all your impurities and all your abominations I shall purify you.” Rebbi Yose said to him, if it had only said, “from all your impurities and all your abominations” and then stopped, I would have agreed with you. But since it adds “I shall purify you<sup class="footnote-marker">311</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted in G; the “etc.” in L is better talmudic style, not quoting the part of the verse to which one really refers.</i>”, from bastardy. Rebbi Huna said in the name of Rav Joseph, practice in the future will not follow Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">312</sup><i class="footnote">A repetition not logically necessary.</i>.

Chapter 4



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Ten marriage classes returned from Babylonia: Priests, Levites, Israel, desecrated priests<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Descendants of the forbidden union of a priest and a divorcee or a prostitute.</i>, proselytes, freedmen, bastards<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Descendants of an adulterous or incestuous union involving a deadly sin.</i>, dedicated ones<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Gibeonites, cf. Notes 46 ff.</i>, silenced ones<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 4:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.4.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>, children with a known mother but unknown father.</i>, foundlings. Priests, Levites, and Israel are permitted to marry one another. Levites, Israel, proselytes, freedmen, and desecrated priests are permitted to marry one another<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The marriage class of the child being that of the father by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:12:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.12.1">Mishnah 3:14</a>.</i>. Proselytes, freedmen, bastards, silenced ones, and foundlings are permitted to marry one another.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Ten marriage classes returned from Babylonia,” etc. Priests, Levites, and Israel, as described<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezra 7:7" href="/Ezra.7.7"><i>Ezra</i> 7:7</a>.</i>: “There came of the Children of Israel, and of the priests, the Levites, both the servants<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This is clearly a copyist’s error; it must read “singers” as in the masoretic text. Even at the end of the Second Temple period, levitic singers and doorkeepers formed two clearly identified classes in the Temple service. All the distinctions in marriage classes here are clearly attributed to Ezra, not to the early returnees under Sheshbazzar and Zerubabel.</i> and the doorkeepers, and the Temple slaves, to Jerusalem in the seventh year of king Artaxerxes.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This quote seems to be missing in G.</i>”
“Desecrated priest.” Following<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezra 2:61-63" href="/Ezra.2.61-63"><i>Ezra</i> 2:61–63</a>. The verses prove that there were desecrated priests coming from Babylonia, as asserted in the Mishnah.</i> “from the sons of Cohanim, the family Ḥavaya, the family Haqqoṣ . .<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Not mentioned here is the statement that their problem was rooted in the fact that their ancestors 500 years earlier had married the daughters of Barzilai the Gileadite; this is the subject of the following paragraph. The quote then shows that the category of “desecrated priest” is hereditary.</i> . they tried to find their genealogical documents; but they were not found and they were freed from priesthood. And the <i>tiršata</i><sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The Persian title of the governor, “the one to be feared”. The title is applied to Nehemiah in 8:9; therefore, traditionally the appellation is interpreted as a kind of nickname of Nehemiah (as is explicit in G) and as such the Iranian word is read as an Aramaic pun. It is clear from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nehemiah 7" href="/Nehemiah.7"><i>Neh</i>. 7</a> that the title in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezra 2" href="/Ezra.2"><i>Ezra</i> 2</a> cannot apply to Nehemiah; it probably refers to Sheshbazzar.</i> told them.” Why was he called <i>hattiršata</i>? Because they permitted him to drink of the wine<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">As cup-bearer to the Persian monarch, Nehemiah certainly had to taste the wine to make sure it was not poisoned. But drinking Gentile wine is forbidden in rabbinic Judaism. Since Nehemiah shows himself to be strictly orthodox in <i>Ezra</i> and <i>Nehemiah</i>, the natural assumption is that he had obtained a special dispensation from the religious authorities of the time. The interpretation reads the name הַתִּרְשָׁתָא (which never appears without initial ה) not as a noun with definite article but a composite הַתֵּר-שָׁתָא “permitted drinker.” The explanation is quoted by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Kiddushin 69b:9:3" href="/Rashi_on_Kiddushin.69b.9.3">Rashi, 69b, s.v. התרשתא</a>.</i>. <i>Hatiršata</i>, “I was the king’s cup bearer.” “That they should not eat from what was dedicated as holy<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the Yerushalmi is in disagreement with the vocalization by the Masoretes. In the Pentateuch, to which the verses clearly are referring, masoretic vocalization strictly differentiates between the noun קֳדָשִׁים “<i>sancta</i>, sacrifices” and the superlative adjective קָדָשִׁים “most holy”. Since the mss. of the Ben-Asher tradition uniformly write קֳדָשִׁים in the verse here, it is clear that the masoretic interpretation of the verse forbade <i>all</i> holy food to the desecrated priests, whether Temple sacrifices or “outside <i>sancta</i>”, i. e., heave and tithes. But the Yerushalmi, based on the Mishnaic distinction between קָדְשֵׁי קָדָשִׁים “most holy sacrifices”, destined exclusively for the altar and/or legitimate priests in the Temple precinct, קֳדָשִׁם קַלִּים “simple sacrifices” (well-being offerings) shared between altar, priests, and the donor’s family, and קָדְשֵׁי הַגְּבוּל “outside <i>sancta</i>”, here reads מִקָּדְשֵׁי הַקָּדָשִׁים “from most holy sacrifices.” This immediately raises the question why desecrated priests are not summarily excluded from <i>all</i> holy food (as they are in rabbinic practice). As far as the fragmentary text of G can be interpreted, this is exactly the question asked, which is missing in L and <i>editio princeps</i>: “[If they are genuine priests] they should eat Temple <i>sancta</i>; if they are not genuine priests [they should be excluded from all <i>sancta</i>.]” The Yerushalmi is quoted by Rashi as explanation of the Babli, 69b <i>s. v.</i> ואמר להם.</i>.” Therefore, from outside<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Maybe better: “Borderline <i>sancta</i>.”</i> holy food they might eat? Rebbi Yose said, great is the permanence of the <i>status quo ante</i><sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.3.3"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 3:4 Note 87</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.4.3"><i>Ketubot</i>5:5 Note 100</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 15:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.15.10.1"><i>Yebamot</i> 15:12 Notes 181</a>. An echo of the discussion here is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 69b" href="/Kiddushin.69b">Babli, 69b</a>.</i> Since they were used to eat there<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia, an impure country, where heaves and tithes were given to the priests as a remembrance of biblical rules, not a biblical obligation.</i>, they may also eat here. One understands there, as it is written: “Put up signposts for yourself.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 31:20" href="/Jeremiah.31.20"><i>Jer</i>. 31:20</a>. The verse is read as exhortation to symbolically observe in the diaspora also those biblical precepts which are intrinsically bound to the Land (<i>Threni rabbati</i> 1:62).</i>” What can one say here<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Since heave and heave of the tithe in the Land require genuine purity and genuine priests.</i>? Following him who said, they accepted tithes voluntarily<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:8:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.8.2-6">Halakhah 1:9</a>, <i>Ševi‘it</i> 6:1.</i>. “Until a priest will be appointed for <i>urim</i> and <i>tummim</i>.” But were there any <i>urim</i> and <i>tummim</i> then, in the Second Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>urim wetummim</i> oracle was never part of the High Priest’s vestment in the Second Temple since its nature was unknown.</i>? But [he is] like a person who says until the dead will be resurrected, until David’s son<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The Messiah.</i> will come.
<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. the discussion in pp. 17–20 of Introduction to Talmudic Literature in the first volume, <i>Berakhot</i>, of this edition of the Yerushalmi.</i> Did those daughters of Barzilai’s<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">There seems to be no indication in the Bible that Barzilai was not Jewish. But in <a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 17:27" href="/II_Samuel.17.27"><i>2S</i>. 17:27</a> Barzilai the Gileadite is associated with Šobi ben Naḥaš from Rabbat Ammon in providing for David’s army in his flight from Absalom.
